HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Product Liability Ruling Favors Consumer Suits
The Supreme Court ruled that consumers injured by faulty medical devices may seek damages, under state law, from the manufacturers even if the devices comply with Federal regulations.
A victory for the manufacturers would have had the effect of barring all private damage suits for such injuries, in any court. [B9.]
 
Quaker Oats Earnings to Be Cut
Quaker Oats said that the cost of a new campaign to increase sales of Snapple drinks would result in losses for the unit this year and that it was cutting most cereal prices an average of 15 percent. The company estimated that its 1996 earnings would be reduced by $30 million to $40 million. [D3.]
 
Astra Executives Are Dismissed
Astra USA, the American subsidiary of Sweden's biggest public company, said that it had dismissed its president and chief executive, Lars Bildman, left, and one other top executive, and that two others had resigned after an in-house investigation of accusations of sexual harassment. [D2.]
 
Bell Atlantic-Nynex Deal Changes
Seeking to avoid a Congressional vote on their proposed $20.8 billion "merger of equals," Bell Atlantic said it would acquire Nynex instead. The deal's financial terms will not change, nor will the ratio at which Nynex shareholders exchange stock for Bell Atlantic shares. [D4.]
 
Cajun Electric Draws Another Bid
The latest bid for some assets of the Cajun Electric Power Cooperative of Louisiana, which is under bankruptcy court protection, came from a unit of the Southern Company -- for $1.04 billion in cash. [D2.]
 
Durable Goods Orders Rise
Strong demand for new commercial aircraft drove up durable goods orders for May at the sharpest rate in nine months, the Commerce Department said. [D3.]
 
Mountain Dew Is Criticized
A promotion for Mountain Dew that offers young people discounted pagers with advertising messages has angered children's advocates who see it as manipulation. But Mountain Dew is trumpeting the promotion as innovative. [D7.]
 
Crude Oil Prices Rise Sharply
Crude oil prices rose sharply on the New York Mercantile Exchange yesterday after a deadly explosion at an American complex in Saudi Arabia raised concern that political and social tension could disrupt oil output. [D6.]
 
Dole Campaign to Focus on Economy
Bob Dole's campaign advisers say they are planning to attack President Clinton's economic record, contending that Mr. Clinton does not deserve credit for improved economic performance, and should be blamed for tax increases and spending policies that have undermined growth. [A1.]
 
Connecticut's Power Shortage
Experts say that no other region of the country has faced such a power shortage as Connecticut, because of nuclear plant safety problems. The state is so short of power that the oldest, most polluting plants are scheduled to run full out for the summer. Three nuclear power plants that can supply half the state's needs on the hottest day are shut and not likely to open soon. [B1.]
 
China Approves Foreign Law Firms
China's Justice Ministry approved the opening of offices by 16 international law firms today -- including five from the United States -- reflecting a brighter climate in Chinese-American business relations. [D8.]
 
Microsoft and Nintendo Team Up
In the first partnership between Nintendo and Microsoft, the companies said they would deliver information and entertainment, mainly from the Internet, to personal computers via satellite broadcasting. This service will be offered only in Japan but might be applied elsewhere, Microsoft said. [D8.]
 
Sumitomo Approves Plan on Losses
Stockholders of Sumitomo approved a company plan to cover losses in what Sumitomo has described as unauthorized transactions over more than a decade by its chief copper trader. [D19.]

HEADLINE: Dole Campaign Plans an Attack On Clinton's Economic Record

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
Bob Dole's campaign advisers say they are planning a stepped-up attack on President Clinton's economic record, having concluded that despite low unemployment and inflation there is enough anxiety among voters about job security and stagnant incomes to leave the Administration vulnerable.
Their strategy, the advisers said, would be to argue that Mr. Clinton does not deserve credit for the ways in which the nation's economy is performing well, and should be blamed for tax increases and spending policies that they contend have undermined economic growth, creating a climate of insecurity and forcing workers to run harder just to stay in place. 
 The advisers said the economic campaign would begin in earnest this fall, after Mr. Dole announced his own economic policy package, probably about the time the Republican National Convention is held in San Diego this August.
Mr. Dole, the party's likely Presidential nominee, is expected to embrace a tax-cutting package as the centerpiece of a "pro-growth" economic platform, although he has yet to decide on details.
If the Dole campaign follows through on the plan to attack Mr. Clinton's economic record, it would be seeking to turn to its advantage -- or at least to neutralize -- an issue that the Administration has long considered one of its strengths.
The President often boasts of the economy's performance in creating more than eight million jobs since he took office, most of them paying good wages. He says the current combination of low inflation and unemployment with steady growth is one that no other Administration has sustained since the 1960's.
Democrats said they viewed the Dole camp as shifting away from a strategy of hoping that the economy would weaken this year, which has not happened, to one of asking whether this is the best the economy can do -- a question they said they are happy to debate.
"If they want to go head to head on the economic record, let's rock and roll," said Gene Sperling, an economic adviser to the President.
While Mr. Dole regularly criticizes Mr. Clinton for raising taxes and for not being truly committed to balancing the Federal budget, he has only occasionally articulated broader economic themes.
The former Kansas Senator has spoken only in passing about such issues as corporate downsizing, and has offered few specific prescriptions for improving the nation's long-term economic performance or addressing worker anxiety.
Mr. Dole's advisers have floated a variety of proposals in the last two months, including an across-the-board tax cut and incentives to increase savings and investment.
While Mr. Dole is likely to adopt some form of tax cut, neither he nor his aides have given any indication of how they could cut taxes without calling for politically unpopular spending cuts or undermining Mr. Dole's longtime reputation for fiscal rectitude.
And while the candidate's advisers clearly see political opportunity in the anxiety felt by workers, they are likely to avoid any direct attacks on corporate America for laying off workers; instead, they will probably focus on broader issues of how to allow the economy to create more jobs.
Formulation of Mr. Dole's economic plan is being overseen by Vin Weber, a former Minnesota Congressman who is coordinating the campaign's overall policy development with Donald H. Rumsfeld, who was White House chief of staff under President Gerald R. Ford.
They are synthesizing research and ideas from a group of informal advisers that includes Senators Connie Mack of Florida and Robert F. Bennett of Utah; Robert Lighthizer, a former Senate aide who is now a Washington lawyer and Dole campaign official, and a group of economists whose work is being coordinated by John Taylor of Stanford University.
Mr. Dole met with Mr. Taylor last week in California to discuss policy ideas, campaign aides said.
Mr. Dole has at times appeared uncomfortable in pressing economic themes. Last February, in the New Hampshire primary campaign, he said he was surprised that jobs and trade had become such big issues. Battling to save his candidacy from the early successes of Patrick J. Buchanan's populist economic themes, Mr. Dole engaged in some tentative corporation-bashing over the issue of downsizing, but then dropped it.
If Mr. Dole's own proposals remain in flux, however, his campaign has already settled on the themes it will use against Mr. Clinton: that although the nation has enjoyed some economic successes in the past few years, they are largely a legacy of Republican policies from the 1980's, and that the nation has been robbed of its full growth potential by the President's 1993 tax increase and his refusal last year to sign the balanced-budget legislation that was passed by the Republican-controlled Congress.
"It's no mystery what's holding America back," Mr. Dole said in a speech in Michigan earlier this month, one of just a handful he has devoted to economic themes. "It's the wrong-headed, outdated, liberal policies that can all be summed up in one word: Clintonomics. And you know, one of the most amazing things about the present Administration is they actually boast about the anemic, substandard growth their policies have created."
A top adviser to Mr. Dole said a full-scale attack on Mr. Clinton's economic policies would be delayed until after the Republican convention to allow Mr. Dole time to finish developing his own package and to avoid "frittering away" an issue that the campaign believes can illustrate the differences between the candidates.
"The economic issues will be a core part of the fall campaign, and Dole's economic package will be, if not the centerpiece of the campaign, then a central element in the positive agenda of why he wants to be President," said the adviser, who spoke on condition of anonymity.
"What we have is an economy that has created enough jobs and is strong enough that the conventional wisdom is that Clinton is less vulnerable on the economy than on other issues," the adviser said, adding:
"What the research we've been doing indicates to us is that there is a significant amount of economic anxiety out there -- not just among blue-collar and the more vulnerable members of the work force, but anxiety that is spread among women and men that does not discontinue when you hit the middle class. There is no question that the anxiety is such that the incumbent is vulnerable."
In addition to tax cuts and fundamental changes in the tax system, the Dole campaign is considering programs to encourage more savings, speed the budget-balancing process, slash government regulation of business, create incentives for more job training programs and overhaul the education system.
"We have the ammunition to undermine Clinton's commitment to growth, to tax reform and to less spending," said Senator Mack, who has been arguing for months that worker anxiety gives Republicans an opportunity to take a more aggressive stance on economic issues.
"That's one reason the Dole folks look forward to that kind of debate," he added. "They sense vulnerability."
In a memorandum last month to summarize the views of Mr. Dole's economic advisers, Mr. Taylor, the Stanford economist, laid out an argument that acknowledges the positive aspects of the nation's economic performance, but denies the President any credit for it.
"Thanks to economic policies put in place in the early 1980's to reduce inflation and keep it low, the U.S. economy is now experiencing, back-to-back, the two longest peacetime expansions in its history," Mr. Taylor wrote.
He said the first expansion begin in 1982 in the Reagan Administration and the second in 1991 after a recession during the Bush Administration.
The most pressing problem now, Mr. Taylor wrote, is a long-term trend of declines in productivity -- output per worker or per hour of work -- which most economists agree is the most important determinant of overall economic growth.
In the Clinton Administration, he said, productivity growth has fallen to new lows, resulting in weak wage growth and a declining savings rate.
Earlier this month ,in an economic debate sponsored by Dow Jones, Mr. Lighthizer said: "We think this is the worst recovery on record, in terms of G.D.P. growth, in terms of productivity growth, in terms of wage increases. The fact is that this economy is good for some people. It is not good for most people. It is clearly worse than it was during the Reagan expansion."

HEADLINE: BOMBING IN SAUDI ARABIA: THE SAUDIS;
Bombing Attack Raises Questions About Stability of Saudi Government

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
The bombing attack against an American target in Saudi Arabia could not have come at a worse time for the ruling royal family, coinciding with a vacuum in the Saudi leadership and increasing opposition to American influence in the kingdom.
After a stroke last November, King Fahd lost much of his short-term memory, but he can still vividly remember stories from his childhood. "He moves in and out of lucidity," a senior American intelligence official said. "He'll be in the middle of a meeting and all of a sudden will say, 'Where am I? What am I doing here?' " 
 After the stroke the King briefly handed power to Crown Prince Abdullah, his half-brother and designated heir. The Crown Prince began to run the day-to-day operations of Government, but without the authority to make major policy decisions.
These tensions within the ruling family have made it more difficult to deal with an increasingly militant opposition, which argues that the conservative Islamic kingdom, which strictly enforces Islamic law, is not Islamic enough.
It is not known whether Tuesday's attack was carried out by Saudi Arabia's growing Islamic opposition or inspired by a foreign power.
But the assumption of the White House, the State Department, the Pentagon and the Federal Bureau of Investigation is that the truck bombing is linked to a similar attack last November on an American military compound in Riyadh, the capital.
"This is the time for decisive decision-making and that's precisely what the Saudis don't have because there is no decision-maker," said Vahan Zanoyan, senior director of the Petroleum Finance Company, a private consulting firm in Washington, and an expert on the kingdom. "Even though the succession is clear if Fahd dies tomorrow, he is not dead yet. So who runs the show?"
Early this year, for example, when Crown Prince Abdullah tried to pay Saudi farmers at least part of $2.8 billion in subsidies the Government owed them, he was blocked by other family members who thought he was overstepping his authority.
Prince Sultan bin Abd al-Aziz al-Saud, the Minister of Defense, and Prince Nayif bin Abd al-Aziz al-Saud, the Minister of the Interior, both of whom are brothers of the King, were particularly incensed, according to people close to the royal family. The result was that in February, King Fahd announced that he was better and that he was resuming control.
The Administration plays down the significance of King Fahd's illness.
"The King did call Clinton yesterday, and my understanding is that it went well," said Ray Mabus, who recently left the post as Ambassador to Saudi Arabia to work on Mr. Clinton's re-election campaign. "Fahd expressed his condolences and said he would spare no effort in tracking down those responsible and punishing them."
But Mr. Mabus acknowledged that since the King was speaking in Arabic through an interpreter, it was hard to judge just how lucid he was.
Other American officials say Prince Abdullah is essentially in charge of the kingdom. They point to the fact that it was he who agreed to a request from Mr. Clinton to help finance the arming and training of the Bosnian Army, contributing $50 million to the effort. He has also filled in for the King in recent months in various Arab summit meetings.
Although the Saudis believed initially that the November attack, which left five Americans and two Indians dead, could only have been carried out by a foreign enemy like Iran, Iraq or the Sudan, it turned out to have been home-grown. Four Saudi citizens were arrested in connection with the attack in April and beheaded a few weeks later.
All four were in their mid-20's, had limited education and worked at low-paying jobs in Riyadh. In confessions, which read as though they had been dictated and may have been coerced, three said they had fought on the side of the Muslim rebels against the Soviet troops in Afghanistan, where they received religious, weapons and explosives training. One said he had fought on the side of the Muslim-led Bosnian Government in the Balkans.
The men also acknowledged ideological links to well-known Islamic extremists abroad, including Osama bin Laden, a wealthy Saudi businessman living in the Sudan, and the Islamic Group, which has tried to overthrow the Egyptian Government for four years.
The Administration was dismayed by the swiftness of the executions, which eliminated any opportunity to further interrogate the four men to determine whether there may have others involved and who may have financed and organized the plot.
Although there is no proof yet, the Administration is concerned that Tuesday's bombing may have been carried out in retaliation for the executions and that opposition to the Saudi Government may be much more serious than was previously thought. That raises the question of the continued American military presence in Saudi Arabia.
The kingdom cracked down on internal opposition in 1994, arresting some Islamic leaders and expelling others. But it has failed to address the basic problems that contribute to the discontent: mounting unemployment, a population explosion (75 percent of the population is under the age of 30) and rampant corruption, particularly within the royal family.
The United States, which is identified closely with King Fahd, is an easy and visible target.
"By striking a blow at a U.S. military target, you're attacking the Saudi regime," an American official said.
The fact that the bombing was aimed at an American military target and not at a Saudi oil installation suggests, to some experts, that the attackers may want to challenge specific policies of the Saudi Government, not overthrow it.
"Damaging the oil sector would damage more than the Government." said Mr. Zanoyan. "It would damage the country."

HEADLINE: BOMBING IN SAUDI ARABIA: THE OVERVIEW;
Saudis, Aided by the F.B.I., Seek Blast Clues

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS JEHL 

DATELINE: DHAHRAN, Saudi Arabia, June 26

 BODY:
Surveying the ruins left by a blast that tore the face from an eight-story apartment building in a military complex here on Tuesday night and killed 19 American servicemen, American and Saudi officials insisted today that the attack would not diminish their close military cooperation.
The enormous explosion, which officials said was significantly more powerful than the bomb that demolished the Oklahoma City Federal Building in April 1995, left bathtubs and mattresses dangling from a structure rent open as if it were a doll house. It blew out windows a half-mile away, and wounded hundreds. 
 Secretary of State Warren Christopher, who interrupted a Middle East peacemaking trip to fly here and survey the damage, declared the blast "a direct and deliberate attack on the citizens of the United States and our friends and allies."
Twenty-four hours after the explosion, neither the motive nor the identity of those responsible was known, and American and Saudi officials said it was too soon to speculate about who would have carried out the attack, at the largest air base in Saudi Arabia.
American officials said a team sent by the Federal Bureau of Investigation was already moving in to assist Saudi investigators search for the clues that might identify who was behind the blast.
But they emphasized that the Saudi authorities would take the lead in the inquiry.
The bombing, the second aimed at American military personnel in Saudi Arabia in seven months, compounded an atmosphere of uncertainty in the country at a time when King Fahd has been weakened by a stroke and the leadership is contending with mounting internal dissent. [Page A11.]
Both Mr. Christopher and Prince Saud al-Faisal, the Saudi Foreign Minister, vowed that the attack would not deter their two countries from their extensive military cooperation, which has brought thousands of American service personnel to bases here as part of an effort to contain the threats posed by Iran and Iraq.
Visibly moved as he walked through broken glass and concrete rubble as dusk fell this evening, Mr. Christopher said, "It's a very bleak day for all of us."
All 19 people killed in the attack were Americans, and all were apparently inside the building at the edge of the sprawling compound just after 10 P.M. on Tuesday when a man stopped a fuel truck packed with explosives just outside the perimeter fence. The man then climbed into an accomplice's vehicle, leaving a fused bomb to erupt four minutes later and dig a crater 30 feet deep. Because of confusion at the scene, the initial death toll was reported to be as high as 23.
Similarities between Tuesday's attack and the bombing last November of a United States military compound in Riyadh, the Saudi capital, which left five American civilians and two Indians dead, prompted suspicions of a possible link. Tuesday's blast came despite or perhaps in retaliation for the public beheading last month of four Saudis who confessed to having carried out the November attack.
A telephoned threat to the United States Embassy in Riyadh after the executions vowed venegeance for the four killings. Today a man called the London-based Arabic language newspaper Al Arab and claimed responsibility for Tuesday's attack in the name of a previously unknown group. He vowed that it would carry out similar attacks unless the Saudis expelled all American and other foreign troops "occupying the holy Saudi land."
Other early theories pointed toward Iraq and Iran, whom the United States and its allies in the region have sought to isolate by a policy of containment backed by military might.
The only mission of the Air Force unit whose personnel bore the brunt of the attack is to patrol the skies over southern Iraq to enforce strict United Nations-backed provisions put in place after the 1991 Persian Gulf war to protect a Shiite Muslim minority.
Among the threats received by the United States Embassy in Riyadh and consulates since the November blast have been crude drawings implying that that bomb was only a beginning.
For the last six weeks news of the drawings has been placed on a recorded message for the attention of callers to the embassy.
Yet while that message has urged American citizens to exercise caution, it avoided sounding outright alarm. Even this morning callers heard an assurance that "threats of this nature are not surprising given the security threat in this region."
Tuesday's bombings followed repeated warnings that American targets in Saudi Arabia might again be hit by such a blast, and accounts provided by military officials here raised early questions about whether adequate security measures had been taken.
While armed United States military personnel backed up by guard dogs patrol extensively inside the Khobar Towers compound, the officials said jurisdiction outside the perimeter fence, where the powerful bomb exploded, is left to the Saudi civil police, who maintain a less visibile presence.
Prince Faisal, the Saudi Foreign Minister, appeared to acknowledge that there might have been shortcomings in that system, saying at a news conference this evening that "from every tragedy, there are lessons."
But officials from both countries took pains to insist that the attack would do nothing to affect a military partnership that has endured for decades and which both regard as vital to maintaining security in the Persian Gulf region.
Only since the Persian Gulf war have the Saudis allowed the United States to base military personnel in the kingdom in substantial numbers. The increased visibility of the Americans has become a focus for criticism by Islamic conservatives who regard the Western influence as corrupting.
Brig. Gen. Terry Schwalier of the Air Force, the commanding officer of the 4404th Air Wing and its arsenal of nearly 70 F-15's, F-16's and other warplanes, said he had been satisfied with the protection provided by the Saudi police.
And as Mr. Christopher visited a military hospital to which many of the more than 400 wounded were taken, Prince Bandar bin Sultan, the Saudi Ambassador to the United States, said of the explosion, "I think it's an attack on U.S.-Saudi relations, and it will not succeed."
Altogether, doctors at King Abdulaziz Armed Forces hospital said, the wounded included more than 250 Americans, 100 of whom were taken to the hospital. About 65 were expected to remain there overnight. The less seriously wounded included 147 Saudis and 118 Bangladeshis, many of whom were workers in the compound and were cut by flying glass.
American military officials said the glass had broken as a wave of pressure unloosed by the bomb passed through the compound, shattering windows and toppling furniture.
Tonight the site was eerily quiet as search efforts came to a halt, with everyone accounted for. Water from the nearby Persian Gulf had already formed a small lake at the bottom of the bomb crater.
From Washington today, there were signs that President Clinton might seek to turn the tragedy to a gain. Leaving for a meeting in Lyons, France, of the world's big industrialized countries, Mr. Clinton said he hoped to use the gathering to rally support for measures aimed at combatting terrorism and those who support it.
Mr. Christopher, who began his day with meetings in Cairo aimed at pursuing the quest for Middle East peace after the election of Benjamin Netanyahu as Israel's Prime Minister, also sought to make a more enduring point after he hurried here to look first-hand at the consequences of terrorist violence.
"We and our friends around the world will not be intimidated," said Mr. Christopher, who stopped to see Mr. Netanyahu in Israel on Monday and who met this morning with President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt and with Yasir Arafat, the Palestinian leader. He vowed "to defend peace and those who take risks for peace."
 
A Trail of Rubble: Mideast and Bombings
 
BEIRUT, April 1983 Muslim fundamentalist suicide bomber wrecks U.S. Embassy, killing 63 people, including 16 Americans.
 
BEIRUT, October 1983 Shiite Muslim suicide bombers kill 241 U.S. servicemen and 58 French paratroopers in attacks on military bases of a multinational force.
 
BEIRUT, September 1984 Explosives-laden station wagon explodes in front of U.S. Embassy annex, killing 11 people.
 
PARIS, 1985-86 Bombings kill 13 people and injure 250. Group demanding release of Lebanese guerrillas takes responsibility.
 
BERLIN, April 1986 Bomb at nightclub popular with off-duty U.S. soldiers kills two U.S. servicemen and injures 200. The United States blames Libya.
 
LOCKERBIE, Scotland, December 1988 Pan Am Flight 103 from London to New York blows up over Lockerbie, killing 270 people. United States and Britain later accuse two Libyans of responsibility.
 
NIGER, September 1989 DC-10 of the French airline UTA blows up over Sahara while on a flight to Paris, killing 171 people. France blames Libyan agents.
 
NEW YORK, February 1993 Six people killed and more than 1,000 injured in bomb at the World Trade Center. Four followers of an Egyptian fundamentalist leader, Sheik Omar Abdul Rahman, are convicted. The sheik and 12 supporters are found guilty of plotting to blow up other U.S. targets and assassinate political leaders
 
CAIRO, 1993-1994 Muslim militants begin a series of bomb attacks, many directed against Western tourists, in a campaign that has so far claimed 920 lives, including 26 tourists.
 
PARIS, July-October 1995 Eight bombings in France, claimed by guerrillas of the Armed Islamic Group fighting to overthrow the Algiers Government, kill 7 and injure 160.

HEADLINE: Storming the Showroom Citadels

BYLINE: By DAVID COLMAN 

 BODY:
WERE Hercules around today, he would have many, many new labors: toxic-waste dumps to clean, monster budget deficits to wrestle, rogue twisters to tame. And if he had recently acquired a sofa, he might be faced with a task that has foiled many a mortal: buying 30 yards of fine fabric without the help of a decorator.
But for consumers flying solo, shopping for designer fabrics like those in glossy magazines need not require epic fortitude, as I discovered over the course of several weeks in several neighborhoods. 
 Intrepid souls have a range of options, from the workaday decorating departments in places like Janovic Plaza and Gracious Home, right up to the more stylish upholstery-and-decor shops that will guide them from start to finish.
For those who really know what they want, there are the high-end showrooms, where fabrics can cost as little as $20 a yard or as much as $2,000. Economic pressures on these fabric houses have helped pave an unofficial path for consumers to the linens, ducks and velvets of their dreams. Their hallowed "to the trade only" policy is beginning to crumble.
 
Behind the Chintz Curtain
If an unscientific but determined exploration of the legendary Design and Decoration Building recently by a decorator-less bachelor looking for 30 yards of cotton velvet was any indication, even the most haughty will not turn consumers away from a little browsing.
Christopher Kennedy, who manages New York's most prominent design center, at 979 Third Avenue near 58th Street, described the building's new gentler, kinder snobbery: "We're trying to make it a nicer place to get thrown out of."
The policy hasn't changed much since the building opened in 1964: showrooms can admit casual customers but will not sell directly to them. Their preferred customers are professionals who return day after day, week after week. And who have threatened in the past to boycott any showroom that does sell retail. (Many showroom owners, as well as Mr. Kennedy, say it is an open secret that certain showrooms in the D & D building are indeed selling to retail customers.)
For amateur decorators, the good news is that the official policy doesn't really matter. While no one made overtures to sell me fabric, nearly all showrooms were happy to furnish the name of a buying service, decorator or upholsterer who could easily arrange a purchase, either for a fee or a percentage above net price. That would have been considered design heresy five years ago, said Arlene Hirst, a senior editor at Metropolitan Home magazine.
"People have lots of other places to shop," she said, "and showrooms have learned that they have to court the public."
Showrooms are not as exclusive as they once were, especially those that overexpanded in the late 1980's to accommodate the expanding upper-middle class.
"The middle market is drying up," said Christopher Hyland, who has a showroom of specialty fabric on the 17th floor. "Twenty-five years ago, if someone earned a high salary they could put their kids through prep school and college and put damask on the walls. Today, you can barely put the kids through school. We're talking about 80 percent of the population having almost 30 percent less buying power."
Garry Greenberg, the owner of Beckenstein Home Fabrics on Orchard Street, a business that acts as a liaison between consumers and showrooms, agrees. "The mid-to-high-end customer -- my customer -- is definitely off," he said. "I hear the same thing from all my vendors in the D & D Building, that business is off by as much as 30 percent." But he and Mr. Hyland say the high-end manufacturers are not suffering.
On my midday expedition in and around the D & D Building, even the most famously imposing names in fabric -- Clarence House, F. Schumacher & Company, Manuel Canovas, Brunschwig & Fils, Donghia, Osborne & Little -- seemed easily accessible for anyone with the gumption to walk past the supposedly mandatory sign-in books that keep out the fainthearted (most professional decorators do not sign in).
Once inside, things were even easier. Almost all the showroom employees were friendly and extremely adept at suggesting durable, subdued and inexpensive fabrics suitable for my tastes, even if they wouldn't quote exact prices. This was a pleasant surprise since showrooms often say they don't want to sell retail because their staff members are not equipped to answer hundreds of naive questions.
One less-than-friendly exception was Scalamandre, a few doors down from the D & D building, at 934 Third Avenue, where a representative was not happy to hear that I was not a decorator nor was I sure that I planned to hire one. And she was not amused when I told her that all I wanted was a way to buy 30 yards of fabric. When I asked for a ballpark figure on what her leopard-print silk-cotton-viscose velvet might run me, she had had enough.
"You're going to upholster a sofa in that?" she asked, clearly appalled.
Hercules, meet Scalamandre.
But at the showroom of F. Schumacher & Company, at 939 Third Avenue, a showroom employee went so far as to furnish an "800" number, which, when called, offered the telephone numbers of seven sources in Manhattan that could order the house's fabric. One of these, the International Design Supply, on Houston Street, said it would charge 15 percent to 20 percent above the showroom's net price. (Traditionally, decorators charge clients 30 percent to 50 percent.)
The Donghia showroom in the D & D building was to me the most cordial, with enthusiastic employees and even mini-samples of house fabrics that I could take home.
Such services circumvent the need to print up fake "decorator" business cards, a trick of the non-trade. Should you be tempted, know that most showrooms now ask for more professional evidence -- a resale number -- before they will sell.
But Mr. Hyland says that getting the fabric is only half the battle, and that the margin for error with costly materials is great. He thinks the glut of how-to magazines and television shows has created a can-do consumer culture that is deceptively shallow.
"Having a surface knowledge of something is not enough," he said. "Arguing against using the trained designer is like arguing against trained nurses in hospitals."
Ms. Hirst of Metropolitan Home, who favors a system in which anyone can buy from showrooms at retail prices, says designers are adapting to changes in the industry by billing for fabrics at cost and then charging clients flat fees or hourly rates for their services. "What younger designers are selling now is their talent, not access to the D & D," she said.
Thad Hayes, 40, a sought-after decorator who bills this way, agrees. "The days when designers just sold access are over," he said.
Scalamandre is trying to keep a foot in both camps. This fall, the luxury fabric house is opening four retail stores to replace its Silk Surplus discount outlets. Bob Bitter, a principal of the company, says the new stores, called Berenzelli Fabrics, will be edited-down, ready-to-wear versions of Scalamandre, with 150 to 200 fabrics priced less than its couture line (forget the tassels).
 
Getting the Whole 9 Yards
Scalamandre may be reacting in part to the success of ABC Carpet & Home, on Broadway at 19th Street, which renovated its vast second floor two summers ago as an antidote to trade-only showrooms. The store recently added casual collections, with dozens of bolts of colorful cottons, at around $20 a yard.
"I could even bring clients here," said Mr. Hayes on a recent excursion around the more than 3,000 bolts of fabric. "The selection is incredible, and what's really important if you're shopping for fabric for a sofa is that you can see what a lot of the fabric will look like. You can't do that with sample books."
Their strength is in cash-and-carry: While silk-and-cotton damasks are $103 a yard, an ABC spokeswoman said that the median price for fabric was $30. The house specialties, Italian chenille velvets, paisleys and jacquards, are $38 to $75 a yard.
Being surrounded by yards of luxurious possibilities, accessible to the touch, made the atmosphere more interactive than the showrooms. Most of the merchandise is in stock, and ABC will upholster furniture and or make slipcovers.
The great strength of Richard's Interiors, a tiny shop at 1390 Lexington Avenue at 92d Street, is its dressmaker's-shop air of authority. Walls are decked to the neufs with full-scale window treatments for the decor-impaired. Behind little desks piled with swatches and order forms, hard-as-laquered-nails staff members trade sarcastic battle stories about dealing with haughty D & D personnel. They will shop there with you, but they can also provide an overwhelming number of sample books from nearly every Manhattan fabric showroom requested. They offer prices 20 percent to 25 percent above net.
"I would feel the most comfortable here if I were a consumer," Mr. Hayes said.
Le Decor Francais, at 1006 Lexington Avenue near 72d Street, is patterned after shops in France or England, where no widespread "to the trade" policy holds. A small, bright, stylish store, it has 500 to 700 fabrics imported from France, some of which are also available in the D & D building.
While the employees call their fashionable selection and in-house upholstery services a welcome alternative to hiring a decorator or dealing with fabric showrooms, their advice left something to be desired during my recent visit. When asked for a simple fabric for my sofa, they suggested several wildly bright and graphic patterns that would look more at home in a Miami penthouse than in my small, dark living room.
 
The Bare Threads
Gracious Home and Janovic Plaza offered rather standard fabric selections from several showrooms, including Schumacher and Kravet, and the selection in their sample books was meager compared with that of Richard's Interiors and ABC. Both stores lacked an atmosphere that was conducive to fantasizing about decor.
But employees at both stores said they were well versed at maneuvering through the D & D building, selling fabric at 25 percent to 30 percent over net prices. Gracious Home designers can also go to showrooms customers to shop for fabric, at $75 an hour. Both stores do upholstery.
International Design Supply, at 50 East Houston Street, near Mulberry Street, has thousands of mid-cost fabrics from places like Kravet and Robert Allen. Employees will also shop at some D & D showrooms for clients, adding for 15 percent to 20 percent to the net price.
"It's great for someone who lives downtown," Mr. Hayes said, "but the industrial atmosphere really seems better geared to professionals. I really would only go here if I knew exactly what I wanted."
 
Looking Ahead
The future may hold even more options. This week, the Merchandise Mart in Chicago (which was bought by Christopher Kennedy's grandfather Joseph Kennedy in 1945) opened a design-buying service in conjunction with Bloomingdale's. Customers can meet store designers in the market and shop with them -- at retail -- charging purchases to their Bloomingdale's card. Asked whether he thought the service would work in New York, Mr. Kennedy would only say, "All the parties are interested and are watching this very closely."
But for some, the tried-and-true method may be best of all: leave all the headaches to a decorator. "The truth is that decorators save people money," Mr. Hyland said. "If only I had $1,000 for every client I heard come in here who said, 'I wish I'd gone to a designer first instead of trying to do this myself.' "
Ask Jane Nicholas, a record industry executive who scoured the D & D building and all the discount houses for fabric to cover a Jean-Michel Frank sofa bought at Palazzetti. Finally, with the help of a designer friend, she bought red-and-gold chenille with a distinctive medallion pattern at Mr. Hyland's showroom.
But the story was just beginning. Following the bad advice of a fabric designer who said she would consult on upholstery for a fee, Ms. Nicholas purchased too little fabric: her adviser forgot to tell her that the intricacy of a fabric's design might require extra fabric. By the time Ms. Nicholas discovered her error, the Italian factory had moved on to a different dye-lot. Then, they closed for vacation.
Nine months passed before she got the sofa home. Looking back on the ordeal, which was not an uncommon one, she had one piece of advice.
"If I could do it over again," she said, "I'd hire a decorator."

HEADLINE: In Health Care's New Era, Catholic Institutions Link Up to Compete

BYLINE: By LISA W. FODERARO 

 BODY:
More than 30 Catholic hospitals and nursing homes in the New York region are banding together in a new health care network intended to help its members compete in an era of managed care and to strengthen the church's mission of treating the poor.
The Archdiocese of New York is expected to announce today the formation of the Catholic Health Care Network of New York, which will serve 2.5 million patients a year from Staten Island to Poughkeepsie. 
 The new network is the latest in a flurry of hospital mergers and consolidations, prompted by cuts in Medicaid reimbursements and cost-cutting pressures from insurers and health maintenance organizations.
The move was hailed as a sign that the Roman Catholic Church, whose health care agencies are praised for their high quality and relatively low cost, was determined to secure a place in the quickly shifting terrain of health care.
"I don't see this as a desperation move but as an affirmation," said James R. Tallon, president of the United Hospital Fund, an independent philanthropic and health services research organization in New York City. "They have affirmed that they will be long-term players in delivering health services in this city."
The 9 hospitals and 22 nursing homes in the new network will share medical information by computer and, to save money, will jointly purchase major new medical equipment. Other possible cost savings could come from the creation of a single medical laboratory, which would replace individual on-site labs, and the joint contracting of laundry services.
"The primary goal of our network is to promote the Catholic health-care ministry," said Dr. Mary Healey-Sedutto, the network's executive director and chief executive officer. "But to that end, we realize that we must do everything that we can to maintain the viability and solvency of our institutions."
Officials said they had no plans for layoffs or reductions in hospital and nursing home staffs. However, the network's two sponsors, the Archdiocese of New York and the Sisters of Charity of St. Vincent de Paul of New York, will bring in a team of executives to identify services for potential consolidation.
The biggest financial benefit will arise from the network's strengthened position in negotiating contracts with health-maintenance organizations. All but one of the network members are in the 10-county region of the Archdiocese of New York, providing geographic reach along with a wide range of services and medical specialties.
Dr. Sedutto said the network was close to signing potentially lucrative contracts with two major managed-care companies, which she declined to name while negotiations are pending. Each company represents several hundred thousand people and would give the new network a flat annual fee for each covered patient.
In the past, under a looser alliance that had existed among some 40 Catholic health care institutions in the region, health-maintenance organizations would pick and choose hospitals for inclusion in their plans. Now, according to Dr. Sedutto, no one will be left out of major deals.
"Through the network, we will be able to negotiate contracts with managed-care companies that are inclusive of all our institutions," she said. "That helps us with our ultimate objective, which is serving the poor in all of our communities."
In New York City, as in the rest of the nation, Catholic health agencies have traditionally served a disproportionate share of poor people. In some of the archdiocese's hospitals and nursing homes, more than half of patients are on Medicaid, the Federal insurance program for the poor. And as many as 90 percent of outpatients in some places, such as St. Clare's Hospital, are on Medicaid.
The flagship of the network will be St. Vincent's Hospital in Greenwich Village. The network will be affiliated with the New York Medical College in Valhalla. Other hospitals in the system include Calvary Hospital in the Bronx, Bayley-Seton Hospital on Staten Island and St. Joseph's Medical Center in Yonkers.
Dr. Sedutto said she expected the network to grow in the future, with the possible addition of health agencies outside New York and even secular health-care institutions. But the members would have to share, in the words of John Cardinal O'Connor, Archbishop of New York, the "values of the church -- charity, justice, respect for life in all its stages and special concern for the poor."
Kenneth E. Raske, president of the Greater New York Hospital Association, which represents 174 hospitals and nursing homes throughout the metropolitan area, applauded the archdiocese's plan.
"This is a solid effort at positioning the Catholic-sponsored institutions in a fashion that can achieve enormous economies of scale and reduce costs and produce highly competitive services," he said.
The archdiocese's effort to become more competitive could not be more timely. On Monday, New York State's rate-setting system is scheduled to expire. Under the current system the state essentially sets all reimbursement rates for patients on Medicaid, as well as those who are privately insured. But starting next week, hospitals will have to negotiate with insurance companies on the prices for services.
Hospitals have increasingly sought to join ranks in recent months, either in loose affiliations or formal mergers. Earlier this month, New York University Medical Center and Mount Sinai Medical Center announced plans to merge.

HEADLINE: THE SUPREME COURT;
Excerpts From High Court's Ruling Against the Male-Only Policy of V.M.I.

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from the Supreme Court's decision today in United States v. Virginia, holding that the equal protection guarantee of the 14th Amendment requires admission of women to the all-male Virginia Military Institute. 
 Justice Ruth Bader Ginsburg wrote the majority opinion, which was joined by Justices John Paul Stevens, Sandra Day O'Connor, Anthony M. Kennedy, David H. Souter and Stephen G. Breyer. Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist filed a separate concurring opinion. Justice Antonin Scalia dissented. Justice Clarence Thomas did not participate in the case.
 
FROM THE DECISION
By Justice Ginsburg
Virginia's public institutions of higher learning include an incomparable military college, Virginia Military Institute (V.M.I.). The United States maintains that the Constitution's equal protection guarantee precludes Virginia from reserving exclusively to men the unique educational opportunities V.M.I. affords. We agree.
Founded in 1839, V.M.I. is today the sole single-sex school among Virginia's 15 public institutions of higher learning. V.M.I.'s distinctive mission is to produce "citizen-soldiers," men prepared for leadership in civilian life and in military service. V.M.I. pursues this mission through pervasive training of a kind not available anywhere else in Virginia.
Assigning prime place to character development, V.M.I. uses an "adversative method" modeled on English public schools and once characteristic of military instruction. V.M.I. constantly endeavors to instill physical and mental discipline in its cadets and impart to them a strong moral code. The school's graduates leave V.M.I. with heightened comprehension of their capacity to deal with duress and stress and a large sense of accomplishment for completing the hazardous course.
V.M.I. has notably succeeded in its mission to produce leaders; among its alumni are military generals, members of Congress and business executives. The school's alumni overwhelmingly perceive that their V.M.I. training helped them to realize their personal goals. V.M.I.'s endowment reflects the loyalty of its graduates; V.M.I. has the largest per-student endowment of all undergraduate institutions in the nation.
Neither the goal of producing citizen-soldiers nor V.M.I.'s implementing methodology is inherently unsuitable to women. And the school's impressive record in producing leaders has made admission desirable to some women. Nevertheless, Virginia has elected to preserve exclusively for men the advantages and opportunities a V.M.I. education affords. . . .
We note, once again, the core instruction of this Court's path-marking decisions in J.E.B. v. Alabama: Parties who seek to defend gender-based government action must demonstrate an "exceedingly persuasive justification" for that action. . . .
The burden of justification is demanding and it rests entirely on the state. The state must show "at least that the (challenged) classification serves 'important governmental objectives and that the discriminatory means employed' are 'substantially related to the achievement of those objectives.' " The justification must be genuine, not hypothesized or invented post hoc in response to litigation. And it must not rely on overbroad generalizations about the different talents, capacities, or preferences of males and females. . . .
"Inherent differences" between men and women, we have come to appreciate, remain cause for celebration, but not for denigration of the members of either sex or for artificial constraints on an individual's opportunity. Sex classifications may be used to compensate women "for particular economic disabilities (they have) suffered," to "promot(e) equal employment opportunity," to advance full development of the talent and capacities of our nation's people. But such classifications may not be used, as they once were to create or perpetuate the legal, social and economic inferiority of women.
Measuring the record in this case against the review standard just described, we conclude that Virginia has shown no "exceedingly persuasive justification" for excluding all women from the citizen-soldier training afforded by V.M.I. We therefore affirm the Fourth Circuit's initial judgment, which held that Virginia had violated the 14th Amendment's equal protection clause. Because the remedy proffered by Virginia, the Mary Baldwin V.W.I.L. [Virginia Women's Institute for Leadership] program, does not cure the constitutional violation, i.e., it does not provide equal opportunity, we reverse the Fourth Circuit's final judgment in this case.
The Fourth Circuit initially held that Virginia had advanced no state policy by which it could justify, under equal protection principles, its determination "to afford V.M.I.'s unique type of program to men and not to women." Virginia challenges that "liability" ruling and asserts two justifications in defense of V.M.I.'s exclusion of women.
First, the commonwealth contends, "single-sex education provides important educational benefits," and the option of single-sex education contributes to "diversity in educational approaches." Second, the commonwealth argues, "the unique V.M.I. method of character development and leadership training," the school's adversative approach, would have to be modified were V.M.I. to admit women. We consider these two justifications in turn.
Single-sex education affords pedagogical benefits to at least some students, Virginia emphasizes, and that reality is uncontested in this litigation. Similarly, it is not disputed that diversity among public educational institutions can serve the public good. But Virginia has not shown that V.M.I. was established, or has been maintained, with a view to diversifying, by its categorical exclusion of women, educational opportunities within the state. In cases of this genre, our precedent instructs that "benign" justifications proffered in defense of categorical exclusions will not be accepted automatically; a tenable justification must describe actual state purposes, not rationalizations for actions in fact differently grounded. . . .
A purpose genuinely to advance an array of educational options, as the court of appeals recognized, is not served by V.M.I.'s historic and constant plan, a plan to "affor(d) a unique educational benefit only to males." However "liberally" this plan serves the state's sons, it makes no provision whatever for her daughters. That is not equal protection.
Virginia next argues that V.M.I.'s adversative method of training provides educational benefits that cannot be made available, unmodified, to women. Alterations to accommodate women would necessarily be "radical," so "drastic," Virginia asserts, as to transform, indeed "destroy," V.M.I.'s program. . . .
Education, to be sure, is not a "one size fits all" business. The issue, however, is not whether "women or men should be forced to attend V.M.I."; rather, the question is whether the state can constitutionally deny to women who have the will and capacity, the training and attendant opportunities that V.M.I. uniquely affords. . . .
Women's successful entry into the Federal military academies, and their participation in the nation's military forces, indicate that Virginia's fears for the future of V.M.I. may not be solidly grounded. The state's justification for excluding all women from "citizen-soldier" training for which some are qualified, in any event, cannot rank as "exceedingly persuasive," as we have explained and applied that standard. . . .
In the second phase of the litigation, Virginia presented its remedial plan: maintain V.M.I. as a male-only college and create V.W.I.L. (Virginia Women's Institute for Leadership) as a separate program for women. The plan met district court approval. The Fourth Circuit, in turn, deferentially reviewed the state's proposal and decided that the two single-sex programs directly served Virginia's reasserted purposes: single-gender education, and "achieving the results of an adversative method in a military environment." Inspecting the V.M.I. and V.W.I.L. educational programs to determine whether they "afford(ed) to both genders benefits comparable in substance, (if) not in form and detail," the court of appeals concluded that Virginia had arranged for men and women opportunities "sufficiently comparable" to survive equal protection evaluation. The United States challenges this "remedial" ruling as pervasively misguided.
A remedial decree, this court has said, must closely fit the constitutional violation; it must be shaped to place persons unconstitutionally denied an opportunity or advantage in "the position they would have occupied in the absence of (discrimination)." The constitutional violation in this case is the categorical exclusion of women from an extraordinary educational opportunity afforded men. A proper remedy for an unconstitutional exclusion, we have explained, aims to "eliminate (so far as possible) the discriminatory effects of the past" and to "bar like discrimination in the future."
Virginia chose not to eliminate, but to leave untouched, V.M.I.'s exclusionary policy. For women only, however, Virginia proposed a separate program, different in kind from V.M.I. and unequal in tangible and intangible facilities. . . .
V.W.I.L. affords women no opportunity to experience the rigorous military training for which V.M.I. is famed. Instead, the V.W.I.L. program "de-emphasize(s)" military education and uses a "cooperative method" of education "which reinforces self-esteem." . . .
As earlier stated, generalizations about "the way women are," estimates of what is appropriate for most women, no longer justify denying opportunity to women whose talent and capacity place them outside the average description. Notably, Virginia never asserted that V.M.I.'s method of education suits most men. It is also revealing that Virginia accounted for its failure to make the V.W.I.L. experience "the entirely militaristic experience of V.M.I." on the ground that V.W.I.L. "is planned for women who do not necessarily expect to pursue military careers." By that reasoning, V.M.I.'s "entirely militaristic" program would be inappropriate for men in general or as a group, for "(o)nly about 15 percent of V.M.I. cadets enter career military service." . . .
Virginia, in sum, while maintaining V.M.I. for men only, has failed to provide any "comparable single-gender women's institution." Instead, the commonwealth has created a V.W.I.L. program fairly appraised as a "pale shadow" of V.M.I. in terms of the range of curricular choices and faculty stature, funding, prestige, alumni support and influence.
 
FROM THE DISSENT
By Justice Scalia
Today the Court shuts down an institution that has served the people of the Commonwealth of Virginia with pride and distinction for over a century and a half. To achieve that desired result, it rejects (contrary to our established practice) the factual findings of two courts below, sweeps aside the precedents of this Court and ignores the history of our people.
As to facts: it explicitly rejects the finding that there exist "gender-based developmental differences" supporting Virginia's restriction of the "adversative" method to only a men's institution and the finding that the all-male composition of the Virginia Military Institute (V.M.I.) is essential to that institution's character. As to precedent: it drastically revises our established standards for reviewing sex-based classifications. And as to history: it counts for nothing the long tradition, enduring down to the present, of men's military colleges supported by both states and the Federal Government.
Much of the Court's opinion is devoted to deprecating the closed-mindedness of our forebears with regard to women's education, and even with regard to the treatment of women in areas that have nothing to do with education. Closed-minded they were as every age is, including our own, with regard to matters it cannot guess, because it simply does not consider them debatable. The virtue of a democratic system with a First Amendment is that it readily enables the people, over time, to be persuaded that what they took for granted is not so, and to change their laws accordingly. That system is destroyed if the smug assurances of each age are removed from the democratic process and written into the Constitution.
So to counterbalance the Court's criticism of our ancestors, let me say a word in their praise: they left us free to change. The same cannot be said of this most illiberal Court, which has embarked on a course of inscribing one after another of the current preferences of the society (and in some cases only the counter-majoritarian preferences of the society's law-trained elite) into our basic law. Today it enshrines the notion that no substantial educational value is to be served by an all-men's military academy so that the decision by the people of Virginia to maintain such an institution denies equal protection to women who cannot attend that institution but can attend others. Since it is entirely clear that the Constitution of the United States -- the old one -- takes no sides in this educational debate, I dissent. . . .
I have no problem with a system of abstract tests such as rational-basis, intermediate, and strict scrutiny (though I think we can do better than applying strict scrutiny and intermediate scrutiny whenever we feel like it). Such formulas are essential to evaluating whether the new restrictions that a changing society constantly imposes upon private conduct comport with that "equal protection" our society has always accorded in the past. But in my view the function of this Court is to preserve our society's values regarding (among other things) equal protection, not to revise them; to prevent backsliding from the degree of restriction the Constitution imposed upon democratic government, not to prescribe, on our own authority, progressively higher degrees.
For that reason it is my view that, whatever abstract tests we may choose to devise, they cannot supersede and indeed ought to be crafted so as to reflect those constant and unbroken national traditions that embody the people's understanding of ambiguous constitutional texts. More specifically, it is my view that "when a practice not expressly prohibited by the text of the Bill of Rights bears the endorsement of a long tradition of open, widespread, and unchallenged use that dates back to the beginning of the Republic, we have no proper basis for striking it down." . . .
And the rationale of today's decision is sweeping: for sex-based classifications, a redefinition of intermediate scrutiny that makes it indistinguishable from strict scrutiny. Indeed, the Court indicates that if any program restricted to one sex is "uniqu(e)," it must be opened to members of the opposite sex "who have the will and capacity" to participate in it. I suggest that the single-sex program that will not be capable of being characterized as "unique" is not only unique but nonexistent.
In any event, regardless of whether the Court's rationale leaves some small amount of room for lawyers to argue, it ensures that single-sex public education is functionally dead. The costs of litigating the constitutionality of a single-sex education program, and the risks of ultimately losing that litigation, are simply too high to be embraced by public officials. . . .
Should the courts happen to interpret that vacuous phrase as establishing a standard that is not utterly impossible of achievement, there is considerable risk that whether the standard has been met will not be determined on the basis of the record evidence indeed, that will necessarily be the approach of any court that seeks to walk the path the Court has trod today. No state official in his right mind will buy such a high-cost, high-risk lawsuit by commencing a single-sex program. The enemies of single-sex education have won; by persuading only seven Justices (five would have been enough) that their view of the world is enshrined in the Constitution, they have effectively imposed that view on all 50 states.

CORRECTION-DATE: June 29, 1996, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An excerpt on Thursday from the Supreme Court opinion striking down male-only education at the Virginia Military Institute characterized the institute's per-student endowment incorrectly. V.M.I. has the largest per-student endowment of any public college in the nation, not the largest of any college.

HEADLINE: 16 Foreign Law Firms Cleared To Open Offices in China

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

DATELINE: BEIJING, June 26

 BODY:
China's Justice Ministry approved the opening of offices by 16 international law firms today, reflecting a brighter climate in Chinese-American business relations now that trade disputes have been put aside.
With foreign companies pouring into China in recent years, the need for business-related legal work has grown tremendously. Yet China has tightly limited the number of law firms allowed to fully operate. 
 Today's new licenses were the first granted since early last year, bringing the foreign law firms in China to 73. Many more are waiting for approval. Of those approved today, five are from the United States and the others are from Japan, Hong Kong, Britain, Italy and Jordan.
This round of license-granting was apparently delayed until a favorable moment in China's relations with the United States. American officials have been pushing hard for better market access for legal, insurance and banking firms.
The moment came, several foreign lawyers said, after Chinese and American trade negotiators reached agreement in Beijing last week on how to fight piracy of intellectual property. One of China's threatened retaliatory measures against prospective sanctions was to suspend the allotting of new business licenses for American trade and service companies.
Until today, only one American law firm, Davis Wright Tremaine of Seattle, had been approved to operate an office in Shanghai. Today, two were added: Altheimer & Grey of Chicago and O'Melveny & Myers of Los Angeles, where Secretary of State Warren Christopher was a partner before assuming his current post.
While several lawyers applauded the Chinese move as a step in the right direction, some complained about the confusion and difficulties of working in a semi-open market.
"Technically, we're not supposed to be here until we're legal," said an American lawyer who insisted on anonymity. The firm he works for was not approved today, though it operates an unofficial office in Beijing anyway. "But we can't become legal unless we're here, wining and dining Ministry of Justice folks, and showing how serious we are about the China market," the lawyer said.
In practice, dozens of similar firms operate preparatory offices in Beijing, Shanghai and other cities, where they act as consultants, keep low visibility, and generally answer telephones by saying "Hello?" rather than announcing the names of their firms.
Xiao Hongmin, an official at the Lawyers Affairs Division of the Justice Ministry, said there was no set schedule for approving new foreign legal firms, many of which applied as long as three years ago. More than 130 law firms have applications pending, Mr. Xiao said.
"The whole process of approval has become ultra-selective," said Louis B. Goldman, a partner at Altheimer & Grey, which will open an office in Shanghai. "Over 50 major American firms were competing for these slots, and 5 got in."
On the long list of things that Altheimer & Grey did to win favor with Chinese officials, Mr. Goldman said, was running a preparatory office in Beijing for two and a half years, inviting Justice Ministry staff members for visits to the United States, where meetings were arranged with business and political leaders, and advising Chinese officials on developing their legal profession.
The Justice Ministry is expected to soon issue a set of regulations on how the Chinese offices of foreign law firms will be supervised, the New China News Agency said yesterday.

Kantor Calms Indonesians
 JAKARTA, Indonesia, June 26 (Dow Jones) -- The United States Secretary of Commerce, Mickey Kantor, minimized differences with Indonesia today over its national-car program, stressing trade links instead.
After meeting here with the Minister of Industry and Trade, Tungki Ariwibowo, Mr. Kantor said:
"We want to see a growing and healthy economy in Indonesia. Part of this includes a healthy auto industry." But he added that Indonesia must abide by international trade rules.
The national-car program grants import-duty and luxury-sales-tax exemptions on cars that use Indonesian brand names, are produced by Indonesian companies and are developed with domestic technology, engineering and designs. The United States, Japan and the European Union have called the program discriminatory.
Mr. Kantor, who is on an Asian tour that also includes South Korea and Thailand, is scheduled to meet with President Suharto on Thursday morning.

HEADLINE: BOMBING IN SAUDI ARABIA: THE SAUDIS;
West's Uneasy Presence: Infidels or Protectors?

BYLINE: By YOUSSEF M. IBRAHIM 

DATELINE: LONDON, June 27

 BODY:
Switch on Saudi state television on any Friday and you are sure to see a panoply of preachers -- officially approved and even paid by the Saudi Government -- routinely referring to Christians and Jews as "infidels," "atheists" and "usurpers of Muslims."
Speaking to millions of Saudis, the preachers inveigh against traveling to the West and call upon believers to shun satellite dishes and all contact with foreigners. 
 Much the same message is heard almost daily on radio and in thousands of mosques around the country, even as the Saudi Government maintains close strategic, military and political ties to those same "infidels" in the United States and Europe.
For decades, the Saudi royal family has operated on the assumption it can maintain a two-track approach to the West: declaring its diplomatic and economic friendship with the outside powers whose support is vital to its survival, while allowing a totally different view of the West to be promoted by the country's vast religious establishment, which provides a vital connection between the ruling family and a large part of the country's estimated 15 million to 18 million people.
For decades that two-track approach served the ruling family well.
Western countries, anxious to secure access to oil, have provided the Saudis with enormous income and protection from neighbors like Iraq and Iran. While tens of thousands of foreigners work in Saudi Arabia, they have been largely confined to plush compounds and isolated along the Red Sea coast and the oil-producing area around Dhahran.
Meanwhile, legions of poorly educated Saudis have been insulated from the modern trends sweeping much of the Middle East, instead hearing the increasingly intense messages of alienation and fear emanating from the deeply conservative interior of the country.
It is an unnerving experience to walk from the compounds of any American, British or French company, where women congregate in bikinis around pools, and then drive a few miles away to a shopping market where Saudi religious police -- men in scraggly beards and sandals -- wield canes to beat any women foolish enough to venture out with any part of their bodies uncovered, even a bit of ankle. But now the two-track approach is breaking down. Especially since the Persian Gulf war of 1991, it has become harder and harder for the ruling family to keep its contradictory approaches to the West on separate planes.
Despite edicts extracted from religious authorities by the regime to sanction the presence of foreign troops as an "exceptional" measure, most of the religious establishment disapproved. A growing number have disapproved violently, leading to scores of arrests among eminent Saudi theologians since 1990 and the growth of an underground opposition that now hopes to bring down the Saudi regime.
"They hate the regime," said a senior Saudi official familiar with investigation of previous violent incidents. "To them the Americans are a symbol of everything they loath: corruption, joblessness, exclusion from decision-making, and this nurtured hatred of everything alien.
"But their real target, and the subject of their wrath, is the regime," he added.
The official, a liberal Saudi who asked for anonymity, explained at length what he and several other officials consider to be a "cancer that is growing".
Unrest has found fertile ground among tens of thousands of graduates of the multitude of recently established Saudi universities. Despite their degrees, most of them are semi-illiterate, their studies having been confined to Koranic subjects with a smattering of other knowledge.
Since the late 1980's, they have been disgorged from their schools unable to find jobs in the sophisticated Saudi private sector, which often uses foreign managers or Saudi graduates of American and European universities. Most cannot be absorbed, either, by Saudi Government ministries. In the days of $40-a-barrel oil, the Government used to give such graduates desks and salaries to do nothing.
Since then, oil prices have collapsed, Saudi coffers have been drained by the estimated $120 billion cost of the gulf war, and the population has grown at a dizzying rate, encouraged by edicts from religious authorities to procreate. (The average Saudi family is composed of 6 to 10 children from each wife.) As a result, the regime's promise of ever-growing wealth for all has broken down.
During much of the 1980's, Saudi religious radicals were given an immense boost by the Government's participation in efforts to resist the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan. Indeed, hundreds of Saudis joined large numbers of militants from other Arab countries in the fighting there.
After the end of the Soviet occupation, hundreds have returned to Saudi Arabia and other countries to continue the struggle at home. Indeed, the four men executed for November's Riyadh bombing had all served in Afghanistan. Not much else is known about them except for two significant facts: They were Sunni Muslims from the Wahabi Islamic school that is the foundation of Saudi Arabia's religious scholarship. Wahabis, who deliver the most rigid interpretation of Islam, have been allied with the Saudi family since the inception of the modern Kingdom a little more than 60 years ago. And, they were young, mostly unemployed and deeply fanatical about their religious belief.
All of which suggests they were not outsiders but a product of the very base of Saudi society. None, for example, were Shiite Muslims who could be thought sympathetic to Iran. And they were followers of other militant Wahabi religious Saudi figures, some of whom are in jail after breaking with the traditional religious establishment whom they accuse of having abandoned the rigid code of Wahabism.
While it is not known who mounted the Tuesday bombing, all those arrested in previous acts of violent or verbal opposition to the regime fit the same general background, suggesting a pool of native Saudi religious militants from the desert regions of Nejd, -- the heartland that begat the regime itself.
This homegrown opposition emerged in distinctive profile in 1990, when a group of 400 religious scholars, clerics and some liberal technocrats put their name to a petition demanding reforms and an end to corruption. The Government rejected their demand, accusing them of breaching the Koran's teachings by challenging the rulers.
Undeterred, the group tried again two years later in 1992, They sent a four-page letter to King Fahd. Again they were rebuffed, and this time the Government made some arrests.
The next year, the Islamist opposition hardened further. Six senior religious scholars left their jobs and challenged others in the religious establishment to follow suit. They founded the Committee for the Defence of Legitimate Rights, demanding more rigorous enforcement of Islamic law and an end to government corruption. Then cassettes began to surface all over Saudi Arabia carrying the same message coupled with fiery speeches by a leader of the group, Safar al-Hawali.
There were more arrests. Mr. Hawali and several of his followers are still in jail and threats began to surface hinting at violence unless they were released.
Many Muslim militants made their final break with the regime during 1993 and 1994: No longer satisfied with demanding reforms, they shifted to calling for the removal of the Saudi royal family from power.
One of them, Muhammad al-Masaari, who was part of the Committee for the Defence of Legitimate Rights, fled to London and continues his opposition to the regime from here. Mr. Masaari, who is a religious militant himself denies any connection with the bombings, says he understands the bombers' motives and their opposition to the American presence in the Kingdom.
"The Americans have become a very convenient target, being a shield for the al-Saud regime," he said in an interview. "They are standing between the people and the royal family. So, I can see that from a religious and moral point of view they are an easy target."
Caught in its own contradictions, the regime has a hard time, politically, cracking down on militant opponents.
"They are heroes," the Saudi official said. "We made them heroes. They fought against atheism and the Soviets. They died for Islam. The whole religious establishment, even those working for the Government, admire them. If you start rounding them up, you lose even more senior clerics, which will widen the ideological base of the militants."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
U.S. Bonds Advance in Thin Trading

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury prices ended higher yesterday, helped by end-of-the-quarter buying by portfolio managers.
That buying activity, bolstered by technical factors and thin trading volume, helped push the yield on the long bond below 7 percent for the first time in nearly three weeks. 
 The price of the 30-year Treasury bond settled up 17/32 of a percentage point, to 87 23/32 . The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from its price, fell to 6.99 percent, from 7.03 percent on Wednesday, and the lowest since June 6, when the yield was at 6.89 percent.
The Treasury market opened weaker from selling in Europe after the Bundesbank left German interest rates unchanged. Currency traders who had expected the central bank to cut rates, which would have weakened the mark, pushed the dollar lower, putting pressure on dollar-denominated securities.
But a rally soon began quietly as portfolio managers bought securities to make portfolios more attractive before the end of the quarter today, traders said.
The rally picked up steam after prices in the Treasury futures market breached a important technical level of 108 in the afternoon. That movement generated more buying and forced short-sellers, who had bet that prices would fall, to try to limit their losses by buying back securities. In a short sale, an investor sells borrowed securities, hoping to buy them back at lower prices and make a profit.
The September bond future contract settled at 108 14/32 yesterday, just off its high of 108 15/32 .
With trading light, the momentum gained quickly enough to become a full-fledged rally in late afternoon trading, said Jack Regan, chief investment strategist at Josephthal, Lyon & Ross.
Prices were also bolstered after a private consulting firm, Johnson Smick International, indicated that the Federal Reserve was not likely to raise interest rates until after the November election. Only a small minority of market participants expect the Fed to raise rates when its policy-making committee meets next week.
Still, the report eased some concerns about when the Fed would act on interest rates, in no small part because Manuel H. Johnson, who heads the firm, was vice chairman of the Fed in the 1980's.
In the corporate market, Teleport Communications Group, priced $925 million of junk bonds yesterday in a two-part transaction through underwriters led by Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette.
The first part included $300 million of senior notes due in 2006 at a price of par to yield 9.875 percent. The notes are noncallable for five years and are rated B1 by Moody's Investors Service and B by Standard & Poor's. In addition, there is an early redemption provision, in which the company can redeem 33.3 percent of the issue if it sells stock at a public offering within three years.
The other part includes $625 million in proceeds from senior discount notes due in 2007 with a 10.875 percent interest rate that are priced to yield 11.125 percent. Like the senior notes, these notes cannot be called for five years and have an equity redemption provision.
Teleport, based in Staten Island, is a provider of telecommunication services. The company was also busy raising cash in the stock market yesterday. Shares of Teleport rose to $17.625 in Nasdaq trading, up $1.625 from its offering price of $16. The offering of 23.5 million shares was underwritten by a group led by Merrill Lynch & Company.
