HEADLINE: G.M. to Raise 1997 Vehicle Prices 1.7%

BYLINE: By ROBYN MEREDITH 

DATELINE: DETROIT, June 27

 BODY:
The General Motors Corporation said today that prices for its 1997 model vehicles would rise by only 1.7 percent, on average, putting pressure on its rivals to hold down the prices of their cars and trucks.
Auto makers have been struggling in recent months to maintain price increases on mini-vans, pickup trucks, sport utility vehicles and particularly cars as customers have grown increasingly cost-conscious. 
 Michael L. Bowyer, an auto analyst at McDonald & Company, a Cleveland brokerage firm, said that the modest size of G.M.'s price increases -- they are below inflation, which has been running at just under 3 percent a year -- were a sign that auto makers were trying to lure less well-heeled customers back from the used-car lots.
But while car buyers might appreciate G.M.'s restraint in pricing, the small size of the increases puts extra pressure on auto makers to reduce their costs in order to stay profitable, he said. Auto workers and shareholders might benefit more from stiffer increases, he said.
George C. Peterson, president of Autopacific Inc., an automotive marketing and consulting firm in Santa Ana, Calif., said, "It looks like their price increases are pretty reasonable." In part, that reflects the fear that would-be buyers will walk away from cars that seem too expensive.
"The manufacturers are really conscious of affordability these days," Mr. Peterson said.
Indeed, the Ford Motor Company has had special difficulties meeting the demand for affordable vehicles. When it redesigned its popular Ford Taurus sedan for the 1996 model year, it gave the car a more sophisticated body and suspension and raised the price, only to lose sales.
As sticker prices for new cars have risen -- they hit $19,459 in the first quarter of this year, according to the Commerce Department -- many customers have headed for the used-car lots.
The Chrysler Corporation, Ford and other auto makers are expected to announce prices for 1997 models later this summer.
The relatively small increases by G.M. also signal that it is continuing its drive to win back market share, which has eroded over the last few years, industry experts said. "What G.M. is doing is trying to hold and gain share back by being the value leader in North America," said Martin L. Anderson, the associate director of the International Motor Vehicle Program at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.
Auto makers are also looking over their shoulders at Japan and at currency fluctuations as they set prices.
Since April 1995, the dollar has strengthened from 79.85 yen to its current level of nearly 110 yen. That has reduced the cost of building cars for Japanese auto makers and will allow them to earn higher profits or reduce prices on the vehicles they sell in the United States. Japanese auto makers were constrained from greatly reducing prices, and were often forced to raise them, when the yen was stronger because they would have lost money on the cars they sold here.
The G.M. announcement covered price increases on the 1997 models of existing vehicles, and the 1.7 percent increase compares comparably equipped models. G.M. said that the price for Chevrolet's popular midsize sedan, the Lumina, would rise 2 percent, to $17,495, for example. Prices for the Geo Tracker will be unchanged. And the base sticker price for Chevrolet's C/K pickup will rise 1.2 percent, to $15,157, a G.M. spokesman, Dean Rotondo, said.

HEADLINE: Keeping Saudi Arabia in Perspective

BYLINE: By Michael Field; Michael Field, a consultant on Middle East affairs, is the author of "Inside the Arab World."

DATELINE: LONDON

 BODY:
The simplest explanation for Tuesday night's bombing in Saudi Arabia, which killed 19 Americans, may turn out to be the correct one. We should not be surprised if the attack was carried out by people from the same indigenous group of militants responsible for the bombing of a National Guard building in Riyadh last November.
Three of the four bombers in that attack stated in their televised confessions that they had fought for Islamic militant groups in Afghanistan, where they had been trained in the use of explosives. Many men like them have returned home violent, unemployable and imbued with anti-Western ideas that make them want to purge their nation of what they see as America's corrupting influence. 
 These militants may have connections with other "Afghanis" in neighboring countries, particularly Lebanon. Recently, a car from Lebanon holding explosives was stopped at the Saudi-Jordanian border. They may also have links with Yemeni tribesmen who worked in Saudi Arabia and left disliking their hosts. Last November's bomb was made from explosives smuggled from Yemen, according to the confessions of the defendants, which were likely coerced. In effect, Saudi Arabia and other Arab states, supported by the United States, are now reaping the whirlwind after encouraging their nationals to go to Afghanistan to fight the Russians in the 1980's.
Saudi Arabia is vulnerable to violence by militants in that the very raison d'etre of the state, since it was established in the 18th century, has been the propagation of an orthodox form of Islam. The royal family, many of whose members have fairly secular attitudes, finds itself bound to encourage anything that might strengthen the Islamic nature of society, in particular the expansion of religious education.
What the bombings do not mean is that Saudi Arabia has become unstable or is approaching the point of revolution. This basic truth is obscured by three misconceptions.
First, the Saudi Government, which is ridiculously sensitive to criticism, tries to link acts of terrorism to any peaceful opposition. Thus, it inadvertently makes the opposition seem more dangerous than it is. It routinely blames all religious opponents for inspiring terrorism, and uses the alleged link as an excuse for not addressing complaints, mainly about corruption among a few royal family members and their hangers-on.
Second, given any opportunity, the United States and Israel try to link any terrorist act to Hezbollah in Lebanon to suggest that Iran is trying to destabilize the region. In fact, Iran is trying, clumsily, to improve relations with most of its neighbors and has no motive to anger Saudi Arabia. There is more than a streak of paranoia in America's and Israel's attitude to Iran.
Third, the liberal Western intelligentsia, particularly journalists, makes it clear that Saudi Arabia is not a model society. It is highly conservative, religious, conformist, rich and somewhat corrupt. In most commentaries on the kingdom, one senses that journalists are looking for parallels with Iran in the late 1970's or Algeria in the early 1990's.
The reality is that there is increasingly open grumbling. The Saudis, including many princes, are concerned about corruption, particularly in arms purchasing. They also complain about the poor quality of the state-controlled media and the inability of the Government, under the incapacitated King Fahd, to address the kingdom's chronic overspending.
Saudi Arabia needs a younger monarch who will be brave and energetic enough to tackle these problems and slowly allow its people greater participation. Unfortunately, the designated heir is Crown Prince Abdullah, King Fahd's half brother, and he is unlikely to take these steps.
If the Government fails to address its problems, the kingdom could gradually become more unstable. But serious civil unrest is likely still a long way away.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Declines on Comments By Group of Seven Officials

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
The dollar fell yesterday as officials of the seven leading industrial nations voiced satisfaction with the currency's current levels, prompting worries that yearlong gains might be coming to an end.
A decision by the Bundesbank yesterday to leave German interest rates unchanged also undermined the dollar. The president of the Bundesbank, Hans Tietmeyer, speaking after the central bank met, said the German economy had been burdened by the strong mark but that the currency's strength had been fully corrected. 
 The dollar dipped toward 1.5200 marks on the statement, and an effort by a Bundesbank spokesman to put Mr. Tietmeyer's remarks in context did little to calm market jitters.
The spokesman said the Bundesbank president's comment referred to the dollar's rise against the mark after it slumped to a post-World War II low of 1.3438 marks in March 1995.
With the comments from the Bundesbank, "a lot of people are getting out of dollars," said Mark Gargano, chief trader at First Union National Bank.
In late trading in New York yesterday, the dollar settled at 1.5180 marks, down from 1.5257 marks on Wednesday, and at 109.10 yen, down from 109.42 yen. The British pound rose to $1.5480 from $1.5407.
Mr. Tietmeyer's comments came as leaders from the United States, Germany, Japan, Canada, Britain, Italy and France gathered in Lyons, France, to discuss the world economy and foreign policy.
France's Finance Minister, Jean Arthuis, said France favored a stronger dollar.
But analysts said France, which is seeking a weaker franc to increase the competitiveness of its exports, might be alone among the Group of Seven richest nations in pushing for a stronger dollar.
Indeed, a Japanese Government spokesman in Lyons said Japan was "more or less" satisfied with the current level of the dollar against the yen.
"What the Group of Seven nations are saying is they're comfortable with the dollar," said Jim Phoenix, director of foreign exchange sales at Canadian Imperial Bank of Commerce.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Federal Reserve Seen Holding Rates Steady
The Federal Reserve will probably not raise interest rates when its policy-making body meets next week, economists said, but that call will be an unusually difficult and close one on both economic and political grounds.
With the economy apparently growing robustly and with unemployment low, the likelihood of inflation taking root might ordinarily be considered high. But there are only scattered and inconsistent signs of inflation, which higher rates would be intended to head off. [Page D2.]
 
The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond fell below 7 percent for the first time in nearly three weeks. [D16.]
 
China's Trade Status Is Upheld
The House defeated an effort to block China's preferred trade privileges with the United States. The resolution lost by a vote of 286 to 141, assuring most-favored-nation status to the Chinese for another year. [A2.]
 
G.M. Plans Small Price Increases
General Motors said it would raise prices for 1997 models of its vehicles, like the Chevrolet Lumina, by only 1.7 percent, on average, putting pressure on its rivals to hold down the prices of their cars and trucks. Auto makers have been struggling in recent months to maintain higher sticker prices as customers have grown increasingly cost-conscious. [D2.]

Westinghouse Buys a Channel
Westinghouse and its CBS subsidiary have acquired Telenoticias, a Spanish-language news channel. The move signals an expansion into cable television by CBS, which expects to expand distribution of the channel throughout Latin America and Spain. [D5.]
 
Approval Is Seen for MGM Bid
The board of Philips, which owns 75 percent of Polygram, reportedly voted to allow Polygram to submit a bid for MGM. [D6.]

Court Victory for Genentech
Genentech said a Federal judge had granted a preliminary injunction preventing Novo Nordisk of Denmark from selling a human growth hormone similar to a product developed by Genentech. [D4.]

Drug for AIDS-Related Blindness
Gilead Sciences, a biotechnology company, said it had won final approval from the Food and Drug Administration to begin marketing a drug for the treatment of an infection that causes blindness in AIDS patients. [D4.]

Dow Down, but Other Stocks Rise
The bond rally and some bargain hunting among depressed technology shares lifted most of the stock market yesterday. Still, the Dow Jones industrial average ended down 5.17 points, at 5,677.53. [D7.]

Path Cleared for Cigarette Suit
The Florida Supreme Court upheld a state liability law, clearing the way for Gov. Lawton Chiles to proceed with his lawsuit against cigarette makers that seeks to reclaim the cost of treating Medicaid patients who have smoking-related diseases. [A24.]
 
Roll Cameras, Roll Dice
ITT plans to develop casinos with Planet Hollywood, hoping that the images of the restaurant chain's founders, stars like Demi Moore and Arnold Schwarzenegger, can draw a younger generation of gamblers to places that are perhaps more often associated with Telly Savalas or Robert Goulet. [D6.]
 
The Stratosphere, a 1,149-foot hotel, gambling and retail tower, was the toast of Las Vegas -- and Wall Street -- when it opened. Then gravity asserted itself. Market Place. [D6.]
 
Shifts in Addressing Gay Market
As more mainstream marketers hire advertising agencies that specialize in aiming sales pitches at homosexuals, advocacy organizations that address issues of interest and concern to gay men and lesbians are turning to mainstream agencies for campaigns. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D5.]
 
All's Well That Ends Well?
Two weeks after they announced that their bookstore on the Upper West Side would close, the owners of Shakespeare & Company say the store may actually survive. [B8.]

SECTION: Section A; Page 32; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 467 words

HEADLINE: Debt Crisis in the Third World

 BODY:
President Clinton and the leaders of six other industrialized countries will devote much of their meeting this week in Lyons, France, to the terrorist attack in Saudi Arabia. But Jacques Chirac, the President of France, is rightly insisting that the summit meeting also grapple with the troubling problem of third-world debt. As many as 20 countries have reached debt levels so high that they are shut out of international capital markets and cannot climb out from under repayment burdens. The seven leaders agree debt relief is needed soon, but they disagree over how to provide it.
The World Bank and International Monetary Fund have proposed a plan, backed by the United States, that would knock down the debt burdens of the poorest countries to sustainable levels. That would allow the countries to repay lenders without imposing punishing taxes on their citizens. Under the proposal, the bank would reduce debt repayments on its loans. The I.M.F. would sell a small portion of its gold stock, invest the proceeds and use the income from the investments to reduce the debt payments of some third-world borrowers. 
 The bank and the fund would also pressure donor countries to write off between 67 and 90 percent of their loans to these heavily indebted countries. For their part, the borrowing countries would be required to adopt a strict program of market reforms that would be monitored by the bank and the fund. The stringent reform package is designed to lock the borrowers into sensible economic habits and dissuade existing or future borrowers from accumulating excessive debt under the cynical presumption that lenders will someday write down the loans.
The proposal makes sense. But divisions exist over its details. Some lenders, like Japan and Italy, are cool to the idea of selling the gold deposits of the fund for debt relief. Germany, which wants the fund's gold used only for true financial emergencies, is adamantly opposed to gold sales. To its credit, Germany is willing to provide direct debt relief, an offer the United States will not match.
The problem with the proposal is not that it risks too much debt relief, but offers too little. Oxfam International, a private organization that lobbies on behalf of third-world debtors, estimates that Uganda spends more than five times more each year on debt repayment to industrialized countries than it does on health care for its citizens. Many countries in sub-Saharan Africa have been crushed by the excessive loans. The proposal would have them wait six years for substantial relief.
The emergency is here. There is no better use for the gold stocks held by the I.M.F. Debt relief tied to stringent economic reform is an idea the industrialized powers should enthusiastically embrace before they depart Lyons.

HEADLINE: Fed Is Not Expected to Lift Rates at Meeting Next Week

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
The Federal Reserve will probably not raise interest rates when its policy-making body meets next week, economists said today, but that call will be an unusually difficult and close one on both economic and political grounds.
The economy appears to be growing at an unexpectedly robust clip of around 4 percent for the second quarter, and with unemployment at a low 5.6 percent, the likelihood of inflation starting to take root would normally be considered high. Under its chairman, Alan Greenspan, the Fed over recent years has taken to heading off inflation in advance by attacking it with higher interest rates long before it has a chance to spread through the economy. 
 But there are only scattered and inconsistent early warning signs of inflation, mostly reports that tight labor markets in some areas are starting to push up wages. Indeed, many economists believe the economy is about to slow substantially, and some even think the Fed will move to reduce rates by the end of the year. A number of analysts, however, contend that the Fed is likely to raise rates slightly sometime later this year.
To a large extent, economists said, the financial markets have already done much of the Fed's inflation-fighting work for it by sending long-term interest rates sharply upward earlier this year. The higher rates are expected to slow key sectors of the economy like housing, and should help cut the growth rate in the the second half of the year, easing whatever inflationary pressures might be building.
"While the economy has been somewhat stronger than expected so far this year, the backup in interest rates that's taken place is going to end up slowing the economy no matter what the Fed does," said Bruce Steinberg, an economist at Merrill Lynch & Company. "So in that sense, I don't think the Fed needs to do anything."
Sentiment in the credit markets has also shifted in recent days toward the belief that the Fed will keep short-term rates steady. As a result, the value of existing bonds has strengthened as rates have dipped, with the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond even dipping below 7 percent today.
But nothing is certain. At the Fed meeting, which stretches over two days starting on Tuesday, the central bankers could still choose to raise rates by the minimum increment available to it, a quarter of a point, just to maintain their status as stalwart inflation fighters. "While they may think the economy is going to slow," Mr. Steinberg said, "they may not want to risk their inflation credibility on that prediction."
The Fed cut rates three times between last July and January, and has since held steady at 5.25 percent the primary short-term rate it controls, the Federal funds target, even as the bond market has sent long-term yields up by more than a full percentage point.
Some Fed watchers believe the central bank may push rates up next week rather than risk having to do so later this year, in the heat of the Presidential election campaign.
Whether the Fed chooses to raise rates at its subsequent meeting, in August, will largely depend on the pace at which the economy creates new jobs, analysts said. Job-creation statistics for June will be issued next Friday, after the Fed's two-day meeting on Tuesday and Wednesday, and the figures for July will be available in early August, ahead of the Aug. 20 Fed meeting.
"By the time we get to August, my guess is that there will be evidence that employment growth is slowing and they won't tighten," said David B. Bostian Jr., chief economist at Herzog, Heine, Geduld Inc., a New York investment firm.
But the American Bankers Association Economic Advisory Committee said in a report this week that it expected the Fed to raise its Federal funds target rate, currently 5.25 percent, by a quarter- or half- point by the end of the summer.
The most visible guide to inflationary pressures in the economy is the interplay between unemployment and growth. Most economists believe that if the economy is growing at a rate above its long-term trend at a time of low unemployment, the inevitable result will be a bidding war for workers, with the ensuing higher wages leading to rising prices. At 4 percent, the economy is growing much faster than the 2 to 2.5 percent that is generally considered the trend growth rate, and the 5.6 unemployment rate is below what many analysts long considered the rate at which wage pressures start to build.
Some economists are particularly wary of inflation now because companies have been slashing inventories, meaning that if demand remains strong, it would be met by increased production. Increased production would translate into higher demand for labor and materials, and upward pressure on wages and prices.
A report last week by the Federal Reserve Banks on economic conditions in their regions found anecdotal evidence of upward pressure on wages, with fast-food restaurants in St. Louis paying $250 bonuses to attract new workers and companies in Minnesota having grown so discouraged about finding skilled employees that they stopped advertising their job openings.
But there are few other signs of inflation. Commodity prices have fallen in recent weeks, in part because of a drop in copper prices after a huge trading loss at Sumitomo. Grain and gasoline prices have also been falling.

HEADLINE: Spy Agencies Faulted for War Focus

BYLINE: By TIM WEINER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
American intelligence agencies devote too much time and money to supporting the military, and far too little to understanding the problems of peace, a new and authoritative critique concludes.
The report, by 19 people from the worlds of espionage, diplomacy, the military, academia and business, says American intelligence "still has not yet come in from the cold" and is "ill-prepared for the 21st century," in the words of the group's executive director, Allan E. Goodman, a dean at the Georgetown University School of Foreign Service and a former top administrator at the Central Intelligence Agency. 
 The militarization of intelligence has sapped the United States' ability to devote brainpower to diplomacy, the global economy and other peacetime problems, the report said. It was made public today by the Twentieth Century Fund, a nonpartisan organization based in New York that organized and paid for the study.
The report, one of four major studies published in recent months critiquing American intelligence, said the intelligence community had been increasingly dominated by the military in the years since the cold war. This "alarming imbalance," said the report's chairman, former Ambassador Stephen W. Bosworth, has corroded the corps of American intelligence analysts in favor of spy satellites and other billion-dollar tools for stealing information abroad.
The result is an intelligence system that spends more than $26 billion a year on machines and less than $3 billion on people to analyze the information the machines collect -- a system that is "90 percent hardware and 10 percent software," said one of the study's principal authors, Philip Zelikow, a professor at the Kennedy School of Government at Harvard University.
While machines can steal secrets, only humans can unravel mysteries like the future course of China, the chances for Russian democracy, or the internal machinations of the Japanese economy. For the cost of one of the least expensive spy satellites -- several hundred million dollars -- the cadres of American intelligence analysts could be dramatically strengthened, the report argued.
The C.I.A.'s spies and their paramilitary culture have dominated the agency at the expense of analysts, said another principal author, Gregory F. Treverton, a former top United States intelligence analyst and a director at the Rand Corporation. The culture of secrecy makes it harder for the agency's analysts to travel abroad, talk to outsiders, learn new lessons and grapple with mysteries, Mr. Treverton said.
The report said the C.I.A.'s spies were responsible for "a long string of embarrassments" and called for a smaller, more specialized clandestine service. It said those spies should focus on far fewer targets -- a small number of nuclear nations, rogue states, terrorist groups and foes to American forces overseas -- and close up shop in less threatening countries. Diplomats, not spies, should be the ones analyzing events in more peaceful places, it said, arguing that the Foreign Service had been crippled by budget cuts and should be given the money and power to reclaim its once-central role in reporting from abroad.
In a dissent, Richard J. Kerr, a former Deputy Director of Central Intelligence, said the C.I.A.'s spies should keep their global presence and their capacity for covert action.
The report is the latest in a series of Government and independent studies calling for major changes at the C.I.A. and the dozen other arms of American intelligence. Those calls began in 1994, after the arrest of Aldrich H. Ames, a C.I.A. officer who spied for Moscow for nine years.
The Ames case and other scandals led to a cry from Congress and outside critics for an overhaul of American intelligence, and led to the series of studies. But they created no significant changes. Their often-contradictory recommendations are bogged down in Congress, where the Senate and House Intelligence Committees have competing visions of the future. The White House has taken few steps to resolve them, leaving the acknowledged problems in the hands of the Director of Central Intelligence, John M. Deutch.
Mr. Deutch has vowed to seek evolutionary, not revolutionary changes, and evolution is slow, often invisible.
Mr. Deutch and other top intelligence officials have said that supporting the military is one of their priorities. They also have said they are trying to refocus the C.I.A.'s espionage efforts on the world's most dangerous places, and striving to improve the quality and utility of their analytical work.

HEADLINE: Not All Aboard: A Big Rail Merger and Its Critics;
Sidetracked Since '13, Plan Still Raises Antitrust Fears

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER 

DATELINE: CHICAGO

 BODY:
Talk about your rocky romances. Born as rivals seeking to build the biggest stretch of the first transcontinental railroad in the late 1860's, the Union Pacific and Southern Pacific railroads came close to marriage by the turn of the century only to have their union scotched in 1913 by a disapproving Supreme Court.
Now, the two railroads are once again hoping to get hitched in a $5.4 billion deal that would create a 37,000-mile rail network linking 25 states, Mexico and Canada. And, once again, the road to the altar is proving rocky. 
 In all, according to Union Pacific, customers, port operators, political leaders, unions and other interested parties have filed more than 2,000 letters of support for the merger with the Government, a record. Trouble is, the opposition is just as intense, including such heavyweights as the Departments of Justice and Agriculture, the State of Texas, and trade groups like the National Industrial Transportation League and the Society of Plastics Industries.
There are also qualms at the Department of Transportation, which oversees the independent three-member agency called the Surface Transportation Board that will rule on the merger proposal July 3.
The heart of the problem: If the Surface Transportation Board, the successor to the Interstate Commerce Commission, allows the merger without demanding major divestitures where the railroads have overlapping service, the new rail giant and its main rival, the Burlington Northern Santa Fe, would control 90 percent of all rail traffic west of the Mississippi River. Such a duopoly, opponents say, could stifle competition.
"This is taking the level of competition back to when there were more buffalo than people in the West," said William H. Brodsky, president of Montana Rail Link Inc., a privately held regional carrier based in Missoula, Mont. Montana Rail Link wants the Government to order Union Pacific to sell it more than 1,000 miles of track through the heart of the country from Kansas to California as a condition for approving the merger.
The Union Pacific-Southern Pacific rail network would carry more than 75 percent of rail traffic to and from Mexico and two-thirds of the traffic in chemicals, plastics and other industrial goods from Texas and Louisiana to the rest of the country. And, it would have no direct competition in the central corridor from the Midwest through Utah to California.
The Justice Department, while lacking jurisdiction over railroads, is lobbying against the merger. If the deal goes through, it calculates prices will rise 19 percent for shippers that previously had a choice between the Union Pacific and Southern Pacific and 10 percent for those that would have two railroads to choose from instead of three.
Union Pacific -- based in Omaha and a subsidiary of the Union Pacific Corporation of Bethlehem, Pa. -- hotly disputes that claim, predicting instead that most shippers will see better service, lower prices or both. It projects annual savings at about $750 million and argues that service on the Southern Pacific will decline steadily without the huge capital investments the Union Pacific has promised to upgrade track and equipment.
The debate over the merger plan has refocused attention on an industry that once dominated the American economy and still holds a huge share of the market for shipping goods long distances.
Railroads are unlikely ever to recapture the near-monopoly over transportation that they enjoyed in the late 19th century -- a lock that prompted a series of Congressional investigations and attempts by the states to curb price gouging and other abuses. In 1886, the Supreme Court held that the states could not regulate the industry, and in response Congress adopted the Interstate Commerce Act the following year, the first Federal law to regulate private business.
Nearly a century later, in 1980, Congress deregulated the railroads, and since then, rates have fallen steadily and worker productivity has risen sharply. But with deregulation came consolidation -- and with consolidation came a resurgence of antitrust concerns.
"This debate is larger than the Union Pacific-Southern Pacific merger," said Warren Erdmann, a spokesman for Senator Christopher S. Bond, a Missouri Republican who is opposed to the merger. "It has to do with how strongly we enforce antitrust."
Many shippers -- from coal-mine operators in Utah and farmers in Kansas to manufacturers in Texas and Louisiana -- oppose the proposed combination, fearing both higher prices and a decline in service. Farm groups are especially skeptical because of the problems Union Pacific has had in digesting the Chicago & Northwestern Railway, which it acquired last year for $1.2 billion.
"It's been a disaster," said Frederic E. Schrodt, director of transportation for Farmland Industries, a huge cooperative based in Kansas City, Mo. Union Pacific's failure to provide prompt service has cost Farmland members $100 million in higher costs for grain storage and other expenses, they say.
Another merger opponent is Lew Meibergen, president of Johnston Enterprises Inc., a grain shipper for about 10,000 farmers that is based in Enid, Okla. "I supported the Union Pacific when they bought out the old Katy in the 1980's," Mr. Meibergen said, referring to the former Missouri Kansas & Texas Railroad Company. "They promised to maintain service and I'm still waiting for them to keep it."
Making matters worse, Mr. Meibergen said, service by his only reliable provider, the Santa Fe Pacific Corporation, has deteriorated since it was taken over last year by the Burlington Northern Corporation in a $4 billion transaction.
Huntsman Chemical, a Houston-based producer of chemicals, said it feared it would lose bargaining power on shipments of products like polypropylene pellets to Mexico and other chemicals to customers in Louisiana. "We have 3,000 shipments a year where we will be down to just one carrier," said Chris VanderHeuvel, a Huntsman spokesman.
Union Pacific has been working hard to mute the criticism. It agreed with the Chemical Manufacturers Association, for example, to make sure that Burlington Northern trains could run against the one-way flow of Union Pacific traffic on tracks in the Texas-to-Memphis corridor, to share storage facilities with Burlington Northern and to pay for a Burlington Northern dispatcher to sit beside its own to make sure Union Pacific trains were not being given favorable treatment. These and other concessions persuaded the trade group and key members like the Monsanto Company to withdraw their opposition. Other shippers have likewise dropped their objections, either after receiving special deals or simply from fear of offending the powerful railroad.
Still others have thrown their full support behind the deal, including Exxon, General Motors and its biggest Government customer, the Department of Defense. And the lobbying to change the minds of opponents is not over. Richard Davidson, chief executive of Union Pacific, said both he and Drew Lewis, the chairman, "have meetings scheduled with customers right up until the hearing" the first week in July that will decide the merger's fate.
Mindful of the problem of reduced competition even before the deal was announced last fall, Mr. Lewis, a former Secretary of Transportation, led a concerted lobbying campaign on Capitol Hill last year as Congress was phasing out the Interstate Commerce Commission to make sure that oversight of rail mergers was given to the new Surface Transportation Board rather than the Department of Justice, which has historically been more hostile to the industry. Most analysts expect the board to approve the merger -- but to attach conditions. The big question mark is what the scope of the conditions will be.
Whether supporting or opposing the merger, many railroad experts agree that Government approval without ordering major divestitures will invite further consolidation in the industry. They foresee a cascade of deals in the next few years, leaving only two or three major coast-to-coast railroads to dominate the industry. "CSX will join one of the Western railroads and Norfolk Southern the other," said Steve Lewins, who follows the industry for Gruntal & Company in New York. "Conrail might stay independent or it could get divided up."
That would be terrific, according to Mr. Lewins, who argues that a few strong rail companies could be efficient enough to save shippers billions of dollars annually and reinforce the nation's role as a crossroads of world trade. But such benefits would be invisible for all but the biggest shippers with the power to negotiate the best rates, according to Michael Havarty, president and chief executive of the Kansas City Southern Railway. "Service will decline and ultimately the Government will be forced to reregulate the industry," said Mr. Havarty, whose railroad would lose substantial traffic if the merger went through.
To allay antitrust concerns, Union Pacific is doing its best to paint itself as a regional player that poses no monopolistic threat. While its fusion with Southern Pacific would create the nation's No. 1 railroad, it says it is not interested in building a transcontinental railroad (though it would like to expand in Mexico).
Union Pacific also argues that it had no choice but to pursue the Southern Pacific in order to remain competitive as the industry consolidates. The Southern Pacific's gem is the carrier's Texas-to-Los Angeles lines, which Union Pacific has coveted for more than a century. And Southern Pacific's tracks north from Los Angeles to Oregon and Washington could fill a gaping hole in the Union Pacific network.
But Southern Pacific, based in San Francisco and considered by many to be the greatest railroad in the country as recently as the 1950's, comes with many liabilities. It diverted too much cash into other businesses and then was hamstrung by a leveraged buyout and a failed merger attempt in the early 1980's with the Santa Fe. It has had operating losses in 14 of the last 17 years. The invaluable tracks heading west from El Paso to California and northeast toward Kansas City are overcrowded and other parts of its network are deteriorating.
But the Union Pacific had to overlook those drawbacks after its first choice as a partner, the Santa Fe, instead embraced the Burlington Northern last year. That merger made the freight hauler, based in Fort Worth, a 31,000-mile colossus spanning 27 states and two Canadian provinces, and it knocked Union Pacific from its cherished position as the nation's largest railroad. The deal also delighted Wall Street by bringing one of the nation's most highly regarded rail executives, Robert D. Krebs of Santa Fe, to Burlington as successor to the retiring Gerald Grinstein.
"The Burlington Northern Santa Fe merger changed the landscape of the Western railroad industry forever," said Mr. Davidson, the Union Pacific chief executive. "It made our merger inevitable."

HEADLINE: Bond Rally Helps Stocks, But Dow Retreats by 5.17

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
A late bond rally and some bargain hunting among depressed technology shares lifted most of the stock market yesterday.
But the tone of trading remained cautious as money mangers sold shares to try to make the performance of their portfolios look good for the end of the quarter. Investors braced for potential surprises in next week's economic data and the Federal Reserve's meeting on monetary policy, at which it will decide whether to adjust interest rates. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average fell 5.17 points, to 5,677.53, recovering from a midafternoon slide of 36 points as bond prices jumped, sending long-term interest rates below 7 percent for the first time in three weeks.
But the Nasdaq market, which is heavily weighted with technology issues, was stronger from the start of the session as bargain hunters took advantage of Wednesday's 19-point tumble.
A series of cautious profit outlooks in the computer industry has led to several rounds of profit taking after the powerful rally in that sector this spring. Since setting a record high at 1,249.15 on June 5, the Nasdaq composite index has lost 6.8 percent of its value.
The Nasdaq rose 12.72, to 1,166.01, helped by big rebounds in computer-related issues like Dell Computer, up 2 3/4, at 48 1/2; Iomega, up 6 1/2, at 27 1/2, and U.S. Robotics, up 7, at 84 3/4.
The Dow industrials were weighed down by Procter & Gamble, which fell 2 1/4, to 91 1/4, after announcing a $150 million after-tax charge to earnings to increase its reserve for changes in pulp prices, and Union Carbide, which fell 2 1/4, to 40 3/8, after the chemical maker reportedly issued a cautious second-quarter assessment to analysts.
The market value index of the American Stock Market fell 5.50, to 570.13, its lowest level since April 11. The Amex has now plunged 7.3 percent since setting a record high at 614.99 on May 22.
Advancing issues outnumbered decliners by a 13-to-10 margin on the New York Stock Exchange, where volume totaled 405.6 million shares as of 4 P.M., up from 386.5 million in the previous session.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index rose 1.71, to 357.58, and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 4.16, to 668.55. Both indexes were lower until midafternoon.
"There's been a lid on the broad market indices with these interest rates," said Robert Streed, senior investment adviser at Northern Trust in Chicago, about the pessimistic tone that's dominated the bond market for weeks. "As soon as you get a little lift in the bond market, stocks can pop up."
"There's been so much of a negative consensus building in the bond market. People have been talking about the Fed raising interest rates, wage inflation around corner, the economy remaining too strong; all of these bad things probably aren't going to happen."
The Labor Department reported yesterday morning that the number of American workers filing first-time claims for jobless benefits fell last week, but remained at a level economists said represented slower job growth.
The weekly report generally draws less reaction than the monthly tally, which is due next Friday. In recent months, that report has disclosed strong employment growth, spurring worries that a rapid increase in consumer spending power would generate too much demand and inflation.
Analysts are divided over whether the Federal Reserve's policy makers, who meet next Tuesday and Wednesday, will decide to raise interest rates as a pre-emptive strike against inflation. Higher interest rates can hurt corporate profits by raising borrowing costs and slowing revenues.

HEADLINE: CBS Adding a Spanish-Language Channel

BYLINE: By BILL CARTER 

 BODY:
Signaling a long-delayed expansion into cable television, the Westinghouse Electric Corporation and its CBS subsidiary announced yesterday the acquisition of Telenoticias, a Spanish-language, 24-hour news channel now distributed in more than 20 million homes in 22 countries.
Westinghouse expects to expand the channel's distribution throughout Latin America and Spain. Beginning in the fourth quarter, Westinghouse also plans to market the channel on cable television in American cities with significant Spanish-speaking populations, including New York, Los Angeles and Miami. 
 Donald H. Mitzner, the president of Group W Satellite Communications, was put in charge of marketing and distribution for the channel, which is based in Miami. The news operation will report to Andrew Heyward, the president of CBS News.
The channel, which was renamed CBS/Telenoticias, would give CBS News its first foothold in cable television, a point that Mr. Heyward emphasized yesterday.
"Obviously it is terrifically significant for the new CBS to be expanding in news rather than contracting or standing still," Mr. Heyward said.
CBS News, under its previous owner, Laurence A. Tisch, underwent several rounds of cutbacks and never invested widely in expansion either internationally or in cable television. Two other networks, NBC and Fox, are already deep into planning 24-hour cable news channels to compete with the Cable News Network.
The Telenoticias channel does not plan to compete with those channels, not only because it will have minimal distribution in the United States, but also because it will be reported completely in Spanish, not English. But Mr. Heyward said that the competition among news organizations in the United States had led to "a saturation in the overall market," while a Spanish-language channel would be able to "speak to one the largest growth areas in the world."
CBS News also plans to make use of bilingual correspondents from Telenoticias and to revise some of its English-language programs for use on the channel, Mr. Heyward said.
Other CBS executives said that the cost of the service was another factor in making the investment attractive. "We will not have to invest hundreds of millions of dollars to make this a viable service as the networks starting up news channels will," a CBS executive said.
Westinghouse bought Telenoticias from a consortium of owners that included Reuters Holdings P.L.C., Artear of Argentina, Antenna 3 International of Spain and Telemundo News Network. The purchase price was not disclosed, although one CBS executive described it as "highly economical" and two executives familiar with the deal put the cost in the $20 million range.
CBS remains the network with the smallest presence in the cable television business, but this deal could signal a change in strategy, CBS executives said. "This is the first step in a multichannel presence," Mr. Mitzner said. "There will be other steps."
He did not specify what those steps would be. CBS, alone among the four broadcast networks, never negotiated deals with cable operators three years ago for the right to retransmit the signals of its broadcast stations on cable systems. The three other networks created new cable channels as a means of gaining compensation from cable operators.
Mr. Mitzner said the Telenoticias channel would not be part of a CBS retransmission strategy, though other CBS executives suggested the network might make limited retransmission deals for the channel in those cities where there is a large Spanish-speaking audience.
Two executives suggested CBS had plans for further expansion into cable. The next channel the company invests in is also likely to involve some participation by CBS News, one executive said.

HEADLINE: U.S. Is Upbeat On Chip Deal With Japan

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: LYONS, France, June 27

 BODY:
In a private half-hour session here today from which their key aides were excluded, President Clinton and the Japanese Prime Minister, Ryutaro Hashimoto, appeared to lay the groundwork for a settlement of a highly contentious trade issue: extending one of the largest accords between the two countries, governing trade in computer chips.
The current agreement expires on July 31, a decade after the Reagan Administration first got the Japanese Government to agree to a target for American penetration of the Japanese chip market. Now United States companies have more than a 30 percent share of the market -- up from less than 10 percent a decade ago -- and the Japanese have refused to renew the accord, saying the problem is solved. 
 American officials were careful today in describing Mr. Clinton's talk with Mr. Hashimoto, in part because only a translator was with the two men when they went into an unscheduled half-hour of direct discussions. But officials familiar with the conversation, which was reconstructed later from the interpreter's notes and Mr. Clinton's own recounting, said they felt far more optimistic that Japanese officials now saw the political need for a deal.
"They're moving a long way," one American official said. Another added, "They both have political instincts telling them this has got to get solved."
A senior Japanese official familiar with Mr. Hashimoto's account of the same discussion interpreted the meeting the same way, though he cautioned that an agreement was not certain.
The public statements were far more neutral. "The President expressed concern that there has been very little progress" on the chip discussions, or on the enforcement of a previous accord providing foreign access to the Japanese insurance market, Laura D'Andrea Tyson, the head of the National Economic Council, told a news conference here, just before the opening of the annual meeting of the Group of Seven industrial nations.
American officials contend that Japan has backed off on its commitments on the insurance market to protect its insurance industry, which has been battered during Japan's banking crisis. It was unclear whether any progress was likely in that dispute.
The two leaders, Ms. Tyson said, are "committed to work together to reach a solution to both problems by the end of July." She said the meeting was "a very positive session."
It was also a very political one. The chip accord in particular is a potent subject for Mr. Clinton in California, the key state he must win in November. His support in California's Silicon Valley has been much weaker than it was during the 1992 Presidential campaign, and donations from entrepreneurs there have fallen off sharply. His political advisers say he clearly wants to demonstrate that he is pressing for broader access to Japan, the world's largest consumer of semiconductors outside the United States.
Mr. Hashimoto, more than most Japanese politicians, has a gut sense of the connection between trade accords and vote-getting. A skilled negotiator, he struck the automobile agreement with the United States a year ago this week, when he was still the Minister of International Trade and Industry. That deal, which resulted in vague Japanese assurances that more American-made cars and auto parts would be sold in Japan, averted a major trade war. It also helped raise Mr. Hashimoto's stature in Japan, paving the way for him to gain the prime ministership.
The outlines of a semiconductor deal the United States will accept are already fairly clear. The United States has said that unlike the previous two versions of the chip accords it would not seek a specific numerical share of the market, or even a commitment from the Japanese Government to guarantee access. Instead, it would likely settle for a Government blessing of an industry-to-industry agreement. That is the deal that still needs to be negotiated.
"We just need something that will keep the heat on," one of Mr. Clinton's top economic advisers said recently. "Otherwise, we know there will be backsliding."
There is still opposition in Japan to renewing the accord, chiefly from bureaucrats who are asking: Why cannot a trade agreement die a dignified death once it has succeeded?
It is a question American officials are loath to answer. But there is little question that the chip accords have helped create an atmosphere that greatly increased purchases of American chips. Much of that increase might have occurred anyway, however, because of Japan's total reliance on Intel microprocessors for most of its personal computer lines.
Nonetheless, the United States has insisted that a "transition accord" is needed before Washington will be satisfied to leave a multibillion-dollar business to what Ms. Tyson referred to today as "a full-fledged, dynamic, competitive marketplace."

HEADLINE: Given Tools, They Work the Language

BYLINE: By Lee Smith; Lee Smith, whose most recent novel is "Saving Grace," teaches at North Carolina State University in Raleigh.

DATELINE: CHAPEL HILL, N.C.

 BODY:
I grew up in the southwest Virginia coal fields in a big family of storytellers who, my mother liked to say, would rather "climb a tree to tell a lie than stand on the ground and tell the truth." I spent my childhood hanging around the Buchanan County Courthouse (where my granddaddy was the treasurer) and at my daddy's Ben Franklin dime store right across the street. I heard a lot of stories.
I also read every book I could get my hands on. What I especially liked were the books that made me feel all wild and trembly inside: "The Secret Garden," which I read maybe 20 times; and "Little Women," especially the part where Beth dies; and "Gone With the Wind," especially the part where Melanie dies. I loved anything at all about horses and saints. Reading, I felt exactly the way I felt during summer thunderstorms: I just had to run out of the house and up the mountain into the very storm to whirl in the thunder and rain while lightning cracked all around me. 
 At the time I was growing up, everybody I knew in Appalachia felt that culture was somewhere else: in Europe, in New York City or in Richmond. Culture was a long, long way from where we were. And when the time came, I was "sent off" to get some.
Well, I did. I ended up becoming a writer and a professor. By then I'd realized that some of the best stories I had to tell were those I'd heard so long ago, in Buchanan County, spoken in the most picturesque and precise language I would ever hear, Appalachian English. (A creek "turkey-tails out in the bottom," in the dialect of my birthplace, and a baby small enough to stay put on a blanket is a "a set-along child.")
 I also realized that I was hearing entirely too much about agents and advances, about "revising the canon" and "privileging the text." I was depressed about writing, which no longer seemed relevant to anything. I had "gotten above my raising," as my family might have said; I was too far removed from the springs of my own language.
So two years ago, when I was offered a Lila Wallace-Readers' Digest Writers' Award to do community work with a nonprofit group of my choice, I went back to the coal fields.
Now, for several weeks each year, I work with the Hindman Settlement School right over the Virginia state line in Hindman, Ky. The school's adult learning center, a no-nonsense brick building overhanging a muddy creek, offers a literacy program, adult education classes and tutoring services for the high school equivalency exam. The need for these programs is great. Half the county's population hasn't made it through high school; most dropouts leave before the ninth grade. Unemployment is high, and incomes are low.
My work in this brick building has helped me remember the enormous sense of empowerment that comes with mastering written language. It was, for example, a revelation for me to meet Connel Polly from Vicco, Ky., a successful, red-haired, talkative contractor who for almost 50 years had kept his illiteracy a secret from everybody but his wife. In "It's Like Coming Out of a Deep Hole," his book of memories published by the Hindman school, he writes:
"One time, the mining company sent me to Canton, Ohio, going after mine parts in a pickup truck. They had told me which roads to take and what the exit was, and I was supposed to find this company that was on Fifth Street. So I drove all around looking for a 5, and I couldn't find it. That's when I realized 'Fifth' was a word, and I couldn't read it. I couldn't find it. That's the only time I ever cried in my life. I just pulled off the road and sat there and cried. I was 8 hours away from home and it was getting dark. That was pitiful. Finally I had to ask somebody, and it turned out I was sitting right at it. I could see it. I felt so bad I didn't even stay the night. After I did my business I drove on back, and I was down all the way home. I was so blue. I felt the worst I've ever felt. There I was -- a grown man -- trying to make a living in that shape!"
Now, he writes:
"I didn't know learning to read would change my life so much. It has made me have more confidence in myself. Before, I even had a fear of going into a public restroom. I had fear of being embarrassed by someone handing me something to read. I stayed away from places such as banks, post offices and doctors' offices. The first visit to a new doctor was hardest because you had to fill out forms. I always had my wife with me. Now, I'll go anywhere. Also, me and my wife leave notes for each other. Now that's something!"
And here's Florida Slone, a lively ballad singer who enrolled just after she was widowed:
"I always thought of myself as a bean planted in a garden, and then someone put a big rock on top of me so that I could not get out of the ground. Now . . . I have gotten my driver's license, and I can write my checks. I can read my Bible and my songbooks. I have always liked to make up songs and stories, but I never could write them down before. Now I can. I am beginning to grow. Maybe one of these days I'll be like Jack's beanstalk!"
Mrs. Slone has written down a book of songs by now. "A Garden of Songs," published by the school, includes hymns, love ballads and party tunes like "Chew Tobacco" and "Big Fat Dog." There are also songs like "Red Hot Election" and "School Bus Wreck in Floyd County," which chronicle local events. Some of her songs read like poetry:
Last night I sat and watched the clouds go by
I heard whippoorwills call from the mountains so high
I heard the water as it dropped soft and low
Seems like death is a secret nobody knows.
Most of my writing students are women; many have enrolled upon discovering, after divorce or widowhood, what they could do for themselves.
Glenda Johnson, who eventually had to drop out of the program to tend ailing family members, first found the time to write about her son:
This is a poem about Roy Glen Johnson.
He has blue eyes
and blond looking hair . . .
He is afraid of my father.
He would like to have
His family whole again.
Then there's Ollie Wallen, who was shy and extremely quiet when I first met her two years ago in the high school equivalency program and who now joins every discussion. She recently wrote to her Congressman with a complaint and was pleased when he called her at home to advise her how to take action on getting benefits she was due. One of her poems is about marriage and divorce, about doing things for herself. Her self-image, she writes, has changed from from "bad" to "great" --
"Like a rope was wrapped around me
But now it is loose."
It's a long, winding road from where I am living now to the mountains of eastern Kentucky. But it has brought me home -- back to what reading and writing were all about in the first place, before book tours and disputes about deconstruction. Helping people express themselves in writing for the first time is like watching them fall in love. For me, it has brought back the old thrill, the lightning storm.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: House Defeats a Move to End China's Favored Trade Status

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
The House defeated an effort today to revoke China's broad trade privileges with the United States.
Lawmakers blocked the resolution by a vote of 286 to 141, assuring China's preferred trade status for another year. Reflecting divisions in both parties, 167 Republicans and 119 Democrats voted against the measure, while 65 Republicans, 75 Democrats and one independent voted for it. 
 On May 21, the White House renewed China's "most favored nation" status for another year. The decision could have been overruled only by a vote of both houses of Congress.
President Clinton and Bob Dole, the likely Republican presidential candidate, support the renewal. "Most favored nation" trade status permits a country's goods to be imported to the United States with the same low tariffs enjoyed by nearly all other nations.
"The strong bipartisan action today is clear evidence that the American people agree that engagement, not isolation, is the best way to advance America's vital interests with China and the world," President Clinton said at the Group of Seven economic summit meeting in Lyons, France.
But opponents argued that the United States should not reward a Government that abridges human rights, violates treaties restricting sales of missile technology and wages unfair trade practices.
Renewing China's trade privileges reinforces a centerpiece of the Administration's Asia policy and opens the way for billions of dollars of uninterrupted trade with American companies like Boeing, Ford and General Motors. Total trade between the two countries has increased to $57.3 billion in 1995 from $4.8 billion in 1980, making China the United States' sixth-largest trading partner.
But opponents of renewing the trade benefits won a small concession, a promise that four House committees -- Banking and Financial Services, International Relations, National Security, and Ways and Means -- will hold hearings on China's checkered record on human rights, spreading of weapons and trade policies. The House approved this resolution by a vote of 411 to 7.
The three-hour debate on the House floor today centered on whether the United States should engage China rather than threaten it with trade sanctions as the best way to promote human rights and protect America's security and trade interests in Asia.
Both sides criticized China's human rights record, citing reports of persecuted Tibetans, Government-imposed abortions and army-operated prison labor camps that manufacture industrial goods.
Both sides condemned China's sale of M-11 missiles to Pakistan and nuclear-reactor technology to Iran, and an apparent attempt by state-owned Chinese companies to smuggle illegal automatic weapons into the United States for sale to street gangs.
Both sides denounced the piracy of American music, movies and computer software in China, and other unfair trade practices.
But supporters and opponents of renewing China's trade status differed sharply on how to influence China.
Supporters said that only by engaging China in free trade would Washington have leverage to influence Beijing.
"Yes, things are bad in China," said Representative Sam M. Gibbons, Democrat of Florida. "By cutting off normal trade relations, we'd only succeed in isolating China again. We should be building bridges instead of burning bridges."
Representative Thomas W. Ewing, Republican of Illinois, said, "When we're involved in China, we can improve the conditions in China."
But opponents of favorable trade status for Beijing accused the Administration of coddling a tyrannical regime and argued that China would change its behavior only if threatened with the loss of American trade.
"These people don't think like us," said Representative Joe Scarborough, a Florida Republican. "They don't share our values. They only understand that the U.S. continues to kow-tow to them."
Representative Gerald B. H. Solomon, Republican of New York, said: "Whatever happened to U.S. foreign policy that looked out for human decency around the world? Appeasement of tyrants does not work."
China's trade status comes up for renewal every year, offering a forum to link human rights with trade policy. But this year's debate was even more spirited because many previous supporters said they were fed up with China's record.
Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House Democratic leader, and Representative David E. Bonior of Michigan, the House's second-ranking Democrat, both bucked the White House and voted to revoke the trade benefits.

HEADLINE: Michigan Asks U.S. to Approve Work-for-Welfare Plan

BYLINE: By ROBYN MEREDITH 

DATELINE: DETROIT, June 27

 BODY:
The State of Michigan asked the Clinton Administration today for permission to undertake a vast welfare overhaul whose central provision would require work in exchange for benefits.
This afternoon the state delivered to the Federal Department of Health and Human Services an inch-high stack of documents requesting the necessary waivers -- 75 exemptions in all -- that would allow it to deviate from the Government's welfare rules. 
 In addition, two Michigan Republicans -- Senator Spencer Abraham and Representative Dave Camp -- introduced in Congress a pair of bills that would grant the waivers through legislation.
Michigan's Governor, John Engler, along with Gov. Tommy G. Thompson of Wisconsin, has been in the forefront of a Republican effort for a sweeping change in national policy that would end welfare's status as a Federal entitlement and grant the states, with virtually no strings, authority to design and administer their own welfare programs.
President Clinton signaled his support last September for one welfare-overhaul bill approved by the Senate but vetoed two subsequent versions, calling them too harsh on children. In the meantime, states like Michigan, granted a variety of waivers by Washington, have proceeded with their own experiments, imposing restrictions on welfare beneficiaries and creating work incentives.
In submitting the new waiver requests today, Mr. Engler, frequently mentioned as a possible running mate of Bob Dole, the apparent Republican Presidential nominee, blamed the Administration for the delay in changing Federal welfare law.
"President Clinton, who has said he wants to end welfare as we know it, has done his utmost to protect a failed system and preserve welfare as we know it," the Governor said.
In response, Melissa T. Skolfield, an Assistant Secretary of Health and Human Services, defended Mr. Clinton's record.
"The President has said repeatedly that he would like Congress to pass national welfare reform legislation," Ms. Skolfield said, "but in the meantime we have used our waiver authority under current law to aggressively reform the welfare system state by state."
In all, the Administration has now granted 40 states a total of 67 such waivers. An additional waiver seemed at hand last month, when Mr. Clinton, in a radio address, appeared to endorse a Wisconsin plan intended to abolish welfare. But Administration officials have since said they have concerns about some of that plan's central provisions.
Michigan and Wisconsin would move in the same direction: They would require nearly all welfare recipients to work, and they would quickly cut off benefits to those who did not. But the Michigan plan stops short of Wisconsin's proposal, which would not guarantee a job and could leave even those who qualify for benefits without government aid.
This difference in the two approaches may make it easier for Michigan to win Federal approval.
In fact, on Wednesday the Administration approved part of an earlier waiver request from Michigan: the Department of Health and Human Services agreed to allow the state to cut off cash benefits to teen-age parents who do not live with their own parents or other adults, and to teen-age parents who skip school.
On the other hand, the Department of Agriculture, which handles waiver requests for the food-stamp program, today denied Michigan permission to cut off stamps to the very same beneficiaries who were the subject of the Health and Human Services waiver. The department said it lacked authority to grant a waiver.
Mr. Engler today called for Mr. Clinton to approve Michigan's new waiver requests within 30 days. But Ms. Skolfield said a decision was unlikely to come so quickly because Michigan's complex application did not meet requirements for so-called fast-track approval. She said the Administration's goal would be to reach a decision within four months.
The Michigan plan takes a carrot-and-stick approach to welfare.
The state would cut off cash benefits and food stamps within 60 days to recipients who refused to take a job offered or, if no job was available, to those who refused to perform community-service work or enroll in job-training programs.
But the state would guarantee access to transportation and child care for those who participated in the program. Michigan would also pay to continue Medicaid benefits for one year for welfare recipients who find jobs in the private sector but lack health insurance.
Michigan's welfare caseload has dropped significantly over the last two years, but it is unclear whether it is the changes in the political climate or in the economic climate that deserve the greater credit.
Mr. Engler has repeatedly pointed to the decline in the state's caseload as evidence that his earlier welfare changes are working. There are now 176,634 people receiving welfare benefits in the state, down 20 percent from the level of two years ago.
But the numbers have been helped by Michigan's strong economy. Buoyed by an automobile industry that is earning record profits, the state has boomed in recent years.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gov. John Engler of Michigan, at a reception earlier this year, has been at the forefront of Republican efforts to change welfare policy. (Jana Menefee/The Detroit Free Press)

HEADLINE: Jack Reynolds, 62, a Journalist With NBC

 BODY:
Jack Reynolds, a broadcast journalist with NBC television for more than 30 years, died on Tuesday at Fairfax Hospital in Fairfax, Va. He was 62 and lived in Great Falls, Va.
The cause was colon cancer, said his wife, Judy. 
 Mr. Reynolds was born in Brooklyn and attended Fordham University. While at Fordham he worked for the college radio station and as a page at NBC. After earning a bachelor's degree in economics in 1955 and serving with the Marine Corps in the Far East, he held a variety of sales and executive positions with NBC and public television.
In 1961, he became public-affairs manager for NBC's New York affiliate, for which he also produced documentaries, and in 1967 he joined the network as manager of Asian operations for NBC News.
Over the years Mr. Reynolds produced and reported on the conflicts in Vietnam, Cambodia, El Salvador and Lebanon, as well as on economics in Japan and Korea. He was one of the first correspondents to visit the People's Republic of China, covering the visit of the United States Ping-Pong team and later, President Richard M. Nixon's trip to China in 1972.
In 1983 he became NBC's Pentagon correspondent. After retiring from the network in 1987, he founded Jack Reynolds Communications, a media consulting and production company.
In addition to his wife, he is survived by a son, Ian, of Boston; a daughter, Megan, who is attending school in London; a brother, Thomas, of Rockville, Md.; and a sister, Mary, of Reiserstown, Md.

HEADLINE: Look to the Peace Scaffolding, Mr. Netanyahu

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re your June 26 news article on the meeting between Secretary of State Warren Christopher and Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu of Israel in Jerusalem: 
 The agreements signed at Oslo and ratified in 1993 on the White House lawn were the most far-reaching initiative taken by an Israeli Government in recent decades.
If Israel's relationships with Egypt, Jordan, the Palestinian Authority, Morocco, Tunisia, Oman, Qatar, Mauritania and Turkey are weakened, its security and prestige in the world community will be seriously undermined.
The United States invested a great deal of its influence in helping Israel build new relationships in the Arab and Muslim worlds. When Arab reactions to new statements of Israeli policies seemed to threaten Israel's hard-won positions in the Middle East, it was natural for Secretary of State Christopher to feel and show concern. The danger of a halt in the expansion of Israel's international relations has not yet been overcome.
The previous Israeli government bequeathed a rich bounty to its successors: a booming economy, a growth of gross national product that most of the seven industrialized countries would envy, a series of recent anti-terrorist successes and the widest breach ever made in the wall of Arab and Muslim hostility.
Israel now has relations with 168 countries and is almost the least, not the most, isolated nation in the international system. The Middle East peace process is responsible for most of this.
Every government stands tall on the shoulders of its predecessors and then boasts of its own farsightedness. The existing 1993 peace process is a strong foundation on which Prime Minister Netanyahu can build. It is not a scaffolding that he can lightly discard.
 
ABBA EBAN
New York, June 26, 1996
 
The writer is a former Israeli Foreign Minister.

HEADLINE: Make Rent Increases Province of City Council

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The New York City Rent Guidelines Board decision to grant landlords the largest rent increase in seven years (news article,June 25) points to a fundamental flaw in the structure of our city government: economic decisions affecting millions of people should not be in the hands of unelected officials. 
 Voters and tenant groups are wasting their time and energy screaming and demonstrating at public hearings of the board, which is appointed by the mayor. Such hearings are little more than political window dressing, and news accounts invariably depict them as political circus.
Instead, voters should be directing efforts at making these decisions the responsibility of their elected representatives on the City Council, who will then be answerable in the most effective way possible.
Politicians will think twice before betraying their constituents when their jobs are on the line, and voters will regain a measure of control over vital economic decisions.
But this won't happen unless New Yorkers start to take city government more seriously. Only then can New Yorkers make city government more responsive to their needs, rather than the needs of banks and landlords.
 
SANFORD KLAVANSKY
New York, June 26, 1996
