HEADLINE: A Drive to Defeat Giuliani in 1997

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS 

 BODY:
The meetings have been held throughout the city for months now, in churches, in offices and in restaurants, and virtually every black elected official in New York City has attended one or more of them. The agenda is always the same: figuring out how to register more than 250,000 new voters in the next 17 months, create a robust "get out the vote" effort and, finally, defeat Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani.
The city's black elected officials have held strategy meetings before with the intention of affecting mayoral elections, with mixed results. In 1985, they tried to support Assemblyman Herman D. Farrell Jr. In 1977, they tried to rally behind the Manhattan Borough President, Percy E. Sutton. 
 Internal bickering derailed those efforts; with them went the possibility of establishing a powerful organization within the Democratic Party. Power gains after the successful 1989 campaign for David N. Dinkins were offset by Mr. Dinkins's defeat in 1993.
Now, perceiving that they are virtually shut out of the Mayor's circle, black politicians are reaching out in a show of unusual strength to other politicians, labor leaders and a cross-section of black clergy members. Notably, they are doing so in the absence of a black candidate.
One indicator of the strength of this effort is the leadership role played by H. Carl McCall, the State Comptroller and the first black official elected to statewide office.
"The idea is to make some collective decisions, to have some impact on the upcoming elections," said Mr. McCall, who, as the state's highest-ranking Democrat, has become a leader for black Democrats.
The meetings are called by the Council of Black Elected Democrats, a statewide organization of politicians. So far, a large number of politicians -- more than three dozen -- have been involved, including Representatives Charles B. Rangel of Manhattan and Major R. Owens and Edolphus Towns of Brooklyn. State assemblymen, state senators and City Council members from every borough have also been involved, including Councilwoman Helen M. Marshall of Queens and Senator David A. Paterson of Manhattan and Assembly members Gloria Davis of the Bronx and Albert Vann of Brooklyn.
In recent months, along with black politicians, the meetings have included black clergymen: the Rev. Dr. Wyatt Tee Walker of Canaan Baptist Church in Harlem, the Rev. Herbert Daughtry of the House of the Lord Pentecostal Church in Brooklyn and the Rev. Al Sharpton, the former candidate for United States Senate. And they have held meetings with labor leaders, like Brian M. McLaughlin, the Queens Assemblyman who is also president of the New York City Central Labor Council, and Dennis Rivera, president of 1199, the National Health and Human Services Employees Union.
At times, the meetings are structured, with officials from the State Legislature, the City Council and Congress assigned to make presentations on issues considered of particular concern to black voters. At other times, the discussions are freewheeling, focusing on a wide variety of topics, from how to raise money for voter registration to estimating the degree to which white Democratic leaders will support their efforts.
"We learned some lessons from David Dinkins's loss," said Gregory W. Meeks, an Assemblyman from Queens who is the chairman of the Council of Black Elected Democrats. "We learned that we can't rely on anyone to do this kind of work but ourselves. We have to be the ones to make sure people are registered and that we pull them out to vote."
The first priority is a voter registration drive, then a "get out the vote" effort for this year's Presidential election. That is meant as a dress rehearsal for the mayoral race.
The officials hope to capitalize on what they see as a strong anti-Giuliani feeling among black voters. That feeling was in evidence at a free concert in Harlem yesterday sponsored by the Fugees, the rap group.
The rally, the idea of the group's lead singer, Lauryn Hill, was dedicated to voter registration; dozens of volunteers circulated among the thousands of people at the concert, trying to get them to fill out voter registration forms. While Ms. Hill said the event was nonpolitical, there was a pronounced undercurrent of animosity toward Mayor Giuliani.
"Giuliani must go," said a 22-year-old senior at City College who gave his name only as Tre. "I never thought that voting accomplishes much. But with what he has done to raise tuition and everything else, I'm registering this time."
Andre Blackman, a recent Lehman College graduate, said: "There is a strong feeling among people that there is a need for change."
Mr. Meeks recently said: "Since we all agree that Giuliani must go, we're trying to use our collective strength to come together in a unified bloc to determine who the next mayor will be. We want to use the Presidential election to establish an infrastructure to be a major force in the 1997 election."
After interviews and discussions with various mayoral candidates, the council expects to endorse a candidate in the Democratic primary.
The clergymen and the labor leaders say they are interested in collaborating with the black officials on election strategies for the Clinton campaign and the voter registration drive. But the labor leaders say it is too early to decide political strategy for the next mayoral election.
"We're having dialogue with the black elected officials," Mr. McLaughlin said. "And when the time comes for us to make an endorsement, we will certainly consult with them. We think it's a powerful coalition that will strengthen with constructive dialogue. But for the 1997 election, it's still premature to discuss what we might do."
Whether this broad coalition lasts and bears fruit is a matter very much under debate. For one thing, many of these officials have already made their private alliances with Councilman Sal F. Albanese of Brooklyn, who has announced his candidacy, or with one of the all-but-announced Democratic contenders: the Manhattan Borough President, Ruth W. Messinger, and Fernando Ferrer, the Bronx Borough President. Still others are waiting to see if other candidates emerge.
Also, there is the clash of egos and interests encountered in any group of politicians. Those are heightened in part by historic political rifts between black Democratic officials in Manhattan and those in other boroughs, particularly Brooklyn.
In the last two mayoral elections, black elected officials were united in support of Mr. Dinkins. But seldom has there been much unanimity among black elected officials in the absence of a black candidate. In fact, there are already differing views among these very officials on whether the group should promote the candidacy of a black official for Mayor.
Then, there is the issue of how to finance such a large-scale voter-registration drive. Some officials in the group are looking for money from President Clinton's re-election campaign organization. But, many officials say, that will not be likely to come because the President is already doing well in the polls in New York.
And some black elected officials complain that the group of black Democrats are placing too much emphasis on political, rather then economic, solutions to problems.
"Frankly, I find it frustrating sometimes that our African-American leadership does not seem to accept the notion that economics and business development will determine the survival of our people," Representative Floyd H. Flake, a Democrat from Queens, said. "The political role is important, but it should not play the predominant role it has played over the last 30 years."
Also, it is unclear how fast an alliance with labor might hold, particularly where opposing Mr. Giuliani is concerned. Stanley Hill, executive director of the city's largest municipal employees' union, has participated in some of the meetings. But he recently stunned a group of Democrats at a labor breakfast by lauding two of New York's most prominent Republicans -- Mayor Giuliani and Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato -- for helping his members, while criticizing the state's leading Democrat, Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan.
Missing among the clergy is the Rev. Calvin O. Butts 3d of the Abyssinian Baptist Church in Harlem. Mr. Butts was so frustrated a few years ago by his lack of impact on the Democratic Party that he began courting Republicans.
Mr. Meeks and Mr. McCall concede that unanimity is an impossible goal and that what they are seeking is some degree of consensus. And while they say it will be impossible for everyone in the group to agree with the endorsement of a mayoral candidate, they add that their emphasis is largely on developing what they call "political infrastructure," voter registration and bringing voters to the polls.

HEADLINE: BOMBING IN SAUDI ARABIA: THE POLICY;
Clinton Asks Summit Partners to Join in Battle on Terrorism

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: LYONS, France, June 27

 BODY:
President Clinton appealed today to the leaders of the world's biggest economic powers to join the United States in a stepped-up international campaign against crime and terrorism in the wake of Tuesday's bombing in Saudi Arabia.
A few hours before the start of the annual meeting of the Group of Seven nations, Mr. Clinton traveled from Lyons, France's second city, to the restored medieval village of Perouges, northeast of here. In a speechthere to villagers, war veterans and schoolchildren gathered in a cobblestone square, he said: "We must rally the forces of tolerance and freedom everywhere to work against terrorism." 
 He said he would put to his colleagues "40 very specific recommendations to combat crime and terror, to increase our efforts to prevent terrorists from committing their crimes and our ability to track, catch and punish them when they do." Apparently the proposals were recast this week following the bombing in Dhahran, in which 19 Americans were killed; last week, officials in Washington described them as a 40-point program to fight crime and money-laundering.
Mr. Clinton plans to cut short a visit to Paris on Saturday, the White House said, in order to fly to Florida, where he will attend memorial services on Sunday at Eglin Air Force Base and at Patrick Air Force Base.
The President was pressed by reporters to say whether greater precautions should have been taken at the Saudi compound where the Americans died, particularly in view of the devastating 1983 bombings in Beirut. He said that a fence gave the Dhahran installation "a 35-yard cushion, and the bomb was just bigger than anyone calculated could be gotten in that close to the building."
After his trip to Perouges, a fortified hilltop village so picturesque that is often used as a set for movies, the President met individually with President Jacques Chirac of France, Prime Minister John Major of Britain and Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto of Japan. He said they had agreed to support a lot of his recommendations and added, "I believe they will be willing to do more."
Asked whether some of the other nations represented here would cease economic contacts with countries the United States considers sponsors of terrorism, such as Iran, Syria and Libya, Mr. Clinton replied: "Let's just wait and see. You know what my position is on that, so we'll just keep working on it."
But several senior American officials said they expected no broad agreement to emerge from the three-day meeting here, which involves Canada, Italy and Germany in addition to Japan, Britain, France and the United States. Prime Minister Viktor S. Chernomyrdin of Russia is scheduled to join the talks on Saturday morning.
At Perouges, Mr. Clinton spoke at length on links between the United States and France, whose relations have greatly improved recently after several rocky decades. He noted that the village's mayor, Guy Passart de la Chapelle, had an ancestor, Pierre, who fought in the American Revolution, and that American troops of the 45th Davision helped to liberate this area late in August 1944.
Standing in the shadow of an ancient plane tree believed to have been planted in 1792, the final year of the French Revolution, the President said that rapid change poses fresh challenges.
"While new technologies and rapid movements of information and money and people across national borders bring all of us closer together," he declared in a 16-minute speech, "they also make all of us more open to common dangers -- crime, drug trafficking and terrorism, as we saw in Saudi Arabia."
Although the crowd was relatively sparse, perhaps because of security considerations, the schoolchildren were animated, waving French and American flags, and the village's ramparts, lane and tile roofs provided a vivid backdrop for the omnipresent television cameras.
The first big event of the summit meeting, as has become traditional, was the "class photo" of the leaders, taken at the Lyons City Hall. They then ate a dinner cooked by four chefs from towns and cities around Lyons whose restaurants have won three stars in the Michelin guide, including Paul Bocuse, long the high priest of French gastronomy.
Of the President's bilateral discussions, the talk with Mr. Hashimoto appeared the most substantive. Laura Tyson, chairwoman of the Council of Economic Advisers, said Mr. Clinton had expressed concern that little progress had been made since 1993 on trade issues involving insurance and semiconductors.
In a 30-minute meeting that "went beyond platitudes and got into real issues," she reported, the two leaders agreed to try to resolve the outstanding questions by the end of July.
Mr. Chirac, the host for this meeting, the 21st of its kind, complained to Mr. Clinton that the decline of American aid to poorer nations was undercutting the United States' leadership. Mr. Clinton blamed Republicans in Congress for the decline. Like other European leaders, Mr. Chirac also protested American moves to penalize countries who continue to trade with Cuba.

CORRECTION-DATE: June 29, 1996, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about an appeal by President Clinton for an international campaign against terrorism misidentified Laura Tyson. She is chairman of the National Economic Council; she left the chairmanship of the Council of Economic Advisers last year.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Climbs Near 110 Yen As Japan Rate-Rise Fears Ebb

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar rose to within a whisker of 110 yen yesterday, reaching its highest level in almost 29 months, as concerns about an increase in Japanese interest rates faded.
The yen fell against the dollar and other leading currencies after Japan said that its unemployment rate rose to a record 3.5 percent in May. The labor report reinforced the view that Japan's economic recovery was still too fragile to withstand an increase in interest rates. Higher rates in Japan would make some investments there more attractive, helping the yen. 
 The unemployment figure "takes away the view of an imminent Japanese rate increase," said Seth Cohen, director of currency sales at Merrill Lynch & Company. "That's firing up the dollar."
The dollar's gains also came as leaders of the Group of Seven industrial nations met for a second day in Lyons, France.
The dollar climbed as high as 109.98 yen, its highest since Jan. 31, 1994, when it reached 110.26 yen. When the dollar failed to breach 110 yen, a psychologically important level, profit takers stepped in.
"We tried and couldn't get over it," said Lizbeth Goldberg, a currency saleswoman at the Bayerische Hypotheken und Wechsel Bank. "It will take quite a push to go through it."
John Trammell, who manages currency investments for the A. Gary Shilling Company of Springfield, N.J., said, "As the dollar approached 110, there was a massive amount of selling."
In late trading in New York yesterday, the dollar settled at 109.72 yen, up from 109.10 yen on Thursday. The dollar stood at 1.5241 German marks, up from 1.5180 marks. The British pound rose to $1.5505 from $1.5480.
The Group of Seven -- the United States, Japan, Germany, Britain, France, Italy and Canada -- issued a statement yesterday welcoming the dollar's recovery from last year's post-World War-II lows against the mark and yen as "positive and promising."
Yet because the group stopped short of calling for a stronger dollar, traders said the impact on currency rates was slight.
"They're in favor of allowing the market to continue seeking an equilibrium level for the dollar," Mr. Cohen of Merrill Lynch said. "That implies tacit approval for a stronger dollar."

HEADLINE: Observer;
Used to Talk, Now Shrieks

BYLINE: By RUSSELL BAKER 

 BODY:
It's all about money now. I saw a man standing on the street corner talking into his cellular phone. He was talking about money. What other subject is there nowadays? If I had a cellular phone I, too, would take it out to a street corner and talk about money. I can hear me now:
"How did gold open in Tokyo?" Or, "Sell 100,000 shares of Allied Apricot before noon." Or, "Buy me the most expensive haircut on the Atlantic Coast, scissors on the side." 
 I saw a man at the beach with a computer. He was asking it to check London to see if he could make a big killing in bonds before teatime; and if not in bonds, in stocks; and if not in stocks, how about pork bellies? How was the price of Joe DiMaggio baseball cards on the Bourse this afternoon?
If I had a computer suitable for the beach, I'd be doing the same thing if I knew what the Bourse was. Oh yes, of course I know it is in Paris and has something to do with money, money, money, money. Doesn't everything nowadays?
I saw the Senate kill a campaign finance reform bill. It was a bill filled with a hostility for money so alien to our age that it seemed to have risen from the antique mire. If I had a Senate seat bought for me at great expense by a lot of gigantic corporations, I too would vote to kill such a bill. I can hear me now:
"What is wrong, senators, with letting the corporations who run not only America but also the world -- what is wrong, I ask you with customary senatorial prolixity -- what is wrong with letting these great corporations choose the Senate which not only makes the laws they must obey, but also provides, protects and preserves the Federal subsidies on which they thrive?"
Money, money, money, money. Who can imagine anything else in the present age? I foresee schoolbook censors any day now banning all writings that suggest, no matter how subtly, that money cannot buy happiness.
The new formulation is thus: Happiness can't buy money.
Here are some other new formulations appropriate for today's America:
". . . o'er the land of the money and home of the big windfall."
"I pledge allegiance to the money and to my brand new Range Rover for which it stands . . ."
". . . among these are life, liberty and the pursuit of money." Or possibly, ". . . among these are life, money, and the pursuit of money."
Though, preferably, ". . . among these are money, money and the pursuit of money."
"Greed is bad, but there are things even worse than greed, like not owning a senator or a Range Rover."
Why all this interest in money? Is it sour grapes? Is it an expression of unseemly bitterness provoked by my failure to accumulate some capital gains?
To quote the philosopher Fats Waller, "One never knows, do one?" What I think I'm trying for, though, is a description of the environment in which the Clinton-Dole campaign is being conducted. The campaign already feels as if it has been in progress since the fall of the Bastille and seems likely to go on until Doomsday. Given so much idle time to think about a campaign that is clearly brain dead, we naturally start asking, "What's going on here -- if anything?"
Politics is mostly about which part of the electorate gets the money. Saying so is usually said to be cynical, but this is one of those periodic times in American history when the question of who gets the money is too blatantly in the open to be ignored.
Our eyes tell us the country is incredibly rich, while our politicians insist we are too poor to afford for millions the amenities of civilization, and economists tell us the middle class is shrinking.
The great beneficiary of the "No new taxes!" movement has been the rolling-in-money class, which, judging from the evidence of our eyes, can well afford to come across with more money. Not a chance. It uses its wealth to club any politician insolent enough to note that the well heeled don't pay their fair share.
Throughout his Presidency, Bill Clinton has respected that threat at least as faithfully as Senator Dole. Or, to put it impolitely, what difference does it make which man wins? Reagan, Bush, Clinton, Dole -- the more names change, the more things stay the same. Money, money, money, money and money rule.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed

HEADLINE: First-Quarter Growth Put Lower, at 2.2%

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
The economy grew at a slower pace than previously estimated in the first quarter, reflecting a lull in export demand, Government figures showed today.
The nation's gross domestic product rose at an annual rate of 2.2 percent, the Commerce Department said. Before the report, analysts had expected a gain of 2.3 percent, the same as the department's previous estimate. In the fourth quarter, the G.D.P. edged up just five-tenths of 1 percent. 
 The Commerce Department regularly revises its G.D.P. statistics as more information becomes available. Today's report marks the second and final revision of the first-quarter figures.
"It still shows the economy is healthy and it won't greatly change what we're likely to see for the third and fourth quarters," said Gary Thayer, an economist at A. G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis.
The report comes as Federal Reserve policy makers prepare to meet on Tuesday and Wednesday. Economists said the revised G.D.P. data confirmed their expectations that the Fed would not raise short-term interest rates next week.
The revision also confirmed the bond market's expectations, and Treasury prices rose sharply. The rally in bonds was also fed by a report from the Chicago Purchasing Management Association.
The association said its monthly index of regional manufacturing conditions rose to 53.3 from 53, suggesting regional manufacturing was improving at a sluggish pace. The prices-paid index rose to 53 from 52.2, a signal that more manufacturers were paying higher prices. Although both indexes rose, the gains were less than some economists expected.
The latest G.D.P. report "does not substantially change the general picture of the economy presented by the preliminary estimate," said J. Steven Landefeld, director of the Commerce Department's Bureau of Economic Analysis. "First-quarter growth was markedly higher than in the fourth quarter of 1995," he said, paced by stronger consumer demand for big-ticket products.
For example, real final sales, which exclude the effects of inventory changes, rose 3.3 percent in the first quarter, after increasing 1.6 percent in the fourth quarter.
By category, consumer spending increased 3.6 percent in the first quarter, compared with a 1.2 percent increase in the fourth quarter of last year. Net exports of goods and services rose in the first quarter at a slower pace than first thought. Exports grew 2 percent in the first quarter, compared with a previous estimate of a 4.9 percent increase and an 11 percent gain in the fourth quarter.
First-quarter business inventories declined by $2.1 billion, a slower pace of liquidation than previously estimated, but still the first drop since the first quarter of 1992. Shrinking supplies of cars and trucks accounted for much of the decline.
The G.D.P. chain-weighted index, which tracks changes in the price of all goods and services made in the United States, increased at a 2.4 percent rate in the first quarter. In this category, too, there was no revision.
On the inflation front, the G.D.P. price deflator, a measure of price increases followed by Wall Street, grew at a 2.1 percent pace in the first quarter, unchanged from the last estimate. The price deflator measures the magnitude of price increases, and detects whether higher costs drive consumers away from a product.
The Commerce Department also reported today that after-tax corporate profits increased 5.9 percent during the first quarter after rising 1.9 percent in the fourth quarter.

HEADLINE: Reporter's Notebook;
Hungry for Respect, France's 2d City Puts On Its Best for Visitors

BYLINE: By R.W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: LYONS, France, June 28

 BODY:
Lyons doesn't get much respect, either from the snooty Parisians or from foreigners otherwise entranced with France.
But it hopes to make a better impression this week on the visitors -- politicians, aides and journalists -- who are here for the annual conference that brings together the leaders of the Group of Seven industrialized democracies. 
 Raymond Barre, a former French Prime Minister who was elected Mayor last year at the age of 71, sees the summit meeting as a kind of "debutantes' ball" that will give Lyons a chance to demonstrate its charms. At the moment, he conceded recently, "Lyons is not very well known or recognized," although it is France's second largest city, with a metropolitan population of 1,200,000.
It is, in fact, a lovely place, full of handsome, well-kept old buildings and elegant squares. For the visitors' benefit, the city has been brilliantly floodlighted and some of the bouchons, the working-class bistros unique to Lyons, have been told to scrub themselves down. This is necessary, French television reported this week, so that Americans will not be frightened away by "their fear of germs."
Giant masts have been built on the east bank of the Rhone, opposite President Clinton's hotel, to fly the flags of the nations represented here. Posters showing childrens' impressions of the participating countries have been put up on street corners; the American one shows the McDonald's logo, the World Trade Center, Mickey Mouse and a forest of Coca-Cola bottles.
Officials estimate the cost of all the preparations at about $4 million, but they expect the visitors to spend more than $5 million.
 
Bringing Tricolors to France
There are certain set-pieces at each of these annual fiestas.
Inevitably, a political issue pops up at the last minute to deflect the leaders' attention from their economic agenda. This time, terrorism, in the form of Tuesday's bomb attack in Saudi Arabia, is the uninvited guest, horning in on a conference that President Jacques Chirac of France wanted to focus on aid to the third world.
Inevitably, too, the police close major streets. The locals grumble. The locals stay home. The cab drivers and restaurateurs (and, on the two occasions when the meeting was held in Venice, the gondoliers) lose money.
This time, by way of compensation for the Lyonnais, a big concert is planned for Saturday night, after the conference has ended, with prominent musicians from each G-7 country. The American representative was to have been Bob Dylan, but "it proved impossible," a local official noted sourly, "for him to be here and in Hyde Park in London on the same night." Mr. Dylan will be performing at a big concert in London on Saturday.
One American who did show up, President Clinton, has been applauded, although the sheer scope of the White House entourage has, as always, raised eyebrows. At the medieval village of Perouges on Thursday, the chief of police, Francois L'Esprit, complained about French flags distributed to schoolchildren by presidential advance men hoping to pep up television pictures.
"Why does he bring everything with him?" the chief asked. "Does he think we have no flags of our own? This is not Africa or China, you know."
 
A Flap Over Chickens
For Mr. Clinton and Chancellor Helmut Kohl of Germany, the champion trenchermen among this week's visitors, Lyons has put its best plates forward.
Tonight the leaders had dinner at Leon de Lyon, a much-beloved establishment in the center of the city that features such dishes as pike dumplings, frog's legs, suckling pig and "six desserts on a praline theme."
The city proper has no three-star restaurants, as defined by the Michelin guide, but there are four within a drive of an hour or so. Their chefs cooked the leaders' dinner on Thursday night -- Paul Bocuse of Collonges-au-Mont-d'Or in the Lyons suburbs, Pierre Troisgros of Roanne, George Blanc of Vonnas and the new boy in the group, Marc Veyrat of the Auberge de l'Eridan on the Lake of Annecy.
No nouvelle cuisine, Mr. Chirac cautioned them, no fancy stuff. So Mr. Blanc, a third-generation chef, made his grandmother's famous creamed chicken, with a few special touches such as a garnish of chicken livers. Of course, he used the local Bresse chickens, France's finest, which have coloration appropriate for a patriotic occasion like this -- red crests, white plumage and blue feet.
Mr. Blanc is president of the resoundingly titled Interprofessional Committee for the Promotion of Bresse Chicken, and he knows Mr. Clinton comes from a big chicken-producing state. In a chat the other day, he even mentioned Don Tyson, the Arkansas poultry magnate and amply certified Friend of Bill.
So it has come as something of a disappointment to the chef that he has been unable to gain permission to introduce Bresse chickens to the United States. Someone told him he would do better if he put some American wines on the list at his restaurant, so now there are a few puddles of Ridge and Mondavi from California amidst the lakes of Burgundy and Rhone and Bordeaux on offer chez Blanc.
"It didn't work," Mr. Blanc said. "It's very sad, because they are wonderful birds. There must be something here that I don't understand."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Keep Trucks Out of Saratoga Park

 BODY:
The mineral springs at Saratoga were first used by the people of the Iroquois Confederacy. Since early colonial days the waters have drawn visitors who came to relax in mineral baths. Today Saratoga Spa State Park includes a grand Georgian hotel, two race tracks, bridle paths, golf courses, a trout stream and a heron rookery. Saratoga is something of a cultural and social icon to New Yorkers. Its environment does not need more stress.
Even so, some local people, citing increased traffic congestion in town, want to put a new truck route through the southern section of the park. Parks Commissioner Bernadette Castro said this spring that she would not allow it, and Gov. George Pataki indicated that he backed her decision. But local business people keep pressuring the Governor, despite the fact that other alternatives -- including roads outside the park -- have not been adequately studied. The Commissioner and the Governor should resist this pressure. 
 Earlier this month the State Senate majority leader, Joseph Bruno, whose district includes Saratoga, introduced a provision in the Senate version of the state budget to set aside $500,000 to study possible solutions to the congestion problem. Mr. Bruno says he does not favor any particular plan.
Some boosters of the park route have resorted to inflammatory language, painting environmentalists concerned about its impact as fanatics and describing the issue as "jobs vs. frogs." They point out that the route through the park would be the most convenient way to link the current truck route with a local industrial park.
But state parks are not created to be partitioned by roads whenever it is commercially convenient. The local task force that studied the problem was weighted with people who favored the park route and did not seriously consider alternatives. It seemed unimpressed by the damage a truck route would cause to fragile wetlands, with their breeding grounds for the great blue heron.
Ms. Castro and Mr. Pataki were on the right track. The money appropriated by Senator Bruno should be used to find a good alternate route. Saratoga Park, which attracts 1.4 million visitors a year and clearly benefits the local economy, should be left intact.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Dow Falls on Mixed Signals In End-of-Quarter Action

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Blue-chip stocks gave back early gains to close lower yesterday, but technology stocks led the Nasdaq market to its second consecutive gain.
The Dow Jones industrial average ended 22.90 points lower at 5,654.63 after gaining 27 points early in the day. For the week, it was down 50.60 points. 
 But broader indexes were higher. The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks rose 2.08 points, to 670.63. And the Nasdaq composite index surged 19.01 points, to 1,185.02 -- its biggest daily percentage gain in four months.
Analysts said they did not think there was much to be gleaned from the day's action. They said the market's mixed signals were typical of end-of-the-quarter portfolio adjustments.
"At the end of the quarter a lot of strange things happen," said Peter Canelo, chief investment strategist at Dean Witter. "It's difficult or dangerous to read things into this."
The stock market largely shrugged off a strong rally in bonds on growing expectations that the economy was not growing fast enough to accelerate inflation.
The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond -- a benchmark for corporate and consumer interest rates -- fell to 6.89 percent, from 6.99 percent on Thursday. Bond prices and their yields move in opposite directions.
Interest-sensitive stocks, particularly utilities, benefited from the rate outlook.
Among utility stocks, PECO Energy rose 1, to 26, and DTE Energy gained 1 1/8, to 30 7/8.
Investors will soon start focusing on second-quarter earnings reports, looking for clues as to which group of stocks will replace technology as market leaders, analysts said.
The market is currently lacking direction, said Ken Tower, an analyst at UST Securities. "Stocks are going through a very tough time," he said.
Several initial public offerings were strong yesterday, with closely watched Donna Karan International Inc. gaining 4, to 28, in its first session.
Among other initial stock offerings, Check Point Software Technologies jumped 10, to 24; Seibel Systems surged 13 3/4, to 30 3/4, and Factset Research Systems rose 3, to 20.
Union Carbide lost 5/8, to 39 3/4, after the company said its second-quarter earnings would be hurt by falling ethylene glycol prices.
Commercial Intertech leaped 6 3/4, to 25 7/8, after United Dominion Industries offered $27 a share in an unsolicited bid Thursday for the diversified manufacturer.
Planet Hollywood International gained 4 1/4, to 27. It agreed to form a joint venture with the ITT Corporation to develop themed casino hotels in Las Vegas, Atlantic City and other resort areas. ITT rose 1/4, to 66 1/4.
American depository receipts of Teva Pharmaceutical Industries of Israel added 3 1/2, to 37 7/8, after saying it expected sales and gross profits at its American generic drug unit to top its first-quarter results and those of last year's second quarter. Each A.D.R. represents 10 ordinary shares.
Stocks overseas ended the week just slightly higher. The Nikkei index of 225 Japanese issues closed up 29.14
points, or one-tenth of 1 percent, at 22,530.75, a rise of only 0.1 point from last Friday. In London, the Financial Times-Stock Exchange index of 100 leading shares closed 32.2 points higher at 3,711.2, its biggest point gain since mid-April. But for the week, the index was down 11.1 points.

HEADLINE: Summit Meeting Endorses Plan Giving Police Freer Rein to Pursue Criminals Abroad

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: LYONS, France, June 28

 BODY:
The leaders of the seven largest industrial democracies and Russia endorsed a 40-point plan today to combat crime and terrorism that would, if fully carried out, give the police far freer rein to pursue criminals abroad.
The plan, disclosed at the 22d annual summit meeting of the Group of Seven countries, would allow the United States and other countries to post investigators on foreign soil and would require the seven allies to crack down on the import and export of firearms. 
 The plan was under development before the terrorist bombing on Tuesday that killed 19 American servicemen in Dhahran, Saudi Arabia. But President Clinton, seizing the moment to drag along allies who have often been reluctant to agree on concrete steps that impinge on their national sovereignty, pressed to tighten some of the provisions.
Some of the steps, originally packaged as a broader anti-crime initiative, appeared aimed more at drug dealers and corrupt business executives than at terrorists. But Administration officials pointed to requirements that nations coordinate prosecutions when crimes occur across borders and that they "insure that criminals and terrorists are brought to justice and tried" even if extradition proves impossible.
The other members of the Group of Seven -- Britain, France, Germany, Italy, Canada and Japan -- are also bound to develop witness protection programs, much like the one used in the United States, to give new identities to people who testify against organized crime groups.
While they were eager to portray the agreement as major progress, Administration officials conceded today that turning those general agreements into national laws and regulations would be a long struggle.
"These are countries that often can't agree on the rules for selling planes to China or moving lumber across borders," a senior Administration official said tonight.
"We'd be fooling ourselves to think any of this will happen instantly. But it's a start."
An example of the complications over extradition was unfolding in Rome as the leaders gathered: this week, Italy's constitutional court blocked the extradition to Florida of a man wanted on charges of first-degree murder because he could face the death penalty. Italian law does not permit the death penalty.
The anti-crime and anti-terrorism principles would require major changes in Italian and Russian laws, and major changes in common practices elsewhere. For example, the new rules call for all the G-7 nations to strengthen "laws to prevent bribery and extortion." While that sounds non-controversial, until a few months ago German companies paying bribes abroad were permitted to deduct them from their taxes.
The most visible divisions among the leaders were over American efforts to isolate Iran, Libya and Cuba and over strategies to relieve the world's poorest nations from their huge debt loads.
Several leaders chastised Mr. Clinton for signing legislation that would punish foreign companies that do business with Cuba and for announcing that he would go along with a bill approved by Congress a week ago that would similarly penalize companies that invest in Iran and Libya.
At a briefing for reporters today, Daniel Tarullo, a top official of the National Economic Council, said that "the President pushed back fairly hard," telling the allies that they needed to "confront terrorist states to take action against countries that are threats to democracy and to the safety of our citizens."
Separately, a dispute flared over a plan pressed by Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin to arrange for the International Monetary Fund to sell part of its $40 billion in gold reserves. The proceeds of a sale of $2 billion to $3 billion in gold would be invested in interest-generating financial instruments, and the resulting income would be used to pay off debt for third-world countries without requiring donor countries to allocate more resources to pay off the loans. The World Bank would pay off roughly $2 billion, and individual nations would forgive other debt as part of a larger initiative.
After a lengthy struggle with countries opposed to the plan, led by Germany, the United States won wording in the economic communique that Mr. Rubin said would allow the gold sales to move forward after the I.M.F.'s annual meeting in the fall.
"We've all agreed that the I.M.F. should fund this primarily through its own resources, and we've all agreed to optimizing the assets of the I.M.F. to do so," Mr. Rubin said.
But just before he spoke, the German Chancellor, Helmut Kohl, insisted that the wording of the communique would allow the sale of gold to be blocked. Germany has steadfastly insisted that the gold reserves should be held for true emergencies.
Further sparring broke out over Mr. Clinton's presentation about the halving of the Federal budget deficit and the creation of 9.7 million jobs by the American economy.
President Jacques Chirac of France, where unemployment rates are running upward of twice the level of those in the United States, acknowledged that "the U.S. has created a lot of jobs," but added, "A lot are underpaid ones that we wouldn't find acceptable in Europe."
The leaders will meet again on Saturday, the last day of the conference. Mr. Clinton will then travel to Paris for dinner with Mr. Chirac, and later fly to Florida for a Sunday memorial service for those killed in the attack in Saudi Arabia.

HEADLINE: Inflation Fear Falls, Sending Long Bond Up

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury prices surged yesterday as investors increased their bets that the Federal Reserve would not raise interest rates next week.
Data indicating that the economy was not growing fast enough to spur inflation confirmed to many in the market that the Fed would have no reason to act when its policy-making committee begins a two-day meeting on Tuesday. Such expectations combined with end-of-the-quarter buying by portfolio managers fed the rally. 
 The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 12/32 points, to 8826/32 . The bond's yield fell to 6.89 percent, from 6.99 percent on Thursday, and to the lowest since 6.89 percent on June 6.
Investors gained about $13 on the day for every $1,000 invested in bonds.
Astrid Adolfson, an economist at MCM Moneywatch, described yesterday's trading as a feeding frenzy. Yesterday's data provided more evidence that the economy was growing at a moderate pace, she said.
Bonds first received a boost after the Commerce Department revised the first quarter's gross domestic product growth to 2.2 percent, down from 2.3 percent. The report indicated the economy was growing at a slower pace than estimated before.
The rally picked up after the a report from the Chicago Purchasing Management Association came in weaker than expected. The purchasing managers' index for June, which had been expected to rise to 54.6, came in at 53.3, compared with 53.0 in May. The prices-paid index rose to 53 from 52.2, but was also weaker than expected.
Ms. Adolfson, however, said, "I think the market got a little too revved up" over the purchasing managers' figures because some of the components should have been down due to seasonal factors, suggesting "some price pressures."
Another source of buying in the market was portfolio managers seeking high-rate securities to dress up their portfolios as the quarter ended yesterday. Managers who had previously stayed on the sidelines, uncertain of the Fed's next move, increased their positions.
Market participants have been increasingly confident that the Fed's policy-making panel, the Federal Open Market Committee, will leave interest rates unchanged after its meeting next week. The market does expect the Fed will increase rates in the coming months.
Still, Ms. Adolfson noted, "There is no guarantee next week when the F.O.M.C. meets."
She said other data still awaited the market, including the National Association of Purchasing Management's report for June on Monday and the Government's June employment report on Friday. "Any of these are factors that could shift the landscape in a hurry," she said.
In the futures market, Robin Mesch, chief fixed-income analyst at Thomson Research of Boston, said buyers bought their way through 108 31/32 and 109 12/32 , "and then it was buy, buy, and more buy." The September bond futures contract closed several ticks below the day's session high at 109 17/32 .
Donald H. Straszheim, chief economist at Merrill Lynch's Global Securities Research and Economics Group, said the bond market's rally from 7.20 percent to 6.89 percent over the last two weeks was striking given the lack of new data.

HEADLINE: Wall St. Draws Record Fees in Stock Issues

BYLINE: By PETER TRUELL 

 BODY:
As the stock market set records, Wall Street firms enjoyed their strongest stock-offering business in many years during the second quarter, reaping record underwriting fees of almost $3 billion.
As of yesterday, the last business day of the quarter, the securities industry underwrote $236.6 billion of domestic debt and equity issues, down slightly from a restated $240.2 billion in the first quarter because of the slower market for debt issues. 
 In the last three months, Wall Street firms underwrote $40.97 billion of common stock and $128.2 billion of straight and convertible debt issues, the Securities Data Company, a New Jersey statistical concern, estimated.
That represents an 81 percent increase in domestic equity underwriting from $22.6 billion in the first quarter, but a decline of 16 percent from $153 billion of debt issues in the first quarter.
The rise in long-term interest rates slowed debt issues in the United States, traders said. Securities firms generally earn significantly bigger fees for underwriting stocks than debt.
In the underwriting rankings, Merrill Lynch & Company led the combined totals for debt and equity offerings. Goldman, Sachs & Company, meanwhile, replaced Morgan Stanley & Company as the leader in domestic equity offerings during the second quarter, Securities Data said.
Investors helped to create this boom in underwriting by continuing to pour money into mutual funds in the last three months. Interest rates also remained relatively low, despite the uptick in long-term interest rates.
A strong stock market that has climbed about 10 percent so far this year, coupled with a climate of business confidence, fueled the underwriting business throughout the quarter, traders and investment bankers said.
"Never underestimate cash," said Brian Hehir, a managing director at Merrill Lynch who helps to run that firm's equity business, highlighting the critical role played by the huge amounts of cash investors are putting in mutual funds.
Fund managers have to place that money, creating a benign environment for the underwriting of debt and equity securities.
This has all led to a bonanza of fee income on Wall Street. "It's been a record quarter for equities, and a record quarter for debt and equity fees, which exceeded $2.91 billion for the quarter," said Richard J. Peterson, an analyst at Securities Data. "The previous fee record was $2.7 billion in the fourth quarter of 1993."
He said equity fees had been particularly strong in the first half, representing more than $3 billion of the more than $4.6 billion of fees for the first six months.
The debt markets have also been busy despite the drop in business for domestic debt issues. "It's been a real strong quarter," said John C. Qua, a managing director in Merrill Lynch's debt capital markets division, "even though rates have been creeping up here." He noted that long-term interest rates had risen more than one percentage point since the beginning of the year.
The continuing boom in mergers and acquisitions has helped to bolster the underwriting business. The second quarter, Mr. Qua said, "saw a re-emergence of corporate bond issues." Many American companies had been borrowing debt overseas, taking advantage of the scarcity in foreign markets of their established names.
Even though the debt and equity underwriting markets seem so healthy, some bankers sounded a note of caution. "The quarter continued strong from the first quarter, but in the last three weeks the market began to exhibit some signs of overcongestion, particularly among small-cap stocks," said J. Richard T. Blackett, a managing director at Salomon Brothers Inc. who is co-manager of that firm's equity underwriting business.
Several mutual fund managers, who are judged on their quarterly performance numbers, backed away from smaller-capitalization technology stocks, which have generally not performed as well as the overall stock market in recent weeks.
But, Mr. Blackett said, "We expect that after July 4 the market will continue strongly, and that the shakeout in small technology issues will be the primary focus of interest. The question is, is it temporary?"
Still, his overall conclusion is "the market looks robust," and he said that this had indeed probably been his department's busiest quarter in years.
The hectic pace of underwriting seems to have taken its toll. "I think all of us are exhausted, everyone in the industry is exhausted at the pace of transactions," Mr. Hehir at Merrill Lynch said. "The question is, can you do it again?"
Several big international new-equity issues are set to come to market in the next week or two, including a stock offering of about $1 billion for Telefonica de Peru, an issue of $1.9 billion for Royal Ahold, the company that is buying Stop and Shop, and an issue of about $1.9 billion for Pharmacia & Upjohn, the pharmaceutical concern.
Reviewing these prospects, Mr. Hehir said the underwriting outlook at the beginning of the third quarter might even be as good as that at the start of the second quarter.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Austerity Plan For Workers Is Approved In Germany

BYLINE: By ALAN COWELL 

DATELINE: BONN, June 28

 BODY:
Parliament approved major parts of an austerity plan today that would reduce workers' sick pay and job security in an effort to increase the competitiveness of the nation's industry.
Since the measure was introduced, workers have staged protest strikes and have accused the Government of destroying Germany's generous welfare state. 
 The measures reflected the wider concerns among European governments that budget deficits and unemployment are both far too high at a time when the European Union is demanding tighter fiscal management as the price for a single currency to compete in an increasingly global market.
Coming soon after Parliament's decision last week to loosen Germany's highly restrictive shopping hours, today's decisions provided further evidence of the pressures exerted by a changing world economy and the dictates of European integration on the country's rigid economic habits and ingrained social assumptions.
While the measures are in no way comparable to the sweeping changes of Margaret Thatcher's Britain or the United States in the Reagan era, they nonetheless represent an intimation for many Germans that the days of ever-expanding comfort are over.
By a narrow margin reflecting the governing coalition's 10-seat majority, the lower house approved the measures as Chancellor Helmut Kohl and other European leaders from the Group of Seven industrialized nations, meeting in Lyons, France, forecast economic improvements later this year.
But outside Parliament, in the streets, hundreds of thousands of Germans have taken part in organized protests since the plan was presented, asserting that Bonn's austerity measures punish workers while making no demands on their employers.
"Today, in every part of Germany, workers are protesting against the Government's savings plans," Oskar Lafontaine, the leader of the opposition Social Democrats, said in Parliament. "People have understood what is going on. This is about domestic peace and the stability of our democracy.
"End this policy of social injustice."
Earlier this month, more than 300,000 labor union members converged on Bonn to protest the austerity package. Across Germany on Thursday, before today's vote, some 200,000 workers staged demonstrations that ranged from brief work stoppages to human chains and processions of cars.
The measures approved today would reduce sick pay by one-fifth, to 80 percent of workers' salaries, and would make it easier for companies with 10 employees to dismiss workers. The existing law on dismissals is limited to companies with five or fewer employees.
In addition, charges for prescription medications were increased while state support for Germans to spend time at health spas was reduced.
A separate vote on increasing the retirement age for women to 65 from 60 by the year 2005 and freezing unemployment benefits was postponed until July 9 because of a parliamentary maneuver by the Government to legislate the proposals without the approval of Parliament's upper house, which is controlled by the Social Democrats.
"We are not making these savings for millionaires, but for millions of people who pay their contributions," the Labor Minister, Norbert Blum, said in Parliament, reflecting the Government's argument that the savings would create jobs by making it cheaper for employers to hire people.
"Our aim is to promote hiring, not firing," he said, arguing that the Government's actions would cut Germany's labor costs -- the world's highest -- by almost $50 billion by the end of the century.
Reflecting the same philosophy, Peter Hintze, the secretary general of Mr. Kohl's Christian Democrats, said on Thursday that Germany "will only achieve more employment if we attain greater flexibility, a cautious reduction of welfare costs and improved opportunities for people to become self-reliant."
Bonn is seeking savings of some $33 billion in public spending next year coupled with cuts of about $13 billion in social security budgets to reduce its overall budget deficit to the margins set by the European Union as criteria for the creation of a single currency, one of Mr. Kohl's main policy goals.
The measures approved today, coupled with the legislation on pensions, should save about one-third of the total, with the rest coming from limited pay increases for public service workers and from fiscal measures that still have to be approved by both houses of Parliament.

HEADLINE: Fund Plans to Lend $50 Million For Jobs, Not Profit, in New York

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
A group of 50 major New York City corporations and business executives, led by Henry R. Kravis, the financier, said yesterday that they had raised $50 million for a new fund intended to make hard-eyed venture capital investments -- not for private gain, but to create jobs and promote economic development.
The fund, which its organizers say is probably unique in the nation in its investment style and operation, will have the power under its charter to take high-risk positions and offer loans to everything from new, promising companies to retailers in poor neighborhoods where ordinary commercial lenders fear to tread. It plans to start this fall. 
 The organizers say they hope to attract another 50 contributors who will provide $1 million each, for a total of $100 million. None of them intends to take any profits from the venture, to be called the New York City Investment Fund. Instead, the organizers say they are creating a large, privately run economic development authority for the city.
Although plans are not final, the organizers say they plan to use their first investments to stimulate commercial and retail development in neighborhoods of upper Manhattan, Brooklyn and Queens that have been the earlier focus of the New York City Housing Partnership. That group's parent, the New York City Partnership, a business and civic organization, is also the parent of the new investment fund.
Mr. Kravis, the man behind many of the biggest corporate takeovers and buyouts of the last decade -- the second K of Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts & Company -- thought up the idea, raised much of the money over the last two years and will be chairman. Participants in the New York City Investment Fund said that Mr. Kravis would also, to a great degree, be the chief asset.
"It's not that Henry has unique knowledge, although he is considered the master of his field," said Richard D. Parsons, the president of Time Warner and a member of the fund's still-forming 12- to 15-member board. "He also symbolizes something: he is the ultimate symbol of both raising and bringing capital to bear on new ventures. The fact that he was involved gave it credibility."
That symbolism and credibility, fund organizers theorize, will be the key to the fund's ultimate impact: leveraging small investments into large, potentially huge ones.
The idea is that Mr. Kravis's reputation as a shrewd businessman and spotter of deals and trends will be a kind of "bell cow," as the fund's new president, Kathryn Wylde, put it. As a result, the theory goes, just as many investors mimic financial gurus like Warren E. Buffett or Peter Lynch, private money people will follow where Mr. Kravis leads.
As chairman of the new fund, Mr. Kravis is likely to become a figure with public and private clout, as he points his fund's spotlight toward what he and his directors conclude are the areas of potential growth.
The investment fund will differ in its structure and aims from the private economic-development partnerships that exist in other cities, many of which either devote themselves to single issues like building convention centers or attracting sports teams, or lock up their resources in long-term construction projects or housing development.
The New York City Investment Fund, which will operate as a wholly owned subsidiary of the New York City Partnership, intends to make loans and get the money back in three years or less. It will focus exclusively on job growth.
Its goal is to be self-sustaining -- a revolving fund where each investment is supposed to be paid back for use on another project. It might buy stock in one company, and give another a loan, but it will give no charity grants that would permanently reduce assets. Each contributor has agreed to keep his $1 million in the fund for 15 years. At the end of that period, according to the plan, each will get $1 million back.
Another characteristic of the fund, Mr. Kravis said in an interview, will be that unlike most recent economic development efforts in New York, this one will not try to protect the existing economic base or try to keep companies from leaving the city -- though investments like that might be made on occasion. Instead, he explained, the idea is to look at what could be, or what might be, and then encourage it. "We're going to be playing offense for a change, instead of just defense," he said.
Although that attitude may bring every dreamer who believes he has just founded the next Microsoft to the fund's door, it also creates what the president of the New York City Partnership, Robert R. Kiley, said is a fundamentally new way of looking at New York City's assets.
"We don't really know everything that's going on in the city," Mr. Kiley said. "That's the other thing that's quite amazing here, because there hasn't been all that much focus on growth and job creation."
The new fund also seems likely to create a new political mix in the city's economy. While City Hall will have no official role in the fund's activities, the organizers say city officials will be invited to offer suggestions. The fund also hopes that local, state or Federal money will be invested in some of the projects that the fund takes on. At the same time, many of the fund's participants have local investments of their own that could be in some cases affected by the fund's activities.
 Ms. Wylde said contributors had to agree unconditionally that the decision making on investments would be wholly at the discretion of the fund's management in order to preclude potential conflicts of interest.
Although the full list of contributors has not yet been released, Ms. Wylde said it included most major New York banks, including Chase, Citicorp and Bankers Trust, and most of the major Wall Street firms, including J. P. Morgan, Morgan Stanley and Lehman Brothers.
Mr. Kiley said the birth of the fund, at a time when city, state and Federal Government resources are shrinking, also marks a resurgence of confidence by city business leaders that New York's economic problems can be addressed, and that new ideas are possible.
Mr. Parsons, the Time Warner officer, said the other major difference between the new fund and past development efforts was that the impact of the new efforts would be measurable. The fund's investments might work, or might not, but it won't take years to find out.
"We've always raised funds around activities -- summer jobs, housing," Mr. Parsons said. "This is going to be the first time, in a significant way, that the business community can put its money where its mouth is in terms of start-up ventures. This allows us to bring significant clout to the table -- catalytic clout."

HEADLINE: New York Dominicans Strongly Back Candidates on Island

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: SANTO DOMINGO, Dominican Republic, June 28

 BODY:
Arturo Ortiz left an assistant in charge of his car parts business on Audubon Avenue in Manhattan. Ramon Infante has taken time off from his job as a furniture salesman in the Bronx. The gypsy cab Demetrio Ortega normally drives is parked in a lot, awaiting his return to Manhattan.
All week long, passengers arriving here on the nine daily flights from New York City have been telling the same story. With this Caribbean island nation of 7.5 million people scheduled to elect a new president on Sunday, several thousand Dominicans living in New York have put their normal routines on hold and are returning home to vote, help run campaigns and serve as poll watchers. 
 "We are working people, not rich folks, so yes, we have sacrificed to come back here," said Rafael Sanchez, a New York City Housing Authority employee who is on a brief leave of absence, as he got off a plane after a crowded, three-and-a-half-hour American Airlines flight Wednesday. "But it is a sacrifice we are happy to make because the future of our country is at stake."
In a campaign dominated by personal attacks, both Jose Francisco Pena Gomez, a candidate of the Dominican Revolutionary Party and winner of the first round of balloting in May, and Leonel Fernandez Reyna of the Dominican Liberation Party, who the polls say has now surged into a narrow lead, are predicting victory. The contenders agree about one thing: the critical, even indispensable role of the huge Dominican community in New York City.
"The part they play is absolutely decisive, especially in terms of campaign finances," Mr. Pena Gomez said in an interview this week. Bienvenido Perez, a New York City resident who is Mr. Fernandez's campaign chief in the United States, agreed. "The Dominican community abroad has tremendous economic weight and political prestige, so of course its influence is being felt," he said.
The importance of the "absent Dominicans," as they are officially known here, has been growing since massive emigration to the United States began in the 1960's. Even though Dominicans can only vote at home, candidates routinely campaign in the New York City metropolitan area, home to the bulk of the estimated one million Dominicans living in the United States, and a large part of their contributions is typically raised at $150-a-head dinners in places like the Bronx and Paterson, N.J.
But interest among Dominican emigrants has been running especially high this year, perhaps because New York City's Dominican community views Mr. Fernandez as one of its own. Born here in 1953, Mr. Fernandez was taken to New York as a child by his mother, who worked simultaneously as a nurse's aide and seamstress, and grew up on the Upper West Side, where he attended Public School 75, Joan of Arc Middle School and Louis D. Brandeis High School.
"He understands our situation and our aspirations because he has felt in his own flesh the same vicissitudes of life that we have," said Jaime Vargas, the owner of a Manhattan insurance agency and leader of a group called Friends of Leonel. "He has experienced the snow up to his knees, the discrimination, the longing to return home. So we look upon him as a brother."
For his part, Mr. Fernandez has not only acknowledged those links but eagerly embraced and capitalized on them. "I am a product of New York City," he said in a recent interview, and as "the first Dominican candidate to have been molded by New York, I want to be a role model for a community that has often been unjustly portrayed in a negative way."
Dressed in the purple-and-yellow colors of their party, Mr. Fernandez's New York supporters have been arriving with tens of thousands of campaign posters, bumper stickers, buttons, baseball caps and photographs produced in New York City by businesses that Dominicans own.
Many of the advertisements appearing in newspapers and magazines for both Mr. Fernandez and Mr. Pena Gomez are also paid for by the Dominican community in New York, as are the airplanes and hot air balloons that fly over their campaign rallies with messages of support.
But returning Dominicans also bring with them the organizational expertise they have learned in New York City, said Guillermo Linares, a New York City Councilman who is the first Dominican to hold elective office in the United States. As part of an observer delegation led by former President Jimmy Carter and as an elected official whose own constituents are deeply divided between the two candidates here, Mr. Linares has been careful to steer a neutral course.
For those Dominicans living in the United States, the election here offers "a way to maintain their links with the Dominican Republic," said Guido Gomez, a young political scientist who recently earned his doctorate at Columbia University and is now working on Mr. Pena Gomez's campaign. Most left their homeland reluctantly, in hopes of bettering themselves, and are appalled to see that, while their own fortunes have dramatically improved, those of the country have not.
"It makes us feel bad when we visit on vacation and see what's going on," said Jose Hugo Rodriguez, an ardent Fernandez supporter who first went to the United States in 1967 and now lives in the Bushwick section of Brooklyn. "This is a country rich in natural resources, but all you see is misery. So if you have patriotic fiber, that is something painful to endure, and you want to do all you can to change it."
Political reforms enacted in 1994, which established the principle of dual citizenship, have made it easier for Dominicans living abroad to act on that impulse and have whetted their appetite to play a larger role here. In recognition of that growing clout, Mr. Fernandez and Mr. Pena Gomez have tailored their message with an eye to increasing their popularity in New York.
Both candidates, for instance, favor legislation that would permit Dominican nationals residing abroad to vote wherever they live, perhaps at the closest consulate, instead of having to come here. If implemented, that proposal would immediately transform the New York City metropolitan area into the second-largest concentration of votes in future Dominican presidential elections, exceeded only by the capital.
In addition, Mr. Fernandez favors a plan to establish a Ministry of the Diaspora. Mr. Pena Gomez says that, if he wins, he will sponsor a provision to amend the Constitution to allow the election of congressional deputies specifically to represent Dominicans living abroad so that Washington Heights and Inwood would, in effect, be treated no differently from Puerto Plata and San Pedro de Macoris. "These people send back more than a billion dollars a year to our country," or more than the combined total of all Dominican exports last year, he said, adding, "Their voices deserve to be heard."

HEADLINE: Mollie Beattie, 49; Headed Wildlife Service

BYLINE: By WILLIAM DICKE 

 BODY:
Mollie H. Beattie, who resigned as director of the United States Fish and Wildlife Service on June 6 because of failing health, died on Thursday at Grace Cottage Hospital in Townsend, Vt. She was 49 and lived in Grafton and Alexandria, Va.
The cause was brain cancer, said a friend, Alexandra M. Marks. 
 A forester by training, Ms. Beattie was the first woman to head the agency. In three years as director, she defended the Endangered Species Act against attacks from many Republicans in Congress and fought efforts to reduce the agency's budget, not always successfully.
"In the Clinton Administration, she was probably the strongest single voice for wildlife conservation," said Rodger Schlickeisen, president of the Defenders of Wildlife, a nonprofit group that works to protect and preserve species.
Ms. Beattie was a strong proponent of the effort to have gray wolves in Yellowstone National Park for the first time since the 1920's. Mr. Schlickeisen said one of his most vivid memories of her was seeing her rub cold water on the belly of a wild wolf at Yellowstone in April to cool it so the animal could be moved to another site for release. Despite the rain in her face, he recalled, she smiled broadly and said, "Any day I can touch a wild wolf is a good day."
As director, she was responsible for the wildlife service's 7,000 employees and an annual budget for resource management of $501 million in the current fiscal year, down from $511 million the previous year. The agency, part of the Interior Department, enforces wildlife laws, administers the Endangered Species Act and carries out wetland protection and management. John Rogers has been serving as acting director.
Ms. Beattie sought to conserve species by managing entire ecosystems rather than waiting until individual species became endangered, and presided over the addition of 15 national wildlife refuges to a system that now has 508.
The agency said that under her direction 140 habitat conservation plans were established and 300 more were being developed. These plans are formal agreements between landowners and the Fish and Wildlife Service that allow the owners to harm individual members of a species listed as endangered or threatened if they take steps to help the species as a whole to recover.
She argued that such plans demonstrated that the Endangered Species Act, under which they are carried out, has the flexibility to conserve species while allowing economic development. In fact, she said, the act "has been one of the biggest success stories of the conservation movement in America," contending that many myths had been spread about the act's supposedly onerous effects.
Just before she resigned, she led efforts to resume the listing of endangered species after a 13-month moratorium imposed by Congress, which sharply cut the agency's money for such listings in the current fiscal year.
Earlier this month, Representative Don Young, Republican of Alaska, introduced legislation in the House and Senator Ted Stevens, Republican of Alaska, in the Senate to name an eight-million-acre wilderness in the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge after her.
Mollie Beattie was born on April 27, 1947, in Glen Cove, L.I. She earned a bachelor's degree in philosophy at Marymount College in Tarrytown, N.Y., in 1968 and a master's degree in forestry at the University of Vermont in 1979. She also received a master's degree in public administration from Harvard University in 1991.
She was program director and lands manager for the Windham Foundation, a nonprofit organization that conducts seminars on critical issues facing Vermont, from 1983 to 1985; commissioner of the Vermont Department of Forests, Parks and Recreation from 1985 to 1989, and deputy secretary of the state's Agency of Natural Resources from 1989 to 1990. She left to become executive director of a public policy institute, the Richard A. Snelling Center for Government in Vermont, before taking her post at the Fish and Wildlife Service.
She is survived by her husband, Rick Schwolsky of Grafton; her mother, Patricia Beattie of Ketchum, Idaho, and a sister, Jane Beattie, also of Ketchum.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Supreme Court Is Split On Banning Smut on Cable
The Supreme Court ruled that operators of cable television systems may ban indecent programming from certain commercial channels but not from the community-access channels used by local governments and community groups. Shifting majorities on the Court upheld part and struck down other parts of a 1992 Federal law intended to protect children from indecency on cable television. 
 The splintered decision indicated deep divisions and great uncertainty over how to approach free speech in such new media as the Internet. [Page 1.]
The Supreme Court extended significant new free speech protection to independent government contractors. [8.]
 
Fund to Create New York City Jobs
A group of 50 corporations and executives, led by Henry R. Kravis, raised $50 million to start a nonprofit venture to create jobs and promote development in New York City. The organizers of the new fund hope to attract another 50 contributors who will provide $1 million each, for a total of $100 million. [1.]
 
Donna Karan a Hit on Wall St.
Donna Karan's shares jumped as much as 25 percent above their initial public offering price in their first day of trading as investors sought to cash in on the fashion designer's prospects. The company's stock closed up $4, at $28, after rising as high as $30.125. The demand for Donna Karan and other new issues provided a sign that the I.P.O. market was regaining its robust form. [35.]

Unexpectedly Slow G.D.P. Growth
The economy grew at a slower pace than previously estimated in the first quarter, reflecting a lull in export demand. The nation's gross domestic product rose at a revised annual rate of 2.2 percent, the Commerce Department said. Economists said the revised G.D.P. data confirmed their expectations that Federal Reserve policy makers would not raise short-term interest rates next week. [34.]

Bond Market Bullish on Rates
Treasury prices surged as investors increased their bets that the Fed would not raise interest rates. The price of the 30-year bond rose 12/32 , to 8826/32 . The bond's yield fell to 6.89 percent from 6.99 percent on Thursday and the lowest since 6.89 percent on June 6. [44.]

Senate Approves Securities Bill
The Senate passed a bill reducing overlapping state and Federal regulation of the mutual fund and securities industries. The bill would eliminate much of the ability of states to regulate the mutual fund industry and large securities offerings, leaving that responsibility with the S.E.C. [34.]
 
Dow Drops, but Nasdaq Surges
Blue-chips stocks gave back early gains to close lower, but technology stocks lifted the Nasdaq market. The Dow Jones industrial average ended 22.90 points lower at 5,654.63. For the week, it was down 50.60 points. But broader indexes were higher. The Nasdaq composite index surged 19.01 points, to 1185.02 -- its biggest percentage gain in four months. Analysts said the market's mixed signals were typical of end-of-the-quarter portfolio adjustments. [36.]
 
Dollar Nears 110 Japanese Yen
The dollar rose to within a whisker of 110 yen, reaching its highest level in almost 29 months, as concerns about an increase in Japanese interest rates faded. The yen fell against the dollar and other leading currencies after Japan said that its unemployment rate rose to a record 3.5 percent in May. [32.]
 
A Roadway Splits the Casinos
With two powerful casino owners pulling in opposite directions, New Jersey's Governor is moving toward a decision on whether the state will pay for a $329 million tunnel and roadway project in Atlantic City. [21.]
 
Corn Futures Prices Rise
Corn futures jumped after an Agriculture Department report indicated that high prices had not deterred demand. [32.]
 
Group of Seven Divided on Gold Sale
Leaders of the seven largest industrial democracies disagreed over a U.S. plan for the I.M.F. to sell part of its $40 billion in gold reserves. The Group of Seven did agree on a plan to fight crime and terrorism. [4.]
