HEADLINE: Developer's Plan Roils a College Town in Vermont

BYLINE: By SALLY JOHNSON 

DATELINE: MIDDLEBURY, Vt.

 BODY:
A PLAN by Middlebury College to sell a parcel of college-owned land to a shopping-center developer has sent shock waves through this town, which, many believe, depends on its image to maintain its economic health.
The proposal calls for Middlebury College, which owns a vacant motel and a 30-acre tract just south of town, to sell off eight acres. The prospective buyer is Myron M. Hunt, a developer and Middlebury alumnus who has applied to the town for permission to build a 44,400-square-foot Price Chopper supermarket and a 5,000-square-foot restaurant on the land. 
The idea of building a supermarket in a town with three grocery stores and a definite traffic problem has rankled critics, who argue that more congestion at the town's southern entrance can only hurt the economy of Middlebury, where the scenery,not the shopping, has been the major draw.
"It's a matter of this developer and this tenant in this site, which has been identified in the Town Plan as a gateway to the village," said John Barstow, a member of the steering committee of Citizens for Middlebury, a group formed in response to Mr. Hunt's proposal.
In many ways, the clash of wills is similar to thousands of development disputes that play out each year in cities and towns all over the country -- a push and pull between those who staunchly defend laissez-faire capitalism and those who don't.
Mr. Hunt, a developer from Buffalo, argues that his project will work to the town's long-term benefit. To the many residents who worry about increased traffic on an already congested road, he has promised that new traffic signals and wider roadways, installed at his expense, will improve the situation, even with the 20 percent traffic increase his engineers predict the shopping center will generate.
To those who argue that Middlebury already has three groceries, he cites a market study by his consultants. "Market conditions dictate an additional supermarket," it says. "It would be a fine asset to this community."
"Besides, Mr. Hunt added, "competition is always good for the consumer."
SUCH assurances were not sufficient to calm the fears of the 350 people who turned out for a public hearing by the Middlebury Planning Commission earlier this month. Residents took the microphone to tell Mr. Hunt civilly but passionately that they did not need or want what he was selling.
"We don't need your groceries," said one young woman to enthusiastic applause from the audience. "Why don't you take your store somewhere else and give us something we do need?"
Another public hearing is scheduled July 10. Mr. Hunt hopes to get the town's approval to begin construction in the fall and open the store next spring.
There is a broader issue at play here than groceries and traffic. The dispute turns on a powerful and pervasive fear that the town (population 8,034, plus 1,900 college students) is losing what makes it distinctive.
Settled in the latter half of the 18th century, Middlebury still has a thriving downtown, filled with locally owned businesses housed in three blocks of 19th-century brick buildings. The village is bisected by the Otter Creek, which takes a dramatic and noisy fall off a ledge in the middle of town under the century-old stone bridge. The bridge was constructed of stone, not iron, at the behest of Joseph Battell, a town father and eccentric millionaire of the last century, whose principal passion was buying enormous tracts of land, much of which he left to Middlebury College.
The town's green, with gazebo, occupies a prominent place visually and socially. Churches hold their fund-raisers on the green and children congregate there after school on their bicycles. Families turn out for Festival on the Green, which takes place each year in early July -- seven evenings of free concerts, juggling acts and community-theater productions.
But there is a sense now that the visual and social tranquility could evaporate under the strain of overdevelopment. In addition to the Hunt application, Rite-Aid, the national chain drugstore, has applied to move in across the road from Price Chopper. To Robin Scheu, a member of Citizens for Middlebury, the future of her adopted hometown is in danger. "To me, this Price Chopper represents the end of Middlebury as we know it," Ms. Scheu said. "Once this starts, you have a domino effect."
To see the domino effect in action elsewhere, Middlebury residents have only to look to strip development 30 miles north or south, to Burlington and Rutland respectively. The two main thoroughfares to the south and west of Burlington, Vermont's largest city, are filled with fast-food chains, gas stations, discount department stores and motels. To many, the Burlington experience is a cautionary tale.
"Middlebury is a different place than the rest of the country, and it has a population that appreciates that difference," said Fred Dunnington, Middlebury's town planner and zoning administrator for 15 years. "Part of the issue here is image -- and that's a very difficult thing to do through zoning."
EQUALLY troubling to opponents of Mr. Hunt's proposal is the role of Middlebury College. The college did not list the property; in fact, Mr. Hunt approached college officials about buying it, according to all those involved. Because the small liberal-arts college, founded in 1800, has long been the dominant economic and cultural force in town, and because the college uses its scenic location as a selling point, many critics are wondering just what the administration is about.
"What is the college doing in the role of developer-broker in a town, and state, trying desperately to preserve its rural, Main Street heritage?" wrote Ron Powers, a recently resigned faculty member, in a scathing commentary that appeared in the local weekly. "And what kind of signals is the college sending into the regional and national debate over sprawl vs. controlled growth?"
For their part, college officials said they were acting as responsible citizens by following existing procedures to determine the appropriateness of the development and that this proposal should be treated no different from any other.
"Middlebury College isn't seeking to build a supermarket; Middlebury College isn't seeking to increase the town's traffic problem," said John McCardell, president of the College, in a telephone interview. "We're selling that land because we were offered what we consider a fair price for it."
Steven Rockefeller, a professor of religion at Middlebury who has given millions of dollars toward the preservation of open land in Vermont, is not buying the college's claim of neutrality.
"I do not think this is a good idea for the town, and I certainly hope the college will reconsider its position," Mr. Rockefeller said. "And, yes, this is certainly a matter over which the college exercises a great deal of control."

HEADLINE: Critical Condition

BYLINE: By Robert W. Merry; Robert W. Merry, the executive editor of Congressional Quarterly, is the author of "Taking On the World: Joseph and Stewart Alsop -- Guardians of the American Century."

 BODY:

BOOMERANG
Clinton's Health Security Effort and the Turn Against Government in U.S. Politics.
By Theda Skocpol.
230 pp. New York:
W. W. Norton & Company. $27.50.
 Back in 1976, when Daniel Patrick Moynihan was seeking a United States Senate seat from New York, he took delight in ridiculing his opponent, the Conservative-Republican incumbent James Buckley, as a man who simply couldn't accept the political realities of his time. "Look," Mr. Moynihan would tell his political audiences in tones of mock exasperation, "there's this particular fringe, and their one fundamental problem is they simply never accepted the New Deal. They're still running against 'that man in the White House.' " Twenty years later, a similar syndrome is evident in American political discourse. Only now "that man in the White House" isn't Franklin Roosevelt but Ronald Reagan. And those struggling with the day's political realities aren't conservative Republicans but liberal Democrats. 
 This turnabout is reflected in "Boomerang," Theda Skocpol's well-researched and highly ideological analysis of what really happened back in 1993 and 1994 when President Clinton attempted to give the country a comprehensive and universal health care system. This bold initiative turned out to be a political disaster, as Ms. Skocpol concedes. Its defeat, she suggests, led directly to the 1994 Republican takeover of Congress and to the Democrats' lack of any meaningful political vision in this Presidential campaign season. "The Democratic Party is by now a relatively hollow political shell," she writes.
 So what went wrong? Ms. Skocpol, a professor of government and sociology at Harvard, dismisses most of the conventional thinking that emerged in the wake of Mr. Clinton's defeat. She evinces contempt for the "punditocracy" that attributed the defeat to the "know-it-all" arrogance and elitist zeal of Hillary Rodham Clinton and the Clinton friend Ira C. Magaziner, who led the President's Task Force on Health Care Reform. "Stories about flawed personalities are fun to read," Ms. Skocpol writes. "Retrospectives blaming the failure of health care reform on scapegoats have easily slid into this well-worn line of condemnation."
 She is equally dismissive of the view that the public was simply too confused to accept the Clinton prescriptions. Ms. Skocpol pronounces this argument "glib and unsatisfying." If the voters were confused, she says, it was largely because of the barrage of opposition propaganda that greeted the President's plan. No, the most compelling explanation for the debacle, in Ms. Skocpol's view, is what she calls, borrowing a phrase from the historian Alan Brinkley, "Reagan's revenge" -- a political legacy that doomed the Clinton initiative from the beginning.
 The argument goes like this: For decades following the New Deal, the Republican right attacked the expanding Federal Government, but these attacks failed to topple the liberal order because the programs put forth by big Government -- particularly the huge entitlement programs like Social Security and Medicare -- were widely popular. But then conservatives discovered a better approach; they attacked high taxes "and the politicans and 'bureaucrats' who spent them in supposedly 'wasteful' ways." These contentions fueled both the property tax revolts of the late 1970's and the 1980 election victory of Ronald Reagan. Mr. Reagan's large cuts in income tax rates, enacted in 1981, served to starve Federal programs of funds, rendering them ineffective and feckless. This in turn fueled voter cynicism about the capacities of government and the value of governmental programs. Mr. Reagan's political legacy was therefore a lingering sump of antigovernment sentiment, while his economic legacy was a Federal Government hobbled by an inadequate tax base. "By the time Bill Clinton got ready to run for the Presidency," Ms. Skocpol writes, "Americans' distrust of the Federal Government was at an all-time high."
 The result was that when Mr. Clinton designed his health plan he embraced the "managed competition" concept, which was designed to use market forces to promote efficiency and lower prices in health care delivery. An important element of the plan was a network of Government-regulated "health purchasing alliances" aimed at making it possible for small and medium-sized companies to purchase insurance at lower cost. Mr. Clinton relied heavily on regulation, on the theory that there was no political support for a less regulated system requiring higher taxes. Yet in promoting his plan, Ms. Skocpol notes, Mr. Clinton studiously avoided any serious discussion of the health purchasing alliances, because he feared an antigovernment backlash. Thus, he never successfully explained his plan to the American people and allowed his opponents to define it in negative terms. The President was caught in a double bind -- Reagan's revenge.
 Ms. Skocpol unabashedly suggests that Mr. Clinton should have ignored the Reagan legacy and proceeded as if the Reagan Presidency had never happened. "I conclude," she writes, "that President Clinton should have been less worried about pleasing deficit and budget hawks. He should have done what his conservative critics falsely charged him with doing -- acted more like a Democrat in the New Deal tradition, by combining new Federal regulations with generous subsidies to those affected." But perhaps Mr. Clinton, in fashioning his plan, understood a political reality that Ms. Skocpol doesn't want to acknowledge -- namely, that the New Deal is dead and gone, and that any health plan with any chance of success must incorporate that reality.
 Beyond that, Ms. Skocpol's analysis raises a fundamental question that goes to the heart of how history unfolds in our democracy: Did the New Deal die because Ronald Reagan was elected President? Or was Mr. Reagan elected because the New Deal had died? Was it really "Reagan's revenge" or merely the voters' wishes? Ms. Skocpol's view that the old days would have remained in place if only Mr. Reagan hadn't been elected is akin to the anti-Roosevelt view of the conservatives so zestfully twitted by Pat Moynihan back in 1976. Both are based on highly debatable premises about how American politics works. Nonetheless, it is Ms. Skocpol's perspective that gives her book its value. She adds a spirited outlook to the post-mortem debate over President Clinton's health care fiasco and its impact on American politics.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Review

HEADLINE: Flush With Success

BYLINE: By David Oshinsky; David Oshinsky's most recent book is " 'Worse Than Slavery': Parchman Farm and the Ordeal of Jim Crow Justice."

 BODY:

GRAND EXPECTATIONS
The United States, 1945-1974.
By James T. Patterson.
Illustrated. 829 pp. New York:
Oxford University Press. $35.
 A few months before the Japanese attack on Pearl Harbor in 1941, Henry Luce called upon the American people to accept their responsibility as citizens of "the most powerful and vital nation in the world." The time had come, he wrote in Life, one of the flagships of his publishing empire, to exert "the full measure of our influence, for such purposes as we see fit" in the dawning "American Century." 
 This sentiment was hardly new. The belief that Americans had a mission to lead -- a special destiny -- was as old as the nation itself. Yet the challenges of World War II had turned this rhetoric into reality by placing the United States at the very center of the international stage. As Walter Lippmann, the nation's leading columnist, noted in 1945, "What Rome was to the ancient world, what Great Britain has been to the modern world, America is to be to the world of tomorrow."
 "Grand Expectations," by James T. Patterson, a professor of history at Brown University, is a spirited, sprawling narrative of American life in that "world of tomorrow," from the death of President Franklin D. Roosevelt in 1945 to the resignation of President Richard Nixon in 1974. Mr. Patterson enters a field so crowded with other books about modern America that the authors appear to be running out of titles. The "G" section on my alphabetized bookshelf already includes "Grand Illusions" and "Great Expectations." Is there need for another account, more than 800 pages in length? The answer, decidedly, is yes.
 Mr. Patterson's book is part of the multivolume Oxford History of the United States, which in 1989 produced "Battle Cry of Freedom," James McPherson's Pulitzer Prize-winning account of the Civil War era. A sweeping synthesis based entirely on secondary sources, "Grand Expectations" covers its subject in meticulous detail, beginning with a vibrant portrait of the nation in 1945.
 Only 50 years ago, Mr. Patterson reminds us, the United States was a very different place. The population contained a small and declining number of foreign-born, the result of strict immigration quotas installed in the 1920's. (Ellis Island would close for good in 1955.) Most blacks still lived in the South, where racial segregation was the law. Blue-collar workers outnumbered white-collar workers, and labor unions, led by charismatic figures like John L. Lewis and Walter Reuther, were at the height of their power. The first Levittown had yet to be built. Few couples got divorced. There were no supermarkets or shopping malls, almost no four-lane highways or air conditioning, no television or ball-point pens. More than half of the nation's farm dwellings had no electricity. Tobacco companies placed cigarette advertisements in medical journals.
 Traditional values were strong. Most Americans believed deeply in the virtues of organized religion, hard work and self-help. Yet their experiences in the Great Depression and World War II had made them more accepting of other ideas -- like a government safety net for the truly disadvantaged and a foreign policy that stressed serious (and sometimes expensive) involvement in places far from home. What worried most Americans in 1945 was the return of hard times as defense spending dropped and factory jobs disappeared. Could the United States handle the difficult reconversion from a wartime to a peacetime economy?
 These worries did not last long. As Mr. Patterson demonstrates, a combination of factors, including generous mortgage and college benefits lavished upon returning veterans, a surge of consumer demand after years of war rationing and the pathetic state of international competitors like England, Germany and Japan made a postwar depression almost impossible, a relic of the past. "The prosperity of the period broadened gradually in the late 1940's, accelerated in the 1950's and soared to unimaginable heights in the 1960's," Mr. Patterson writes. "By then it was a boom that astonished observers."
 Prosperity brought "grand expectations." For the growing middle class it meant advances in science and medicine, a home in the suburbs, a college education for the children, the abundant consumption of new products and America's unchallenged leadership in the world. For the millions below them it meant economic opportunity and expanded political rights.
 Covering enormous ground, Mr. Patterson weaves a narrative that includes everything from the battle against polio and the meaning of Alfred Kinsey's sexual studies to the rise of McCarthyism and the struggle for civil rights. Yet absent from "Grand Expectations" is the role of popular culture in American life. There is little of importance about movies or music, and almost nothing about sports. The book also ignores regional change, leaving Sun Belt and Far Western growth largely unexplored.
 Mr. Patterson is particularly adept at explaining the long-term consequences of events that have faded from public memory. In discussing the Korean War, for example, he reminds us that President Harry Truman set a modern precedent by sending American troops into battle without consulting Congress; that defense spending more than tripled between 1950 and 1953, rarely to fall again; that General Douglas MacArthur's insubordination served to reaffirm civilian control of the armed forces; that Chinese Communist involvement in the war forced the United States to guarantee Taiwan's future security; and that total victory -- an American tradition -- is not always possible in the modern, atomic world.
 Mr. Patterson's judgments are in line with the most recent scholarship of this era. Harry Truman is portrayed as a determined New Dealer and a tough anti-Communist who adequately filled Franklin Roosevelt's shoes. Dwight Eisenhower comes across as a strong, confident leader, committed to fiscal conservatism and limited government, yet determined to keep New Deal reforms mostly in place. John Kennedy is viewed as a fierce cold warrior, intensely competitive, with little interest and few accomplishments in domestic affairs. Lyndon Johnson is described as a "bread-and-butter" liberal who unwisely measured success in quantitative terms, from the number of people receiving new benefits to the number of North Vietnamese lying dead on the battlefield. An overbearing man, often a bully, he once surprised the Pope dur-ing a ceremonial gift-giving in Rome. The Pontiff handed the President a 14th-century painting; Johnson gave the Pope a bust of himself.
 Mr. Patterson's account of the 1960's, the pivotal decade of his book, is both grumpy and shrewd. He sees the intense divisions over national goals and traditional values as the natural outgrowth of postwar events. For one thing, the huge baby boom generation came of age in this decade, fueling permissive feelings about sex, drugs and divorce. For another, the well-publicized successes of the Southern civil rights movement encouraged other groups, like feminists, homosexuals and Native Americans, to make their own voices heard. Finally, the escalating conflict in Vietnam, part of the larger cold war struggle, undermined the Government's credibility and sapped its programs for the poor.
 A backlash was inevitable. Most Americans had supported a civil rights movement devoted to political equality and economic opportunity for individuals. They were "much less sympathetic," Mr. Patterson notes, "when people demanded the 'right' to social equality or special entitlements for groups." Furthermore, the public theater of the 1960's -- the posturing of Black Panthers, the violent demonstrations of privileged college students, the bold complaints of previously marginalized groups -- played into the hands of politicians like George Wallace and Richard Nixon, who claimed to speak for America's patriotic, hard-working "silent majority."
 Mr. Patterson ends his learned book with the self-inflicted downfall of Nixon, aptly described as the only postwar President who "never seemed to enjoy the job." In these final pages the White House is under a cloud and the economy is in trouble. Americans are less optimistic, their grand expectations dimmed by the painfully visible limits of progress. Yet for all of these troubles, Mr. Patterson concludes, the United States in 1974 "remained one of the most stable societies in the world," still com- mitted to hard work, equal opportunity and respect for the law. "These were among the many attitudes and values that flourished from 1945 to 1974 and that remained alive and well thereafter."

LENGTH: 1080 words

HEADLINE: American Heritages

BYLINE: By David S. Reynolds; David S. Reynolds is a Distinguished Professor of English at Baruch College and the Graduate Center of the City University of New York. His latest book is "Walt Whitman's America."

 BODY:

AMERICANS
A Collision of Histories.
By Edward Countryman.
294 pp. New York:
Hill & Wang. $25.
 What is an American? When J. Hector St. John de Crevecoeur posed this question in his 1782 volume, "Letters From an American Farmer," he supplied an answer that has proved resonant over the generations. The American, Crevecoeur argued, represented a new composite race made up of different nationalities. According to this view, ethnic or cultural differences inevitably dissolved in the melting pot of the New World, producing a hybrid breed. 
 In recent years, the melting pot thesis has been challenged by those who say that America is not one culture but many cultures, each with a distinct identity and heritage. Multiculturalism has had a ripple effect through American society, affecting school curriculums, affirmative action programs and the academic culture wars. To its defenders, multiculturalism is just in its recognition of diversity; to its critics, it can become divisive and fractious.
 All too often, the debate over multiculturalism displays more spleen than reason. This is not true of Edward Countryman's new book, "Americans: A Collision of Histories." Mr. Countryman, a professor at Southern Methodist University and the author of several previous books on American history, offers a solid historical rationale for those who see the United States as a multistranded tapestry rather than a synthetic carpet. "I have not sought a spurious 'inclusivity' or 'multiculturalism' that merely mentions names," he announces in his introductory note. Rather, he provides a dispassionate history of different ethnic groups -- principally European whites, African-Americans and Native Americans -- during America's formative period, roughly from 1600 to 1900.
 His aptly worded title stresses that there is no single type that can be identified as American. There are instead Americans. Likewise, there is no single American history; there is "a collision of histories."
 His story of this collision is often unsettling. Especially moving is his rendition of the suffering of Native Americans. When whites arrived in the Americas in 1492, he reminds us, the aborigines in the hemisphere numbered perhaps as many as 100 million. Before long, this figure dropped about 90 percent, because of the disease, alcohol and uncontrolled slaughter that accompanied the arrival of Europeans. This "demographic disaster," Mr. Countryman writes, "has no equal in history, not even the Black Death." The plight of the Indians never improved during the period he covers. He tells the pathetic story of many once-proud nations and tribes forced steadily westward by the encroaching white settlers.
 He is equally strong on the bleak experience of African-Americans. Although in the early years blacks enjoyed a certain measure of independence, with the growth of slavery they were subject to widespread degradation in the South and increasing social ostracism in the North.
 More dramatically than previous historians, Mr. Countryman underscores the ironies associated with the unfair treatment of these and other oppressed groups. He points out that it was precisely in the half-century following the signing of the Declaration of Independence -- with its proclamation of human equality -- that actual inequality and injustice became particularly severe in America. Andrew Jackson's unremitting campaigns against Native Americans led to the Indian Removal Act of 1830, which sent the Cherokees and other tribes west into the Oklahoma Territory along the infamous Trail of Tears. The entrenchment of slavery and the passage of several pro-Southern laws impelled Northerners like William Lloyd Garrison and Wendell Phillips to bring attention to the terrible gap between America's egalitarian ideals and its heinous practices. A similar gap began to be perceived by many women, like those who gathered in 1848 in Seneca Falls, N.Y., at the landmark women's rights convention, where the most famous passage of the Declaration of Independence was pointedly rewritten: "We hold these truths to be self-evident: that all men and women are created equal."
 While sensitive to social wrongs, Mr. Countryman avoids making a blanket indictment of the American mainstream. His book is not another expose of historical victims and their victimizers. He avoids painting any ethnic group in uniform colors. "There was no single way to be a slave," he writes, "any more than to be an Indian or a white." True, among the whites were oppressors and ravagers, but also among them, he points out, were canny inventors, dedicated workers and contributors to world thought. He demonstrates that Native American culture was remarkably varied too. Some Indians, like the Creeks in Alabama, took pride in their tribal heritage and tried to preserve it; others, like some Cherokees in Georgia, readily adopted Christianity and white laws. Some American blacks, likewise, clung to African folkways, while others, like Frederick Douglass, did their utmost to identify themselves as Americans.
 The picture of Americans that emerges in this book, then, is diverse in the best sense. In delineating the contours of the nation's ethnic makeup, Mr. Countryman avoids pat generalizations and political partisanship. Although he adds few new facts to the record, he innovatively synthesizes relevant, often unfamiliar information and presents it with disarming directness.
 To be sure, his bare-bones approach results in omissions and foreshortenings. One of the major "collisions" in American history took place when hundreds of thousands of European emigrants flooded to America's shores during the 19th century, creating enormous political shake-ups. Mr. Countryman offers statistics about the immigration issue but gives little sense of how the new foreign-born citizens lived or were treated. Similarly, even though he is generally perceptive about economic developments, he gives short shrift to the widening class divisions that spurred vigorous labor protests and utopian socialism during the antebellum period.
 What the book occasionally lacks in depth, however, it more than makes up for in breadth. Mr. Countryman rightly says that it would be "incredibly arrogant" of him to try "to write the whole history of my subjects." As it stands, "Americans" admirably provides what we have long needed: a compact, illuminating overview of the intricate strands of early American history.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Review

TITLE: AMERICANS: A COLLISION OF HISTORIES (BOOK)

SECTION: Section 7; Page 9; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1260 words

HEADLINE: Accidental Empires

BYLINE: By Allister Sparks; Allister Sparks's most recent books are "The Mind of South Africa" and "Tomorrow Is Another Country: The Inside Story of South Africa's Road to Change."

 BODY:

DIVIDE AND RULE
The Partition of Africa, 1880-1914.
By H. L. Wesseling.
Translated by Arnold J. Pomerans.
Illustrated. 446 pp. Westport, Conn.:
Praeger. $75.
 It is a fact almost as remarkable as the event itself that for nearly a century after the imperial powers of Europe carved the continent of Africa into 47 territorial slices and served them out to one another like so many helpings at a banquet table, no comprehensive study of this historical phenomenon was published -- and then, in 1991, two appeared in quick succession. The first was by H. L. Wesseling of the University of Leiden in the Netherlands. Six months later there arrived Thomas Pakenham's monumental book "The Scramble for Africa." Although Mr. Wesseling won the race, so to speak, his book was in Dutch and so remained virtually unknown outside his own country. But now it is being published in English (in a capable translation by Arnold J. Pomerans) and will present a much wider audience with a useful counterpoint to Mr. Pakenham's better-known work. 
 The closeness of their original publication makes comparison all the more interesting, since it meant neither could influence the other. They cover the same events over a vast continental expanse in an amazingly compressed period of time, a mere 35 years from 1880 to 1914, but that is where the similarity ends. In both literary style and historical analysis, the two books differ sharply.
 Mr. Pakenham's is racier, more journalistic and, despite its greater length (738 pages compared with 446), more readable. Mr. Wesseling's is more scholarly, and somewhat drier for the nonspecialist reader.
 For Mr. Pakenham, the scramble was a feeding frenzy set off by the heroic death of the missionary-explorer David Livingstone, whose sun-dried body was returned to London for ceremonial burial in Westminster Abbey after he had issued a ringing call to the developed world to redeem Africa by the three C's -- commerce, Christianity and civilization. For Mr. Wesseling, who mentions Livingstone only in passing, there was no single trigger event and no general hypothesis to explain Europe's intervention in Africa. Instead, the partition was "the result of a series of independent and more or less 'accidental' decisions." For Mr. Pakenham, again, the race for territory was driven by a vision of Africa as an El Dorado, a huge new market and treasure house of raw materials, while for Mr. Wesseling, Africa was of little economic interest to the colonizers and "came low on the long list of preoccupations of contemporary politicians."
 The essence of Mr. Wesseling's analysis is that the partitioning of Africa was a sideshow that played itself out on the periphery of European geopolitics. The second half of the 19th century was a time of great transition in Europe. France suffered a crushing defeat in the Franco-Prussian War of 1870, Germany emerged as the most powerful nation in Europe and the Ottoman Empire was in terminal decline, leaving its colonies in the Balkans and North Africa as vacuums waiting to be filled. To stabilize this situation, Otto von Bismarck called the European powers together for the Congress of Berlin in 1878.
 It has long been a popular assumption that this meeting was convened specifically to divide up Africa. Not so, says Mr. Wesseling. Its main purpose, he contends, was to sort out the affairs of the Balkans following Turkey's defeat at the hands of Russia in a war that had just ended. Bismarck also wanted to secure Germany against any future threats from Britain and France in the West and Russia in the East. The settling of colonial boundaries in Africa was a byproduct of these pressing European considerations.
 Thus Bismarck, anxious to placate the French after their painful loss of Alsace and Lorraine, prevailed upon Britain to allow France to take over the Ottoman colony of Tunisia. Meanwhile, an uprising in Egypt, where an Ottoman sultan had exercised suzerainty, caused Britain to step into that power vacuum. Once there, Britain felt impelled to secure the Nile, on which Egypt was totally dependent. This meant moving into the Sudan and ultimately on to East Africa. And so on.
 Mr. Wesseling's analysis is probably closer to the truth than the more popular theories of a systematic and planned process of occupation. Colonialism was certainly the spirit of the times and was obviously driven by capitalism's need for raw materials and new markets. But the reality of politics is that events tend to take place more haphazardly than the retrospective chroniclers would have us believe, with leaders compelled to weigh up competing options and interests at any given moment before making their decisions.
 Eschewing any broad explanation or hypothesis as he does, Mr. Wesseling paints for us a large canvas covered with intricate detail. It is a work of considerable scholarship if not easy reading. It will also not sit well with contemporary Africans, who view colonialism as the original sin that has precipitated nearly all their present-day woes. As Africans see it, colonization not only stripped them of their sovereignty and rent the fabric of their societies, but was also an exploitative system -- one that sucked out Africa's mineral and other wealth and left it without the broad-based development it now needs to sustain itself in the postcolonial world.
 They will not like Mr. Wesseling's assertion that in historical terms the colonization of Africa was a fleeting and not very damaging experience, that the colonizers were not able to reshape anything very much and that "in economic or social respects, colonization brought nothing essentially new." Nor will they like his observation that there was no significant African resistance to the conquest. "Local chiefs jostled one another to sell their 'sovereignty,' not for a mess of pottage but for more attractive things such as bottles of gin and uniforms," he writes stingingly. Mr. Wesseling also contends that for the most part Africans collaborated passively in their supposed oppression. "Without their collaboration colonial rule would not have been possible. . . . Even under colonialism Africans thus largely remained masters of their fate."
 That is a judgment that cuts across the heroic mythology that modern Africa has created for itself, and certainly goes too far in exonerating the imperial powers of all blame for Africa's current condition. That said, it is time Africans discarded the crutch of blaming the legacy of colonialism for all their ills and faced up to the fact of their own misrule and the extent to which they themselves are to blame for turning Africa into the most marginalized continent on earth.
 Nonetheless, this issue points up the one serious flaw in Mr. Wesseling's work. It is too Eurocentric in its approach. It may indeed be true that Africa was partitioned by Europeans acting exclusively in response to political events in Europe, but the book cries out for a perspective on how all this affected the Africans themselves. The nature of the colonial occupation meant that many African social and political institutions were broken down but little was put in their place. Arbitrarily drawn boundaries cut through tribal units and across demographic and geographic belts, rendering many of the territories incoherent and unviable as nation-states. To suggest, as Mr. Wesseling does, that the colonization of Africa was of little importance to Europe may be true. But it certainly had a profound and lasting impact on Africa.

HEADLINE: A Plague of Bosses

BYLINE: By Louis Uchitelle; Louis Uchitelle reports on economics for The New York Times.

 BODY:

FAT AND MEAN
The Corporate Squeeze of Working Americans and the Myth of Managerial "Downsizing."
By David M. Gordon.
320 pp. New York:
Martin Kessler Books/The Free Press. $25.
 Economics, as practiced in America, is laced with tradition and accepted wisdom. Standard theory is repeated and embellished in many convincing ways, and volumes of data are harnessed to demonstrate a fairly rigid set of mainstream views. Such a straitjacketed social science cries out for iconoclasts -- not naysayers, but skilled economists who take the same raw data and come up with different insights. When they do, they force open the debate over what is happening to the economy and to ourselves at work. 
David M. Gordon, a professor at the New School for Social Research in New York, was such an economist. He died in March at 51, just weeks before the publication of his last book, "Fat and Mean." He had not expected to die. Until a day or two before his death, he had been confident that a heart donor would be found in time and that after a successful transplant he would go on with his life as if he had been momentarily distracted by nothing more significant than an appendectomy. "Fat and Mean" rings with the tone of a man still warming to his case.
 The Gordon case is this: Standard economic theory has masked the most important reason for the stagnating incomes and the rising inequality of income levels that have plagued the nation for nearly 25 years. According to standard theory, the main culprits are low-wage foreign labor, global competition, not enough improvement in productivity and the abundance of unskilled Americans in an age of high technology that requires workers with ever greater aptitude and education. But the real malefactor, Gordon argues, is corporate America's approach to management.
 Rather than relying on a cooperative approach -- featuring raises and bonuses as "carrots" -- American corporations deploy a huge bureaucracy of bosses. This bureaucracy embraces what Gordon calls the "stick strategy," issuing commands, threatening dismissal for failure to obey and, in wielding its power, holding back wages for tens of millions of "nonsupervisory" workers. And the more wages are held down, the less the incentive for workers and the greater the need for supervisors to sustain performance.
 From this "fat" bureaucracy and its "mean" management style flow "the economic anxieties shared by scores of millions of Americans," Gordon writes. I don't know whether he is correct in drawing so broad a conclusion. He suggested this view before, most notably in "Beyond the Waste Land," written in 1983 with two other economists, Samuel Bowles and Thomas E. Weisskopf. These three, all highly trained in traditional economics -- Gordon, for example, a Ph.D. from Harvard, taught for a while at Yale -- came of age in the 1960's, and set themselves up as left-wing economists. But they engaged in the same data analysis and the same computer modeling as their mainstream counterparts, and out of their research came a different, less sanguine view of American capitalism -- what James Tobin, the Nobel laureate at Yale, once described approvingly as "left-wing political economy come alive."
 Since the late 1970's, Gordon gathered data for what is now his legacy. "Fat and Mean" offers more than enough evidence to force his view into the public debate as an antidote to the prevailing wisdom. In the hands of a Paul Krugman or a Herbert Stein or a John Kenneth Galbraith that almost certainly would happen. They are as skilled at writing for the general reader as they are at economics; Gordon didn't share those communications skills. One must have an affinity for statistical data, vivid as the data in "Fat and Mean" are, to get through this book. Fortunately, the first 100 pages tell most of the story.
 "Fat and Mean" is cast not as a polemic, though plenty of polemic slips through, but as a different twist on the same collection of statistics and research papers that other economists draw upon. Thus Gordon reports, accurately, that while layoffs have spread through white-collar and upper-level ranks, where managers reside, pink slips are still more numerous among blue-collar workers and their office equivalents. The ranks of the supervisors are thinning, but more slowly than those of their charges, and the ratio of the first to the second has risen, not fallen.
 Why this rise, just when downsizing and the participative workplace, featuring workers who manage themselves, have become so fashionable? Here Gordon's analysis is at its most original. The stick strategy, by its nature, requires ever more supervisors, starting with low-level supervisors who command only two or three workers. This is particularly the case when the incentive of rising wages is absent. And once in place, he argues, this bureaucracy tends to appropriate to itself a growing share of the national income earmarked for wages.
 While some economists offer various statistical measures that suggest that incomes have not really stagnated, Gordon has developed a different guidepost, called "real spendable hourly earnings," for production workers. This is the amount left in an average worker's pocket from his or her hourly wage, after stripping out income and Social Security taxes, adjusting for inflation -- and subtracting the wages that go to bosses. Since the early 1970's, that spendable hourly wage has fallen by nearly 1 percent a year. Gordon finds one central reason. An ever larger share of the national income has gone to the horde of supervisors, "almost entirely at the expense of production workers."
 Most economists argue over whether too much of the national income is going to profits rather than pay, or whether the wealthiest 20 percent of Americans are getting rich while the bottom 80 percent lose ground. They fail to note that supervisors are, in effect, taking money from the paychecks of their charges and, in the process, encouraging the sort of conflict and tension that undermines efficiency and productivity. This income shift, Gordon asserts, "is one of the best-kept secrets of the U.S. economy." His last act was to try to lift the secrecy.

LENGTH: 2999 words

HEADLINE: Developers Are Bullish On Wall Street

BYLINE: By ELLEN KIRSCHNER POPPER 

 BODY:
WHEN the union of two pivotal engineering achievements, the elevator and the steel frame, spawned the skyscraper at the close of the 19th century, the new form flourished in lower Manhattan. In 1911, intrigued by the potential to accommodate multiple uses in a single framework, Theodore Starrett of the Starrett real estate family proposed a 100-story tower to house industry, business, residences and a hotel in a self-contained vertical city. But the idea of living as well as working in lower Manhattan became the exception rather than the rule; the skyscrapers that were built downtown were monolithic "cathedrals of commerce."
Now Starrett's vision may be coming to pass. Less than a year after an incentive plan for residential conversions in lower Manhattan was approved by the State Legislature, at least 20 buildings are slated for alteration, and developers are mining the canyons of Wall Street for more. 
 Candidates for conversion range from Gothic skyscrapers considered obsolete by modern business standards to contemporary towers of glass and steel left vacant by economic downturns and corporate downsizing. Developers hope to make some buildings wholly residential, while others are planned for mixed use with stores, offices and parking on the lower, broader, floors and apartments in the slender towers. Plans are also underway for restaurants and retail shops.
Richard T. Kennedy, an associate director for Cushman & Wakefield's Wall Street office, estimated that construction would start on 1,200 to 1,800 residential units in lower Manhattan in the next year. But banks and other institutional investors are taking a conservative stance.
Not all buildings are suitable for conversion, according to architects and developers involved in the downtown plans. Certain architectural features are key in assessing a structure's residential potential. They are floorplate size, distance from window to core, natural light, ventilation and view. Drawbacks include facade problems like crumbling stonework or corroded curtain walls, inoperable windows and asbestos.
To determine economic viability, developers calculate the ratio of usable, meaning sellable or rentable, square feet to overall square feet. An abundance of windows allows for flexible apartment layouts, promoting efficient use of space.
In lower Manhattan, where there is a dense concentration of vintage skyscrapers, many buildings meet the requirements for successful residential conversion.
"Skyscrapers built between the 1890's and the 1940's are beautifully adaptable to residential use," said Carol Willis, author of "Form Follows Finance; Skyscrapers and Skylines in New York and Chicago" (Princeton Architectural Press, 1995). "They are naturals for conversion." Ms. Willis is seeking to establish a skyscraper museum in the district.
"Before the advent of fluorescent lighting and air-conditioning, buildings relied on natural light and air," she explained. "First-class office space had large, operable windows and a maximum distance of 28 feet from window to corridor wall."
A depth of 27 to 30 feet is considered optimum for new residential construction.
New York City's first skyscrapers rose skyward from Wall Street in unbroken planes. Standing cheek-by-jowl along the crooked, narrow lanes of old New Amsterdam, they produced the shadowy, windswept canyons that lend the district its distinct architectural flavor.
But while these turn-of-the-century towers may be closed in at street level, because they were built on angled streets they provide stunning views from above.
"Wall Street has a mixture of buildings," said Francis Greenburger of Time Equities, who is converting the Delmonico building at 56 Beaver Street to apartments that will be available in about two months and is also working on loft conversions at 47 West Street. "Some have spectacular views of the harbor and others are quite hemmed in. Some that seem closed in have architectural views, looking out on good buildings."
Many of Wall Street's commercial skyscrapers were built as architectural statements for corporations. They feature high ceilings, expansive lobbies, ornate materials and elegant architectural details.
"These buildings were built to service an affluent commercial tenant," said Paul Bentel, an architect who teaches preservation at the Columbia University Graduate School of Architecture, Planning and Preservation. "They were prime office space in today's terminology, corporate headquarters designed with incredibly beautiful lobbies and interior spaces and great views from the upper levels."
POSTWAR buildings have also been targeted for conversion. Their "wedding cake" setbacks are conducive to mixed-use conversions with residential units in the towers and commercial spaces in the base. Some of these buildings are empty, which makes asbestos removal easier.
At 25 Broad Street, a classical building whose facade is graced with fluted columns, an unusual Y-shaped footprint created an ideal layout for residential conversion, said Costas Kondylis of C.K. Architects, who is adapting the building for Bruce A. Menin of Crescent Heights Investments.
Known as the Broad-Exchange Building, the 525,000-square-foot edifice was first designed in late 1899 by the architect Robert Maynicke, to grandfather the site under a building code that was about to expire. The architectural firm of Clinton & Russell took over the project by 1901 and reorganized the plan. Both architects worked for a syndicate that included Henry O. Havemeyer, the sugar manufacturer and art collector; James Stillman, head of the National City Bank; and Henry Morgenthau, later the United States Ambassador to Turkey.
Mr. Kondylis is designing 345 rental units with luxury finishes that are to be available in about a year. Half will be one-bedroom units of about 800 square feet. The rest will be two- and three-bedroom apartments ranging in size from 1,032 to 1,500 square feet. The lowest ceilings are 10 feet, and five floors have 15-foot ceilings. All units have large, double-hung windows.
While the upper floors of the building were gutted, the marble-clad lobby, with its carved terra-cotta ceilings and palatial staircases, was kept intact. One elevator bank will be turned into a seating area and mail room. On the floors above, the elevator shafts will be made into laundry rooms and storage bins.
Mr. Kondylis called this "found space," explaining that office buildings require many more elevators and hallways than residential buildings because of the increased traffic that business generates.
Said Mr. Menin: "This will be one of the most luxurious residential lobbies in Manhattan. There was a different notion of space for commercial tenants, and now the residential tenant will benefit."
One-bedroom apartments are to start at $1,300 a month and two-bedroom units at $1,900.
Joseph Pell Lombardi, an architect who has adapted more than 75 commercial buildings to residential use throughout the city, lives in the walnut-paneled former board room of the Sinclair Consolidated Oil Company on the 29th floor of 55 Liberty Street. From his spacious, airy apartment he can see the Brooklyn Bridge, the Statue of Liberty and the Hudson River piers.
Mr. Lombardi converted the building to 89 co-op apartments in 1980 under the old tax abatement plan known as J-51. The tower was built in 1909 to the designs of Henry Ives Cobb, one of the first architects to adapt Gothic details to the new skyscraper form.
Mr. Lombardi is now converting the 15-story 33 Rector Street and the 27-story 11 Hanover Square into lofts for Tony Goldman of Goldman Properties, a developer who was instrumental in the renewal of SoHo and of South Beach in Miami.
Mr. Goldman plans to offer what he called "enhanced vanilla boxes," open lofts that "meet all code requirements in a well-secured, aesthetically pleasing, structurally solid, clean, elegant building." His market is "the new urbanites like me, who live and work in the same place and don't want walls put up for us," he said. The lofts will sell for between $150 and $250 a square foot.
"SoHo is now $350 per square foot and TriBeCa is $300," Mr. Goldman said. "The buying opportunity of this decade is in Wall Street."
"Now older office buildings are being converted but the trend will spread to newer ones; both are suitable," said Mr. Lombardi. "Office buildings in general are more adaptable than manufacturing buildings or warehouses because the infrastructure is newer and they are more geared to people."
For example, before World War II office buildings were built with a sink in almost every room. "The pipes are not right for bathrooms or kitchens but the shafts are there," he said. "You only need to add plumbing, and risers are not expensive."
As real estate speculation at the turn of the century accelerated, fear of increasingly sunless streets prompted passage in 1916 of the city's first zoning resolution, requiring buildings to have periodic setbacks so that light and air could reach the ground.
Office buildings constructed after 1916 lend themselves to mixed use, with retail and offices in the bottom floors and apartments in the towers.
The Hong Kong developer Li Ka-Shing has purchased 60 Broad Street, built in 1961, for mixed-use conversion. The 39-story curtain-wall building comprises one million square feet.
"The strategy," said Jonathan Coven of Concord Properties, a spokesman for the owner, "is to use the residential tax breaks to convert the tower floors to apartments and to use the lower floors for a big commercial tenant who needs lots of contiguous space."
Similar plans are being drafted for 127 John Street on behalf of Rockrose Development by the architect Avinash K. Malhotra.
"Asbestos removal takes a huge amount of money," said Thomas Elghanayan, a partner in Rockrose. "To do it properly you need to empty the building, redo it, and put it back together." This accounts in part for the wholesale abandonment of office buildings constructed in the mid-1970's, he noted.
ONCE the building is empty, as 127 John Street and others like it are, asbestos can be removed more economically. And renovating these buildings for residential use is far less costly than bringing them up to current commercial standards.
"There are at least half a dozen office buildings in lower Manhattan which are substantially vacant," said Merrill L. Roth, senior managing director of the Edward S. Gordon Company. Mr. Roth is also chairman of the Lower Manhattan Rental Conditions Committee of the Real Estate Board of New York.
Mr. Elghanayan said that the location of 127 John Street, on an open plaza across from the South Street Seaport where residential amenities like a supermarket, retail stores and restaurants are already in place, more than compensates for the building's drawbacks. "This building is the answer to skeptics who say that Wall Street has no shopping amenities and no light and air," he said. "You won't feel like you're in nowhere land."
When artists and other loft pioneers turned faltering manufacturing areas like SoHo and TriBeCa into chic residential enclaves, they did so in spite of existing zoning laws and municipal policies. But in lower Manhattan, where the commercial vacancy rate approaches 25 percent, the Giuliani administration is actively promoting conversions with tax abatements and zoning reforms.
The plan, which was signed into law by Gov. George E. Pataki on Oct. 29, 1995, provides a 14-year phased tax abatement program for conversion of office properties to residential use, 12-year phased tax abatement programs for commercial renovations and mixed-use conversions, a 12-year phased program of reductions in electric rates for commercial owners who make improvements costing at least 20 percent of their building's current assessed value, and an 8-year phased tax abatement program for construction of new buildings that are computer-ready.
The law also provides five-year phased abatement programs for real estate taxes and commercial rent taxes, applicable to newly leased space or renewals in commercial office properties built before 1975. To qualify for benefits, which must be passed on to tenants, owners must make a threshold investment to improve tenant space or common areas. These incentives come at a time when the residential real estate market is tight. Citywide, the vacancy rate is about one percent, the lowest in 15 years.
A pressing demand for new apartments combined with extraordinarily low prices for empty downtown office buildings and monetary inducements for conversions make Wall Street a favorable investment, real estate professionals say.
"People looked at residential conversions before," Mr. Kennedy noted, "but without the Giuliani plan they couldn't make the numbers work." Under the new tax benefit package, developers say they can produce stylish lofts and apartments at prices at least 20 percent lower than in more established neighborhoods uptown. But banks are reluctant to lend money in what they consider an untested market.
"The downtown incentives are the good news," said Rosina K. Abramson, a real estate lawyer in private practice in the downtown area. "The bad news is there is no financing." As a result, a segment of the real estate community that typically wants to invest up to one third equity and leverage the rest is cut out of the market, she added.
"I'm financing myself to some degree," said Mr. Goldman. "Banks have extended me letters of credit, I have mortgages on some smaller pieces and for the big stuff I'm using venture capital. It's early for the conventional lender."
A similar pattern emerged in SoHo, Ms. Abramson noted. "The first residential conversions in SoHo could not get financing," she said. "My sense is that there comes a tipping point when the banks will come in."
"From the point of view of lenders," said Michael D. Lappin, president of the Community Preservation Corporation, which finances low and moderate income housing, "it is difficult to understand what 'comparable living' is and to make a judgment about financing.
"Banks want to know 'What will people pay?' " Mr. Lappin said. "To find out they see what people are paying down the street. But here there is no 'down the street' yet."
Mr. Lappin said the Community Preservation Corporation is working on a deal downtown because "if we can help to seed the market, that is a good role for us to take."
"It is consistent with our purpose," he added. "Once one project gets off the ground it will provide the basis for future financing."
Mr. Goldman said that "the conventional lender will be in the marketplace in a couple of years when there are comparables to give comfort." He added: "We are in the business of making the comparables."
Still, some real estate professionals are reserving judgment.
"I would love to see downtown succeed," said Anthony E. Malkin, president of W & M Properties. "But in an old building every time you knock a hole in a wall you find a surprise. Unless a building is conservatively capitalized and has a significant equity investment, revenues may not meet expenses. This is the challenge investors face."
DEVELOPERS recognize that attractive apartments alone will not turn the Financial District into the urbane quarter that they envision. "A neighborhood doesn't just pop up," said Mr. Goldman. "It must be nourished, incubated, marketed and presented." Mr. Goldman is building a restaurant, the Wall Street Kitchen & Bar, at 70 Broad Street. The four-story stone building embellished with classical columns was built in 1908 as the headquarters of the American Bank Note Company, one of the nation's original printers of paper currency and stock certificates. The upper floors will house the new offices of Goldman Properties.
In addition, Mr. Goldman has purchased several small buildings in and around the newly designated Stone Street Historic District, including 15 and 17 South William Street, 85 Pearl Street "and a couple of others I can't mention," he said.
His intention is to generate "a firebox to ignite the neighborhood" by converting a row of low-scale warehouses into shops, restaurants and cafes to create what he called a "SoHo scene."
"Small buildings in a big area make the pedestrian more comfortable," he added. "They are embracing."
Mr. Goldman sits on the board of directors of the Alliance for Downtown New York, which manages a Business Improvement District formed by property owners to promote the neighborhood's turnaround. The area served extends roughly from City Hall to the Battery and from the East River to West Street.
Led by Carl Weisbrod, who formerly headed the New York State Economic Development Corporation, the Alliance lobbied forcefully for zoning revisions and tax abatements to spur redevelopment below Chambers Street. It is developing alternative sources of financing such as pension, investment and venture funds.
The executive director of the American Institute of Architects' New York Chapter, Carol Clark, said that for Wall Street to succeed "the city should be an aggressive advocate of mixed use, loosening up the rules to promote greater flexibility."
Amendments to the zoning code have been proposed to make allowances for mixed uses that the current "gray-flannel" zoning does not permit, said Joseph A. Rose, the chairman of the New York City Planning Department. Other zoning changes include reduction of the minimum unit size in older buildings from 1,800 square feet to 900 square feet on average.
"Wall Street is a vertical place," said Ms. Clark. "There is nothing analogous to it in form, except maybe Hong Kong, but the culture there is completely different. We have to encourage mixed use to insure that a vital residential community takes hold in this canyon atmosphere.
"The big problem downtown is about economics, not architecture."

HEADLINE: Books in Brief: NONFICTION

BYLINE: By CAROL PEACE ROBINS 

 BODY:

AMERICAN ORIGINAL
A Life of Will Rogers.
By Ray Robinson.
Oxford University, $30.
In this pleasant, reverential biography, two scenes linger in the mind: Will Rogers in a small plane over Alaska, hours before he died at the age of 55; and the rugged cowboy philosopher lassoing a mouse with string. Maybe they stand out because they stray from the usual image. In "American Original," Ray Robinson presents the legendary Rogers: born in 1879 in the Indian Territory and one-quarter Cherokee, he grew up to become the nation's "poet lariat." Stage philosopher who poked fun at politicians while roping celebrities in the audience, movie star, newspaper columnist, he was even urged to run for President. Puritanical and oafishly unromantic ("My dear old pal," he wrote to his future wife during their courtship), he was also extraordinarily generous: he offered handouts to unemployed actors, paid Flo Ziegfeld's medical bills and gave vast sums to relief organizations during the Great Depression. The book does raise some questions -- for example, why did this political commentator apparently never vote? One wishes that Mr. Robinson, whose many previous biographical subjects include Lou Gehrig and Christy Mathewson, had dug deeper. Still, Will Rogers has plenty of surface worth writing, and reading, about. Carol Peace Robins

TYPE: Review

HEADLINE: In the Region/Long Island;
From Army Base to a Step Toward Home Ownership

BYLINE: By DIANA SHAMAN 

DATELINE: SHOREHAM

 BODY:
AN experiment in helping people get off public assistance and into home ownership is under way on a quiet dead-end street of 16 two- and three-bedroom ranches built here in the 50's to house Army personnel.
The houses, on Defense Hill Road, were recently purchased by the nonprofit Community Development Corporation of Long Island (C.D.C.). They are being rented to families earning no more than $30,000 a year, who get rent subsidies under the Federal Section 8 program. 
 The nonprofit group's goal is to enable all the families to own their house within five years by providing support services that include job training and money management. The first five tenants moved in this weekend.
Defense Hill Road, which is about a quarter mile east of the William Floyd Parkway, leads from Route 25A to a 27-acre park on its north end that is owned by the Town of Brookhaven.
The park, where model airplanes are now flown, at one time had been a Nike missile site. The Army dedicated the property to the town in the 70's. But the 6.3 acres on which the 16 houses stand remained in Government hands until May 30 when, following a sealed-bid process, the property was purchased for $560,000 by the C.D.C., which was the high bidder.
The group, which has offices in Centereach and Babylon, was created in 1969 as the Suffolk Community Development Corporation to develop housing solutions for low- and moderate-income families. The name was changed last year.
Among other functions, it is the Suffolk County administrator of the Section 8 program for New York State. It has also developed houses for first-time buyers under various affordable-housing programs, and plans to build a 36-unit housing-for-the-elderly project in the Village of Amityville.
As part of its Section 8 responsibilities, the group also runs the Federal Family Self-Sufficiency Program, which teaches participants to attain economic independence.
The voluntary program provides families with career counseling, job training and placement, child care, budget counseling, and personal counseling among other benefits. Families are counseled on how to pay off their debts and how to establish a good credit history. The 16 families who will move to the Defense Hill Road project were selected from the roughly 250 families enrolled in the program.
Wilbur Klatsky, president of C.D.C., said the Defense Hill Road endeavor would be a practical demonstration that, given support and motivation, people could turn around their lives.
"You are doing a real disservice to people if you force them to stay in subsidized housing forever," he said. "What we are trying to do is allow people to extricate themselves from poverty and become productive members of our society, both financially and socially."
The 16 houses, which had been vacant but always maintained by the Army, are receiving a basic overhaul. Broken pipes are being repaired and new roofs, new cesspools and new sheds are being installed, along with new front doors. When the families are ready to purchase their houses, each will receive a new kitchen and a new bathroom.
The site is surrounded by dense woods, owned in part by the town and in part by the Long Island Lighting Company. The bright and airy houses occupy quarter-acre lots, and except for a sign at the entrance to the enclave that still reads "U.S. Government Property," they look like dwellings in any typical suburban subdivision.
The EAB bank of Uniondale gave the C.D.C. a $632,000 loan at 8.57 percent interest and payable in five years for the $560,000 acquisition and for legal and other costs. The nonprofit group is investing $168,000 of its own funds for repairs and road improvements that it promised the town it would make.
THE $10,000 cost of a new kitchen and bathroom is expected to be covered by a Federal loan that will be forgiven after the house has been owned for 10 years.
Although families initially will pay the C.D.C. only 30 percent of their income in rent under the Section 8 program, the Government will pick up the difference between that figure and $1,285 monthly for the three-bedroom house, and $885 monthly for the two-bedroom house. The rents will cover operating costs.
The houses are priced at $55,000 for the two-bedroom dwelling and $60,000 for the three-bedroom residence. In addition, there will be a $25,000 second mortgage placed on each house, which will only have to be paid if the house is resold within 10 years of ownership.
After 10 years, the $25,000 loan is forgiven. EAB will also provide mortgage loans to families, with down payments as low as 3 percent.
"Defense Hill Road will be an important example of how government, community groups and financial institutions can work together to save taxpayers money by assisting people in becoming self-sufficient," said Robert J. Klein, an EAB vice president.
Down payments will not be a major problem, said Marianne Garvin, the executive vice president of C.D.C., because families enrolled in the Family Self-Sufficiency Program build up savings in an interest-bearing escrow account.
The savings are attained, she explained, by the fact that rent increases the family would have to pay if its income rises are put into the escrow account. Once a family completes the five-year commitment to stay in the program, the escrow money is turned over to it. If the family drops out before the five years are up, it forfeits those savings.
Among the first five families moving into the Defense Hill Road houses are Diane Wood, 36, and her two daughters, Christine, 14, and Danielle, 12.
Separated from her husband in 1983 after three years of marriage, and later divorced, she was forced to seek Section 8 assistance so that she could afford a decent place to live, Ms. Wood said. She has been enrolled in the Family Self-Sufficiency Program since 1993.
SHE said she had earned an associate degree in accounting in 1989 at Suffolk Community College in Brentwood, and four years later received a bachelor's degree in business administration at St. Joseph's College in Patchogue, going to night school while working days. She recently began working as an accounting clerk for the Town of Huntington and now earns $24,000 a year.
"This deal is a wonderful opportunity because without it I don't think I could ever have afforded a house on Long Island with what I'm making," said Ms. Wood of the Defense Hill Road project.
Now she hopes to close on the two-bedroom house she selected within a year, using the $2,800 she has accumulated in her escrow account for a down payment.
"I look at this as a jump start in my life," said Jolene Novak who, with her husband, Frank, and their three children ranging in age from 6 to 8 will occupy one of the three-bedroom houses on Defense Hill Road.
Mrs. Novak works as a medical assistant, but her husband, a construction worker, is unemployed. Nevertheless, Mrs. Novak said, she hopes that they will be able to purchase their house within two years.
The Novaks signed up for the Family Self-Sufficiency Program three years ago because, Mrs. Novak said, "we wanted something better for our children than being on Section 8."
The story "still to be told," said Mr. Klatsky of the C.D.C., will have to wait another seven years or so.
"We have the sense that when we come back then to revisit Defense Hill Road we will be amazed at what these people will have achieved in contributing to the community," he said, "giving back in taxes, and making better lives for their children."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Community Development Corporation members visit house that is part of home-ownership program. (Michael Shavel for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: In the Region/New Jersey;
A Full-Service Hotel for Mount Olive's Trade Center

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
WORK has begun on a 141-room full-service hotel at the International Trade Center in Mount Olive in Morris County -- a first for the 670-acre multi-use business park off Exit 25 of Interstate 80.
The five-story Wyndham Garden Hotel, three years in the planning and estimated to cost $14 million, is expected to be completed within a year. The developers are the Rockefeller Center Development Corporation, a subsidiary of the Rockefeller Group, and Leslie E. Smith Jr., a builder and the center's executive director, who are also developing the Trade Center. 
 It will be managed by Wyndham Hotels & Resorts, the Dallas-based national hotel company that owns and operates 66 lodgings, including the 41-hotel Wyndham Garden chain.
The project reflects New Jersey's improving hotel industry, which has shaken off its recessionary doldrums to see occupancy and room rates rise. But since financing for new construction remains somewhat tight, especially for full-service hotels, it is being built with state, Federal and private funds.
"Three years ago we made a commitment to get this project done and at the time the public assistance was essential," said Jonathan D. Green, president and chief executive of the Rockefeller Center Development Corporation. "It is an easier financial picture today, but it's purely speculative whether we could have put it together today without government assistance."
The developers received a $6.5 million loan from Skylands Community Bank of Hackettstown and the Operating Engineers Local 825 Annuity Fund and Pension Fund; the New Jersey Economic Development Authority is guaranteeing $1.5 million of the loan. The developers also secured $1.5 million in Federal funds under a program that offers incentives to create jobs for low- and moderate-income people. The rest will come from the developers' own equity.
At the ground-breaking on June 18 Gov. Christine Todd Whitman stressed public and private partnerships in spurring economic development. "I am eager to see the International Trade Center realize its enormous potential for creating good jobs, permanent jobs, New Jersey jobs," she said.
Estimates are that the new hotel will create about 200 construction jobs as well as 60 permanent ones. Because Hackettstown, a neighboring Warren County community, was instrumental in securing the Federal grant, the permanent jobs will be offered first to residents of that town.
While there have been, and continue to be, a smattering of lodgings built in the last two years, few have been full-service hotels like the one begun at the Trade Center. Lenders generally have an investment ceiling of $5 million on the loans they make available, said Patrick Ford Sr., president of National Hotel Realty, a research firm in Portsmouth, N.H. Loans for more, he said, are more difficult to secure.
Lenders also require at least 20 to 30 percent equity participation from builders, and it still costs less to buy an existing hotel than to build a new one, said Mark V. Lommano, executive director at Smith Travel Research in Hendersonville Tenn., which reports on the market for hotel companies nationwide.
As a result the trend in new development in New Jersey and the nation has been toward economy, limited-service and all-suite hotels because lower construction costs are easier to finance, Mr. Ford and other analysts said. Typically, they do not have the large restaurants, ballrooms shops and abundance of meeting rooms found in traditional, full-service lodgings.
Among hotels not offering full-service facilities that are now under way or planned in New Jersey is the 134-suite hotel that Summerfield Hotel Corporation of Wichita, Kan., is building on Route 10 in Hanover. It is on that town's side of the 600-acre, 1.5-million-square-foot Prudential Business Campus that straddles the Parsippany/Hanover border.
In another project, Prime Hospitality Corporation of Fairfield signed a ground lease on June 19 with Hartz Mountain Industries of Secaucus to build a 159-room AmeriSuites hotel. It is to rise over the next year in the mixed-use Harmon Meadow development, which has more than 2 million square feet of office and retail space as well as five hotels. Harmon Meadow is in the Secaucus Meadowlands, which Hartz has almost completely built out.
IN Jersey City, Garden State Development of Jersey City continues to package its proposal for a full-service hotel a block from its hometown's waterfront. The developer had been negotiating for $9.3 million in public funds for the project, but "we just could not come to an agreement on the terms," said Peter G. Mangin, president of Garden State.
Nevertheless, he said, Garden State will proceed with the project, though it has been scaled back to 147 rooms from 245 and its cost reduced to $18.5 million from $40 million.
Mr. Mangin said financing would come from the partners and several lending institutions. Construction is to start by year's end.
The development activity, industry analysts said, is being nudged along by an improving economy. A rise in tourist and corporate travel, the latter in spite of cutbacks and restructurings, over the last two years has resulted in an increased demand for rooms at a time when there has been little new construction. Statistics kept by the New Jersey Hotel and Motel Association in Trenton show that the state's supply of 90,000 hotel and motel rooms relatively unchanged since 1994.
Over the last 12 months ending in April the state's lodging occupancy rate was 68.09 percent, up from 67 percent for the same period a year earlier, according to Smith Travel Research. The national rate was 65.8 percent. At the same time, daily room rates in New Jersey hotels averaged $75.42, up from $72.70 a year earlier; in the nation it was $68.71, up from $65.22.
Indications are that both the state and national markets will continue to build momentum into 1997, said John A. Fox, senior vice president at PKF, a Manhattan-based hotel and real estate consulting firm.
Meanwhile, developers of the International Trade Center feel the time is right for the Mount Olive project. When built it will be the only full-service hotel in the area.
"We needed a critical mass to support the hotel," said Mr. Green, of the Rockefeller Development Corporation. With the center's population nearly tripling to 4,300 workers in 35 concerns over the last year and a half it "has come of age," he said. Of the 7 million square feet of space planned at the center, about half is built and occupied.
THE Wyndham Garden Hotel, which will surround an open garden, has been designed to cater to individual business travelers and small business groups. It will have 3,723 square feet of meeting space, a 60-seat restaurant, lounge, a pool, a health club and parking for 248 cars. The daily corporate room rate will be $80 to $100.
Leslie V. Bently, president of the Wyndham Garden Hotel Division, said the Mount Olive site was an "opportunity" to do new construction because "the corporate infrastructure is in place and the market has an undersupply of rooms." This is the second hotel for the company in New Jersey, but its first new construction. Its other hotel, with 210 rooms in Piscataway, was a renovation.
Mr. Smith, executive director of the trade center, said signed commitments were in place with two major corporations for about 25 percent of the Wyndham Garden rooms for five years. He said he expected the hotel to be busy on weekends with demand for social functions from about 400,000 people living within a 15-mile radius of the site and from visitors to the nearby Waterloo Village historic and cultural center.
