HEADLINE: In the Region/Connecticut;
Hartford and New London Recycle Space for Artists

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES 

 BODY:
TWO derelict buildings, one in Hartford and the other in New London, are in different stages of restoration to provide housing at a moderate cost solely for artists, writers and musicians.
In so doing, the developers say they are salvaging worthwhile architecture and offering better homes than most of the targeted tenants could otherwise afford. 
 Hartford officials hope that the project will also help regenerate the city core. "It's a critical part of downtown," said Mayor Michael Peters. "We've designated a 20-block arts and cultural center near the Union Place railroad station, and this will create activity in the western end that pulls the whole thing together."
Artspace, as the project is called, is scheduled to open early in 1998. Design work has begun on a $5.9 million renovation of the four-story, 84,000-square-foot abandoned industrial building at 555 Asylum Avenue. The open floor spaces of the 1910 structure will be divided into 45 apartments where tenants may work as well as live -- a dual use that zoning laws often prohibit. Kai Soo Kim, principal of Kai Soo Kim Partners, a Hartford architecture firm, is the designer.
A state grant of $150,000 and a Federal loan of $600,000, plus city tax abatements of one-third over a 15 year period are in place. In addition, the state's Housing Finance Authority is considering low-income-housing tax credits amounting to $3.85 million and Fleet Bank has submitted a nonbinding letter of intent to supply a $1.4 million first mortgage to the developers, Artspace Hartford Limited Partnership.
In New London, the renovation of Sapphire House, a Granite Street mansion built in 1929 by Benjamin L. Armstrong, co-owner of a silk mill, has almost been completed. Five of the 10 apartments in the project, which has been entirely paid for by private donations, are already occupied.
The building was purchased for $150,000 in 1994 by the nonprofit Griffis Art Center on Pequot Avenue. An additional $250,000 was spent on restoration and renovation. According to Sharon Griffis, director of the center, "the building is now appraised at $960,000."
Its one- and two-bedroom apartments -- one with a 30-foot living-dining room, another with two bay windows, and still others with the original marble sinks in the bathrooms -- rent for $450 to $600 a month, including utilities, plus $25 to $60 a month for optional basement storage.
"I thought I had died and gone to heaven when I moved here from New York," said Robin Hornbuckle, a freelance magazine writer who was the first tenant to move in while renovations were under way more than a year ago. "This is the smalltown life I always wanted."
Anne Seelbach, a painter who exhibits at the Kouros Gallery on the Upper East Side of Manhattan, had been paying $900 a month in Brooklyn for a one-room apartment where "I went to bed breathing paint and turpentine all night," she said. She moved into Sapphire House in May and said she didn't know yet "whether or not I feel isolated."
"New York drives me nuts but I need to be there," she said.
A third resident is Lou Bonamarte, another painter who moved in when his lease in Old Lyme expired. "I have a lot of light and the apartment is bigger than I'm used to," he said. "It reminds me of a time when artists lived in boarding houses like the Florence Griswold in Old Lyme," a reference to what is now the Old Lyme Historical Society Museum, which is filled with American Impressionist paintings.
IN Hartford's Artspace, the one- to three-bedroom apartments, with 1,095 to 1,587 square feet of space, will be rented only for the first 15 years and income limits will be imposed.
Of those who will occupy Hartford's Artspace, 36 households must have incomes of no more than 60 percent of the Hartford area's median of $44,800 for a couple or $55,600 for a family of four; four must be at or under 50 percent of the median and the remaining five households must be at or under 25 percent of the median.
According to Marc S. Levine, a partner in Artspace and a West Hartford developer, the units will rent initially for about $550, $650 and $750 a month, respectively, including utilities, for the three different income levels.
After 15 years the tenants will be invited to buy the apartments and form a co-op. Prices will be commensurate with their incomes, said Mr. Levine. "The investors' fund or group will receive tax credits of about $600,000 a year for 10 years," he said.
"This is an incentive for artists to stay and eventually have an equity position," said Peter DeWilde Shapiro, a Hartford lawyer and a second partner in Artspace. Elie Pallandre, the third partner, is president of the real estate consulting firm in West Hartford that bears his name.
Mr. Shapiro's daughter, a performing artist in St. Paul, Minn., brought to his attention Artspace Projects of Minneapolis, which is now affiliated with Artspace Hartford. Over the last 15 years 300 apartments have been created in Minneapolis, and now St. Louis and Atlanta have projects of their own.
Plans envisioned by Mr. Kim and the partnership include restoring the building's brick exterior, installing 90 parking spaces and a small garden on the property, creating practice rooms for musicians as well as extra studio space for welders and potters, and providing a black-box theater and an art gallery in the building.
The Hartford Stage Company, Hartford Ballet, Hartford Symphony, Wadsworth Atheneum, Bushnell Hall and other members of the city's arts community view the project favorably. But Will K. Wilkins, director of Real Art Ways, an avant garde visual and performing arts center in Hartford for almost 30 years, is skeptical about what he calls "the use of the arts to revitalize downtown."
"That worked in the 70's," he said, "but during the 80's real-estate boom arts organizations were moved out to make way for office buildings, and the city had a great exodus of artistic talent. If the arts are used as marketing again, what happens when the city gets vital again?"
Larry A. Smith, dean of the Hartt School of Music at the University of Hartford, thinks that "it's possible that symphony and ballet professionals in the area might want to put down some roots, but artists tend to be a fairly mobile group."
FOR the last 15 years a number of artists have inhabited spaces in the former Colt firearms building -- first as studios, then as work/living spaces. Today these quarters make up about a fourth of the 155 occupied spaces in the half-vacant, 755,000-square-foot, 20- building complex, with the remainder of the occupied areas used primarily for light industry. The work/living spaces rent for $625 to $1,200. "We are trying to secure funding to demolish some nonlandmarked buildings and make this into an artists' community," said David Richard, manager of the property for its owner, Water+Way Properties of Hartford.
"Arts and entertainment is becoming a serious idea," said Robert Hankins, executive director of the Hartford Arts Council, and former director of five arts councils across the country.
"It's a lot cheaper for an artist or arts company to live and work in Hartford and still do business in New York and Boston. There is art and history here to build on for tourism -- a niche midway between those two cities that Hartford hasn't developed a strategy for yet."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Sharon T. Griffis outside renovated Sapphire House; Hartford Mayor Mike Peters, second from right, with Artspace partners Mark Levine, left, and Peter Shapiro, and the architect, Kai Soo Kim. (Photographs by George Ruhe for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Renewing The Loft Law

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Thank you for your balanced story on the renewal of the loft law [May 19]. It highlighted the contribution of sweat equity, money and creativity by loft tenant/artists and explained how renewing the loft law and preserving this productive sector of our economy will help downtown Manhattan continue to be a vibrant, mixed-used community. But please let us clarify: 
 Loft dwellers' average base rent is far higher than cited because loft tenants bear so many costs other tenants don't. These often include heat, hot water, electricity, repairs (internal and external), water taxes and maintenance duties.
You didn't mention that the law allows landlords a 20 percent rent hike en route to code compliance. This is in addition to the owner's right to pass along the cost of compliance work to the tenant in a restructured rent.
About Jesse Bryant's letter [June 2]: Has the writer considered what the value of these then-derelict buildings in downtown New York would be today if loft tenants hadn't come along and fixed them up at their own expense? The self-sufficient efforts of these artists/tenants created neighborhoods like SoHo and TriBeCa, which are now world-class tourist destinations. Rather than be a drain on New York these pioneers have helped create a booming art and cultural market that brings in billions of dollars in revenues annually to the city and state.
 
CHUCK DE LANEY ,
MARIE DORMUTH , BILL HALL
Lower Manhattan Loft Tenants
 
Executive Committee

HEADLINE: SPENDING IT;
Accounts That Give Piggy Banks a Run for the Money

BYLINE: By DEBRA NUSSBAUM 

 BODY:
WHEN Alana Miller was 11, she decided that if she wanted to go to college, she had to start saving her baby-sitting money and weekly allowance.
A neighbor in Massillon, Ohio, told her that a Key Bank branch in nearby Canton had a program geared for children. Youngsters who opened and maintained an account could earn model dinosaurs and recognition in a national newsletter mailed to other "Dinosavers." 
 In 18 months, thanks to interest and additional deposits, Alana's initial deposit of $20 has grown to almost $300, she has received two dinosaurs and has become a member of the Key Bank Youth Advisory Council, whose young members advise the bank on how it can appeal to children.
"When I save at home, I end up spending the money," Alana said. "It's safer there, at the bank."
Many banks have long made it easy for children to open savings accounts, by waiving minimum balances and service fees. For the most part, industry experts say, the banks are still doing so today, even as pressure to cut costs is leading many of them to establish or raise fees and minimums on adults' accounts.
The banks are not simply being benevolent toward children. Rather, the savings programs and special treatment are marketing strategies intended to pay off in both the short and the long term.
Children who learn to save at the bank, the reasoning goes, will continue to do so as adults. In the meantime, the children's parents will see the bank as family-friendly, with something for everyone. Scrapping the children's accounts, or burdening them with fees, could give the bank a Scrooge image.
"It's good business sense, and it's good customer relations," said Patricia Boerger, spokeswoman for the American Bankers Association in Washington. "This helps build a loyal, long-lasting relationship. Many people reach adulthood, but they don't know about savings. They charge, charge, charge."
The most recent Commerce Department figures, for the first quarter of this year, show that Americans save less than 4 cents of every dollar; in 1970, it was 8 cents. By comparison, the savings rate in Japan is 15.3 percent and in Canada, 8.3 percent.
In an effort to increase saving, bankers are working with the A.B.A. Education Foundation to present consumer education programs for adults and children, teaching them to become "more financially literate," said the foundation's chairman, Lloyd Lyons.
Of the 32,000 presentations last year, the foundation said that more than half were focused on children. The programs, often developed with local school officials, should be considered a community service, Mr. Lyons said, though he acknowledged they were "not without some marketplace orientation."
"Part of financial management habits are developed early in life, so kids are a target," said Mr. Lyons, who is also a first vice president of First Chicago NBD Bank in Indianapolis. "I don't think it can be done too early."
Key Bank's Dinosavers program, geared toward children 5 to 13 years old, lets them open accounts with as little as $10 at 1,300 branches in 11 states. An adult would need $50, with varying minimum balances to avoid fees.
Each deposit in a child's account is rewarded with a dinosaur sticker "to encourage savings behavior," said Eric Chester, vice president of retail investment product management for Cleveland-based Keycorp, Key Bank's parent.
After nine deposits of $10 or more, the child's name is published as a Super Dinosaver in a newsletter that reaches more than 100,000 households. Children who make regular deposits are rewarded with the model dinosaurs produced by the Carnegie Museum of Natural History in Pittsburgh. Though the balances are generally smaller than in an adult's account, and thus more costly to the bank, the children's accounts nonetheless receive the same interest rate.
"It's part of our strategic plan for the future," Mr. Chester said. "If a young family is driving by, they see that we have products and services for the whole family."
At the Republic National Bank of New York, the Bank for Kids program has more than 40,000 participants. Children aged 17 and younger can open an account with only $2. Each receives a membership card, a quarterly newsletter, an annual birthday card and the same interest rate as for an adult savings account, which was 2.75 percent last week, said Irene Ferrall, the bank's associate managing director. An adult would need $100 to open a savings account and would need to maintain a minimum balance of $500 to avoid a monthly fee.
"We want children to learn the value of savings and to be familiar with the banking environment and not intimidated by it," she said.
These young savers may also start thinking about banks in terms of loans and business propositions.
In Leavenworth, Kan., second-grade classes have been receiving "cookie loans" from the Army National Bank the last 10 years, to help finance their bake sales.
This year, students from MacArthur Elementary School in Leavenworth toured the bank, heard a lecture on fiscal responsibility and signed the papers for a collective $30 loan. The students used the money to buy ingredients and hold their sale. With the proceeds, they repaid the loan, including interest, and decided how to spend the profits. After the loan was repaid, each student received a certificate signed by the bank president.
The bank also offers a savings presentation to teach students about concepts like interest and Federal deposit insurance.
Credit unions are also getting into the youth market with programs that encourage savings. Some have set up shop in high schools with student managers, said Jerry Karbon, a spokesman for the Credit Union National Association in Madison, Wis.
"We want to encourage young people to save and not to put everything on a credit card," Mr. Karbon said. "We look at some of these programs as training grounds. Maybe these students will stay with it and we'll find them as tellers or managers in a credit union someday."
THE youth program of the D. Edward Wells Federal Credit Union in Springfield, Mass., began in 1988 and now has 2,000 members. Students act as clerks and tellers for their transactions, have board meetings and make loans to each other. They are taught to balance a checkbook, understand stock investments and to budget money for charity.
Shaheed Harris, a 16-year-old high school junior, opened an account 18 months ago with $10 and has now saved $170. He serves on the youth program's board and makes presentations to other students about financial issues. "I've been taught about stocks and bonds," he said. "It's basically training you about the business world."
Alana Miller has jumped into the world of high finance, too. The American Banker newspaper recently published a picture of a key that she drew for Key Bank, bearing the slogan, "This is your key to the future." Her idea to enhance the Dinosaver newsletter with a dinosaur-drawing contest is under consideration. But she probably won't use her savings to get a business degree.
"It's interesting," she said of the banking business. "But I'd rather be a teacher."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Alana Miller, 12, has saved almost $300 in 18 months in her passbook account. Her mother, Jenny, is at right. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: FUNDS WATCH;
Bank Funds Still Popular

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:
The recent spate of bank mergers has reduced the number of bank-sponsored money market mutual funds. The funds, however, are increasingly popular with bank customers, according to a study by IBC's Money Fund Report in Ashland, Mass.
This year, 14 funds have closed. That leaves 468 bank money funds holding $175.1 billion in assets, more than a fifth of all money fund assets. Assets of all money funds had grown 6.7 percent as of mid-May, but those of bank money funds had grown 8.3 percent. 
 Tracy Burk, editor of the Money Fund Report, said, "As demand for money funds continues to strengthen and more banks focus on business lost in traditional bank products, it is likely that bank-sponsored funds will become even more of a force."
 
Lowering the Fees: The Next Generation
The apple never falls far from the tree, the saying goes, and that's the case now with John C. Bogle Jr., son of the chairman of the Vanguard Group, the fund company long known for its insistence on minimal fees and expenses.
The younger Mr. Bogle has left the Quantitative Group of funds, where he had managed Quantitative Numeric and Numeric II, to start his own fund group in Cambridge, Mass. The reason: He was unhappy with the expenses charged by the Quantitative Group. The Numeric funds carried back-end loads of 1 percent plus a continuing 12b-1 marketing fee of one-half of 1 percent. Because 12b-1 fees cover marketing expenses and are meant to let funds increase in size -- to create savings based on economies of scale -- he contended that it was particularly inappropriate to assess such a fee on a fund that no longer accepted new investors.
His new group, called the ni group, consists of no-load funds, and expenses for each fund are capped at 1 percent. The ni Micro Cap fund invests in domestic equities with capitalizations of $100 million to $1 billion; ni Growth is a domestic small-cap fund that invests in companies with capitalizations up to $1 billion, and larger companies with above-average earnings growth rates, and ni Growth and Value invests in the largest 1,000 American companies.
 
A New Fund
Meyers, Sheppard & Company of Beverly Hills, Calif., has introduced the Meyers Sheppard Pride fund, an open-end, no-load, diversified fund that will seek long-term capital appreciation by investing in what it sees as undervalued, fundamentally sound companies with progressive policies toward the gay and lesbian community.
 
New Managers
Edmond D. Villani, president and chief investment officer of Scudder, Stevens & Clark, will assume the newly created office of chief executive tomorrow. Mark S. Casady, deputy chairman of Scudder's United States mutual fund group, becomes director of United States mutual funds.
 Derek Webb, who runs GT Global Natural Resources and GT Consumer Products, has picked up the lead manager position at Global America Growth from Kevin Wenck, who has left the firm.

HEADLINE: EVENING HOURS;
For Pursuits Both New and Venerable

 BODY:
A ballet troupe opens, two top restaurants close (but just for a while) and two centennials get the honors they deserve.

HEADLINE: BEHIND THE WHEEL/Volkswagen Passat TDI;
Knock, Knock. Who's There?

BYLINE: By JEFFREY J. TARAS 

DATELINE: ANN ARBOR, Mich.

 BODY:
THEY are called "oil burners," and you can hear them coming before they arrive. The frugal diesel engine, an 1892 invention of the German engineer Rudolf Diesel, has been embraced by thrifty Europeans since the first diesel passenger car, the Mercedes-Benz 260D, was introduced in 1936.
But the efficient, cacophonous, long-running diesel engine never really caught on in the United States, where gasoline has been cheap and plentiful. Now, after nearly disappearing from American roads, the engine is making another run at this country under the hood of the Volkswagen Passat TDI, which stands for "turbocharged direct injection." Available in Passat sedans and wagons, the 1.9-liter four-cylinder TDI engine differs in many respects from conventional diesels. 
 The engines operate by compressing intake air to high pressures before injecting fuel into the combustion chamber. This generates high internal temperatures, which cause the fuel to burn spontaneously without the spark plugs and ignition systems of gasoline engines. Though efficient, the diesel combustion process is also noisy, a chief reason most people won't accept diesel engines in their cars.
The TDI is a gentler engine that goes a long way toward quieting a diesel's clatter while sharply raising the efficiency of an already efficient design. Volkswagen disdains the separate combustion "pre-chamber" used for years on indirect-injection diesels. The pre-chamber was used to first combine fuel and air before passing it into the cylinders for combustion. This two-stage process helped to quiet the engine but reduced its efficiency. Doing away with the pre-chamber might have led to an unacceptably loud engine, yet the TDI is fairly quiet and well-mannered, thanks to a two-stage injection process in which two precisely metered amounts of fuel are infused as mists directly into the cylinder. Injecting fuel twice mimics the quieting effects of the pre-chamber without sacrificing efficiency.
Precise fuel delivery is handled by electronic controls that help to enhance performance and reduce exhaust emissions. The Passat TDI produces 20 percent less carbon dioxide than a comparable gas engine would, and it meets the emissions requirements of all 50 states.
Diesel engines use a fuel similar to home heating oil. Diesel is typically priced slightly higher than regular unleaded gasoline (it is selling in Manhattan for about $1.45 a gallon, midway between regular and premium grades). Though widely available at truck stops across America, diesel can be difficult to find at urban and suburban filling stations. But with the Passat's driving range of nearly 850 miles on a tank -- greater than the distance from New York City to Chicago -- fill-ups aren't needed often.
All in all, Volkswagen's TDI variation on the diesel theme results in an engine that is surprisingly powerful as well as remarkably efficient. Although it has just 90 horsepower, the 149 foot-pounds of torque allowed the five-speed test car to move smartly from a stop and to dart in and out of traffic. What's more, the four-door, family-sized car returns impressive fuel economy. The Environmental Protection Agency figures that the Passat TDI is capable of 37 m.p.g. around town and 45 m.p.g. on the open road.
To put this frugality in perspective, consider the 39/43 rating of the Geo Metro sedan, which weighs two-thirds as much and has a tiny a 1.3-liter gas engine.
But stellar fuel economy is only half of the Passat TDI story. The engine is quick to start; it idles smoothly, and it pulls the 3,000-pound Passat with authority. Driving with a heavy foot and a willingness to rev the engine reveals a sporty side to the car, largely because of the well-tuned suspension. The ride is firm but comfortable, even over the potholes and broken pavement of southeastern Michigan's roads. Accurate, well-weighted steering also contributes to the car's sporty demeanor.
Inside, the Passat features the accessories one expects in a $20,000 family car, including air-conditioning and power windows, locks and mirrors. And aside from the bleak expanses of black plastic in the test car, the interior has comfortable seats, easy-to-read controls and copious space.
Volkswagen charges $940 for the TDI engine, above the price of a gas-powered Passat. Despite the diesel's efficiency, it will take an average owner about four years to make up the extra cost of the purchase.
Volkswagen will add two more diesel-powered models this year when the Golf and Jetta go on sale with the same engine.
The Passat TDI illustrates how far the diesel-powered car has come. The mix of practical packaging, fuel-sipping efficiency and surprising performance make it a feasible alternative to mainstream family sedans like the Ford Taurus or Toyota Camry. Though still not for everyone, the TDI may win more Americans over to the diesel.
MERCEDES-BENZ E300 DIESEL: The Passat TDI may be a niche vehicle, but it is not all alone. Just one other diesel automobile is sold in the United States today, a version of the Mercedes E-Class sedan that has won raves in its gas-engine iterations.
The Mercedes diesel shares its distinctive retro styling and its chassis with two stable mates, the E320 and E420. The platform is so competent and well mannered it could probably make a Briggs & Stratton lawn-mower motor seem smooth and responsive. The body is solid. The steering is precise. The supple suspension imparts a lithe athleticism to the whole package.
But while the E300 is a good car, it is probably not the Mercedes that Janis Joplin was pining for. Its 3-liter, 6-cylinder engine uses 24 valves, dual overhead camshafts and a three-stage variable intake manifold to produce 134 horsepower. From inside the car, diesel clatter is held to a bare minimum at all engine speeds, with the only roughness coming just after the engine is started. Otherwise, the E300 is pleasant enough around town, but even better at covering highway miles at a fast, efficient clip.
INSIDE TRACK: The Passat TDI and E300 may be diesels, but they're nice nonetheless.

HEADLINE: VOICES: VIEWPOINT;
Why Our Pensions Aren't Really Portable

BYLINE: By JAMES H. SMALHOUT; James H. Smalhout is a visiting fellow at the Hudson Institute and author of "The Uncertain Retirement" (Irwin, 1996).

 BODY:
MILLIONS of Americans will never get their money's worth from their pensions because, in a very crucial way, the nation's pension system is hopelessly backward.
Workers covered by traditional defined-benefit pension plans can lose as much as 90 percent of their pension wealth as a result of routine job changes or termination of their plans because there is no accounting for the corrosive effects of inflation. When pension coverage ends, the earnings benchmark used to determine benefits is frozen, and inflation can then whittle down those benefits to almost nothing at retirement. 
The stakes here are large. Defined-benefit plans cover 25 million workers in the private sector as well as most government employees, Federal, state and local. The plans appear safe because they promise a monthly payment -- based on length of service to a single employer and a measure of earnings while on the job -- but they do not recognize what is being lost to inflation.
Protection against such erosion, however, is the point of pension "portability" -- a buzzword resonating through Washington that refers to maintaining the value of a pension beyond a worker's years at each specific job.
The Clinton Administration proposed legislation in early April that focused on new ways to help workers take their money from one employer-sponsored savings plan, known as a 401(k), to another when changing jobs. That misses the point and, even worse, the proposal was strangely silent on the core problem of portability for defined-benefit pensions.
The logistics of moving retirement funds with workers who change jobs should not really matter. What should matter is whether pensions promised by traditional defined-benefit plans can withstand the pressure of inflation and retain their real value until the workers retire.
Furthermore, portability is not a problem for 401(k) money. Most workers already withdraw it when they change jobs.And mobile workers can then "roll over" their 401(k) savings into individual retirement accounts without losing any tax advantages.
Congressional hearings began this month on ways to simplify and improve pension coverage. For their part, Republicans have emphasized a need to set up more savings plans -- not pension reform. The House of Representatives passed a bill in late May abolishing rules that make those plans costly and complicated for small companies to provide.
But people ought to focus on the real portability problem. Portability losses defeat the purpose of tax breaks that subsidize employer-sponsored pensions. Congress intended to encourage employers to protect families who would not otherwise save enough for retirement. The lack of portability means that many families are not well protected and that employers do not need to spend as much on workers who stay long enough to collect pensions.
Each year, the Treasury could collect more than $60 billion from employers if their pension contributions were not tax deductible and if returns on pension investments were taxed when the pension plan received them. These provisions represent the second-most-costly tax expenditure for the nation, after the deduction for mortgage interest. But they never show up explicitly in the Federal budget. Instead, they are reflected by higher tax rates and increased Government debt.
Portability losses are not just a threat to a financially secure retirement. The flip side is a powerful incentive -- subsidized by Washington -- for companies to send their workers packing. Employers reap a windfall when workers leave. This is no doubt contributing in a big way to voters' fears about job security.
A first step in providing portable pensions involves indexing benefits to inflation after workers move on to new jobs. Unfortunately, a gaggle of lobbyists has been whining that this would be too expensive and would result in the termination of many plans. Unions are not keen on portable pensions because it is easier to organize workers when they stay put. And companies can offer benefits that look good on paper while allowing only a privileged few to ever qualify for a decent pension.
But inflation protection for deferred pensions has not reduced the appeal of defined-benefit plans in countries like Ireland, the Netherlands and Britain. Pre-retirement indexing also need not result in higher annual pension costs for employers. With indexing, mobile workers could still have much better pensions, without raising total pension costs, if these plans offered initially to replace a smaller share of earnings in return for each year of service.
PROVIDING portable pensions would not be difficult. The Treasury recently announced plans to issue inflation-indexed bonds. This will suddenly make it simple for employers to calculate the cost of deferred pensions, similarly indexed.
Safe and secure pensions must be portable. President Clinton responded to concerns about unsafe pensions during the second Presidential debate in 1992 by saying he would regulate pension financing more effectively, but the goal of providing workers with a financially safer, more secure retirement has largely eluded his Administration so far.
Many important issues will be debated before the election. But it is still not too late for real progress in protecting mobile workers against horrendous pension losses. What better way to stand before the voters than to take the important step of making America's pensions safer and more secure?

SECTION: Section 3; Page 8; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: EARNING IT;
Casual, Worker-Friendly, And a Moneymaker, Too

BYLINE: By ANDREA ADELSON 

DATELINE: VENTURA, Calif.

 BODY:
SURFBOARDS are stacked by the mail room, except when the surf's up two blocks away. There is a volleyball court out back, and showers in the restrooms. The cafeteria does not serve beef. Job interviews may touch on mysticism. Business cards have no titles. The corporate uniform is shorts and sandals. Work spaces are communal. Even the chairman does without an office.
A parody of the Southern California life style? Not at all. This place exists, and despite its unconventional conventions, business is thriving. Patagonia Inc., an outdoor-clothing retailer, did $154 million in sales in the last year, its best ever. 
 "We started working to create an atmosphere to maintain our life style," said Kris McDivitt Tompkins, who spent 20 years with Patagonia, until she resigned as chief executive in 1993. "We worked hard so we could take time off to go skiing and climbing."
More importantly, Patagonia has made a commitment to avoid the barriers that women can face in business. Though the number of women among the nation's highest-paid managers has nearly doubled in the last decade, only about 5 percent of senior managers at the largest corporations are women. And 96 of the top 500 companies have no women on their boards.
At Patagonia, a privately held retailer with 700 employees, women hold more than half of Patagonia's top-paying jobs (the top 20 percent of the pay range) and nearly 60 percent of managerial jobs. And five of the eight directors are women. Women account for 52.7 percent of the work force.
While its policies and practices appeal to and benefit women, Patagonia is not a company founded by women to make products for women. Ms. McDivitt Tompkins' successor is a man, David B. Olsen, as is Patagonia's chairman, Yvon Chouinard. Mr. Chouinard, 57, founded the company with his wife, Malinda, in 1973 to design and sell high-quality outdoor clothing for both sexes, and in the process, the company's mission statement says, "do no harm."
Last month, President Clinton hailed Patagonia as an "impressive" example of corporate responsibility, and he praised its family benefits and corporate tithing -- 10 percent of pretax earnings or 1 percent of sales -- to various environmental causes. Last year, Patagonia's donations amounted to $1.5 million, the company said, and the Department of Labor lists Patagonia among garment makers and retailers that monitor contractors for labor law compliance.
Barbara Reskin, a sociology professor at Ohio State University who studies corporate benefits, said, "Patagonia is defining the upper ideal of what people would like to see in family-friendly policies."
DECISIONS are made mostly by consensus. After the company had a round of layoffs in 1991, the books were opened to employees to show them why expenses needed to be reined in. They responded, current and former officials say, by helping managers cut costs significantly.
Benefits and work rules are generous and applied evenly. For example, new fathers, as well as mothers, get paid leaves. Employees can often be seen lunching with their children in the child care center, which the company subsidizes to hold down costs.
An aggressive recycling program led the single trash Dumpster to be filled up just twice in the last year. Indeed, Patagonia's high-priced products, sold by catalogue, in company stores and at other retailers, include items made from recycled soda bottles, like the Synchilla fleece shirt ($50), or from organically grown cotton, like the Topical T-shirt with surf logo ($24).
Patagonia is consistently profitable, said Ronald Jones, director of the apparel practice in Los Angeles for the accounting firm Moss Adams. But earnings fall short of the exceptional 25 percent pretax profits of apparel companies like Guess, he said.
"It takes that premium profit and puts it in programs, environmental investment and family benefits." he said. "They never set significant financial success as a priority. They've stuck to their noneconomic goals. Yvon wants the company to be a role model for a different objective."
Patagonia's culture evolved from the passions of Mr. Chouinard, an outdoors enthusiast who has developed a deep interest in environmental causes. Mr. Chouinard (pronounced shuh-NARD) started in retailing nearly 40 years ago, when he began selling the climbing pitons that he first fashioned for his own use. These days, he spends half the year away from the office, surfing and fly fishing, often using these activities to field-test new clothing.
Requests to interview him were politely rebuffed by a company spokeswoman who explained that Mr. Chouinard was on such a sojourn, or "off the map."
At the single-story corporate headquarters here, where patterns are designed for farming out to sewing contractors, evidence of Mr. Chouinard's philosophy and personality abounds. The ban on beef, for example, reflects his own diet.
While Mr. Chouinard may exercise his influence over company cuisine, he does not do so from a private office. Nor do other executives. Indeed, Patagonia's managers do without customary perks and work at desks out in the open with everyone else.
Patagonia's defining moment was probably the naming of Ms. McDivitt Tompkins, a longtime Chouinard family friend, as general manager in 1979, entrusting her with the day-to-day management -- and shaping -- of the company. She was then 28, an accomplished ski racer with no real business experience beyond working with the Chouinards since Patagonia began as a shoestring operation.
"We were a renegade group, not shunning society the way the hippies did, but rejecting cultural assumptions of the times," said Ms. McDivitt Tompkins, who is 45. "Between the three of us, the glass ceiling never existed."
Ms. McDivitt Tompkins now lives in Chile, but remains on the board; she and the Chouinards are the only stockholders.
People familiar with the company say many interwoven factors have created and fostered Patagonia's unusual corporate culture and a work environment that women find especially supportive.
As is often the case elsewhere in business, people are hired at Patagonia because they seem likely to fit into the corporate culture. Kate Larramendy, the company's design director for 15 years, said the "lack of sexism is an outgrowth of this being a nonhierarchical company."
"A lot is not clearly defined," she said. "You can work outside the box."
Karyn O. Barsa, 35, the company's chief financial officer, said a flat hierarchy meant decisions made by consensus, a "quality considered feminine and very welcomed here." Ms. Barsa said prospective employees' business skills were secondary to their being ardent outdoor types and product users, like the receptionist who is a world Frisbee champion or the manager of water sports products, who is a top kayaker.
"In self-propelled, single-participant sports there is little ego, little bias," she said. "Bias and prejudice are a result of insecurity. These are not insecure people."
Alison May, the company president from 1994 until last April, when she completed a financial overhaul of the company, recalled that her own job interview with a group of senior managers had touched on topics like herbalism and mystic practices of ancient Druids. She said that the company sought people who are gentle, self-sufficient and not domineering, and that an outdoors orientation and a commitment to preserving the environment seemed to fit that profile.
Patagonia's human resources director, Terri Wolfe, said employees were encouraged to use their benefits, a policy that she said helped retain workers, especially women. Just 4.5 percent of Patagonia's 700 workers leave in a typical year, compared with 25 percent at some rivals, she said.
PATAGONIA has not been an unqualified success. After experiencing sales growth averaging 26 percent a year in the 1980's, it overhauled itself in 1991 at the behest of nervous lenders. The company laid off 120 of its 620 employees at the time, cut its clothing line by 40 percent and catalogue distribution from four times a year to two.
It is now back on track, with an even larger work force, more retail outlets and, people familiar with the company say, a firmer hand on the throttle.
Francine Blau, who teaches labor economics at Cornell University, believes that Patagonia's renewed fiscal vigor should encourage other companies to follow suit in establishing progressive policies, especially toward women. But Sheila W. Wellington, president of Catalyst, a professional-women's advocacy group, contends that women achieve management parity only when top executives make it a goal, which she considers a hard task in a large corporation.
Ms. McDivitt Tompkins is skeptical that the Patagonia experiment will be widely repeated. "I don't think corporate America is reformable," she said. "If you want to create an environment like Patagonia, you have to create it yourself. It's not in the big sweeping decisions. It's in the minutia, in the thousands of tiny decisions made every day. You can't teach it. You can't sell it. It's only from people whose natural inclination is to live that way."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 2432 words

HEADLINE: TALKING MONEY WITH: KI-JANA CARTER;
Risk? A Running Back Takes a Pass

BYLINE: By LARRY DIGNAN 

 BODY:
WHEN Ki-Jana Carter of the Cincinnati Bengals suffered a season-ending knee injury in his first preseason game last year, he learned a lesson that many professional football players don't learn until too late: a career in the National Football League, and the pay that comes with it, can be short-lived.
Mr. Carter, 22, the top draft pick in the N.F.L. in 1995, has a $19.2 million, seven-year deal that included a $7.125 million signing bonus, the richest to date for a rookie. The contract is lined with incentives -- an extra $100,000 for scoring 12 or more touchdowns in a season, for instance, and a clause that could make Mr. Carter, a running back, a free agent after four years. 
 "I came out of college and felt invincible," Mr. Carter said. "After the injury happened it was like, 'Wow, I may not be making any more money.' " Though Mr. Carter expects to come back and play well this season, he now views his career and his pay on a year-to-year basis. "I counted the first four years of the contract in the beginning," he said. "Now I just look at the money I have."
It is a prudent attitude not only for athletes, but also for models, entertainers and others who may have lucrative but relatively brief working lives.
In the case of Mr. Carter, the knee injury deepened an already cautious nature. Last season, he decided to put all his money into low-risk, fixed-income investments, mostly municipal bonds from Florida, where he is building a home. It was an unusual choice; most people of Mr. Carter's age who have money to invest are advised to put it primarily into stocks.
He will soon shift some of his portfolio to riskier, and potentially higher-yielding, equity investments. But the focus on tax-free bonds reveals his penchant for caution, as well as the educational role of planners who specialize in young, newly wealthy athletes.
"I knew what was safe," said Mr. Carter, who majored in business marketing at Penn State. "I got a $7 million bonus, so there was no need to gamble. I wanted to be safe and then branch out."
Mark Griege, Mr. Carter's financial planner, acknowledges that the unorthodox allocation was not ideal. But he says it was important that Mr. Carter's initial investments match his risk tolerance and his knowledge of investing.
"Rule No. 1 is, 'Don't invest in something you don't understand,' " said Mr. Griege, a principal at Robertson, Griege & Thoele, a financial planning firm in Dallas that also works with Troy Aikman, the Dallas Cowboys quarterback, and other prominent N.F.L. players.
Mr. Carter is as conservative in spending money as he is in investing it. He may be a millionaire several times over, but he still shops for the best prices on watches, clothes and other goods. That attitude stands out among professional athletes, many of whom spend their first contract as if it were going to last forever. (It won't. Some N.F.L. contracts can be ended by the team if a player is injured or performs poorly and, anyway, the length of the average N.F.L. career is just five years.)
Mr. Carter went to particularly great lengths to comparison-shop for his financial planner. He interviewed 14 of them before narrowing the pool to 7, all of whom then paid to fly themselves to interviews with Mr. Carter. Nearly all the planners were "fee only," charging a set amount regardless of the investments their clients made. But some of the planners receive commissions on such investments; many experts, believing that such advisers may give self-interested advice, discourage investors from using people with these pay arrangements.
How was Mr. Griege chosen? Compensation played a role, though neither he nor Mr. Carter would divulge the terms of his pay, except to say that Mr. Griege is a fee-only planner who is paid a percentage of the assets he manages. But the clinchers in Mr. Griege's selection were personal service -- Mr. Griege flies around the country to meet Mr. Carter -- and interaction with the client.
"Many tell you that you should do this or that," Mr. Carter said. "Mark tells you the reason why you should."
MR. CARTER not only interviewed the candidates but had some advisers do so, too. All told, he now has an agent, a planner, marketers, a family friend who is a lawyer and his mother all helping him make financial decisions.
"I didn't know all the questions to ask, so I had people around me who knew them," he said. "I knew the basics, but questions like, 'What if my career lasts only four years?' had to be asked. My mom was there and asked, 'Would you talk to my son a lot?' "
Awareness of his limitations is valuable for Mr. Carter, who said he learned little about investing as he grew up because his family did not have much money. They lived in a two-bedroom apartment outside Columbus, Ohio. Mr. Carter's father left when Ki-Jana was a baby, and he spent much of his childhood watching his mother, Kathy, work 12- to 15-hour days in her beauty salon to provide for him and his little brother, Nathan.
Mr. Carter is similarly family-oriented. "My mom worked so hard when I was little and she sacrificed everything for her kids," he said. "The least I can do is pay her back." His financial goals are straightforward: support his mother and brother, build his home in Florida and achieve financial security for life.
He also would like to help the Big Brothers/Big Sisters program with money and time. Indeed, Mr. Carter, who as a child had a Big Brother from age 10, is becoming a spokesman of sorts for the group. He also plans to contribute to other charities and is thinking about starting his own foundation someday.
He has made a good start toward these goals. Mr. Carter has bought his mother a Mercedes-Benz, a Chevrolet Blazer for the winter and a spacious house in Columbus. He also provides her with a monthly stipend that is "a little better than she made at the hair salon," said Michael Schiff, the family lawyer. He has already started work on building his home in Florida.
BUT, relatively speaking, Mr. Carter does not spend a lot, even though he gets his annual salary in 17 tempting weekly installments over the football season. Consider last year's earnings of $7.875 million -- the $7.125 million signing bonus and the $750,000 first-year salary. (His salary climbs each year. The 1996 figure is $946,000.) Of the 1995 income, 42 percent was saved, 42 percent went to taxes and 16 percent was spent. But excluding big expenses -- the fee for Leigh Steinberg, the well-known agent, for instance, and the average annual premium of $50,000 he pays Lloyd's of London for a $3 million disability policy -- Mr. Carter's 1995 expenditures equaled only 10 percent.
Much of that was used to satisfy Mr. Carter's self-imposed family obligations. But while he is generous with his family and is a good saver, too, he is not completely self-denying. He has the young athlete's obligatory sports car, a Mercedes, as well as a Lexus. He has a nice watch, and he says his biggest weakness is clothing. But, like many young people, he's talking Polo and Tommy Hilfiger, not Armani.
"It's like I'm still in college," he said. "I go out and spend money and go, 'Damn, I shouldn't have spent that much.' I know I can spend without worrying about it, but I still can't really picture a million dollars."
There is one danger, however, that the prudent Mr. Carter may be courting: inflation. Mr. Griege says Mr. Carter, with his heavy investments in tax-free bonds, needs some equities in his portfolio to insure that his money outruns inflation. "The time he has can work for him or against him," Mr. Griege said, and if it works against him, it can really hurt.
"Inflation is a significant threat for athletes," he added. "Ki-Jana is looking at 50-plus years of inflation."
So at the end of May, Mr. Griege offered Mr. Carter two portfolio allocations that would move him more into equities while still recognizing his conservative bent.
The first would have put 55 percent of Mr. Carter's assets into municipal bonds and spread 30 percent among no-load equity mutual funds. The 30 percent would have been divided equally among three categories: small-capitalization domestic funds, large-cap domestic funds and international funds. The portfolio would also have had 5 percent in real estate, primarily real estate investment trusts, and 5 percent in oil and gas investments, through mutual funds.
The remaining 5 percent would have been used to introduce Mr. Carter to individual stocks in fields he knows, like sports. Mr. Carter's early favorite is Nike, which happens to handle Mr. Carter's marketing through its Nike Sports Management division. As Mr. Griege's clients mature as investors, they typically put about 10 percent to 15 percent into individual stocks.
The second allocation would have assigned 30 percent of assets to municipals, 20 percent to small-cap domestic funds, 12 percent to large-cap domestic funds, 18 percent to international funds and the remaining 20 percent to real estate (7 percent) oil and gas (7 percent) and cash for buying individual stocks (6 percent).
But when the planner and the player met on June 17 for a periodic portfolio review, Mr. Carter chose neither option. He did not feel bold enough for the second nor conservative enough for the first. He decided to allot 45 percent to municipals and 40 percent to equity mutual funds (15 percent in small-cap funds, 15 percent in large-cap funds and 10 percent in international funds). As in the first option, there will be 5 percent each in real estate, in oil and gas and in a pot of cash for buying stocks based on Mr. Griege's evaluation and Mr. Carter's input.
"I was impressed with his focus," Mr. Griege said after the meeting. "He felt he was up to speed and felt comfortable with the new portfolio." But Mr. Griege said he still wanted the portfolio to evolve. Mr. Carter "was still nervous with investing overseas, so we're underweight there," said Mr. Griege, who also wants to increase the portfolio's overall equity portion.
While putting 45 percent of a 22-year-old's portfolio into tax-free funds may seem overly conservative to Mr. Griege and others, the wealth of many young athletes today may make such a strategy plausible, some advisers say.
"Players are making so much that there's no need for a lot of risk" to achieve their goals, said Bernard Wolfe, who runs a financial planning firm in Rockville, Md. He tends to use municipals for 45 to 55 percent of the assets of his clients, many of whom are professional hockey players.
IN any event, Mr. Griege and Mr. Carter are forging ahead with their new allocation. Mr. Griege will split each of the three equity fund categories among five or six funds, chosen for their performance, the records of their managers and their expense ratios, along with an eye toward a mix of growth and value investing styles.
Mr. Griege's picks among small-cap funds, some of which have substantial mid-cap holdings, include Columbia Special, Strong Opportunity, T. Rowe Price Small-Cap Value, Mutual Shares, Heartland Value and the Brandywine fund. Among his favorite large-capitalization funds are Dodge & Cox Stock, the Janus fund, Twentieth Century Ultra, the Clipper fund, the Yacktman fund and Fidelity Value.
His favorite international funds are Sogen International, T. Rowe Price International, Scudder International, Warburg Pincus International and Twentieth Century International.
As for individual stocks, Mr. Griege is willing to go with Nike, Mr. Carter's favorite, but not yet. "If there's a correction in the future of Nike, we'll probably buy it," Mr. Griege said. The stock closed last week at $102.75, up from less than $65 four months ago.
He said Mr. Carter would invest in a few initial public offerings. Mr. Griege has a stack of candidates, underwritten by the likes of Merrill Lynch, Smith Barney and Goldman, Sachs. Usually, he said, his clients sell some shares of a new issue in two to four days, a process called flipping, for the quick price run-up, but they hold onto other shares of that issue. This double approach, he said, means that he and his clients look for stocks that will not only soar early on, but also appreciate in the long term.
Mr. Carter has already invested a small amount in one recent public offering: Planet Hollywood, the restaurant chain with a celebrity theme, whose stock has bounced between $22 and $28 since its mid-April debut, closing on Friday at $27.
Because young, wealthy people can sometimes be gulled on financial matters, Mr. Griege's plan also involves teaching Mr. Carter about investing. For example, he boils down information about each fund to a one-page summary that addresses tax issues, risk and asset allocations, with graphics showing how the funds perform in relation to one another.
Mr. Carter, who is learning to use the reports of Morningstar, the fund research company, will soon also learn to analyze a prospectus and to use Quicken, the financial planning software. Cash flow and budgeting, a full-time job when dealing with lots of zeros, is handled by Mr. Griege.
"I don't expect him to understand all of the fund terminology, but he knows why he has to diversify," Mr. Griege said. "He's a second-year guy and the goal is to have him grow in his education."
MR. CARTER said he wanted to learn as much as possible, but he knows that playing football is his best investment at the moment. If he plays well this season, he notches another year's earnings and possibly income from endorsements. Professional athletes who focus too much on business in their first contract often don't get a second.
Mr. Carter said his goal was to understand investing from a common-sense perspective. He and Mr. Griege sometimes discuss the risky things that other football players are doing with their money. Some young players are starting restaurants, which saps money from the core of a portfolio and should only be done toward the end of a career, Mr. Griege said. Other players simply spend a lot. Marshall Faulk, a running back for the Indianapolis Colts who is the same age as Mr. Carter, bought 11 cars within 2 years. And until he got some financial discipline, Dennis Rodman of the Chicago Bulls was earning $2.5 million a year playing basketball, but still managed to fall $1 million into debt.
Mr. Carter is determined to be different. "I see a lot of guys come into the league and want to go out and spend to show they have a lot of money," he said. "That's good now, but I'm trying to live like this when I'm 50, 60, 70."

HEADLINE: An Invitation to Terror

BYLINE: By Chas. W. Freeman Jr.; Chas. W. Freeman Jr. was United States Ambassador to Saudi Arabia during the Persian Gulf war.

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
On Feb. 25, 1991, just three days before President George Bush proclaimed the end of allied combat operations, the last Iraqi Scud of the Persian Gulf war hit a barracks in Dhahran, killing 28 newly arrived United States Army reservists and wounding scores more.
Last week's carnage in Dhahran recalled that earlier horror. It also brought home to Americans that five long years after the spectacular victory of Desert Storm, American troops are still on combat duty in the Persian Gulf. Thousands remain in Saudi Arabia and other gulf countries, flying missions with full combat loads, manning anti-missile defenses and enforcing a naval blockade against Iraq. And they will stay there, far from home and vulnerable to more terrorist attacks, as long as Saddam Hussein remains in power in Baghdad. 
This is far cry from the return to normalcy that Americans and our Arab coalition partners looked toward in 1991. We had given President Hussein a terrible licking. We were sure his days were numbered. We had been careful not to weaken his country to the point where his successor would be unable to play Iraq's traditional role as a counterweight to Iran.
We expected Baghdad to see that it was in its interest to comply with United Nations demands that it dismantle its weapons of mass destruction -- its missiles and its nuclear, chemical and biological arsenal -- and to seek a renewal of peace with the Gulf Arabs, including Kuwait and Saudi Arabia.
We thought the Gulf Arabs would form an effective collective defense organization, backed by Egypt. United States land and air forces would then be no more necessary than they had been before Iraq's invasion of Kuwait. Americans and Gulf Arabs alike celebrated as our troops headed home.
But Saddam Hussein is still there, cheating all he can on United Nations efforts to halt his drive for nuclear, chemical and biological weapons with which to gain revenge on Kuwait and Saudi Arabia. And with the Gulf Arabs as militarily disunited as ever, the only thing stopping him is the United States military presence in the region.
Operation Desert Storm burdened the Saudi economy with huge debts. Saudi living standards have fallen substantially as the kingdom's generous subsidies to its citizens are squeezed to pay off wartime loans and subsidize the coalition forces (Americans and a few Britons) still in the kingdom. The presence of these foreign forces chafes at the country's social and political stability, and resentment swells the ranks of extremist groups hostile to the Saudi royal family and the West. The royal family's sensitivity to this resentment accounts for the initial Saudi press reports' identifying the victims of last Tuesday's bombing only as "foreigners," not as United States airmen.
Americans and Saudis would be a great deal more comfortable if the situation allowed what both expected after the gulf war: the withdrawal of United States forces from the kingdom. But because Mr. Hussein managed to escape the political consequences of his military debacle, the United Nations effort to curb his military ambitions must continue as long as he remains in power, and a United States military presence is essential to that effort.
Perhaps it's time to get serious about removing Mr. Hussein from power. But current American policy isn't really behind such an effort. It is based on the notion of "dual containment" -- isolating both Iraq and Iran in the region, cutting them off from the world economic and trading system, and balancing the two nations' military power with American forces while waiting for Mr. Hussein to fall.
The best that can be said of this strategy is that so far it has not brought Iraq and Iran together against the United States, as many of its critics feared it would. The worst may be that it has handed Baghdad and Teheran the initiative. Either can yank huge deployments of American forces to the gulf whenever it wishes to do so. Iraq has successfully pulled this particular string several times. Iran could do the same.
Iraq may be isolated, but Iran is not. The Gulf Arabs have joined our European and Asian allies in normalizing diplomatic relations and carrying on a thriving export trade with Teheran. The only coalition member with a policy of "dual containment" seems to be the United States. Efforts to force our allies to join us in isolating Iran anger them more than they hurt Teheran.
The Iraqi threat is military. United States forces in the region help contain it. But it is hard to see the relevance of these forces to the Iranian threat, which has consisted of terrorist actions in places far from the gulf, like Lebanon. The destabilizing effects of the American military presence in Saudi Arabia, Bahrain and other Gulf Arab states may, in fact, be generating new targets for Iranian-backed terrorism.
Meanwhile, George Bush and Margaret Thatcher have been removed from office. King Fahd is visibly nearing the end of his reign. But Saddam Hussein is still in Baghdad, plotting revenge. Even if he didn't give the perpetrators of Tuesday's atrocity a helping hand, he must have been pleased by what they did.
"Dual containment" is a policy with no apparent benefits and many risks. It is hard to argue that it amounts to more than a catchy slogan for keeping United States forces in the gulf indefinitely until Mr. Hussein falls from power. Five years have gone by, and that has not happened. It might happen tomorrow, of course. Then again, it might not happen anytime in the next five years. Until it does, American forces will be stuck in the gulf, Saudi Arabia will remain troubled by their presence and extremists will see them as attractive targets for terrorism.
There are plenty of Iraqis eager to get rid of Saddam Hussein. The United States spends less to support them than its Air Force spends to fly a day's missions in the gulf. We need to get our priorities straight.
Our interests and those of our Gulf Arab allies call out for a strategy that does not depend on permanent stationing of United States forces in the gulf. Helping the Iraqi people rid themselves of Saddam Hussein, not hunkering down behind barricades in Dhahran or picking fights with our allies over Iran, is the key to security in the gulf.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 2; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1102 words

HEADLINE: DIARY

BYLINE: By HUBERT B. HERRING 

 BODY:

POLITICS
 
Hit 'em Where They're Strong
 
Just a little recession -- Bob Dole could have really used one. Was that too much to ask? He would have floated to Election Day, thundering, "Clintonomics has failed!" (In four years, please, let's not have any "an" or "on" candidates. No more Reaganomics or Nixonomics or God-knows-who-nomics.) But apparently Mr. Dole's given up, for the word is now that he'll attack the President on the economy anyway. You can't ignore voters' wallets. This will be tough, considering that Mr. Clinton has all the right numbers -- growth, with unemployment and inflation low. So what Mr. Dole will say is that things could be even better, and zero in on the weak spots, like job-security worries and stagnant incomes. And, oh yes: being a politician, he'll surely not forget to suggest tax cuts.
 
MUTUAL FUNDS
 
Rainy Day? What Rainy Day?
 
More money has flooded into stock mutual funds so far this year than in all of 1995 (which, by the way, was a record year). Where does it all go? Straight into this red-hot market, of course. Can't have cash sitting around growing mold. (No, it's not really in bundles of $20's sitting in suitcases in fund offices.) In fact, even with all that money coming in, the cash held by funds is now at the lowest level in nearly two decades: just 6.7 percent. What the cash is for, of course, is to pay investors who want out. So you see what this could mean: a handful of investors want out, the cash vanishes, stocks are sold, the market falls, more investors get scared and want out. Panic. But that can't happen. Not in an election year.
 
NEW YORK TRANSIT
 
One Commute, One Fare
 
It has long been a complaint of New York City commuters. Even with an amazing network of buses and subways, many people simply can't get where they're going on one fare. Thus, the dreaded two-fare commute, a $6-a-day burden that, on a monthly basis, rivals the cost of travel to many a leafy suburb. But last week, the transit authority said that beginning next year, it would offer free transfers between subway and bus. This could cost the city $168 million in revenue, but officials hope some of this will be balanced by more riders. There's a catch: you'll have to start using those fare cards. Metrocards now account for just 6 percent of all fares paid.
 
MONEY
 
Those Voracious A.T.M.'s
 
You've got to be pretty old to remember this, but -- yes, this is scary -- there was a time when, on a Friday, you had to stockpile enough cash to get through the weekend. Ancient, ancient history. A.T.M.'s are everywhere now, open at all hours, their siren calls saying: "C'mon, big spender. Grab a few more $20's." What these modern-day sirens don't say, though, is what fees lurk inside, fees that have been sprouting like sidewalk pennies lately, with surcharges for noncustomers piling on top of banks' own fees. Pressure is mounting for legislation to tame this fee epidemic, with a proposal last week in the House to require banks to post fee warnings by A.T.M.'s and on screen. But that's not enough, some critics say -- people are so addicted to instant cash that being told the fee won't deter them. Those surcharges, they say, should be banned.
 
ON-LINE HEALTH
 
Is Information Treatable?
 
Nonstop radio and TV news. Sports statistics. Up-to-the-second stock quotations. The Internet. Enough already! Information overload! No, wait. There are times when we can't get our fill of the stuff, when, say, we're sick and the doctor hasn't a clue. Then we ask total strangers if they've had the symptoms, scour horoscopes and check crystal balls for new wrinkles. Sounds tailor-made for the data-clogged Internet, doesn't it? Sure enough, on-line health is on a roll. Jim Clark, of Silicon Graphics and Netscape fame, recently started Healtheon, and last week Allina Health Village joined the hordes of sites loaded with anecdotal and clinical data. Yes, you may stumble on the perfect mix of herbs or pills, but a more likely result might be paralysis -- an information overdose that, if untreated, could be fatal.
 
TOBACCO WARS
 
Killer Crackers?
 
In "Thank You for Smoking," his over-the-top satire of the tobacco wars, Christopher Buckley has a tobacco spokesman suggest that Vermont cheddar, that ruthless artery-clogger, kills more people than cigarettes. Meanwhile, back in the real world, Philip Morris Europe has been running ads suggesting that second-hand smoke is less dangerous than eating a biscuit a day. Last week, though, a French court banned the ads, upholding a complaint from the biscuit industry, and ruled that Philip Morris would be fined nearly $200,000 each time the ads appeared. The public, Europe's public health commissioner said, should treat the company's claim "with the scorn it deserves."
 
CYBERSPACE
 
On Line Is Not Enough
 
America Online. The company's name speaks clearly of both its pitfalls and its vast promise. This nation has gone feverishly on line, and the company has cashed in on that, becoming one of the brightest cyberspace stars. But the problem is that as Americans go on line, many find they don't need this escort. They can do it themselves, thanks. So as this all sorts itself out, America Online has taken a wild ride, its stock leaping ahead, then skittering back. Just last week its new president, recruited just four months ago from Federal Express, left abruptly, perhaps because of confusion about where the company is going. A "relaunch of A.O.L." is promised, but it's still not clear how to keep making money from stuff that, for the most part, is out there waiting to be plucked, free.
 
SHAREHOLDER MEETINGS
 
Questions? Don't Be Silly
 
A shareholder meeting. The words conjure a warm, fuzzy image of democracy in action, the just plain folks who own a company sittin' a spell to talk things over. A fantasy even in America, but look at Japan. First, nearly all meetings are held on the same day, so you can't exactly hop from company to company bearing democracy's pollen. And loyal employees, who've arrived at dawn to crowd the front rows, chant a well-rehearsed "Oh, yes!" or "No objection!" no matter what executives on the podium say. Take Sumitomo's meeting last week. The decorum was broken briefly by a shareholder with the temerity to suggest that that $1.8 billion copper loss needed to be dealt with a bit more, well, strenuously. Not to worry. The president and the loyal crowd drowned him out, the board was re-elected, and the gathering was wrapped up in a tidy 40 minutes. Any questions?

HEADLINE: Desert Storms;
For Danger In the Mideast, Just Look Around

BYLINE: By JOHN KIFNER 

 BODY:
JUST when Americans might have thought the Middle East was safe -- at least for oil and Israel, the usual concerns -- there was a sharp, ugly reminder last week of just how unstable the region really is: a huge truck bomb that took 19 American lives. Ironically enough, the explosion was in Dhahran, Saudi Arabia, which six years ago was the main base for the American-led gulf war that undid Iraq's invasion of Kuwait.
At the time, the war seemed part of a chain of events that would change the Middle East forever. The vital oil fields of Kuwait and Saudi Arabia, if not democracy, had been saved. And, in the way events in one part of the Middle East reverberate in another, the Palestine Liberation Organization, having thrown in with Iraq, wound up so weakened that it had to make peace with Israel, heralding a new era. 
 An Arab summit meeting was held in Cairo shortly after the Iraqi invasion and shattered the myth of Arab solidarity (memorably so in a food fight in which the Iraqi delegation hurled lunch at the spotless white robes of the Kuwaitis). It was a myth maintained by ritual gatherings and by fist waving at Israel, with the illusions and diversions only crippling chances for change.
As last week began, Arab leaders gathered again in Cairo to resurrect the myth of Arab solidarity. In doing so, they papered over their sometimes lethal differences, as well as the threats many of the governments face from their own people. But it is those differences that continue to make the region so dangerous and unpredictable.
For centuries, religion has inspired the most violent actions in the Middle East. A Cook's tour from Iran to Algeria, from Turkey to Sudan, would show that militant Islam is the common thread (there is an echo in Israel, with the growing power of its nationalist Orthodoxy).
To pure belief add more mundane factors: poverty, frustration and a demographic time bomb. Throughout much of the region there is a dangerous population bulge of the young, the restless and the volatile. In Saudi Arabia, 58 percent of the population is under 24; in Egypt 57 percent, in Jordan 65 percent.
 
 
 
Afghan Legacy
 Iran is frequently pointed to as the principal sponsor of terrorism, but a more important threat may be the presence of veterans of the Afghan war -- the ex-mujaheddin, or holy warriors, who were backed by the Central Intelligence Agency in one of the last struggles of the cold war. Saudi Arabia helped pay for that war and sent hundreds of fiery-eyed zealots from its puritanical Wahabi sect who, along with other Islamic volunteers, fought with the Afghans and now want to continue their struggle elsewhere. The men who bombed the World Trade Center in New York came from these ranks, as did four Saudis who set off a car bomb that killed five Americans in Riyadh last November. It is called "blowback" in the intelligence community. The four were beheaded last month, and the latest bombing is believed to have been in retribution, aimed not only at the "infidel" Americans but also at the Saudi royal family.
 
 
 
Appearances
 So the tour of the region can start in Saudi Arabia itself, outwardly oil rich and seemingly stable. Unlike America's previous best friend in the Persian Gulf, the Shah of Iran, who tried to secularize and modernize a deeply religious country, the Saudi royal family is deeply entwined with the ulema, the religious leadership. Islamic law is strictly enforced. Indeed, King Fahd's very legitimacy rests on his role as the Sherif of Mecca, protector of Islam's holiest place. The dynasty's founder, Ibn Saud, seized this role with his desert warriors -- the Ikhwan, or brotherhood, of fundamentalist Bedouin -- from the Hashemite family in 1924, charging that they had failed to protect pilgrims and enjoyed a corrupt monopoly on the sale of dried lizards, which are used as a tonic.
But, particularly since American troops arrived to defend the kingdom five years ago and were allowed to stay on, the Government's policy of juggling friendship with the West and fundamentalism at home has come under growing attack. Senior religious scholars have posed a series of open challenges, amid widespread accusations of corruption and, worse, decadence. The criticism has grown as the oil boom that supported a safety net of free education, easy jobs and cheap housing collapsed and the country had to absorb $120 billion in war costs. The Government itself is in disarray since King Fahd suffered a stroke in November, with power now apparently centered in Crown Prince Abdullah.
America's other main supporter in the Arab world is Egypt's President, Hosni Mubarak. Under him, Egypt remains perpetually in ruins and a breeding ground for fundamentalism. Its attempts to industrialize are a shambles, its Government bureaucracy legendary for sloth and inefficiency. Its schools spew out the uneducated into a nonexistent job market and its tiny strip of farmland along the Nile is being gobbled up into jerry-built urban slums that sometimes collapse into the sewage on which they are built. Its Islamic fundamentalists periodically shoot up tourists, the police, Coptic Christians or Government officials and are shot up in return.
Next door to Saudi Arabia, Jordan has joined Egypt as a leading backer -- and hopeful beneficiary -- of the peace with Israel. Six years ago, Saddam Hussein was on posters all over Amman and King Hussein had taken to calling himself the Sherif, the title his family lost to the Saudis (in the Middle East alliances can shift like the proverbial sands). But the King's embrace of Israeli officials and his grief at the funeral of Yitzhak Rabin, the assassinated Israeli Prime Minister, have cost him badly at home. "We are very unhappy with the King," a money changer volunteered to a foreign reporter recently, in an open display that would have been unthinkable in the tightly controlled kingdom of a few years ago. There has even been rioting among the Bedouin, traditionally the King's base of support in a population that is largely Palestinian. Government officials worry, too, about a growing social disparity, with a tiny rich elite living in enormous new mansions surrounded by increasing poverty. The King has accused Syria of smuggling in terrorists to cause trouble, and last week Palestinian guerrillas, apparently coming from Syria, sneaked over the Jordan River and killed three Israeli soldiers.
In Israel, the peace process with the Palestinians has been on hold since the election of Benjamin Netanyahu as Prime Minister. Settlers and the religious right are elated and plan new settlements. Palestinians are frustrated at being kept from jobs in Israel and resentful of the highhanded leaders who with the peace have returned from their headquarters in Tunis.
Keep traveling and it doesn't get much better. Syria and Iraq remain gangster states, each ruthlessly ruled by a small clique from a religious minority -- Alawites in Damascus, Sunnis from the village of Tikrit in Baghdad. In Algeria the military dictatorship that replaced failed socialism is battling Islamic revolutionaries who murder foreigners and liberals. Turkey, where Kemal Ataturk introduced secularism after World War I, is on the brink of getting an Islamic fundamentalist Prime Minister (though in coalition with secularists). By the way, Turkey is also suppressing a Kurdish insurgency in its southeast. The Kurds have been hiding and training in Syria, and the Turks got so fed up, intelligence officials say, that they recently set off a big bomb in Damascus -- one more reason to be wary of rosy forecasts for the Middle East.
