HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE UNIONS;
Citing Abortion Bill Veto, Union Head Rejects Clinton

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
The head of the 100,000-member bricklayers' union has broken ranks with the A.F.L.-C.I.O. and rejected its endorsement of President Clinton by announcing that he will not vote for the President because he vetoed a bill that would have banned a type of late-term abortion.
The president of the International Union of Bricklayers and Allied Craftsmen, John T. Joyce, said the veto so outraged him that he could not support Mr. Clinton even though the President, in his view, would be much better for working people than would Bob Dole, the all-but-certain Republican Presidential nominee. 
 "I can only go so far as my mind and conscience are going to let me go," Mr. Joyce said in explaining why he would not back Mr. Clinton. "But if the Republicans should win, it is going to make life a hell of a lot more difficult for working people."
Although endorsements by unions are not believed to carry the weight they once did, Mr. Joyce, with this move, has sought to bring some influence to bear on the election by raising an issue that the labor movement has steered clear of for years because it is so divisive.
Several union leaders criticized Mr. Joyce, who has a reputation as a iconoclastic Catholic intellectual, saying he was wrong to use a single issue as a litmus test, when in their view labor's agenda would be severely set back if Republicans win the White House and the Congress. They feared that Mr. Joyce's stand would undermine the A.F.L.-C.I.O.'s $35-million education and get-out-the-vote effort aimed principally at defeating Republicans.
At the same time, some labor leaders praised Mr. Joyce for having the courage to speak his conscience and stray from the official line.
In April, Mr. Clinton vetoed a bill barring a type of abortion in which a fetus is partly extracted feet first and its brain is then suctioned out to allow the head to pass through the birth canal. Abortion opponents condemned the procedure as particularly gruesome, but Mr. Clinton opposed the bill because it did not include an exception to allow such abortions to avoid serious health consequences for the woman.
In an article to appear in the July 12 issue of Commonweal, the liberal Catholic magazine, Mr. Joyce explained that as a result of the Clinton veto he, for the first time in his life, found his personal convictions clashing with "the immediate economic needs of workers."
After attacking Mr. Dole for taking anti-union positions, he said he still could not back Mr. Clinton. "If I support someone who does not take every step humanly possible to prevent abortions, I practically condone such actions, and, to some degree, become complicit in the act of killing unborn human beings," he wrote.
Mr. Joyce said he would not vote for Mr. Clinton or Mr. Dole. He said he was unsure whether his union would also refuse to back Mr. Clinton, although he said that if his union had a much larger number of women it might take a different position from his.
In the Commonweal article, Mr. Joyce also attacked Mr. Dole. "The election of Bob Dole as President and the return of a Republican Congress will be ruinous for working people," he wrote.
Gerald W. McEntee, president of the American Federation of State County and Municipal Employees, said he was disappointed by Mr. Joyce's position during a campaign the A.F.L.-C.I.O. sees as pivotal for unions.
"What the labor movement is trying to do is get away from single-issue litmus tests," said Mr. McEntee, who is overseeing the A.F.L.-C.I.O.'s political efforts.
Karen Nussbaum, the head of the A.F.L.-C.I.O.'s new Women's Department, said, "The position of the A.F.L. on abortion and reproductive rights is that we don't take a position as an institution, but we refer the matter to our affiliate unions." Geoffrey Garin, a pollster, said his surveys showed that union members' views on abortion mirrored those of the nation at large.
"By and large union leaders have credibility with their members on economic issues and the well-being of families," he said. "But union members do not necessarily want or expect union leaders to take a position on issues, like abortion or gun control, where there's no obvious connection to their lives as a union member."

HEADLINE: Hanoi Struggles to Reconcile Past and Future

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS 

DATELINE: HANOI, Vietnam, June 29

 BODY:
The biggest applause of the day today went to the most enthusiastic speaker at Vietnam's Communist Party Congress, Jarvis Tyner, vice chairman of the American Communist Party.
"Long live proletarian internationalism!" he cried out. "Long live the unbreakable friendship between the Vietnamese Communist Party and the Communist Party of the United States!" 
 His optimism seemed to offer a release for the Vietnamese delegates, still struggling after months of debate to redefine their party's role in a rapidly liberalizing economy, still unable to accomplish a transition to a new generation of leaders.
As a succession of foreign visitors took the floor beneath giant portraits of Marx and Lenin on the second day of the four-day congress, one Vietnamese delegate, Do Phuong, told reporters that a new, considerably younger Central Committee would be elected this weekend.
But Mr. Phuong, who as director general of the official Vietnam News Agency has become a de facto spokesman for the gathering, acknowledged that the party had failed to agree on changes at the top.
Though their replacements are ready to step in, he said, the ruling triumvirate of septuagenarian leaders -- the party's General Secretary, Do Muoi; its Prime Minister, Vo Van Kiet, and its President, Le Duc Anh -- will remain in place, perhaps for another year or two.
"At first," he said, "they wanted to retire. But after some discussions it was decided that this was not the time to make the change."
Mr. Phuong said the situation could change in the coming months. But the men likely to move up in the hierarchy, he added, include the liberal Mayor of Ho Chi Minh City, Troung Tan Sang, 47, and another rising star, Nong Duc Manh, 56, the chairman of the National Assembly.
He also cited two deputy prime ministers in their 50's, Tran Duc Luong and Pham Van Khai.
Mr. Phuong said the recent party discussions included debate over the pace of liberalization, a process that began 10 years ago and has accelerated over the last five years. During this period, growth has begun to transform Vietnam in ways that make some party members uneasy.
The leadership has decided to continue its attempt at an economic balancing act, aiming for a growth rate of 10 percent a year while continuing to accord primacy to less productive state-run enterprises.
A senior Western diplomat said this insistence on maintaining central control could make it difficult to meet targets for growth.
"They fear that if they give the private sector more room, it will become a political force," he said. "But you cannot maintain a 10 percent growth rate without very, very skillful management of the economy."
Caught in this dilemma, he said, the congress seems to want to pause for economic and political consolidation after years of rapid change.
Since 1990, when outside investment in Vietnam was negligible, foreign companies have committed $20 billion in investments. Taking advantage of Vietnam's new policies, some 25,000 foreign enterprises have registered to do business here.
"They are now dependent on foreign investment and foreign trade," the diplomat said. "But they want to retain the party system. They all wonder, 'Can we move with speed and protect our system?' "
While the leadership wrestled with these problems, it was the outsiders like Mr. Jarvis, and some of the old guard from the war years, who voiced the most unfettered idealism.
Mai Chi To, a former interior minister and brother of Le Duc To, who negotiated for peace in Paris with Henry Kissinger, insisted to foreign reporters that Communism was the wave of the future.
"It is the best system in the world," he said.
For Nikolai Bindyukov, the lone visitor from the resurgent but impoverished Russian Communist Party, the congress -- with its red bunting, heroic slogans and interminable speeches -- brought nostalgia "for the kind and good days of the past."
"But nostalgia is nostalgia," he said, as philosophical as Mr. Jarvis was buoyant. "We have to live with the political realities of today."

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE REPUBLICAN;
Dole Makes Appeal to Patriotism in Texas

BYLINE: By KATHARINE Q. SEELYE 

DATELINE: DALLAS, June 29

 BODY:
With his veteran's cap tilted across his brow, Bob Dole glorified America today in a tribute to honor, duty and country that highlighted his own military service and, by omission, President Clinton's lack of it.
Addressing the Texas convention of the Veterans of Foreign Wars, Mr. Dole said that America's emergence as a world power in World War II "left its mark on people like Lieutenant John F. Kennedy, Lieutenant Richard Nixon and Lieutenant George Bush. It created a tradition of American leadership, shared by Republicans and Democrats." 
 Mr. Dole, 72, who earned two Purple Hearts and a Bronze Star as a second lieutenant in Italy in World War II, added: "I want to preserve that legacy as President of the United States."
These were familiar themes for Mr. Dole, the likely Republican Presidential nominee, but Texas is an important place for him to underscore them as he seeks to bolster his candidacy. The state has 32 of the 270 electoral votes needed to win the Presidency in November.
He did not overtly criticize the President today because Mr. Clinton has been overseas, Nelson Warfield, Mr. Dole's press secretary, said. The President has been at a summit of the Group of Seven industrial democracies and Russia in Lyon, France.
In his most overt partisan reference, Mr. Dole said: "There's nothing flashy about me. I'm so dull, I hear someone fell asleep at the White House reading my F.B.I. files."
Like Utah and Arizona, Texas should almost automatically be in the Republican column, and most analysts predict that Texas will eventually fall to Mr. Dole. One of the most conservative states, Texas has not voted for a Democratic Presidential candidate since 1976. And it has evinced a deep antipathy toward President Clinton.
But the Texas Poll shows Mr. Dole's lead has narrowed. In March, he was ahead of Mr. Clinton by 56 percent to 40 percent. A week ago, that margin had slipped to a statistical dead heat.
"He has to reverse this pervasive mood of defeatism," Richard Murray, a professor of political science at the University of Houston, said. "He needs to convey that he thinks he can win and he will put together a campaign that can do it. A lot of Texans don't think it will be there, and that's why this intraparty fighting has broken out," he said, referring to last week's divisive state convention, in which many of Mr. Dole's delegates were knocked off the slate for the party's national convention because they did not take a sufficiently hard line against abortion.
"If Dole can't get his act together in Texas, he might as well go fishing," said George Christian, press secretary to Lyndon B. Johnson and a longtime Democratic political adviser. "If I were predicting, I'd say Dole is going to carry Texas, but it's not a lay-down as it was during the Bush years."
Mr. Dole's visit is his second to Texas since winning enough delegates to claim his party's nomination in March. But on Friday, his visit included few public appearances. Mr. Dole attended two private fund-raising events closed to reporters and spent a brief period with children in physical therapy at the Texas Children's Hospital, an event were he was joined by his just-appointed Texas chairman, Gov. George W. Bush Jr., who, known for being a natural campaigner, planted a kiss on a little girl while Mr. Dole looked on.
The Governor, a loyal Republican, told reporters that it did not matter that Mr. Dole had said little at the event. Rather, he said, the images of his presence at the hospital sent a powerful message. Asked what that message was, since Mr. Dole had made no comments, Mr. Bush said it was this: "I'm in Houston, and this is an important part of the economy."
There was a strong military flavor to Mr. Dole's speech here today. Referring to the killing of American soldiers in Saudi Arabia last week, Mr. Dole said, "I would say to those -- and we may know some of those -- who sort of look down on the military, who say we spend too much on defense, it takes maybe a tragedy like this for those people to understand what liberty and freedom and duty and honor and God and country are all about."

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
Away From the Football Field, 'Reggie Lives the Life He Preaches'

BYLINE: By DAVE ANDERSON 

 BODY:
HARDLY a day passes without a name athlete on the sports blotter. Illegal drugs. Guns. Sexual assault. Spousal abuse. Alcohol abuse. Driving under the influence.
Most athletes, like most people, are solid citizens, but by the nature of what constitutes news, the rotten apples' names are in headlines, their faces on television. More often than not, it seems, the rotten apples are the familiar names. 
 And then there's Reggie White.
He's not just the Green Bay Packers' defensive end, he's an ordained minister. He's not just the National Football League's career sack leader, he's the voice behind a campaign that could contribute as much as $8 million from N.F.L. players to help rebuild America's burned black churches.
"It just shows," he has said of the burned churches, "we've got problems we don't want to believe we have."
One of the burned churches is his church, the Inner City Community Church in Knoxville, Tenn., where he is the associate pastor, where he preached occasionally before flames lighted up the snowy darkness there Jan. 8.
"I believe that out of every bad situation, something great will happen," he has said. "When my church burned, it showed me that God is calling me to have an impact, to rally people from different ethnic backgrounds."
Of the $1.2 million required to rebuild his church, about $230,000 has been raised, mostly from predominantly white Packer fans in Wisconsin.
"Reggie lives the life he preaches about," said the Rev. David Upton, the Inner City pastor. "He's always reached out to help other people."
Now 34, White preached in high school in Chattanooga and at the University of Tennessee, where he was an all-American. In his years with the Eagles, he preached on Philadelphia street corners to teen-agers: stay in school, stay off drugs.
With a four-year, $19 million contract, the 6-foot-5-inch, 300-pound All-Pro has put his money where his mouth is. Two years ago, he helped organize the Community Development Bank in Knoxville for those who couldn't qualify for loans.
"To get it going," Upton said, "Reggie put up $1 million of his own."
Over the last 18 months, the despicable burning of some three dozen black churches, primarily in the South, has created the largest Federal arson investigation in the nation's history: nearly 250 Federal Bureau of Investigation agents along with another 250 state troopers and local police, some $21.5 million in additional funding.
And now pro football's Minister of Defense has challenged N.F.L. players to join the cause.
"Reggie mentioned this idea to me before his church was burned," said Gene Upshaw, the executive director of the N.F.L. Players Association. "He remembered how we did the same thing for the United Negro College Fund a few years ago. The players would sign over their $5,000 rebate checks from the union."
Each union member's annual dues are $5,000, but if licensing revenue pays the union's costs, all or part of that $5,000 is returned.
"I'll talk about it to the players, we'll send out letters, the player rep on each team will discuss it," Upshaw said. "The potential is $8 million for a foundation that would administer the money. This problem of churches being burned is not going away."
Upshaw remembered his Baptist church in Robstown, Tex., a few hours from the Mexican border.
"It was the glue to the community," he said. "Every Sunday you went to church all day. You had Sunday school at 9 in the morning, a worship and a sermon at 11, a service at 2 in the afternoon, another service at 6, then a worship and sometimes another sermon at 7:30. And if you weren't there, everybody knew it."
And when churches burn, everybody knows it.
"People always ask me," Reggie White has said, " 'As a minister, how do you justify the violence in football?' But I tell them: 'Football's not a violent game, football is an aggressive game. Violence is people shooting other people in the street.' "
Or people burning other people's churches.

HEADLINE: Columbia Trying to Show What a Gem It Is

BYLINE: By JANNY SCOTT 

 BODY:
Not long after Emily Lloyd decided to leave New York City government to become a vice president of Columbia University, she began noticing how rarely Columbia seemed to surface on the city's psychic radar screen.
It was not like Harvard, which she remembered reading and hearing about constantly in the years she had spent in Boston, or even like the University of Pennsylvania as she recalled it from her Philadelphia days. 
 And when Ms. Lloyd started work in July 1994 and began making the rounds of Columbia trustees, faculty and alumni, she kept hearing the same complaint: interesting things were happening at Columbia, but who knew?
Now Columbia, arguably the most respected educational institution in New York City and its only Ivy League school, has decided it needs to pump up its public profile and draw attention to itself, especially within the city.
So it has dismissed the two top administrators who had served for years as the guardians of the university's image and replaced them with a former White House speechwriter with Hollywood experience.
To assist him, Columbia has signed up Howard J. Rubenstein, whose public-relations company attends to the image of the likes of George M. Steinbrenner 3d and Kathie Lee Gifford from his office 30 stories above midtown Manhattan.
The well-connected Mr. Rubenstein can now be found squiring Columbia's President, George Rupp, around the city, lining up audiences with editors and speaking engagements with civic and business groups.
Mr. Rubenstein's company has also enlisted Richard Valeriani, a former correspondent for NBC News, to "media train" Columbia scientists in how to speak, sit and even dress when talking to reporters.
In addition, the newly overhauled public affairs office is upgrading the choreography of Columbia's media events, calculating clever ways to attract the attention of reporters, especially television reporters.
When a group at Columbia began planning a news conference about a meeting of foreign environmental leaders, Allen Stone, the new public-affairs chief, had three suggestions: Center the news conference around the release of a document, bring in children (to give the event life) and hold the briefing in the morning (when reporters and television cameras are most likely to show up).
So, one May morning, 45 fifth and sixth graders from the nearby Bank Street School who had been studying conservation trooped across Broadway, onto the Columbia campus and up the Low Memorial Library steps. They settled in opposite environmental officials from places like Brazil and China, who were arrayed stiffly behind a long table to unveil "the Morningside Declaration."
As expected, the video cameras locked instantly onto the children's faces and stayed there throughout much of the briefing, which was turned over largely to the students' questions.
The lengths to which Columbia is going to attract attention illustrate a fact of life for universities today: the intense competitive and financial pressures brought on, in part, by waning government support.
In response, universities are vying to draw attention to their research and other programs in hopes of catching the eye of donors, funding agencies, potential students, even professors in the market for jobs.
Columbia's efforts also show the degree to which it let slide the business of self-promotion at a time when many schools, including New York University, 110 blocks south, were turning it into a science.
And while N.Y.U. embraced New York City in a kind of bearhug, Columbia had long seemed ambivalent about its hometown. Only in recent years has it seemed willing to treat the city as an asset more than a liability.
Dr. Rupp, not exactly a familiar fixture in the city, recalled recently how a Taiwanese woman had recognized him in the airport in Taipei after seeing his photograph in a local newspaper.
"In many ways, I think Columbia's strengths are appreciated more, well, in Tokyo or Taipei or Seoul than they are in New York," he said. "And that's a failure of communication, I guess."
Columbia is, after all, a significant force in New York City -- one of its biggest employers, a major property owner, a powerful stimulator of the economy, a health-care provider, a center of research on many aspects of city life.
When the university set about three years ago to catalogue the ways in which it contributes to New York, it ended up publishing a book, "Columbia and the City," that was 170 pages long.
Yet many people, including some inside Columbia, feel that its presence in the city is insufficiently felt and that for that reason neither the city nor Columbia take full advantage of their connection.
Part of the problem is geographic; Columbia falls outside the orbit of many New Yorkers. And, as Kenneth T. Jackson, chairman of the history department, put it: "New York is so big. Nobody dominates New York."
But Columbia has also been reticent about making its virtues more widely known, in part out of a belief that its successes should speak for themselves.
For years, the public-information office has been a modest operation -- a half-dozen people answering media queries and cranking out news releases on matters like appointments, prizes and actions by the trustees.
"We have had a very traditional approach to what we tell the world about ourselves," said Ms. Lloyd, executive vice president for administration. "If people ask, we are forthcoming. But if they don't ask, we don't go out and beat our drum because there is something unseemly about it."
Dr. Rupp suspects there was another reason, too. He believes Columbia developed "a defensive orientation" after 1968, when students seized the president's office, went on strike and severely disrupted the campus for six weeks.
Dr. Rupp, who found a panic button under the president's desk when he arrived from Rice University in Houston in 1993, said the attitude had been, "The best news was no news at all, just so we didn't get creamed the way Columbia got creamed in 1968."
When he proposed in 1993 to "exorcise the ghost of '68" by initiating talks with community groups in Harlem, he says, the two administrators he has since dismissed quickly shot back, "What are the downsides?"
"Their immediate reaction was, 'We shouldn't get exposed, we shouldn't take a risk,' " Dr. Rupp said. "So I think the communications strategy was designed to be self-protective."
But the times were changing. Other schools have become more enterprising about what they call "getting out the message," raising their profile in the eyes of prospective students, faculty, alumni, businesses and possible donors.
Take Washington University in St. Louis, a major research institution. Ten times a year, it sends out to 150 television stations around the nation videotaped reports about ongoing research, put together by a producer hired from a local station.
It dispatches radio news stories to stations worldwide, op-ed articles to newspapers and four fully executed feature stories every month to 1,200 print reporters and editors.
The university offers up professors to comment on public events, publishes a directory of faculty experts and goes out of its way to sponsor high-profile events, such as a Presidential debate this fall.
N.Y.U., too, has been aggressive. Under John Brademas, who spent 22 years in Congress before becoming N.Y.U.'s president, and now under his successor, L. Jay Oliva, the press office has prided itself on being user-friendly.
When the Democratic National Convention was held in New York City in 1992, the N.Y.U. administration recognized an opportunity and rounded up the names of 35 faculty members whose expertise might be useful to journalists.
Then Dr. Oliva invited them to a reception and explained why their cooperation with the media could be important to the school. The press office took the phone numbers at which they could be reached during the convention.
The efforts, aided by Mr. Rubenstein, who worked for N.Y.U. for years, seem to have paid off. The press office estimates that references to N.Y.U. appear in publications, excluding New York papers, more than 10,000 times a year.
The publicity has not gone unnoticed uptown -- in particular, an article on the front page of The New York Times in March 1995 describing how N.Y.U. had used fund-raising to rebuild itself from a mediocre school to a much better one.
"I heard from a lot of Columbia people, 'Why the hell is this story about N.Y.U. . . .?' " recalled Dr. Rupp, who contends that Columbia had raised more money during the same period "and invested it more wisely."
"That did irritate me," he said. "And it also got the goat of a number of Columbia people who let me know that they thought that, since we are a better institution, we ought to get at least reasonably comparable press notices."
Within months, a committee headed by Ms. Lloyd recommended that the two top administrators in charge of government and public affairs be replaced by Mr. Stone, a speechwriter for President Clinton.
A lawyer, former community organizer, longtime Congressional committee staff member, Presidential campaign aide and aspiring scriptwriter, Mr. Stone, 51, went to work last fall reinventing Columbia's approach to communications.
As for Mr. Rubenstein, N.Y.U. declined to renew his contract after a 20-year relationship -- right around the time that he went to work for Columbia. Purely coincidental, according to N.Y.U.
"While there was a time that we needed consultants, we don't need them now," said Naomi Levine, the senior vice president for external relations at N.Y.U. "The real way to get press is the quality of the institution."

HEADLINE: Immutability vs. Adaptability;
An Environmentalist Continues Her Fight

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN 

 BODY:
It has been more than a decade since Marcy Benstock slew her dragon, Westway, the superhighway, park and luxury real estate development that was to be built on land dumped into the Hudson River. It is an achievement the historian Robert Caro calls "one of the great victories in the history of New York in the 20th century."
The dragon is dead and buried, but the fight goes on. The enemy is now a park -- an emerald sliver to replace five miles of industrial riverfront desolation -- that many of Ms. Benstock's allies in the Westway wars favor. Some of them wonder why she can't just declare victory and move on. 
 "She's now fighting her friends," said State Senator Franz L. Leichter of Manhattan, who has been allied with Ms. Benstock in many battles. "She's opposing the very things we always thought she was for. In a certain sense, it's a personal tragedy."
In Manhattan's leftish political circles, Ms. Benstock's soft, clear voice and take-no-prisoners intensity made her the personification of the Westway opposition and of a distinctively New York style of environmental activism. Now she has become a more ambiguous symbol, of the power of opposition as an end in itself.
"It would be nice to have her on your side," said former Mayor Edward I. Koch, who said that he thinks the demise of Westway cost the city billions of dollars in economic development.
For Ms. Benstock, being a living symbol has had a price.
She works 12-hour days, has never married and lives on the $25,000 a year she earns as sole employee of the Clean Air Campaign, the environmental group she founded. But friends insist she is not one-dimensional. They describe her as warm, funny and there when they need her. One friend, Bunny Gabel, recalls repeated visits Ms. Benstock made to her and her new baby in the hospital at the height of the Westway wars.
But it remains a fact that friends feel the need to say Ms. Benstock is more than her cause. "She has other things she cares about and they are the normal human concerns," said Dr. Harold Varmus, now the director of the National Institutes of Health and a friend of Ms. Benstock's since she was 19. "She is a complex human being with tremendous attractiveness from many points of view."
But her intensity is a round-the-clock proposition, indicated by her habit of sometimes grabbing the phone -- she has no secretary, and it might be the Governor -- and shouting, "Don't bother me! I'm too busy!" Her knowledge is such that public officials call her for information on Hudson River issues. Even late at night she takes the subway rather than cabs, reflecting her small income as much as her frequently expressed commitment to mass transit.
"Commitment keeps you going," said Ms. Benstock a Radcliffe graduate once known as the "soot lady" for organizing West Siders to fight air pollution in the early 1970's. This grew into the Clean Air Campaign, which now has 600 members.
Ms. Benstock, who has strands of gray in her jet-black hair and says she is over 50, comes across as an articulate businesswoman. She giggles before she gets to her own punch lines. But her view of the world is as dark as midnight: Powerful businessmen use campaign contributions to push needless, environmentally deadly projects, then trumpet them with sophisticated public relations. To Ms. Benstock, governments are rife with secret agendas, slush funds and blind arrogance.
Her contention is that the Hudson River park, the latest in a long chain of park proposals, is the subsidiary of a public authority, the Empire State Development Corporation, which can do anything it wants in the way of development despite the fact that the subsidiary has its own board. Sometimes she accuses her enemies of plotting to pave the river, other times of planning to build pyramids there. The point, she insists, is to preserve it as is. In that case, delay can equal victory.
"Every year that the critical Hudson River is left in its natural state is another year for this extraordinary East Coast ecosystem," she has said.
Issues Shift,
But Fight Continues
Her persistence provokes some people to use words like saintly to describe her, and others, words like crazy. Can Marcy Benstock compromise and work toward a solution? Critics, even friendly ones, suggest that the fight-to-the-death approach of 1960's environmentalism is no longer relevant, particularly since the issues along the waterfront may have basically shifted against big development. She counters that the fight is the same, the difference being that her big-bucks enemies have become stealthier.
The proposed Hudson River park is meant to be for the 21st century what Central Park was for the 19th: a place of refuge and a spark to development in adjacent neighborhoods. Planners envision the refashioning of 13 rotting piers into recreational and cultural meccas that would include ballfields, playgrounds, boating facilities, lawns, fountains and a broad bikeway, all in an area presently dominated by razor ribbon.
Moreover, planners say that private concessions at three points comprising less than 10 percent of the park's 160 surface acres will enable the park to pay for itself at a time when state and city parks agencies are strapped for cash. This vision is sufficiently appealing to have struck some longtime Benstock allies as reason to consider dropping their opposition to development along the Hudson.
"There are many people who will less automatically agree with what she says," said Richard N. Gottfried, an Assemblyman representing the West Side who regards Ms. Benstock as "a genuine civic hero."
One of those people is Albert Butzel, the lead attorney in the Westway battle, who has been meeting with environmentalists to persuade them to support the park. "There's not as much difference as she thinks between what she would want from a West Side park and what's presented in that plan," Mr. Butzel said. But in two hours recently spent with Ms. Benstock, he could not sway her.
The dilemma is common among advocates, said Gene Russianoff, the senior attorney for the New York Public Interest Research Group and a Benstock ally. "What percentage of the loaf are you willing to take?" he asked.
Of course, many of Ms. Benstock's detractors are part of the power elite she has spent a lifetime fighting. "People who blame her are selfish pigs anyway," said Bill Bowser, president of the West Village Committee, a group he helped the urbanist Jane Jacobs establish more than 40 years ago to beat back a proposed expressway smack through Greenwich Village.
"Marcy is our modern-day Jane Jacobs," he declared.
Others make a similar point in less flattering terms. "There is a value in someone being a cutting-edge fanatic," allowed Ross Graham, a West Side community advocate who opposed Westway but supports the new park.
A So-So Park
Or No Park at All?
But this is a touchy time. Although Gov. George E. Pataki's top economic advisers have endorsed the park and released a long-embargoed preliminary plan, the Governor has yet to agree to spend $100 million over the next 12 years. That would match the amount the city has already budgeted for the park, which is estimated to cost between $275 million and $300 million.
Park advocates are thus hoping that Mr. Pataki will approve the expenditure in a forthcoming environmental bond issue or in about 18 months when the environmental impact statement is done. On the other side, Ms. Benstock hopes the Governor rejects the park and reallocates money from other highway projects to do some simple landscaping and build a bike path.
Opponents, who already have three lawsuits pending against the park, can fight in environmental hearings, city land use hearings and in court. But as they do this, city and state agencies are already nibbling away at what would be the park by agreeing to pave land intended for grass at Chelsea Piers and elsewhere, asking for development proposals for other piers and making long-term plans for parking garbage trucks on one of them.
"In another six or eight months, the park is not going to be able to be constructed the way it was intended to be," said Peter Rothschild, a landscape architect who worked on the plan.
Ms. Benstock responds that no park is better than a bad park, and insists her back-to-basics alternative could be done in less than half the time at a fraction of the cost. She says she is waging the same fight she has fought for nearly a quarter of a century.
"The principles we fought Westway over are the same principles we hold today," she said, as she sat beneath towering piles of documents in her modest office near City Hall. "You either work for principles you believe are right or you don't."
Ms. Benstock grew up in Buffalo, and as a girl, enjoyed hiking in the woods. She recalls feeling environmental outrage for the first time at age 12 when a copper mine polluted the lake at a camp she attended in Ontario. Her role model was her generous grandfather, whom she called Mr. B and with whom she spent almost every Sunday puttering about the laundry he owned. He paid for her college education. "Mr. B was the world's most responsible person," she said.
At Radcliffe, she remembers being "a dreamy English major." She came to New York and got a job as a copy editor for the Book of Knowledge encyclopedia. She found an apartment on West 100th Street where she still lives. She became fascinated with studying people's faces in subway cars. She began to read a newspaper for the first time.
Linda Davidoff, who was briefly her roommate and went on to become president of the Parks Council, remembers her as nonpolitical but socially concerned. She said Ms. Benstock regularly read literature to a blind person in a nearby welfare hotel. She became interested in her block association and "went to a march or two."
A Mission
Founded in Soot
That, Ms. Benstock said, was "a drifting, searching" period. There were bouts of unemployment and a series of small editing jobs. She earned a master's degree in economics from the New School for Social Research, specializing in economic development. For two years, she left New York to work for Ralph Nader in Washington. There, she helped write a book on water pollution.
She returned to New York to discover soot all over her apartment -- on books, windowsills, everywhere. She got a small grant from the Fund for the City of New York to study why this happened and find ways to stop it. A first step was to get the city to agree to conduct an anti-pollution class for building superintendents if she could recruit 25. She got 114.
With volunteers, she conducted a survey that found 700 oil burners and 300 incinerators on the West Side were out of compliance with the city's clean-air code. After two years, soot was reduced 35 percent over a 200-block area. "It was a great lesson on what could be accomplished if ordinary citizens worked together," she said.
She says her opposition to Westway was a logical next step in her clean-air battle, because it would have meant greatly increased auto emissions. Other issues came later, from the health of the Hudson's striped bass to the possibility of using Westway funds for mass transit. Ms. Benstock honed her specialty: distibuting mountains of well-organized information, often typed single-space on both sides of the page to save paper.
The result of the efforts of Ms. Benstock and hundreds of others was that in 1985 Mayor Koch and Governor Mario M. Cuomo threw in the towel and asked Washington to transfer more than $1 billion in Westway appropriations to mass transit. "She was virtually intractable," said Mr. Cuomo, who wrote her a congratulatory note.
Ms. Benstock has made enemies. Edward Kirkland, a member of Community Board 4 and a supporter of the original Westway, calls her "a disingenuous and dishonest opponent." Tom Fox, first president of the Hudson River Park Conservancy, which was formed to manage the new park, calls her a "zealot" and "dictator."
Friends say such attacks come with the territory. "Marcy can take a lot of heat and a lot of abuse," said Deborah J. Glick, a Manhattan assemblywoman who is Ms. Benstock's closest political ally. "This is a tough fight, not because we're wrong, but because there's a lot of money at stake."
Many of today's comments about "the woman who killed the big road" tend to be softer, but somehow more troubling. People wonder why she has repeatedly refused to accept legislation that would protect the Hudson, on the grounds that the protection was not absolute. They point out that she won a slew of awards from environmental and civic groups a decade ago, and virtually none since then.
"The great crusaders sometimes become tired and frayed," said Ms. Davidoff, who is now working on Ruth Messinger's mayoral campaign. "What's the personal cost of being Joan of Arc all your life?"

HEADLINE: At Summit Talks, Serbs Are Pressed on Ouster of Leader

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: LYONS, France, June 29

 BODY:
President Clinton and seven other international leaders threatened Serbia today with eventual reimposition of United Nations economic sanctions if it did not use its influence to press Radovan Karadzic, the Bosnian Serb leader indicted on charges of war crimes, to step down from political office.
The leaders set no deadline for Dr. Karadzic to step down, but they made it clear that he had to do so well before the elections to choose new leaders in all parts of Bosnia on Sept. 14. 
 Although they have been pressuring Serbia for months to use its influence to get Dr. Karadzic and the indicted Bosnian Serb military commander, Gen. Ratko Mladic, to step aside, today's explicit threat carried a new sense of urgency.
Carl Bildt, the international civilian coordinator for Bosnia, came here asking the leaders to set a Monday deadline for Dr. Karadzic's resignation, which is called for under the Bosnia peace agreement that was negotiated in Dayton, Ohio, last November.
The leaders of the Group of Seven industrialized democracies, joined at their summit meeting by Prime Minister Viktor S. Chernomyrdin of Russia, said they were ready to consider reimposing sanctions on any party to insure compliance with the Dayton accords, but did not explicitly endorse Mr. Bildt's deadline.
"We will support appropriate action by Mr. Bildt, though I have to say we have no information that there is a Monday deadline," Mr. Clinton said today at an outdoor news conference.
But a German diplomat said the expectation was that Dr. Karadzic would step down over the weekend, and Prime Minister John Major of Britain said, "By Monday, we will hear something from Mr. Karadzic; otherwise swift action will follow afterwards."
United Nations sanctions banning all trade with Serbia were lifted after Belgrade agreed to the peace accord last November, but similar restrictions on trade with the Bosnian Serbs remained in place until February.
Mr. Clinton said that both Mr. Bildt and the commander of the NATO-led peacekeeping force in Bosnia had authority under the Dayton accords to recommend a reimposition of sanctions.
Leaders of the seven countries -- France, Italy, Germany, Britain, the United States, Canada and Japan -- pledged to contribute "substantially" to preparations for the Sept. 14 elections in Bosnia by helping to bolster independent news media there and by sending 2,000 observers to insure that the vote is free and fair. They also urged the NATO-led peacekeepers to increase their activities to provide the atmosphere of safety and security the electoral process needs.
Earlier, they had condemned the bombing on Tuesday that killed 19 United States Air Force personnel in Dhahran, Saudi Arabia, and today Mr. Clinton announced that Wayne A. Downing, a retired Army general and former commander in chief of the United States Special Operations Command, would lead an investigation and make recommendations within 45 days on how to prevent further attacks like it.
The President and his wife, Hillary Rodham Clinton, then flew to Paris late this afternoon for dinner with President Jacques Chirac and his wife, Bernadette, before flying on to Florida for memorial ceremonies for the victims of the Dhahran bombing.
Before a meeting with the United Nations Secretary General, Boutros Boutros-Ghali, whose re-election Mr. Clinton opposes, and heads of three other international organizations here, the President joined in a pledge of financial and political support for the United Nations.
All eight leaders promised "scrupulous respect by member states for their financial obligations" to the United Nations, including American payment of hundreds of millions of dollars in back dues. But they also called for "a more equitable scale of contributions," something long called for by the United States, which now has to pay a quarter of the organization's budget, more than any other country.
Mr. Clinton's spokesman, Michael D. McCurry, described the atmosphere in the leaders' meeting with the beleaguered Mr. Boutros-Ghali as "correct and dignified." Mr. Chirac and the German Foreign Minister, Klaus Kinkel, both said today that all the European leaders present were strongly supportive of Mr. Boutros-Gahli's re-election.
All the leaders came here to do was talk, and mostly there was little beyond broad, droning declarations to show for the three days of meetings that took place in the Museum of Contemporary Art here, a building designed by the Italian architect Renzo Piano set in a park on the banks of the Rhone River that thousands of police sealed off from the public for the duration.
On Thursday, the leaders condemned the Dhahran bombing, and called a conference in Paris for mid-July to work out specific measures governments can take to combat terrorism more effectively.
If Mr. Clinton expected to get his European allies to agree to break off trade and diplomatic relations with Iran, which the United States regards as a leading source of terrorism, he succeeded only in getting them to join in calling on Iran to stop supporting "extremist groups that are seeking to destroy the peace process in the Middle East and to destabilize the region."
The summit leaders asked Iran to reject terrorism and stop endorsing threats to the life of the British author Salman Rushdie, who is under an Islamic sentence of death for writing a novel that Iranian religious leaders considered blasphemous.
On the rest of the Middle East, the leaders had little to say beyond urging Syria and Israel to resume negotiations on a peace agreement and all parties to live up to accords reached before momentum stalled over security concerns.
"We note the preeminence of the theme of security in Israel's recent election campaign," their statement said. "We are convinced that the security of all people of the region can eventually be achieved only through comprehensive, equitable and lasting peace."
President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia had been invited to come to the last two days of the summit gathering, but stayed home to campaign for the second round of his country's presidential election on Wednesday and then was reported to have fallen ill with laryngitis.
Mr. Clinton said today that Mr. Chernomyrdin had reported that Mr. Yeltsin was down with a sore throat. "Something that I can identify with in a campaign year," the President added.

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Clinton signed autographs for children yesterday after the Group of Seven summit meeting in Lyons, France, where sanctions were threatened if the Bosnian Serb leader, Radovan Karadzic, did not resign. (Associated Press)

SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 5; Foreign Desk

HEADLINE: Saudi Kingdom Shows Cracks, U.S. Aides Fear

BYLINE: The following article is based on reporting by Elaine Sciolino, Jeff Gerth and Douglas Jehl and was written by Ms. Sciolino. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
When a bomb struck Saudi Arabia's National Guard headquarters last November, killing five Americans, King Fahd was thunderstruck. "Catastrophes are coming at us one after the other," he confided to a close associate.
Two weeks later, the King experienced a catastrophe of his own: he suffered a debilitating stroke, forcing the royal family to grapple with the issue of who will rule the country. 
 Until recently, the United States took for granted the stability of its most strategic Arab ally. But a decade of overspending, the rise of a militant domestic opposition and uncertainties about the royal succession have opened new cracks in the House of Saud.
This week's catastrophe -- the truck bombing that killed 19 American military personnel in Dhahran -- deepened the worries in Washington about the future of the world's largest oil producer and raised hard questions about how the United States can best protect its enormous stake in Saudi Arabia.
"There has been an unwillingness to confront the Saudis," one senior Administration official said. "We all know they're in transition, but no one wants to go to them and say that there is a danger in the kingdom and ask, 'Who's in charge?' An episode like the bombing forces you to think hard about doing these things."
There are limits to what the United States feels it can do to effect change in the country.
Greater tolerance of political and religious voices, an American goal elsewhere in the world, might open the door to broader unrest. The dispatch of additional American troops, either to improve security at American bases or to help strengthen the Government, could prove counterproductive in a country where much of the population views foreigners with suspicion.
For the moment, the Clinton Administration's official policy is to refrain from putting pressure on the royal family.
In an interview shortly before he left for Saudi Arabia, Defense Secretary William J. Perry said he did not plan to press the Saudis on the issues of succession or internal opposition.
"I certainly don't push them on either of those issues," he said. "This, as far as I'm concerned, is an internal Saudi problem."
The American-Saudi relationship is based on a series of delicate balances.
 
The Alliance
 
Oil and Arms Bind U.S. and Saudis
The defense of the country and its oilfields depends on the United States military presence in the region. But the Saudis barely tolerate the visible presence on their soil of roughly 5,000 American troops, which stems from the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait in 1990, and two years ago they refused to house an armored brigade's worth of equipment needed for American security plans.
To preserve this crucial relationship, the United States does not press the kingdom on a whole range of issues, from human rights problems to the lack of political and religious freedom.
While American military contractors have benefited greatly from Saudi purchases of equipment, these arms imports have contributed to Saudi Arabia's financial woes and have become a source of friction within the royal family.
Even so, the consensus among outside experts and American officials is that the royal family maintains a firm grip on power and that Saudi Arabia's fundamental alignment with the United States is unlikely to change. Any questions about the kingdom's stability take on disproportionate weight because of its irreplaceable role as the world's largest oil producer by far -- pumping eight million barrels a day. If anything, uncertainty is likely to tighten the embrace of the United States and the Saudis.
"There are places in the world where our national interest is in doubt," said Ray Mabus, the former Mississippi Governor who recently left his post as Ambassador to Saudi Arabia. "This isn't one of them. That's because of the impact oil has on the world economy and on our economy. We went to war over that principle."
 
The Economy
 
From Petrodollars To Debt Pileup
In the past, the Saudi Government has dealt with problems like internal royal family disputes, popular discontent and external threats in a straightforward way: it has thrown money at them.
The estimated 17 million residents of Saudi Arabia live in one of the world's most generous welfare states. They pay no taxes and enjoy an elaborate array of subsidies from education to housing to health care.
Now, the country suffers from diminished financial resources, the result of 12 consecutive years of budget deficits and the $55 billion cost of the Persian Gulf war against Iraq, an amount that exceeded the Government's total revenues in 1992.
The transformation of Saudi Arabia from a country of unlimited economic horizons to one suffering from financial problems is captured in the most recent confidential analysis by the International Monetary Fund.
The fund's annual review last September found that even though Saudi Arabia had made progress in reducing Government spending, the Government's debt continued to increase because of large trade and budget deficits.
Domestic debt -- what the Government owes to local lenders -- was almost $100 billion in 1994, 77 percent of the country's gross domestic product. The monetary fund's staff projected that the proportion would reach 110 percent by 2000. This means that Saudi banks will have great difficulty financing private enterprise.
Since the fund's report was written, oil prices have risen, improving the prospects that the kingdom might be able to achieve a balanced budget in 1996. But the country's per capita income has declined by 50 percent in the last 12 years, and structural financial problems remain, which contribute to a sense of uneasiness in the country.
Although the Clinton Administration applauds Saudi efforts to control spending, officials acknowledge that those efforts are insufficient.
"They are controlling the overall budget much more carefully than they did over the last few years," said Jeffrey R. Shafer, the Undersecretary of the Treasury for International Affairs. But, he added, "there is more they need to do."
One of the most widely criticized aspects of the Saudi relationship with Washington is arms deals. In the 1980's the kingdom paid more than $28 billion to the Pentagon for military goods and services from American contractors. By 1993 the total of pending Saudi military orders was roughly equal to what the Pentagon spent for itself that year on major weapons systems.
Those days ended abruptly. The Saudis have placed no significant new arms orders in the last few years, following the advice of Pentagon officials to curb their appetite for weapons.
The Saudis' financial problems became so great that they have had to stretch out the repayment schedule for pending orders, avoiding the shutdown of American production lines.
Proportionally to the size of their economy, the Saudis still spend as much on defense as any other country in the world. Yet a forthcoming book on Saudi security by Anthony Cordesman, a military analyst, argues that the kingdom will always need the United States to defend it against an ever-present external enemy, whether Iraq or Iran, and that its primary security threats are internal.
One key to resolving them, the book says, is to create more private-sector jobs to meet the needs of a population that is growing at a staggering rate.
 
The Opposition
 
Splinter Groups And Afghan Vets
Who is the favorite target of the discontented? The United States military presence on Saudi soil.
"America -- we urgently call upon God Almighty to destroy its economy and society, to transform its states into countries and turn them against each other and to make it an example to all nations."
That anti-American diatribe was contained in a much-circulated sermon delivered in early 1993 by a man who is considered the most charismatic cleric in Saudi Arabia, Sheik Salman al-Awdah.
Militant preachers like him and Sheik Safar al-Hawali, the former dean of Islamic studies at Umm al-Qura University in Mecca, have exposed deep pockets of opposition to the current rulers, and worry the Saudi leadership.
Although there are divisions among the dissidents, much of their message is the same: the royal family is secular and corrupt and has betrayed the laws of Islam; the presence of infidel American troops is against Islam, and they must go.
Rather than open up the rigid political system after the gulf war, the response of the Saudi leadership has been to muzzle all dissent.
In the fall of 1994, the two sheiks were arrested with more than 400 others termed dissidents as part of a major crackdown against religious opposition. The two men are believed to be in prison -- although they have never been tried -- according to the State Department's 1995 global human rights report.
Despite the Government's efforts to snuff out their message, cassette tapes of the two sheiks' sermons circulate clandestinely in pockets throughout the country.
"The most charismatic of the preachers is al-Awdah," said Richard A. Smith, a former United States Government analyst on Saudi Arabia who has prepared CD-ROM's about the kingdom for his consulting company, Ardaman Industries.
It is impossible to judge how much of a following the dissidents have.
In confessions -- which may have been coerced -- the four Sunni Muslim Saudis convicted last month for the bomb blast against the National Guard in November said they had read the writings of Islamic extremists abroad.
The men, who were later executed, said they had smuggled the explosives used in the bomb attack from Yemen. Three said they had served with Islamic fighters in Afghanistan, and one of the three in Bosnia as well.
While it is still not known who carried out last Tuesday's attack that left 19 Americans dead, Mr. Perry says the investigation is centered on the possibility that it was the same group that carried out the November bombing. Whatever the case, the gap between the kingdom's rulers and its people seems to be growing, and a generation of Saudis radicalized by militant Islamic teachings may have the will and the capacity to carry out more violence in the future.
Indeed, given the extensive American arming and training of Islamic fighters challenging the Soviet military in Afghanistan, it is not inconceivable that the killers who attacked the Dhahran air base had earlier worked in concert with the Central Intelligence Agency.
"There are so many underground parties -- so many splinter groups, many of them made up of people who fought in Afghanistan," said Mohammed al-Masaari, an exiled Saudi religious militant, when asked in an interview in London about who might have been responsible for the recent bombing. "I expect more of the same. The political environment breeds it."
 
The Leadership
 
Family Tensions As Worries Grow
The Saudi Government is worried by the ease with which explosives are smuggled into the country.
In March, Saudi border guards intercepted a Chevrolet Caprice from Jordan that was packed with sophisticated high explosives.
The royal family has begun to face the same kind of violence that has wracked other countries in the region at a time when its leadership is in transition.
King Fahd, who is in his mid-70's, is not always lucid, Western associates close to the royal family say, and a transfer of power is already under way. Crown Prince Abdullah, the King's half-brother and designated heir, has essentially assumed the day-to-day operations of the Government.
But there are underlying tensions among the senior royals, particularly between the Crown Prince on one side and the King's full brothers Prince Sultan, the Defense Minister, and, to a lesser extent, Prince Nayef, the Interior Minister, on the other. Some of the royals have urged the King to retire to Spain, American officials say.
"The Crown Prince's support structure is simply not in place," said one Administration official who knows Prince Abdullah. "He will be King, but in terms of how things get done, there is a competition for influence and he is not as good a player."
The Crown Prince, who is head of the National Guard, is an austere, no-nonsense leader who is uncomfortable making small talk because of a noticeable stutter, say Administration officials who have dealt with him.
Prince Sultan, by contrast, is expansive, effusive and very comfortable with foreigners.
The tension between the two Princes is most dramatically illustrated in their different attitudes toward military spending.
Prince Abdullah has been struggling to rein in military spending even more. His efforts have alienated family members aligned with Sultan, who have much to lose financially if new projects do not go forward.
Prince Sultan, on the other hand, is much more willing to spend. In the wake of the 1991 gulf war, he came up with a number of ambitious military projects that were approved by the King but apparently not by other important ministers.
The Crown Prince seems determined to forge strategic relationships with other Arab countries without becoming anti-American.
American military contractors train his National Guard, and five Americans were among those killed in last November's bombing of its headquarters.
After the most recent bombing, Mr. Clinton and other senior Administration officials have expressed America's firm commitment to the stability of the kingdom and to a continued American presence there.
This ferment has not moved the Administration to press the Saudis harder, underscoring the mutually dependent if reluctant relationship.
"We're not doing them a favor by being there," Defense Secretary Perry said in the interview just before he left for Saudi Arabia. "They're not doing us a favor in letting us stay there. Each of us believes that this contributes to the stability of the region."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 10; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 2956 words

HEADLINE: Where, Oh Where, Have All the Corporate Raiders Gone?

BYLINE: By MARGARET ISA 

 BODY:
THEY were the much-feared and often reviled commanders of the takeover wars of the 1980's. Fueled by junk bonds, they raided corporate America seemingly at will, and made vast fortunes. In their heyday, they hobnobbed with the junk-bond king Michael R. Milken at his annual "Predators' Ball."
But this is the 1990's. Junk bonds and greenmail are out, Mr. Milken is a private citizen after spending two years in jail and paying $1.1 billion in fines and settlements for securities-laws violations, and a restructured corporate America has made itself resistant to the unwanted attentions of wheeler-dealers. 
 And the former corporate raiders? They are older, of course, and some are ailing. Some are prospering, but others have been forced to seek Chapter 11 protection for their debt-laden companies -- or give them up altogether. A few have scaled back their ambitions to such undertakings as running Burger Kings in Israel or doing consulting work for a little computer company in the West. A couple have run afoul of the law, receiving punishments ranging from 20 months in prison to serving meals to the homeless.
Here is a look at some of the men -- there are no women in their ranks -- who with Irwin L. Jacobs helped define the decade of greed, and where they are today.
 
THE BASSES
Sid, Lee, Robert (left) and Edward Bass, whose holdings include oil and real estate, scored one of their biggest and most publicized victories in 1984 with a huge but friendly investment in the Walt Disney Company, which was busily fighting off an assault by Saul P. Steinberg. The tale of that convoluted transaction was told in a 1987 book by John Taylor, "Storming the Magic Kingdom." All four brothers, as well as their father, Perry, and Sid's former wife, Anne, are on the Forbes 400 list of the richest Americans. The brothers divided up their fortune in the 1980's to pursue separate business strategies and interests. Sid, who had been most active in managing the family's business affairs, has largely escaped the limelight. Earlier this year, however, he created a stir in the art community by increasing his investment group's stake in Sotheby's Holdings Inc. The youngest, Lee, was embroiled in a controversy over a $20 million donation he made to Yale University five years ago. The university returned the money last year because Lee had stipulated that it be used for a Western Civilization curriculum and wanted to approve the instructors himself. What one brother taketh away, another brother giveth: Last month, Robert made a $20 million donation to Yale to renovate its residence halls. The entire brood has joined in a venture called Sundance Square to renovate the historic downtown area of Fort Worth, with brother Edward in charge. Edward, an avid environmentalist, also financed the Biosphere II project near Tucson, Ariz., a $150 million attempt to create a self-sustaining ecosystem inside a glass bubble, where eight people lived for two years.
 
THE BELZBERGS
Canada's Belzberg brothers -- Samuel (left), Hyman and William -- failed spectacularly in their 1990 attempt to acquire Armstrong World Industries. Not only did their prey escape, but the Lancaster, Pa., home-products company also lobbied successfully for passage in Pennsylvania of some of the country's toughest anti-takeover legislation.
The Belzbergs ended up losing $13.6 million in their aborted bid, and their Vancouver flagship company, the First City Financial Corporation, showed a $228 million loss for 1990. Though the family then said it was shifting its focus to West Coast real estate, environmental services and friendly transactions, things went from bad to worse. In 1991, American regulators seized a California savings and loan owned by First City Financial, saying it was poorly managed. As losses of the Belzberg company widened to $324 million, Swiss bondholders gained majority control and renamed it the Harrowston Corporation. The Belzbergs, however, kept a 15 percent stake and installed a member of the next generation, Brent, as president.
 
PAUL A. BILZERIAN
Mr. Bilzerian is doing worse, in some ways, than most. In top form in 1988 when he acquired the Singer Companies, a military contractor, in a $1.06 billion hostile takeover, he was convicted a year later of nine felony counts; fined $1.5 million and sentenced to four years in prison (later reduced to 20 months) for securities-law violations, including filing false information with the Securities and Exchange Commission. In sentencing him, the judge said he was increasing the prison term because he believed Mr. Bilzerian had perjured himself. The charges stemmed from his acquisition of large blocks of stock in two companies: Cluett, Peabody & Company and the Hammermill Paper Company. After filing for personal bankruptcy in 1991, Mr. Bilzerian has stayed out of the public eye of late, leading a quiet life in the 22,901-square-foot Tampa compound that he fondly calls the "Taj Mahal." (Florida law protects homes -- no matter how big -- from creditors.) In 1994, he was hired as a management consultant by Cimetrix Inc., a small computer company in Utah.

ROBERT CAMPEAU
Mr. Campeau's department store empire tumbled two years after he completed it. The Canadian financier's hostile takeovers of Allied Stores Inc. for $3.4 billion in late 1986, and then Federated Department Stores for $6.6 billion in 1988, left his Campeau Corporation awash in debt just when junk bond prices collapsed in the late 1980's. In 1990, both retailing units were forced to seek bankruptcy protection and Mr. Campeau was ousted as chairman and chief executive. In a 1992 reorganization, Campeau Corporationwas renamed the Camdev Corporation and Mr. Campeau ended up with a 2 percent stake. Camdev now deals only in real estate.
 
ASHER B. EDELMAN
Back in 1985, Barron's magazine called Mr. Edelman "the newest kid on the takeover block" and described the "fierce struggle" by the Datapoint Corporation to fend off him off. Datapoint lost the battle, and Mr. Edelman continues to run the San Antonio-based maker of computer networks as chairman and chief executive. Datapoint, which had $174 million in revenue last year, has been losing money for several years. But it is mostly Mr. Edelman's lavish life-style, rather than his raiding skills, that has captured the public's attention in the 1990's. He has a large, and by some accounts risque, art collection. Last November, he sold Jasper Johns's "Winter" from the "Four Seasons" series for $3 million, and in May he put another Johns work, "Gray Painting With Ball," up for auction but failed to get the $1.5 million minimum he was asking.
Mr. Edelman also has a taste for spacious living quarters; in 1994, he sold his 23-room penthouse on East End Avenue in Manhattan, complete with a 63-foot-long entrance gallery, two libraries, a gym, a four-bedroom children's wing and planted terraces overlooking the East River -- for $5.85 million. His artworks haven't immunized him from the nitty-gritty of the business world, however. Mr. Edelman and his company recently settled a lawsuit over the sale of land in Texas for an undisclosed sum after a jury had awarded the plaintiff a total of $63 million. And five years ago, he agreed to pay $436,858 in a settlement with the Securities and Exchange Commission, which had accused him of waiting two days before disclosing his additional purchases in September 1989 of Datapoint stock.
 
SIR JAMES GOLDSMITH
Sir James has been devoting himself increasingly to politics. The English-French financier, who once owned the American supermarket chain Grand Union, sold most of his business interests before the stock market crash of 1987. A committed conservative who wrote editorials for the French magazine L'Express when he controlled it in the 1980's, he is turning his guns these days on European unification. He has written a book, "Le Piege" (The Trap), that criticizes plans for a single European currency. In 1994, he won a seat on the European Parliament. He also founded the Referendum Party in Britain, which has threatened to sponsor single-issue candidates in any election where the Labor and Conservative parties refuse to support a national plebiscite on "the sort of Europe of which Britain wants to be a part."
 
CHARLES E. HURWITZ
Mr. Hurwitz has made headlines recently with his fight to end Federal logging restrictions on his redwood forests. Maxxam Inc., a conglomerate that he controls, went heavily into debt in 1986 to buy the Pacific Lumber Company for $900 million, and now Mr. Hurwitz wants to chop down thousands of acres of giant evergreens on Pacific Lumber property to pay down his debt. Environmentalists want to protect the old-growth trees and the endangered coastal birds, marbled murrelets, that live in them. In May, Pacific Lumber sued the Federal Government, demanding an end to the logging restrictions on the grounds that they violate the Constitutional prohibition on taking private property without compensation. The Federal Government wants to buy some of of Pacific Lumber's land, but the two sides have not been able to agree on a price.
 
CARL C. ICAHN
Mr. Icahn is perhaps the busiest of the bunch. He is now trying to wrest control of RJR Nabisco Holdings to force a spinoff of the food business from the tobacco operations, a move that he contends would produce a bonanza for stockholders. Mr. Icahn failed to win control of the board of the RJR Nabisco Holdings Corporation with his ally, Bennett S. LeBow, in April, but earlier this month he severed his agreement with Mr. LeBow, an increasingly unpopular figure with Wall Street.
Mr. Icahn has also changed with the times, seeking out stakes in distressed companies that he can turn into good investments. In 1995, he bought up debt of Rockefeller Center Properties Inc., the trust that holds the $1.3 billion mortgage on Rockefeller Center. Mr. Icahn has also gone into the consolidator business in a deal he made with Trans World Airlines Inc., a company he once owned. As part of its bankruptcy restructuring, T.W.A. gave Mr. Icahn the right to buy tickets with a face value of $610 million at a discount and then resell them. The airline plans to use the money it gets from selling him the tickets to pay him back the $190 million it owes him.
 
BENNETT S. LEBOW
Mr. LeBow has been making waves in the tobacco world. With his fellow financier, Carl C. Icahn, he fought a fierce battle this year for control of the RJR Nabisco Holdings Corporation, which the two men wanted to split up into separate food and tobacco companies in the hope of driving up the stock value. Their bid was soundly defeated in April. Separately, Mr. LeBow's Liggett Group Inc., the nation's fifth-largest tobacco company, broke ranks with the rest of the industry in March and settled with the lawyers for anti-tobacco plaintiffs.
Mr. LeBow also came close to forging an alliance in April with an entrepreneur, Jay MacDonald, to buy three troubled women's magazines: Working Woman, Working Mother, and Ms.Mr. LeBow agreed to pay off the magazines' $25 million in debt and put up $3.5 million in new working capital, but he pulled out after NatWest, the biggest creditor, demanded that he forfeit the $3.5 million if the deal fell through. Mr. MacDonald later linked up with Paxson Communications to make the purchase.
 
RONALD O. PERELMAN
Mr. Perelman, who acquired Revlon Inc. in a $1.8 billion hostile takeover in 1986, is doing better than most of his counterparts. He took the cosmetics company public in February to raise money to repay some of Revlon's $2.2 billion debt.Mr. Perelman also owns the comic-book and sports-card publisher Marvel Entertainment Group Inc.. and New World, an entertainment empire that includes television stations and a production company. The flayboyant financier has probably made as many appearances in the gossip columns as he has on the business pages. He recently married Patricia Duff, a Democratic Party activist and mother of his one-year old daughter.
Mr. Perelman, who is a Republican, has five other children from two earlier marriages. His previous wife, the gossip reporter Claudia Cohen, walked away with an $80 million divorce settlement and then had a short-lived romance with Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato of New York. Mr. Perelman can frequently be found at charity dinners, parties and nightclubs.
He was embroiled last year in a high-profile spat with the former chief financial officer of his MacAndrew & Forbes holding company, Fred Tepperman, who said he had been wrongfully dismissed by Mr. Perelman for taking time off from work to care for his wife, who had Alzheimer's disease. The company had its own side of the story, contending in a countersuit that it had dismissed Mr. Tepperman for neglecting his duties. The two sides reached a out-of-court settlement, the terms of which were not disclosed.
 
T. BOONE PICKENS
Mr. Pickens, the Texas oilman who railed against entrenched managers who were no friends of the common stockholder, came full circle this month by stepping down as chairman and chief executive officer of Mesa Inc., the energy company he founded 40 years ago. Mesa had been straining under more than $1 billion of debt, and a group of shareholders, led by David Batchelder, a board member and former Mesa president and Pickens protege, had been seeking to unseat Mr. Pickens on the grounds that he had mismanaged company finances. Richard Rainwater, whom Mr. Pickens had recruited to help defend Mesa against the dissidents, is expected to install his own team now. Analysts attribute Mr. Pickens's downfall in part on his losing gamble that natural-gas prices would jump. His quiet departure contrasts vividly with the high-profile runs he made in the 1980's on energy giants like Gulf Oil, Phillips Petroleum and Unocal.
 
VICTOR POSNER
Mr. Posner once reigned over a financial empire with substantial stakes in about 40 companies. But the Sharon Steel Corporation, the linchpin of his holdings, filed for Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection in 1987, and Mr. Posner has spent the last decade in and out of court. In a 1987 conviction for tax evasion, the Miami financier was sentenced to serve meals to the homeless and to help pay for homeless shelters. In 1989, he was sued by shareholders of the DWG Corporation, who accused him of plundering the company. To resolve the lawsuit, Mr. Posner agreed in 1992 to resign as chairman of DWG and to give up his controlling stake in the conglomerate, which owned the Arby's Inc., fast-food chain; the Royal Crown Cola Company, and the National Propane Corporation. In 1993, he was convicted of securities fraud relating to his 1984 bid for the Fischbach Corp., a New York electrical contractor. Mr. Posner was permanently barred from serving as an officer or director of any publicly traded company and was ordered to give up $3.5 million. Last year, he was sued by his son Steven, who accused his father of plundering the closely held Security Management Corporation, an owner of rental property controlled by the elder Mr. Posner. His lawyer at the time said Mr. Posner intended to vigorously defend himself.
 
JAY A. PRITZKER
Mr. Pritzker, the Chicago financier whose family owns several closely held businesses, including the Hyatt Corporation and Regency Cruises Inc., has spent the last few years feuding with another, albeit part-time, raider, Donald J. Trump. In 1993, Mr. Trump had sued the Pritzker family over its management of their jointly owned hotel, the Grand Hyatt in New York. Mr. Trump asserted that Hyatt's management had engaged in questionable accounting practices, and in 1994, Hyatt countersued for breach of contract. They settled the suits last year for an undisclosed sum.In 1993, Jay's brother Robert, who runs the Marmon Group, the family's private industrial empire, became chairman of the National Association of Manufacturers and used it as a bully pulpit to expound his fairly conservative views on Washington's role in economic matters.
 
MESHULAM RIKLIS
Mr. Riklis's empire has been coming apart since the late 1980's. He still has McCrory, the chain of five-and-dime stores he bought in 1960, but it has been operating under Chapter 11 protection for four years. The three other major companies Mr. Riklis ran in the 1980's also ended up in bankruptcy proceedings as a result of heavy debts. Mr. Riklis recently fought a battle with prostate cancer. And, to pay the settlement for his divorce from the entertainer Pia Zadora, his homes and art collection have been put up for sale. He has recently started smaller businesses, like Burger Kings in Israel.
 
HAROLD SIMMONS
Mr. Simmons is holding steady, still in control of a number of companies. Earlier this month, one of them, Valhi Inc., agreed to sell its Amalgamated Sugar Company refinery to the Snake River Sugar Company, a cooperative of 1,600 sugar-beet farmers, for $250 million in a tax-favorable transaction. Valhi is likely to use the proceeds to pay down debt and put capital into its other businesses, which include forest products, fast food and hardware products.
 
SAUL P. STEINBERG
These days, Mr. Steinberg's family owns just under 50 percent of Reliance Group Holdings, a seller of property-casualty insurance that Mr. Steinberg purchased in 1968 and used as a vehicle for his other financial dealings. Analysts say the company has been restructured well, posting strong earnings in some recent quarters. Trouble is, Mr. Steinberg suffered a stroke about a year ago. The Brooklyn-born financier needed several hours of physical therapy a day to regain the use of his left arm and hand.
