SECTION: Section 5; Page 12; Column 1; Travel Desk

LENGTH: 1460 words

HEADLINE: At Hotels, A Separate Peace

BYLINE: By BETSY WADE; BETSY WADE writes the Practical Traveler column.

 BODY:
IF you ask directory assistance in Dallas for the telephone number of the Nonsmokers Inn, you are given (214) 631-6633. If you ring, the person answering says, "Classic Motor Inn." In the difference is the history of the grand 1981 dream of Lyndon Sanders, the owner, who yielded to his managers' recommendations in 1991. Smoking is now permitted in 20 percent of the 135 rooms.
On the other hand, if you call the Country Inn and Suites by Carlson in Roseville, Minn., between Minneapolis and St. Paul, you can learn that all 57 rooms have been designated for nonsmokers. This property, which opened two years ago, operates on a franchise from Carlson, a major group of travel companies, which owns the Radisson chain. According to Thomas J. Polski, a Carlson spokesman, all Country Inns must offer at least 65 percent nonsmoking rooms, but the Roseville manager bought the idea 100 percent. 
 With the exception of the Roseville motel and apparently only a few other places, most smokers and nonsmokers should be able to find what they want in the way of a hotel room. Managers keep fine-tuning their percentages to match demand, with the result that the number of hotels providing at least some nonsmoking rooms keeps rising.
The key to getting a room to suit a need is to specify it on the reservation, and the more important this is, the more urgent the request should be. It may not always occur to those who smoke, but they should ask about rules affecting lobby spaces and hotel restaurants, unless they want to smoke standing outdoors.
The short history of the nonsmoking hotel room, which a 1985 academic study said began in 1973 at a single hotel owned by the Hotel Investors Group, demonstrates the impact of market forces, in contrast with the history of nonsmoking in public spaces -- aboard airlines, trains and buses and in restaurants -- where health advocates have turned to legislation and regulation.
California did enact hotel legislation, after what Jim Abrams, executive vice president of the California Hotel and Motel Association, describes as a long, bitter fight. As of January 1995, California hotels must provide 35 percent nonsmoking rooms. The bill was championed by the Heart Association, the state restaurant association and other groups, but passed only after the hotel group won certain amendments, Mr. Abrams said -- for instance, allowing 25 percent of lobby space for smoking, and permitting smoking at meetings except when meals are served.
But demand is having a greater impact than legislation: in most hotels, a year and a half after the law went into effect, half the rooms are nonsmoking.
In Dallas, Mr. Sanders was a premature nonsmoking innkeeper. "One time we had an oil company reserve 32 rooms," said Mr. Sanders, who now raises ostriches and other exotic animals in Fredericksburg, Tex. "When one of those people wanted to smoke, all 32 decamped." Despite such setbacks, the place did well, he said. But when other lodgings in the city began to offer smoke-free rooms to health-conscious, allergic or fastidious travelers, customers stopped flooding his inn, and his managers pressed him to let them welcome smokers, too, to fill more rooms. Since by then he had leased the motel to them, he gave in.
At the Roseville Country Inn, health issues played only a minor role in the decision to ban smoking. Thomas Robey, who has been manager since the opening, said that the driving issues were reducing cleaning expenses and capital costs for replacing carpets and furniture. The occupancy rate, he said, is running 88 percent -- remarkable in comparison with the 1995 national figure, 65.5 percent, which itself was the highest in years.
Mr. Robey said he thought it was easier to fill a nonsmoking inn with 57 rooms than one with 200. Other factors he cited were his inn's lack of meeting rooms and the presence among his steady clientele of parents of students at nearby religious colleges.
The Roseville Country Inn makes no secret of its smokelessness: it is noted on airline computer reservation systems, Country Inn directories and at the central reservation system for Carlson. But when people reserve directly, he said, the motel tries to modulate the message. "We say: 'Fine, we have reserved a queen-bedded nonsmoking room for you for that night.' And if they say they want a smoking room, we tell them we are all nonsmoking."
There are other all-nonsmoking hotels -- the 30-room Inn at Union Square and the 15-room Washington Square Inn, both in San Francisco, have been nonsmoking since 1991 -- but there is no comprehensive list.
La Suite Worldwide, a fledgling chain, planned to begin operations in 1995, franchising economy-rate motels that would be entirely nonsmoking. A year and a half later, its first property, in Kissimmee, Fla., is opening, but only 80 percent of the rooms will be nonsmoking. The retreat from 100 percent came about, said J. D. Nana, the chief executive, because about 25 potential franchise-holders rejected the idea of no smoking at all.
THE American Hotel and Motel Association says that in 1992, 86 percent of its members reported an average of 40 percent nonsmoking rooms; by 1994 the average had risen to 46 percent. A new survey is in the works.
In the last few years three divisions of the Promus Hotels group in Memphis -- Embassy Suites, Hampton Inns and Homewood Suites -- have raised their required level of nonsmoking rooms to 75 percent from around 50 percent.
At most of the hotels that make up Choice Hotels International -- Quality, Comfort, Clarion, Sleep and Rodeway -- managers are now required to provide 50 percent nonsmoking rooms. The level was raised in June last year, from 15 percent. At the Econo Lodges, the least expensive of this group, the figure is now 20 percent, according to Anne Curtis, director of communications for Choice.
At Radisson, managers are allowed to select their own levels to accommodate regional habits; the permissible range is 30 to 85 percent nonsmoking, Mr. Polski said.
At the high end of the price range, the 700-room Parker Meridien in New York, a French-owned chain, currently maintains a high 72 percent of its rooms as nonsmoking; in 1994, it raised the percentage to 67 from 47.
Hotel-motel association surveys show no clear pattern based on price, hotel size, location or type of property. Experts say the patterns are more affected by how popular a hotel is with foreigners, especially visitors from countries of heavy smokers like Japan. At the Kitano on Park Avenue in New York, 20 percent of 150 rooms are reserved for nonsmokers.
In Texas, the rise in nonsmoking accommodations is continuing. Don Hansen, the executive vice president of the Texas Hotel and Motel Association, says that the "health craze" is driving it, and that 50 to 60 percent of Texas's quarter-million hotel rooms are nonsmoking. Nonsmoking rooms at the Doubletree in Austin went from 20 to 60 percent in three years.
Many efforts have been made in Texas towns and cities to eliminate the right of hotels to provide smoking rooms, Mr. Hansen said, but there has been no statewide legislation or referendum. He described Austin as virtually a nonsmoking community so far as public spaces are concerned; however, he said, smoking is permitted in the Capitol. In the city of College Station, he said, an effort was made to eliminate smoking in all hotel rooms, but "we talked them out of it."
Two problems loom with nonsmoking rooms. The first is typified by Mr. Robey's report on the Roseville Inn. "People do smoke in the rooms," he said. "It's just once every two weeks instead of every night."
What does a hotel do when this happens, or when it must convert a smoking room to nonsmoking? All bedding and curtains go out to the laundry; pillows are replaced; rugs and carpets are shampooed; walls are washed. The ceiling and walls may be repainted and the beds replaced. Then the room may be sealed for some time with a portable air-freshener inside. The days of creating a nonsmoking room by scooping up the ashtrays and putting a tent card on the table are gone: rooms now have signs affixed to the doors, and usually on the hallway walls.
The other challenge is the reservation system. If a guest wants to book a nonsmoking room, the reservationist will usually say that the preference will be noted, but there is no guarantee. At the end of May, however, Hilton began offering confirmed reservations for nonsmoking rooms at 200 of its domestic hotels. The Hilton chain requires that at least 50 percent of rooms in its hotels be nonsmoking; the range rises to 80 percent. If a room is not available on a guest's arrival, the hotel may offer a free nonsmoking room at a nearby hotel.

GRAPHIC: Photos: At the Waldorf-Astoria in New York, where 80 percent are non-smoking. (pg. 12); The Waldorf tries to keep smokers and nonsmokers happy. (Jonathan Atkin for The New York Times) (pg. 20)

SECTION: Section 13NJ; Page 1; Column 3; New Jersey Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 751 words

HEADLINE: JERSEY;
The School Bell Tolls for Shop Class

BYLINE: By JOE SHARKEY

 BODY:
IT was the last day of school at Hoboken High, with dismissal scheduled for noon. A side door in Bob Callahan's wood shop class was opened onto the sidewalk and the sun tumbled in, lighting specks of sawdust adrift in the air. The workbenches were tidy now, the machines cleaned and stowed.
For Mr. Callahan, after 23 years of teaching wood shop, it was literally the last day of school. The same for Nick Burke, the print-shop teacher, who was unexpectedly retiring after 31 years. At Hoboken High School and at many other schools throughout New Jersey, shop classes were dismissed forever when the school year ended. 
 Mr. Callahan will return to Hoboken High next year, after spending this school year cramming in 18 new credits for certification to teach biology and earth sciences to special-education classes. But Mr. Burke, who was a fireman before he became a teacher, will not be back in September. Shortly after learning last October that his print-shop class was being eliminated, he put in for early retirement. So far, he hasn't made any new career plans.
"Luckily, I can still drive a nail," said Mr. Burke, whose shop classes -- composed of both boys and girls -- produced nearly all of the printing for the Hoboken school district, a million pages this school year alone.
"Nick is being very modest," said Mr. Callahan, who considers his colleague a friend and mentor. "There's not a printing company in the country that wouldn't pick him up like that." He snapped his fingers.
School districts are eliminating high school shop classes for a number of reasons, but the bottom line, of course, is money. The American secondary educational system pumps as many of its competent students as possible into college. At the same time, large numbers of marginal students are placed into special-education programs, which qualify for large amounts of Federal and state funds.
Unfortunately for those in the middle, who might want to make a living with their hands, not to mention those who might want to hire them to do a job, trade classes like print shop and wood shop and home economics no longer fit easily into the educational bureaucracy's pattern of what constitutes a comprehensive liberal education for the 21st century.
"We taught students who are now doctors, lawyers -- professionals who still know something about woodworking, printing, even cooking and sewing," Mr. Burke said sadly, lamenting the loss of mainstream shop courses. And Mr. Callahan added:
"Beyond the needs of kids who aren't college-bound, that's one of the things that worries me. What about the ones going to college? Later in life, they won't need to change an electrical switch? Put in minor plumbing?"
"Hang a door?" said Mr. Burke, laughing.
"Right, try asking a contractor to come in and hang a door," Mr. Callahan said. "They don't want to be bothered with a small job. Today, you see good carpenters on television, like Bob Vila, more than in real life."
At Hoboken High, the industrial arts department once had a staff of 20. This year, it was down to four teachers and three courses: wood shop, print shop and mechanical drawing.
In Hudson County, as elsewhere, industrial arts classes are being consolidated at central vocational-technical schools. But offering shop classes at the vo-tech schools effectively puts them out of the reach of the general academic population, if only because of the time it takes out of the school day to commute between two schools.
Attempts to save shop classes failed last winter, so those arguments were mute the other day at Hoboken High, where the only project remaining in Mr. Callahan's now-quiet wood shop was to offer a simple farewell to a fine teacher, Nick Burke, about to go home and take 31 years of history with him.
A few minutes before noon, Mr. Burke glanced at the clock, sighed and shook hands with Mr. Callahan and with Jackie McClellan, a special-education math teacher who had stopped down to say goodbye.
"Anything you need, you sound off, hear?" Mr. Burke said. His two colleagues nodded.
The school band should have been there playing the alma mater with trumpets and snare drums. Instead, the bell on the wall clanged noisily exactly at dismissal time. Mr. Burke winced at the bell one last time. He smiled tightly, nodded, and walked out the wood shop door, disappearing in dazzling sunlight on Clinton Street.
A full minute passed in silence. Then in a thick voice Mr. Callahan just said, "A right guy, Nicky Burke."

SECTION: Section 13LI; Page 1; Column 4; Long Island Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1440 words

HEADLINE: Mites Killing Bees Vital to L.I. Fruit

BYLINE: By LINDA TAGLIAFERRO

 BODY:
THE discovery seemed like a postscript to Rachel Carson's "Silent Spring." In April, when Frank Reiser, a field biologist at Nassau Community College, went to check on the beehives kept in his backyard, instead of the familiar buzzing, he found nothing but dead honeybees.
When Mr. Reiser called other members of the Long Island Beekeepers' Association, he found he was not alone. Most of the 90 members had suffered major losses, and one man who managed 80 hives had lost 60 of these. 
 The reason for the deaths was not merely a severe winter. The real culprits were two species of mite, the honeybee tracheal mite, Acarapis woodii, and the Varroa mite, Varroa jacobsoni, which have plagued Britain's bees for many years, and nearly wiped out beekeeping in that country in the 1940's.
These mites have already caused the mass destruction of honeybees in other states, including Florida, California and Washington, but it was not until this year that they affected Long Island's bees in such epidemic proportions.
The common honeybee, Apis mellifera, is more than a source of interest to hobbyists on Long Island and elsewhere. The insects provide pollination for over 90 commercial crops in the United States.
Pollination is essential for seed production, and the greater the number of seeds produced, the greater the quality of the fruit or vegetable. Certain fruits require multiple pollinator visits. The muskmelon flower, for example, may require 15 visits from honeybees or other pollinators to produce a high quality melon. Absent or misshapen fruit is the result of lack of pollination.
Dr. Nick Calderone, a research entomologist with the United States Department of Agriculture Research Station in Beltsville, Md., said: "There has certainly been a serious decline in both the number of wild and managed colonies, and we expect that to continue in the future. This may be the worst year in terms of colony loss that New York State has ever seen."
Some beekeepers say this could mean lower fruit and vegetable yields on Long Island and higher prices for consumers. Others hope that different pollinators in the wild will take over the job of the decimated honeybee population.
Raymond Lackey, the president of the Long Island Beekeepers' Association, estimated that 50 to 80 percent of all bees in managed hives on the Island have succumbed to the parasites. He speculated that all of the wild bees may also have died.
Mr. Lackey said Long Island honeybees were probably spared previous epidemics because there are no migratory beekeepers that serve Long Island farms. He said the mites may have entered through packaged queens and attendants, which are sold through the mail by beekeepers in other states.
Mr. Lackey said the tracheal mite is microscopic and lives within the breathing tubes of the honeybee. "We found that it moves into the breathing tubes during the first four days of the honeybee's life," he said, "because after that time period, the trachea becomes stiff enough so that the mite can no longer force its way in."
Once inside the breathing tubes, the mite penetrates the tracheal wall and sucks the blood of the honeybee. It then lays its eggs. When these hatch, the offspring mate and lay eggs, and all of these eventually penetrate the tracheal wall and parasitize the bee.
"The males are blind and never leave the host," Mr. Lackey said. "But some of the females leave the honeybee and look for a new one. As the bee moves around, the female mite can jump over to a new host."
Honeybees normally only live for about six weeks. "During the warmer months," Mr. Lackey said, "when bees gather nectar, they work themselves to death. Their wings are literally worn out." Several cycles of the tracheal mite can flourish within this brief time span.
"The problem with this mite is winter kill," Mr. Lackey said. "Bees that are born in the fall live through the winter, when it is impossible to gather nectar. During this cold weather, the bees cluster together in their hive and vibrate their wings to heat the colony.
"During the long winter months, the tracheal mites produce to the point where the bee can no longer breathe or do heavy exercise. It's like someone with a heart condition who can't walk across a room without getting winded." As a result, the bees are too weakened to maintain heat in their hives.
Another mite that has killed many honeybees is the Varroa mite. It attaches itself to the body of the bee, often under its joints, and is so large than it is visible to the naked eye.
Dr. Calderone said infestations of this mite are invariably fatal to honeybees if untreated. "Colony mortality occurs within six months to two years after becoming infested," he said.
Some Long Island beekeepers have given up as a result of the epidemics, because of the costs and labor-intensive nature of starting up again. Ray Mathie, a resident of Southold who has managed hives for 20 years, said: "The mites knocked me out this year. I had 30 hives and they all died. I can't start over again."
Thor Eriksen of Ridge, who has kept hives for 15 years, has decided to persevere despite his losses. He now manages 70 hives. He said his colonies were not affected this year, but were devastated last year, when he lost 40 out of 50 hives to the parasites. "My colonies seem to have gotten the mites before everyone else's," he said. "Maybe it's because I have so many of them all over the place."
Mr. Eriksen bought tracheal-mite-resistant bees from a breeder in Georgia. The bees were developed by the Agriculture Department. The colonies have been thriving, and Mr. Eriksen rents them out to farmers in the spring.
Suffolk is the leading agricultural county in New York State, based on the wholesale value of its agricultural products. Cornell Cooperative Extension of Suffolk County estimates that the total value of agricultural production comes to $130 million a year. This has an economic impact of $750 million on Long Island, according to the Long Island Regional Planning Board.
William Sanok, agent at Cornell Cooperative Extension of Suffolk County in Riverhead, said: "The trouble with this honeybee situation is that it doesn't just show up as an acute problem. It's not as if one day, we just don't have pollination and everyone gets excited about it. It's more of a chronic problem. You have reduced pollination, reduced fruit set and reduced crops. It's almost insidious."
He added, "There are other insect pollinators, but the honeybee is really the one that we depend on, because it's the hardest worker and the most productive."
Some farmers are not too concerned. Thomas Wickham of Wickham's Fruit Farm in Cutchogue said he did not consider the situation catastrophic. He said, "We have had more difficulty pollinating our fruit crops in recent years, and that could be a reflection of a reduced honeybee population."
Richard Anderson, owner of Anderson Farms in Riverhead, rented colonies from Mr. Eriksen this year. He said pollination has been a problem during the last few years, and that it has got progressively worse.
"I don't see the wild bees any more," Mr. Anderson said. "They used to come out of the trees and our abandoned barn. They were always visible."
Andrew Amsler, an owner of Richter's Orchard in East Northport, said the orchard lost a dozen hives over the winter. "There were always colonies of wild bees out in the woods," Mr. Amsler said, "but this year, I noticed very few of them around. We depended very heavily on the ones that we rented this year."
Treatment for the mites is limited. This is partly because beekeepers must be careful to avoid killing the bees or contaminating the honey. Menthol is used to hold down tracheal mites.
The only federally approved mite killer for the control of Varroa is Apistan, a synthetic pyrethroid. Although this has proved effective, Dr. Calderone said, "The big problem on the horizon is news out of Europe that the mites there have developed a resistance to Apistan in the past few years, and so we anticiate that will happen here in the next few years."
Dr. Calderone said he had worked with the Agriculture Department on developing an integrated pest-management strategy to deal with the infestation, and he will continue this work at Cornell University when he assumes a position there this July.
"In the long run," he said, "we'll probably have to keep modifying that program to take into account changes that the mites undergo. They have the ability to adapt to our control strategies. The long term goal will be the development of a population of mite-resistant bees."

HEADLINE: Long Island Q&A: Stanley Bergman;
From Small Beginnings to a Company With Millions in Sales

BYLINE: By SUSAN KONIG

 BODY:
BACK in the 1930's, a pharmacist in Woodside, Queens, began sending product fliers to local doctors to try to increase sales. He knew that he would have to offer lower prices. What Henry Schein did not know, however, was that he had discovered a market that would transform his tiny business into the world's largest direct marketer of health care products and services to office-based practitioners.
Today, customers of Henry Schein Inc. in Melville include 200,000 dental practices and labs, physicians' offices and veterinary clinics in North America and Europe. Many products are shipped from a huge, automated distribution center in Port Washington -- one of eight the company has built worldwide. 
 But, the company had its share of rough times, too. In the early 80's, a slew of competitors left it barely profitable. That is where Stanley Bergman came in. With an aggressive plan of corporate restructuring that included hiring hundreds of highly skilled Long Island employees, Mr. Bergman turned the company around.
Last year, Henry Schein reported sales of $616 million. The company went public in November. Since 1990, it has grown at a compounded annual rate of 22 percent. In 1995, it acquired 14 operations with $100 million in revenue, and 10 more acquitions are in the works. And it is a leading Long Island employer, with a regional staff of more than 900 (the company employs 2,700 people worldwide).
Mr. Bergman, 46, who has been the chief executive officer since 1989, earned a bachelor of commerce degree from the University of Witwatersrand in Johannnesburg. He came to the United States in 1976 and became a certified public accountant. Before coming to Henry Schein, he was a manager at the accounting firm of BDO Seidman. He lives in Northport with his wife, who is a physician, and two teen-age sons.
Q. Your company had some pretty humble beginnings. How did it prosper?
A. It was in the 70's that things really took off. That's when the Schein family discovered the dental market. There were around 3,000 dental distributors back then. But they sold products at relatively high prices and with relatively poor service. So the Scheins decided to put together catalogues which would offer them at lower prices and have them ready for immediate delivery. That was a pretty new concept at the time. And it's what really gave rise to the Henry Schein business. The pharmacy was phased out and, by the late 70's, Henry Schein Inc. was doing around $40 million in sales. It had to be moved to Port Washington for more space. This is a company which, up until the 60's, was basically a soda fountain.
Q. You have the largest automated catalogue business in the health care field. How did it begin?
A. In the late 70's, Henry's older son, Jay, joined the company. He was a Wall Street attorney, and he'd witnessed the automation of Wall Street. He saw all the big brokerage firms putting in computers, and he brought that notion to us. We spent the first few years of the 80's putting in computer systems to let us do "automated" processing -- the picking and packing of products. This was one of the first companies to automate the distribution cycle, and we went all the way through to automated catalogues. These catalogues were really the start of electronic publishing.
Q. Your automated warehouses help you keep discount prices?
A. Yes. We compete with about 900 local and regional distributors. They usually work from small branch offices, and it's expensive for them to ship too far. So we built eight giant automated warehouses -- three in the U.S. and five in Europe -- so we could reach more customers more economically
Q. Five years ago, the company was divided in two. Why?
A. Henry had invested in a generic drug company. And his son Jay formed a lobby group asking Congress that methods be developed to bring generic drugs to market in an orderly fashion. In 1984, Congress passed a law which provided a fair way of getting F.D.A. approval for these drugs. That really gave birth to the generic drug industry as we know it. So Henry's investment took off -- it became known as "Schein Pharmaceutical" -- and we spent the next five years growing it. But we didn't pay much attention to Henry Schein Inc. So, we had to decide whether to get out of it or focus more time and money on it. We decided to split it into two separate companies so we could focus more on each.
Q. But, you had your work cut out for you re-building Henry Schein ?
A. Yes. It was barely profitable, because we hadn't paid much attention to it. We needed a strong plan to turn it around. The first step was to diversify -- to move further into the medical and veterinary markets. Then, we decided to move into Europe.
Q. What prompted that decision?
A. I was at a dental show in Europe in 1989, and each time I gave someone a business card, I found they already knew our company. I guess we had a good reputation there because American dentistry is still considered the best in the world. If you're a dentist in Western Europe, for example, you'll probably come to the U.S. periodically for continuing education. So we decided to bring our marketing concepts there.
Now, we're in seven countries in Europe and hope not just to increase our market penetration there, but to expand into Asia and South America. Our distribution system is something that many countries are ripe for. We think the Japanese market is ready for the catalogue direct marketing business but, so far, they've done everything possible to keep us out.
Q. Still, going international was not enough to turn the company around?
A. No. In the early 80's, our competitors started copying us. They came out with catalogues, they started discounting their products and improving their service, and the bottom line was we started looking just like them. We had to differentiate ourselves again in the dental world, and we did it with value-added service.
We took the view that the dental practice was actually a small business: We went to our customers and suggested they run their practices as such. We developed practice management software, which is really no different than the software that runs any kind of business -- it handles billing, scheduling, etc. And now, 16,000 dental practices in the U.S. are using it. We also got into patient financing -- giving dentists a line of credit which they could offer to their customers who didn't have insurance, for example. And we did credit card processing -- we got our clients credit cards at lower rates than the traditional banks.
Q. So you became problem-solvers of sorts?
A. Yes, but it wasn't something that Henry Schein thought up. Large drug wholesalers have built huge businesses around the notion that, if you help your clients to generate more income, you'll be more successful. Selling products becomes a byproduct of that. We went looking for ways our customers could increase their business -- the software and lines of credit were part of it. In the 80's, we made dentists aware of the need to glove and mask up for protection from AIDS and hepatitis B. Universities had been telling them this, but we commercialized it by developing seminars and manuals on infection control. These were an important ingredient of our success.
Q. Is this how you developed the concept of 'veterinary dentistry'?
A. Yes. We've always sold some products to veterinarians. It's a market with great growth potential. And, we knew dentistry was a way for vets to generate some additional income. So, we worked with several universities to develop programs to teach it to them. Most vets in the country now practice it to some extent. This may be simple teeth cleaning, or more sophisticated procedures like dental implants. We do a lot of work with zoos, where they're caring for some very expensive animals.
Q.Although two-thirds of all dental practices in the United States and Europe are your clients, dollar-wise you hold a relatively small share of the market. Why?
A. Because, up until last year, we were precluded from the large equipment market. We sold less expensive, consumable goods. Now, we've purchased a company which has gotten us into high ticket items, like medical and dental diagnostic equipment. But we're still the Wal-Mart of this business, not the Bloomingdale's. We appeal to the cost and value-conscious consumer. We introduce new equipment when the prices settle down.
Q. How has managed care affected your business?
A. Hospitals and drugstores were the first to feel reimbursement pressures. But that revolution occurred about 10 years ago. Now, it's our customers -- the doctors and dentists -- who are feeling the pressure. They're expected to do a lot more procedures while getting less and less reimbursement. So we're in an environment that's very concerned with cost containment.
We're also witnessing the "industrialization" of health care. Networks are forming -- not in a major way yet on Long Island, but in California, for example, there are businesses which buy practices. They want structured purchasing arrangements. So we try to get prime vendor contracts with them. If they agree to buy only from us, we can negotiate better prices with the manufacturers, because they're getting more business. So far, we have contracts with the U.S. Army and several major U.S. dental schools. And we've implemented a low price guarantee, just like when you buy a TV at Nobody Beats the Wiz. So far, we're the only ones doing that in health care.
Q. You have extended your marketing efforts beyond just direct mail?
A. Yes. Our competitors tend to market either through direct mail, telephone sales, or through field sales consultants -- the people who sell door-to-door. But we use all three. We're the only ones in this business using that mix. Before that, we were basically waiting for our phones to ring, waiting for the catalogue orders to come in. Now we have catalogues which let you order by touch-tone phone or electronically. We back them up with tele-sales, and then we send out field sales consultants to cement the relationships. It's been very cost-effective, and a very productive way of generating sales.
Q. When you needed more space, you chose to expand on Long Island. Why?
A. Because we have one of the best tele-sales operations in the whole health care industry right here, and that's hard to replace. These aren't just salespeople -- they have to know health care products. We either hire people who can sell and then teach them about the products, or we hire people like dental assistants, veterinary assistants and nurses -- people who know the products -- and teach them how to sell. This is a highly skilled work force. We could open a tele-sales center in Nebraska and pay people less. But it would be hard to get that expertise. And, we got assistance from the state, Suffolk County and the Town of Huntington to stay here.
Q. Which of your markets is best poised for growth?
A. The medical market is a $4 billion market, and we have a relatively small share, so there's some good growth potential there. And the international market is infinite. You just can't do everything at once.

HEADLINE: Connecticut Q&A: Vahid Mohsenin;
Need for Sleep in a Cacophonous World

BYLINE: By ALIX BOYLE

 BODY:
ALMOST everybody needs more sleep. Newborns do tend to get the 20 hours a day they need, but by high school -- what with television, homework and social pressures -- youngsters are getting as little as 6 hours a night. Working adults, stressed-out by job pressures and also glued to the television, are equally sleep-deprived. And elderly people tend to throw their nighttime sleep off schedule by napping during the day.
Then there are the medical sleep disorders: insomnia affects 40 million Americans, and 400,000 have narcolepsy. Fifty percent of adult men snore, a sign of sleep apnea, which affects an estimated 18 million people in the United States. 
 This is the picture presented by Vahid Mohsenin, an associate professor of medicine at the Yale University School of Medicine and director of the Yale Sleep Disorders Program. Dr. Mohsenin, who attended medical school at Shiraz University in Iran and completed his training in San Francisco and New Haven, discussed his work in a recent interview. Following are excerpts from the interview:
Q. Are we getting enough sleep?
A. The consensus in the United States is that we're not. Over the last 100 years or so, we've lost about an hour and a half to two hours from our nighttime sleep due to many societal and environmental changes: industrialization, the need for shift work, a 24-hour society, economic needs and competitiveness. People try to adapt to sleep loss over time, but then insidiously, they start experiencing problems at work, problems at home; they're spending less time with the family and friends. Yes, we're chronically sleep deprived.
Q. How much sleep do we need?
A. It's estimated between seven and eight hours. Kids need more, about eight and a half to nine hours, because they need to grow. Just one or two hours makes a major difference in their performance.
Older people tend to divide their sleep; they nap in the daytime and get less sleep at night. On average they get the same amount of sleep, but they spread it out.
Q. How do we develop good sleep hygiene?
A. You have to have a regular bedtime and a regular rising time. No work-related things, no television, in the bedroom. No coffee, no alcoholic beverages, within two to three hours of sleeping. It's a common myth that alcohol is good for sleep. Yes, you do fall asleep, but then your sleep quality is actually markedly disturbed in frequent wakenings or disruptions. Medications that can interfere with sleep should be taken in the morning. A small snack would be useful before bedtime. Smoking is not good. Reading something boring is good.
Q. How does insomnia start?
A. If it takes too long to fall asleep, people start to get nervous and then they get into a vicious cycle. They start concentrating on their sleep pattern and the lack of sleep in the nighttime, then they have a preoccupation in the daytime about not being able to sleep the night before. They get into a cycle we call psychophysiological insomnia.
Q. What's the treatment?
A. You treat the underlying medical or psychiatric condition -- heart conditions, diabetes and depression, for example. You treat the conditions with behavior therapy. Or you can use sleeping medications, but not for more than three to five weeks because people develop a tolerance to them and become dependent.
Q. What are the main sleep disorders?
A. There are four.
There are those that originate from within the body like sleep apnea, or narcolepsy or seizure disorders. There's something within the body physics that creates these problems.
Then there are external factors like noise, shift work, time-zone changes.
There are primary disorders not related to sleep itself, what are called parasomnias -- night terrors, sleepwalking, bedwetting and REM behavior disorders, people acting out their dreams. There's nothing wrong with their sleep, but other mechanisms are faulty.
Finally, people who abuse drugs and alcohol have trouble. Sometimes they have a transient sleep problem, then they define alcohol as being the treatment. Or they abuse sleeping medications; they get hooked.
Q. What role does sleep play in traffic accidents?
A. Sleep deprivation is a major cause of accidents. The National Commission of Sleep Disorders, formed by Congress in the late 1980's, came up with staggering statistics.
The Exxon Valdez accident was directly related to the third mate's lack of sleep in the preceding 48 hours. It was not due to the alcohol consumption of the captain. The captain was not in charge at the time; the third mate was. They were loading cargo and he did not get more than like six or seven hours of sleep in the preceding two days. Then, despite lookouts telling him about the reefs, he didn't hear them or couldn't pay attention and he ran aground.
Most of the major industrial accidents occur between midnight and 6 A.M. Most truckers get into trouble in those hours despite the fact that there are fewer trucks on the road. Truckers are known to be sleep-deprived. A survey by the Department of Transportation found that on average they get five hours of sleep.
Q. Melatonin is being touted as cure for insomnia. What do you think?
A. Melatonin is a natural hormone produced by the pineal gland and it surges in the body around sleep time and falls off before rising time. Elderly people lose the diurnal pattern of secretion. This group, in addition to transmeridian travelers, pilots and shift workers, can benefit from melatonin. In the general population, melatonin has not been shown to be effective.
Q. What is narcolepsy?
A. An abnormality of the neurotransmitters in the brain that regulate the sleep and awake state. It causes excessive daytime sleepiness; people have an urge to nap and have spells of muscle weakness and falling. The treatment is medication: stimulants like Ritalin will keep people awake during the day and tri-cyclic antidepressants will prevent weakness.
Q. What is sleep apnea?
A. The cessation of breathing during sleep; people and can't get oxygen. If they stop breathing more than 20 times per hour then they are at major risk of developing hypertension, stroke, heart attack, and congestive heart failure.
It's a very serious condition if it goes untreated. It's estimated that there is $400 million of direct cost associated with treating sleep apnea not to mention the indirect cost of loss of productivity, loss of property. People wake up in the morning and feel as if they never slept. They don't have any recollection of the awakenings because they were very short. The brain doesn't register anything unless you're awakened for more than three minutes.
Some people tell me they gasp for air, but most say they sleep through. When you ask them the next morning, "How many times were you awake last night?" they say one time, two times. But if you monitor them in the laboratory, they awakened 100 times. That's the crux of this problem because every time they're aroused, the blood pressure goes up, so they are exposed to these multiple episodes of hypertension during sleep.
Q. How do people realize they have sleep apnea and then get help?
A. They are brought in by their bed partner or spouse because they are snoring loudly, thrashing around. They come in because they can't function during the daytime, they can't stay awake, their mood changes, they become depressed, they develop hypertension. They get involved with accidents and injuries; they go to cardiologists for heart failure, hypertension; they go to psychologists and psychiatrists because of depression and mood changes. It's not recognized commonly because doctors are not sensitive to these symptoms.
Q. What are the treatments for sleep apnea?
A. The standard treatments now are dental prostheses, devices that create more space behind the tongue. This only works in people who have mild apnea.
Nasal CPAP (continuous positive airway pressure) is a pump connected to a mask worn over the nose that will provide the pressure throughout the respiratory system preventing the collapse of the airways. It's highly effective, compliance is reasonable, and many people benefit from this. It's not ideal but it's the best we have.
Surgery and laser, to open up the airway, is reserved for people with specific conditions.

HEADLINE: Keeping Legal Help Reachable to the Poor

BYLINE: By MELINDA TUHUS

 BODY:
DESPITE fears of restructuring legal services for poor people, a consensus among lawyers in the field seems to be building that, "It's not as bad as we feared it would be."
The backdrop to the changes is a 30-year battle by conservatives to reduce or eliminate support for the Legal Services Corporation, the federally funded Great Society-era umbrella agency that oversees legal help to the poor -- whether it be landlord-tenant disputes, consumer fraud or family issues like domestic violence, child custody and divorce. 
 Last year Congress voted to slash the corporation's budget by one third, to $278 million; eliminate specialized projects including those for migrant workers, and prohibit lawyers who receive any income from the corporation from filing class action suits (often used in the past to alter public policy), challenging new welfare laws or lobbying the government on behalf of their clients.
Until last year's changes, Connecticut's legal help for poor people consisted of four agencies: the New Haven Legal Assistance Association, serving New Haven with a branch office in Derby; Neighborhood Legal Services and the Legal Aid Society of Hartford, both serving Hartford, and Connecticut Legal Services, serving the rest of the state.
The new Federal mandate initiated a great debate and a lot of soul-searching among staff attorneys about whether to take the money -- with the strings attached -- or refuse it to maintain their options, albeit with considerably less funding. What they came up with was a compromise, a two-tiered system they hope will allow maximum flexibility.
The directors of the legal help groups pooled their Federal money and created Statewide Legal Services, an entity that is abiding by the restrictions laid down by Congress. Meanwhile, the two Hartford agencies (soon to merge into one) and the New Haven office, with no Federal funding, remain free to file class-action suits and to lobby.
Formerly, clients would walk in or call the offices and be screened to determine what kind of help they needed and how quickly they needed it. Some -- victims of spousal abuse or those threatened with eviction -- were given help immediately.
Now, when people contact one of the offices, they talk to a receptionist who gives them a toll-free number for Statewide Legal Services, where they are screened by a central staff. They are often asked to fax or mail in relevant material. If the person is eligible, help is provided either by lawyers at the central office, or the case may be sent back to the office where the initial contact was made.
Glenn Falk, a long-time lawyer in the New Haven office, argued that his office should take the Federal money, even with the strings attached. "There was $1.5 million for Connecticut that came with restrictions," said Mr. Falk. "That's 30 lawyers' worth of money. That's an awful lot of helping people. I'm not so naive as to think that class action suits are not important -- I sued the New Haven Housing Authority numerous times."
But by taking the money, he felt, attorneys could provide concrete legal help to more poor people while the public policy advocacy pursued through class-action suits and lobbying could be done -- and was being done -- by many other agencies.
Mr. Falk said he feared people who need help would fall through the cracks. He worries especially about illiterate clients who are likely to have a harder time navigating the new system.
Amy Eppler-Epstein, a colleague of Mr. Falk's in the New Haven office, took a completely different view. "I'm really glad we made the choice we did," she said. "It was certainly not without pain. Part of the pain was that a couple of intake screeners were laid off. But I think the telephone screening is beginning to work pretty successfully and better than I was afraid it might.
"We are getting lots of people referred back to us," she continued. "I know some people have not been able to get through on the phone; I also know that, before, people would wait for three hours in our waiting room."
She said that people with mental disabilities or those with complicated paperwork would still be seen in the office rather than being given the toll-free number.
She added that neither the old system nor the new one was perfect; "the frustration with both," she said, "is the lack of resources compared to the problem." But she doubted that more people were falling through the cracks now than under the old system. "I used to get 40 cases a week in the housing unit," she said, "and that's what we get now."
Patricia Kaplan, director of the New Haven office, is even more positive about the changes. Given both the Federal cutbacks and a 50 percent drop in Interest on Lawyers' Trust Accounts funds due to the sluggish economy, she says, "Setting up this new entity was a godsend because it meant I could carve off my intake function, and it can be done more efficiently from a central location. That meant I could keep my advocacy staff of lawyers and paralegals."
Norman Janes, former director of Connecticut Legal Services and now director of the new Statewide Legal Services, calls the new plan "an excellent response to a difficult situation. I call this functional specialization." Funding cuts over the years have meant a drop in total staff throughout the state from 185 in 1980 to 112 in 1995 to 88 in 1996.
Mr. Janes says the fact that his agency is barred from taking on class action suits is not a problem "because we are part of a network that will get people what they need."
He expects the organization to handle 45,000 to 50,000 clients this year. He adds that it's hard to make an exact comparison with 1995, but that the numbers are very similar. "From 9 to 3 the phones are busy constantly."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Battling Dumpsites in Poor Neighborhoods

BYLINE: By MELINDA TUHUS

 BODY:
WHAT do urban parks and illegal dumping have to do with environmental justice?
Plenty, according to those involved with the environmental equity program of the State Department of Environmental Protection, which announced its policy in 1993: "No segment of the population should bear a disproportionate share of the risks or consequences of environmental pollution or be denied access to environmental benefits, such as parks and forests, fishing and outdoor recreation." 
 To address the issues, the department proposed several ways to promote environmental health as well as to stimulate economic development in urban areas. To carry out the program, the department's air, waste and water management bureaus prepared plans.
The department also created regional environmental justice community advisory boards in Hartford and New Haven that meet monthly and provide environmental activists, neighborhood residents and representatives of municipal departments an opportunity to hash out problems and discuss solutions.
In October 1994 the department helped sponsor a statewide conference with the Connecticut Civil Liberties Union that attracted 350 people.
The environmental equity program is led by Edith Pestana, a public health and environmental consultant.
"The D.E.P. founded the environmental equity program," Ms. Pestana said, "in response to the growing body of evidence both nationally and statewide that indicates that low-income, racial and ethnic minority groups are exposed to higher than average amounts of environmental pollution."
Sources of exposure, she says, include illegal dumping, sewer overflow, toxic air emissions, leaking underground storage tanks, solid- and hazardous-waste disposal centers, abandoned hazardous-waste sites and substandard housing.
Ms. Pestana pointed out that the success of environmental organizations and governmental agencies in preventing the establishment of hazardous-waste sites in other areas pushes such sites to areas where poor people live.
One way to help safeguard poorer communities from getting more than a fair share of such sites, Ms. Pestana says, is to create partnerships among industry, municipalities and the community. Another is to minimize the creation of pollution anywhere.
A recent meeting in New Haven brought together more than a dozen people from community groups, the Yale School of Forestry and Environmental Studies and the city's parks, business development and public works departments.
A detailed discussion of the number of urban dumpsites that have been cleaned up so far (140) took place. Garbage that littered sites, it was reported, is being hauled away and in its place is something much more attractive: wood chips. "Wood chips are magic," says Ms. Pestana. "It gives the impression that there's ownership there. It's not an abandoned lot and it looks like it's going to be put to use. So people say, 'I better not dump here.' " The first lots were cleaned more than a year ago and some have been turned into community gardens.
In case the magic is not strong enough to keep dumpers at bay, however, the activists are aiming to increase their arsenal.
The city is proposing legislation to increase the fine for illegal dumping from its current $99 a day -- which many say is low enough to make dumping worth the risk -- to perhaps 10 times that much.
New Haven is also considering putting a lien on the personal property of those caught dumping illegally. Both these actions require state approval. The environmental equity program has pledged to support the city in its efforts.
Carmen Mendez, a development planner with the New Haven Department of Public Works, says, "Illegal dumpers' attitude is that it's O.K. to dump in poor, minority neighborhoods. Our attitude, in terms of environmental justice, is that it's not O.K."
Among other weapons are palm cards being distributed in targeted neighborhoods and signs posted at former dumpsites; the signs urge residents to report illegal dumping and encourage them to help maintain the cleaned-up sites.
"We let neighbors know they also have a responsibility to their neighborhood," says Ms. Mendez. "Some people feel, 'Someone else should clean it up because I didn't dump it there.' We understand that, but it's not the most helpful attitude."
Another issue for the New Haven advisory board is West Rock Ridge State Park. Once a city park, it was given to the state because New Haven lacked the money to operate it. When the state took over, it added other property to the park but closed the paved drive to the top of the traprock ridge and Three Judges Cave, a popular destination. As a result, though the park is technically open to hikers and bikers, it is inaccessible to many people: those with younger children in tow, elderly men and women, and people who get around in wheelchairs, for example.
Shortly after the meeting, the state agreed to open the park on weekends from spring through fall.
The Environmental Protection Department's commissioner, Sidney Holbrook, says of the decision, "It is a direct result of feedback received from local residents and the advisory board. This is just one example of the results than can be seen as the D.E.P. works more closely with urban communities in response to their needs for access to natural resources."
The mandate of the environmental equity program is broad, encompassing research and follow-up of exposures to environmental hazards in both the community and the workplace. In collaboration with the State Health Department and Southeast Asian community organizations, for example, fish advisories have been translated into Cambodian, Laotian, Vietnamese and Hmong.
Immigrants from Southeast Asia as well as from Latin America (the advisories have also been published in Spanish) have traditionally eaten large amounts of fish and therefore depend disproportionately on seafood from polluted waters.
Tangible successes include the clean-up of 200 illegal dumpsites in urban areas around the state. And, in New Haven at least, there seems to be a lot of good will on both sides.
Shunte Corley is a New Haven high school student and a member of the Natural Guard, a group of New Haven young people who work on environmental justice issues.
Mr. Corley is impressed by what he's seen so far. "I think they're doing a lot," he said, "because they're taking their time to come down here and work in neighborhoods that some people feel no one ever does anything for."
He said the group went to the program with a list of sites that needed a cleanup "and they acted immediately and got things done, so we really appreciate that." One of the worst eyesores was a big dumpsite adjacent to a community garden in the Newhallville section of the city, which is where Mr. Corley lives. Now, it is clean.
Diana Edmonds, executive director of the Natural Guard (which created the community garden), said of the D.E.P., "They have really come through with everything they said they'd do."
"But," she added, "the community advisory board is not representative of all the neighborhoods and issues" that should be at the table.
Ms. Edmonds says it's not for lack of trying on the part of those already active to get more people involved, but that people are overwhelmed with meetings and other commitments.
There are more city employees than residents from the targeted neighborhoods at the meetings, although, Ms. Pestana points out, some municipal workers are also city residents.
In Hartford, the program suffered a major setback recently when, despite support from the environmental equity program, the Environmental Protection Department granted a permit to expand the regional landfill over neighbors' opposition.
"The D.E.P. couldn't find any regulatory reason not to grant the permit," Ms. Pestana said. "The community is upset about it. That's one of the reasons it's been difficult to get people out in Hartford -- they're disappointed with that decision."
She added that her program pushed the department to require more stringent regulation of the operator, and a permit for expansion would not be issued until some compliance issues were resolved. Ms. Pestana described the situation as a good example of the tightrope walked between state bureaucracy and the communities most affected by pollution. "It's all about trust."

HEADLINE: Corrections

 BODY:
An article in the Neighborhood Report last Sunday about the Pathmark supermarket planned for East Harlem misstated the nature of a $1 million low-interest loan being sought by the developers, a consortium of nonprofit groups called the East Harlem Abyssinian Triangle, from the Empire State Development Corporation. The loan is not a new request, but was part of the original financing package. The article also misstated the portion of the financing package that consists of government and private grants, low-interest city loans and promised tax breaks. It is roughly one-third, not one-half.

PUB-DATE: June 30, 1996

SECTION: Section 13; Page 1; Column 1; The City Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 3023 words

HEADLINE: To Be Young, Indian and Hip

BYLINE: By SOMINI SENGUPTA 

 BODY:
JAY DABHI, 20, steps out of the dim red deejay booth, lights a Newport and scans his territory, the main floor of Planet 28 in Chelsea. It is just after midnight on a warm spring Friday, and the discotheque is thick with smoke and attitude. Over to the right, in front of the mirrored wall, an ex-girlfriend of Jay's in a retro halter, puckering her red lips around a red straw, tries not to gaze at herself. On the sidelines, young men sporting carefully pruned goatees stand in crescent-shaped clusters.
The house is packed with young Indians and Pakistanis in satin mini-skirts and perfectly slack blue jeans that hang just-so over their sneakers. This is a strictly "desi" crowd, desi being the Hindi word for "homeboy" or "homegirl." It seems as if everybody at Planet 28 is checking out everybody else. There is a crisp post-adolescent tension in the air. A bad-boy bravado. Jay, known in this scene as deejay Lil Jay, can taste it. "Gonna be a fight," he says from his perch. "Too many guys." 
 He turns around, climbs over a man in dark glasses who seems to have passed out on a worn couch and returns to his booth. He scratches for a few bars, throws in the Fugees and then, some Junior Mafia, its "get muh-ney" chorus punctuated by a mystery voice that cries "India" on the downbeat, over and over like a mantra. The dancing masses nod in unison. Later, Lil Jay tosses a few surprises -- a Hindi song from the popular Bombay movie, "Disco Dancer" or a perennial "bhangra" favorite, a folk song from the wheat fields of Punjab, laid over a frenzied house track.
A distinctly South Asian youth culture, rooted in hip-hop and Hindi pop, is flourishing in New York City, as children of the growing numbers of immigrants from the Indian subcontinent come of age. Lil Jay, along with the dozens of deejays and party promoters, is one of its creators and caretakers.
The fruits of their subculture can be found this night at Planet 28 -- one of several clubs and restaurants rented out by party promoters and packed by crowds of South Asian teen-agers and college students. It can be found in the hip-hop groove Lil Jay mixes with a classic Hindi ballad, in the rumble that breaks out at Planet 28 between young boys in informal cliques, in the tapes and CD's produced in local studios and basements and sold in Jackson Heights music shops, in the culture clash between young South Asians looking to loosen the strictures of their subcontinental parents.
Although this group is neither old enough nor has been around long enough to make an impact on the city's cultural mainstream, Jay and his friends offer a window into a new generation's acculturation. And an important part of this is the music and club scene that many people would write off as frivolous. Lil Jay's musical specialty is Hindi remix, a melange of pop songs from Bombay movie musicals and house, rap, reggae, tribal -- whatever gets the crowd dancing. Grounded on the principle of sampling and syncretism and spawned by the British-Asian musical brew "bhangra," Hindi remix is a fitting sound for Jay's generation -- South Asian youths nourished by two worlds, trying to create their American style.
The task requires delicate balance, says Madhulika Khandelwal, the acting director of the Queens College Asian-American Center. "They're struggling with both parts -- how to package the Indian part and how to find their place in American society," she says. "I see in them a big confusion in where they fit in the racial schema in the United States."
In recent years, a number of academic books and articles have wrestled with the way in which the children of today's immigrants are assimilating into American culture. Will the second generation, the scholars ask, follow many of their European predecessors and gradually assume American cultural norms and climb the economic ladder? Or as members of racial minority groups, will their experience be different?
Whatever. So far, one thing is certain: this generation of South Asians wants to have fun.
 
 
 
'Hoody Down
 Jay and his friends don't see themselves as white. Nor do they describe themselves as Asian, except when it is to their advantage, as when they have a hard time renting clubs. Indian parties have acquired a reputation for rowdiness -- partly as a result of frequent fights between cliques -- so "when we want a space, we don't say it's an Indian party, we say it's an Asian party," Jay says.
At Planet 28, Jay correctly predicts a rumble. By 1 A.M., the line outside has grown long and impatient. On the main floor, the crowd moves to Jay's mix. Vodka tonics are poured, joints lighted up in the ladies bathroom.
Downstairs, DJ Red, named after his naturally reddish locks, spins his specialty reggae. In a flash, two young men start pushing and shoving. One belongs to Punjabi by Nature, or PBN, a clique for those whose parents come from the northern Indian state of Punjab. The other is Afghani and hangs with Madina, a crew of Pakistani and Indian Muslims.
Jay rushes downstairs to mediate, but the brawl quickly spills out into the street. A friend of Jay's grabs a beer bottle and shatters it over the head of a PBN member before the police arrive to break it up.
Later that night a PBN carload cruises through his neighborhood. The PBN crew spots another teen-ager who was in the fight outside Planet 28. Shots are fired. A bullet grazes the back of the young man's head.
Jay describes the night the next afternoon. He has called the PBN guys, urged them to chill. But he says he is tired of mediating, tired of watching the same thuggery every weekend. If it wasn't for the music and money, he would find something else to do. "They're boring," he says of the clubs. "Same people. Same fights. Same gossip."
Gang rivalries have quieted down in the last year, Jay and his friends say. Some boys have matured. Others have gone to jail. Some cliques have disbanded and the activities of others are limited to occasional brawling and gunfights along with credit-card and illegal phone scams. "They're not real gangsters," Jay says.
At Planet 28, Jalak Patel, 19, a frequent clubgoer, attributes the violence to machismo. "It's like an ego thing," she says. "They can't even say sorry if they bump into you. They have no class. They come all, like, 'hoody down. They don't care. They just want to fight."
'Hoody means tough. Its traces can be detected in a swagger, an accent, a hairdo, the rolling of an eye, a weapon.
But it's hard to break the club habit. Jalak and a friend, Sonal Patel, 18, have been doing the scene since they were 15. Just about every weekend there's a new party to check out, a new promoter renting some new nightclub in Manhattan and charging $10 to $15 a head to get in.
At Planet 28, the dance floor is bumping with 'hoodies. Tommy Hilfiger's red, white and blue gear -- the sartorial rage of the hip-hop nation -- dominates. Near the bar, a lanky young man has corn-rowed his hair in a style reminiscent of an earlier Snoop Doggy Dogg. But as in many nightspots popular among the desi crowd, the only black people are security guards.
"Indian people are secluded," Jay observes. "They like to hang out only with themselves."
 
 
 
Knowing the Culture, and the Guys
 Hetal Patel (no relation to Jalak or Sonal) hangs only in the desi scene. In the summertime, much of her time is spent in her Jackson Heights home watching Hindi movies on ITV, the local Indian cable station, or waiting for her favorite Bollywood stars (Bollywood is Bombay's Hollywood) to lip-sync their latest hits or dipping into an Indian chat room on the Internet. Hetal, 17, who is headed for York College in the fall, does not go to hip-hop shows or rock concerts. She rarely watches a Hollywood movie and buys non-Hindi pop music only occasionally. "I'm not interested," she says.
She is interested in scenes like the one at the Nassau Coliseum one recent evening. A main attraction of the five-hour marathon performance by the Bollywood heartthrob Shahrukh Khan and other entertainers was the cruising that went on in the lobby before, during and after. "Oh my God," Hetal whispers to her sister, Krupa, "All my exes are here." Hetal is busy meeting and greeting tonight, coy with the boys who seem interesting or interested, short with girls she doesn't particularly like.
"These are my own people," she gushes. "I feel we share something. We're all Indian. We all know our heritage. We all know our culture." Onstage, Mr. Khan prances about in snug black leather pants, lip-syncing to a hit from a recent film. Hetal and Krupa mouth the words, grooving in their seats. Later, Mr. Khan offers a shout-out to "the Gujaratis in the house." Hetal and Krupa scream and cheer for their parents' home state. Then, he offers a shout-out to "the Pakistanis in the house." The sisters whisper "Boo."
 
 
 
Outside Desiland
 Far from Hetal's desi universe, Mike Khahera of Floral Park, Queens is steeped in the world of the hip-hop kingpin Afrika Bambaataa's Universal Zulu Nation. Nation members call themselves a youth organization, but its detractors, including Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, call it a dangerous gang.
Mike is 18 and burly, the youngest son of Punjabi immigrants. Fresh out of Martin Van Buren High School, he works at the local Taco Bell.
He and his brother Mac, a student at St. John's, are waiting for Jay on their parents' front lawn one afternoon. Mike clutches a pair of Zulu Nation amulets. He waits until Lil Jay drives up before slipping them on. His parents are fiercely disapproving of his involvement in Shaka Zulu, the Nation's security squad, he says, so he doesn't wear the amulets around the house.
Afrika Bambaataa, Mike announces, is interested in the sounds of bhangra and Hindi remix. Mike led him on a tour of Jackson Heights record stores last weekend and today, he wants to hook him up with Lil Jay. "Be cool," Mike's older brother, Mac, instructs Jay. "Say 'whassup, whassup!' He's real cool. He's got a lotta juice."
It's an exciting prospect. Jay is itching to venture beyond the Indian scene, to have a broader, more diverse following. He asks Mike how to join the Nation, if they have a chapter for deejays.
Mike, who was turned onto Zulu Nation by some non-Indian friends in Queens Village, has been asked to recruit some Indians to start a chapter in Jackson Heights, but he is a bit skeptical. Mike prefers his black and Hispanic friends over the Indian scene. "I just don't like chillin' with them," he says. "See, a lot of Indian people, they see me wearing Zulu stuff. They don't know nothing about Zulu Nation. But they'll be, like, 'Oh, he's trying to be black.' They don't ask. A lot of Indian people think they're better than blacks."
 
 
 
New Worlds? No Thanks
 A few years ago, Jay wasn't hanging in the Indian scene either. Most of his friends were Hispanic. They lived in the same neighborhood. They looked alike and dressed the same: hair slicked back, jeans hanging perfectly over sneakers, fixed to the ankles by carefully concealed rubber bands. They liked the same kind of music: free style and house, KRS-One and Tribe Called Quest. "Back then," he said, "it was, like, the Indians didn't party."
Then in October 1990, Jay reluctantly checked out a party at the Gujarati Samaj, a community center in Flushing frequented by his father and his uncle. That's where he heard Hindi remix for the first time. The next year, he heard the British deejay Bally Sagoo's seminal album, "Star Crazy." "After 'Star Crazy,' it all blew up," he says. "I was, like, wow! I had never heard Indian music mixed before. So I started doing it."
He was barely 14, a scrawny kid who had to perch on top of a milk crate to reach the turntables. (The nickname Lil Jay has stuck ever since.)
The Gujarati Samaj party scene exploded in the next couple of years. But the gang fights got so heated that no one wanted to party there anymore. Lil Jay slipped deeper into the desi scene. There were India Day parades to attend. Shows at the Nassau Coliseum to check out, if you wanted to pick up girls. Clubs to be at. Clubs to spin at. Albums to produce. The first was two years ago for a Queens rapper who calls himself Style Bhai. (Bhai is the Hindi word for brother.) The second, Jay's own remix collection, is due out later this summer.
These days, he occasionally runs into his Hispanic friends from the neighborhood, gets a quick update under the rumble of the el along Roosevelt Avenue. But Jay's closest friends are Indian. He sees them on the Indian party circuit. They all have Indian girlfriends. And they won't say a word about the non-Indian women they have dated.
Only one of Jay's friends dates a non-Indian, a young woman from Puerto Rico. Jay's girlfriend, Vicky Sheth, 19, is not only Indian; her family, like his, is from the Gujarat province. While dating a non-Indian woman would open up new worlds, the thought is also scary. "You would learn a lot, I guess," Jay says. "But you also couldn't share a lot. Like, I can talk to my girl in my language. We have a lot in common. If her parents say 'You can't go nowhere,' I understand. She's Indian."
 
 
 
A Defining Difference
 In most Indian families there is a decided difference between how boys and girls are raised. Jay's sister, Binita, 28, scowls when she compares her teen-age years with her younger brother's. Binita was held to curfews. Jay gets to come home at any hour. "He gets away with more than I ever did in my whole lifetime," she says.
Sulabhi Dabhi stirs the Saturday afternoon tea for her husband, Pravin, in silence, wordlessly affirming her daughter's pronouncement.
Jay is the Dabhis' only boy, the baby. He was born under a bright morning sun, she says, "so the nighttime is his time." Still, she frets and fusses over his safety. Will he get caught up in a rumble? Will he get shot? Her concern has dampened her initial enthusiasm for her son's immersion in the Indian scene. "Until he is here," she says pointing to the couch, "I am right here."
Jay is his mother's boy. She, his advocate. He has inherited her delicate skin, her quick, dancing eyes and her passion for music. Occasionally, she brings home Hindi tapes for him to sample. She knows which voice belongs to whom, which song belongs to which film, its plot, its stars, the year it was made.
"Who is that, Ma?" Jay says looking up from his tape deck. It's a melodramatic Hindi ballad jammed into a middle of a boisterous 11-minute house mix, one of nine tracks on Lil Jay's coming album.
She holds up her right index finger, squints up at the ceiling. "Disco Dancer, na?" she guesses. "Must be Amit Kumar or Kishore. Yes, Kishore. 1982." She hands her husband his milky tea.
Mr. Dabhi, an accountant at Atlantic Records, doesn't pay much attention to the goings-on in the music industry (except, he is fond of recounting, the time Donna Summer came into his office in search of a phone). And he cautions Jay against a career in music. "He wants me to own a candy store," Jay says.
Mr. Dabhi demurs. "It's up to him," he says. "My main goal is that he gets a degree, at least one degree. I always tell him, 'No one can take that away.' "
Jay groans as his father recalls his earlier years of toil in America -- first earning his business degree and now working at Atlantic and running his candy store on Roosevelt Avenue on weekends. "C'mon, we're Indian," Jay pipes up.
Jay is anything but certain about his future. He cannot see himself behind a candy store counter, or poring over accounting ledgers. He tells his father he will graduate with a business degree from St. John's University, where he is a sophomore, but he cannot drum up the least bit of enthusiasm for a business degree, or the career it might lead to.
Jay is deep into his music. And he makes a good living at it, as much as $300 a party. Still he is dead-set against pursuing it professionally. Too unstable, he says. He'd like a salaried job. A paycheck every week. A wife and some babies. A house in Queens, maybe on Long Island. Preferably a mixing studio in the basement.
With a summer job at a capital management company in Manhattan, his girlfriend, also a sophomore at St. John's, seems well on her way into corporate America. "You know what's the biggest influence on me and my friends?" he says later. "Parents, but also girlfriends. Girlfriends nag. They're always like 'Go to school. Go to school.' "
For Jay, the reopening of school in September will mean the start of the serious party season. Passing out fliers. Searching out new clubs. Making money. He hopes that by then, his own remix album will be out. He'll take it himself to Indo-Pak music shops around town, and maybe hire a distributor to take it to South Asian groceries and record shops around the country. Marketing Hindi remix beyond the desi circuit is beyond his wildest imagination. "They won't buy it. Will they?" he wonders. "Even white people?"
His album will feature one original track: his friend Dee Rock rapping over Alisha's high soprano hit, "Made in In-di-aah." There's a Caribbean lilt in Dee Rock's rhymes, offering homage to the deejay. "Lil Jay dropping bombs like the U.S. on Hiroshima." The rest are remixes of American and Indian pop. "All illegal," Jay says gleefully. Underground remix tapes are a risky business. Indian music producers have recently begun suing Indian-American remix artists for lifting their songs without permission, and deejays, Jay says, have lifted each other's remixes and packaged them as new music.
Sitting in his living room, Jay plays his demo tape. One track weaves the delicate voice of Bombay singer Asha Bhosle with the booming refrain, "Shake what your mama gave 'ya." Jay doesn't understand much more than a few stray words. He furrows his brows and tries to translate. "Come dance? Come dance hard? Or something like that," he says tentatively. "So that's why it goes with 'shake what your mama gave ya.' " Then he giggles and peers into the kitchen to make sure his Mama doesn't hear him.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

COUNTRY: INDIA (92%); ASIA (91%); UNITED STATES (90%); PAKISTAN (79%); SOUTHERN ASIA (54%);

PERSON: JAY DABHI (95%); LIL JAY (79%);
