SECTION: Section 13; Page 1; Column 3; The City Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 797 words

HEADLINE: COPING;
Suspicion: The Renters' Story

BYLINE: By ROBERT LIPSYTE

 BODY:
THE chicken was perfectly cooked. I swore to that, but Mildred Blechman, recently blind, distrustful of her new, giant Lighthouse stove timer, wasn't so sure. She urged us to eat more of the rice, very healthy, and the cranberry sauce, refreshingly tart. The sky was just beginning to darken beyond the 24th-floor windows of the Waterside Plaza apartment, but she couldn't see the East River below, or the needles of midtown, still sparkling to the west. She couldn't read the United Nations reports piling up or the legal brief that her husband, Rafael, was studying in preparation for the tenants' meeting. Macular degeneration had reduced the vision in one eye to near uselessness, then last Dec. 20 vision in the other eye disappeared as suddenly as if a shade had been pulled down.
"I really hate this," she said, looking in the direction of my voice. "It's another reason I don't want to move. At 78, I just don't want to have to get used to a place I've never seen." 
 Mr. Blechman, who is 85, insisted: "If they raise the rent, I want to move. I didn't work 60 years, and put a few bucks away for my old age to give it to some landlord."
The landlord in question is Richard Ravitch. His tenants now regret his failures in politics and baseball negotiations since he now has more time for them.
Twelve years ago, the Brooklyn-born Blechmans sold their Merrick, L.I., home and moved back into the city to be near their three children and the first of three grandchildren. They considered Waterside a renters' paradise, an interracial 1,500-apartment complex with good security, convenient shopping, community rooms, discount theater tickets, lavish holiday parties put on by the management (all thanks to Mr. Ravitch) and the feel of a small town. They paid only $1,322 per month (it is up to only $1,520 now) for a two-bedroom, two-bathroom apartment with eastern and southern views. This is below market value because the complex was built under the Mitchell-Lama laws intended to create affordable housing. Tenants had to qualify. There are less expensive apartments in the four-building complex for low-income families. The builder, Mr. Ravitch, got tax and mortgage breaks and, in exchange for a limit on profit, a limit on risk.
For the Blechmans, it was the good life of concerned middle-class seniors. Mr. Blechman, a retired certified public accountant, played bridge and golf. Mrs. Blechman, a semiretired interior designer, represented the Gray Panthers at the United Nations; Maggie Kuhn had personally picked her. They read current books, went to plays and concerts, wrote to politicians demanding that poor people and peace be given a chance. They spent the three winter months in warm Western states.
"In 1989, the place started going downhill," Mr. Blechman said. "Ravitch lost the Democratic mayoral primary, and he said, 'Now, I'll be a landlord.' "
"He said it to you?" I asked.
"Never met the man," Mr. Blechman said.
This was just before dinner, while he was giving me the tour. Set between the F.D.R. Drive and the East River around 25th Street, Waterside Plaza did seem like an oasis in the city; young familes and old folks strolled the plaza, kids skated and practiced smoking, all a footbridge away from First Avenue. But Mr. Blechman, who is a member of the tenants' executive council, pointed out leaks in the garage (where many cars had M.D. plates), and ugly patches in the concrete and the carpets, results, he said, of a
reduction in services and full-time personnel. He says many tenants think it is all part of a plot to raise rents to market value, drive the renters out and eventually sell the complex as a co-op. This may well be renters' paranoia and the demonization of Mr. Ravitch.
All of this was repeated and chewed over in the tenants' crowded, heated, two-hour meeting last Tuesday night. They listened to their president, Steve Smollens, and a council member, Jim Mahon, discuss the organization's lawsuit against Mr. Ravitch and the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development. It seeks to overturn a 27.5 percent rent increase over four years, surcharges and vacancy decontrol. The fighting words "rent strike" filled the air.
Back upstairs afterward, over dessert and coffee, Mr. Blechman was debriefed by Ms. Blechman.
"It's our fault," he said. "When we abandoned the poor and the homeless, the rich like Ravitch came after us. To get their welfare."
"We need to reach out to other tenants' groups," she said, ever the Panther.
"This is a microcosm of what's happening in the city, the entire country," he thundered. "An economy created for big business, the hell with everybody else. And I'm not going to pay for it."
"So, Ralph," Ms. Blechman asked, "where are we going to go?"
